# Ductus Decision Log

Decision-of-record for the Ductus language. One atomic decision per numbered entry: `N. <sentence>: <example> (§ref into SPEC.md)`.
This log is the lossless WHAT; SPEC.md is the normative elaboration holding the WHY — follow an entry's (§) reference for rationale and full nuance. SPEC.md must conform to this log; divergence between the two is a defect.
Ordering: generic → specific, topic-contiguous (## headers); reading any prefix yields a coherent partial model. Position carries this gradient; numbers are stable identities — never renumbered, never reused. New decisions take the next free integer and are placed by topic; amendments keep their number; revoked numbers are retired (gaps allowed).
Edit protocol: change this log FIRST, then update the referenced SPEC.md section to conform.
If you discover a contradiction, ambiguity, or incoherence in either document: stop and disclose; never silently resolve it.
Quarantine: the contradictions/ambiguities/incoherencies discovered in SPEC.md during serialization are recorded in DECISION_LOG_FINDINGS.md with their contested wordings (see that file for the live count); contested content is deliberately NOT serialized here until ruled on.

<!-- BEGIN LOG -->

## 001. Identity & Philosophy — 35 Rules

001-1. The specification is the authoritative source for the type system, evaluation model, and runtime semantics. (§1.1)
001-2. Implementation details (compiler internals, optimizations, runtime representation) are out of scope except where they constrain user-visible semantics. (§1.1)
001-3. Lexical and syntactic structure is specified directly in this specification and decision log; there is no separate grammar document. (§1.1)
001-4. This specification is the single authoritative source for the language's syntax and semantics. (§1.1)
001-5. The specification carries no postponed design decisions: every described feature is specified definitively and every excluded feature is excluded definitively with an idiomatic alternative. (§1.2)
001-6. Where the specification stops short it does so only by an explicit boundary — standard-library delegation or implementation-defined behavior — never a deferred decision. (§1.2)
001-7. Ductus is a general-purpose, statically typed language in which compositional reactive systems are first-class. (§1.3)
001-8. The language commits to no domain-specific primitives. (§1.3)
001-9. Every value has a concrete type known to the compiler at code generation time. (§1.3)
001-10. Most types are inferred from context; annotations are required only where inference is ambiguous or to pin a choice deliberately. (§1.3)
001-11. External state — module-level declarations, type definitions, signals, function parameters, and record fields as a property of types — is always immutable. (§1.3)
001-12. There is no module-level `mut` and no globally-mutable state. (§1.3)
001-13. Controlled local mutation is available inside function bodies through `mut` bindings. (§1.3)
001-14. Mutation is bounded to the function body that declared it; callers never observe a function's internal mutations except through its declared return value. (§1.3)
001-15. Time-varying external behavior is expressed through the reactive system, not through assignment. (§1.3)
001-16. Every value has exactly one real owner at any moment. (§1.3)
001-17. Function parameters default to borrow-equivalent access: the function gets a read-only alias of the caller's value for the call's duration, and the caller's binding survives. (§1.3)
001-18. Consumption of an argument is opt-in via `own` in the signature and `move` at the call site. (§1.3)
001-19. Returning transfers ownership to the caller when the result is a real owner or the signature opts into `-> own T`. (§1.3)
001-20. A default return rooted in a borrowed input is itself borrow-equivalent. (§1.3)
001-21. There is no garbage collector and no language-level reference counting; the runtime may use refcounting internally for specific types such as `string`. (§1.3)
001-22. There is no shared mutable state. (§1.3)
001-23. From the caller's perspective every user-defined function is referentially transparent: same inputs produce the same outputs with no externally observable side effects beyond the declared return value. (§1.3)
001-24. A function may use `mut` bindings, indexed assignment, and `while`/`for` loops internally; these are invisible at the call site. (§1.3)
001-25. Any expression not involving a signal or external input is compile-time evaluable. (§1.3)
001-26. Records, enums, traits, nodes, and connections are nominal: identity is by name, not structure. (§1.3)
001-27. Tuples, closure types, and trait-constraint intersections are explicit structural exceptions to nominal typing. (§1.3)
001-28. Behavior abstraction uses nominal traits with explicit `satisfies`/`fulfill` declarations. (§1.3)
001-29. Trait coherence is enforced structurally via orphan rules. (§1.3)
001-30. Methods and free functions are interchangeable syntactic forms for the same operation: `f(x, y)` may equivalently be called as `x.f(y)`. (§1.3)
001-31. Operator application uses the `|>` token. (§1.3)
001-32. `>>` is bitwise right shift only, never operator application. (§1.3)
001-33. Bug-class failures trap and abort the process; there is no catch mechanism. (§1.3)
001-34. Recoverable conditions are expressed as `Option`/`Result` values with `?` propagation. (§1.3)
001-35. The failure track (bug vs recoverable) is chosen at the operation site, not retroactively. (§1.3)

## 002. Source Form & Lexical Rules — 27 Rules

002-1. Naming conventions: concrete primitive types (`i32`, `bool`) and built-in placeholder keywords (`numeric`, `integer`, `float`, `signed`, `unsigned`) are lowercase; trait and user-defined type names are PascalCase; functions, variables, and fields are snake_case. (§1.4)
002-2. Keywords are always lowercase with no capitalized form; this rule is normative and takes precedence over any conflicting grammar. (§1.4)
002-3. The declaration keywords are `node`, `connection`, `trait`, `type`, `fn`, `operator`, `effect`, `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, `derived`, `stream`, `const`, `let`, `mut`, `repeat`. (§1.4)
002-4. The clause keywords are `parts`, `incoming`, `outgoing`, `expose`, `when`, `satisfies`, `fulfill`, `default`, `otherwise`, `from`, `to`, `pairs`, `on`, `where`, `desired`, `observed`, `ring`, `gate`, `keyed`, `at`, `dynamic` (the supply-mode marker). (§1.4)
002-5. `pair` and `exposition` are reserved instance-field names; the instance fields `from`, `to`, `incoming`, `outgoing`, `parts` double as clause keywords. (§1.4)
002-6. The control-flow keywords are `if`, `else`, `match`, `for`, `in`, `while`, `break`, `continue`, `return`. (§1.4)
002-7. `here` and `module` are the scope-anchor namespace keywords. (§1.4)
002-8. `subject` is the instance-value keyword. (§1.4)
002-9. `as` is the naming/alias keyword, used for placement names, import aliases, and `repeat` view names. (§1.4)
002-10. `as` is not a cast operator; explicit conversion uses call syntax `T(value)`. (§1.4)
002-11. The operator-context keywords are `is`, `and`, `or`, `not`, and `weak` (the weak-reference operator). (§1.4)
002-12. The sole reserved type identifier is `Subject`, the subject-type alias usable in trait and `fulfill` type positions; it is a capitalized type alias, not a keyword. (§1.4)
002-13. Identifiers may contain `#` as a leading, infix, or terminating character, and `#` behaves like a letter in every position — normative, taking precedence over any conflicting grammar: `#default`, `audio#main`, `note#`. (§1.4)
002-14. The semicolon `;` is not a token; a `;` anywhere in Ductus source is a lex error. (§1.4)
002-15. Ductus delimits with newlines and indentation; there is no statement separator. (§1.4)
002-16. Every separated list (call arguments, generic parameters, tuple components, `parts:`/`incoming:`/`outgoing:` entries) uses the comma, the newline, or both. (§1.4)
002-17. A line comment begins with `//` and runs to end of line; there is no block-comment form; this rule is normative and takes precedence over any conflicting grammar. (§1.4)
002-18. "Compiler" means the static analysis phase, "runtime" means execution, and "codegen" is the boundary at which all types must be concrete. (§1.4)
002-19. An identifier begins with a Unicode letter, `_`, or `#`, continues with those plus Unicode digits, and may not begin with a digit: `audio#main` ✓, `2nd` ✗. (§1.4)
002-20. Indentation is significant; its unit is the ASCII space, and a tab in leading whitespace is a lex error. (§1.4)
002-21. Layout is suspended inside `(...)`/`[...]` and string literals, letting those span lines freely. (§1.4)
002-22. Blank lines and comment-only lines do not affect indentation. (§1.4)
002-23. A `trait`/`type`/`enum`/`node`/`connection` body is always an indented block, never written inline after the `:`. (§1.4)
002-24. A function body and `if`/`else` and `match` arms may be a single inline expression after the `:` or an indented block: `fn double(x: i32) -> i32: x * 2`. (§1.4)
002-25. Every keyword is reserved in every position and can never be an ordinary identifier; no keyword class is contextual. (§1.4)
002-26. A keyword in a field-like position (`from`, `to`, `parts`, `pair`, `exposition`, `desired`, `observed`) is the keyword used in that context with compiler-assigned meaning — like `this`/`super` in other languages — not a user-definable field. (§1.4)
002-27. The language's syntax is specified directly in this log and SPEC.md; there is no separate grammar document and no normative content is delegated to one. (§1.4)

## 003. Modules, Packages & Visibility — 76 Rules

003-1. `public` visibility exports a declaration across package boundaries to dependent packages. (§10.1)
003-2. `shared` visibility reaches any file within the declaring package. (§10.1)
003-3. `shared` is the default visibility level — no keyword is required for it; `public` and `private` are explicit keywords. (§10.1)
003-4. `private` visibility reaches only the declaring module — the folder containing the declaration. (§10.1)
003-5. Visibility levels are linearly ordered by permissiveness `private < shared < public`; a declaration's level determines the maximum reach of any reference to it. (§10.1)
003-6. A reference to a declaration from outside its visibility reach is a compile error at the reference site. (§10.1)
003-7. The unit of `private` is the module (folder), not the file; files in the same folder share scope regardless of visibility level. (§10.1)
003-8. A package is the unit of distribution with a single package root; the root folder is itself the root module, addressed in absolute paths via the `root` keyword. (§10.2)
003-9. Each subfolder of the package root is a distinct module addressed by its folder path: `<package_root>/audio/` is `root::audio`. (§10.2)
003-10. A folder is a module iff it directly contains `.duc` source files. (§10.2)
003-11. There is no module marker file, no in-file module declaration, and no module manifest; folder location alone determines module membership. (§10.2)
003-12. A folder without `.duc` files is a pure path-segment folder: it cannot be the target of a `use` or qualified path and has no declarations: `use root::audio::*` ✗. (§10.2)
003-13. Path-segment folders do not prevent subfolders from being modules; paths resolve through them: `use root::audio::effects::reverb::Reverb` ✓. (§10.2)
003-14. There is no special parent/child submodule relationship; every module folder is independent and references between modules are ordinary cross-module references. (§10.2)
003-15. Mixing code and non-code files in the same folder is permitted; the language imposes no convention beyond the `.duc`-presence rule. (§10.2)
003-16. Files inside the same folder share scope: each file sees all sibling declarations automatically, with no `use` statement or path qualification. (§10.2.1)
003-17. The unit of identity in the type system is the module; files inside it are purely organizational. (§10.2.1)
003-18. A `private` declaration is reachable from any file inside its module and from no other module of the same package nor any external package. (§10.2.2)
003-19. A `shared` declaration is reachable from any file in the same package; cross-module access requires a `use` statement or a path-qualified reference. (§10.2.2)
003-20. A `public` declaration is reachable within its package and additionally from any file in any package depending on the source package. (§10.2.2)
003-21. Within a single module all three visibility levels behave identically; visibility matters only for references outside the declaring module. (§10.2.2)
003-22. Absolute path bases are `root` (current package root module), a bare name matching a manifest-declared external dependency, and `std` (the standard library root). (§10.2.3)
003-23. The `std` path base is built-in and implicitly available to every package without manifest declaration: `std::vec::Vec`. (§10.2.3)
003-24. All `use` statements use absolute paths starting from a path base; there is no relative-path "current module" form for imports. (§10.2.3)
003-25. The `module::` qualifier inside node or connection bodies resolves only the enclosing module's own top-level declarations as a disambiguation anchor; it is not an import mechanism and cannot reach other modules. (§10.2.3)
003-26. Every visibility-bearing grammar position accepts `public`, `shared`, `private`, or absence, which denotes `shared`. (§10.3)
003-27. The older `pub` keyword is replaced throughout by the three-level model. (§10.3)
003-28. Visibility of methods within a trait declaration is uniform with the trait's visibility; there is no per-method visibility. (§10.3)
003-29. Free functions and `const` declarations carry a visibility specifier directly on the declaration: `private const SECRET_KEY: u64 = 0xDEADBEEF`. (§10.3)
003-30. `operator` declarations carry visibility specifiers under the same rules as functions. (§10.3)
003-31. Module-level `signal`, `derived`, and `recurrent` declarations accept visibility specifiers on the declaration line. (§10.3)
003-32. Node and connection type declarations carry a visibility specifier on the type declaration. (§10.3)
003-33. Any top-level instantiation (`Foo bar:`, `signal x = ...`, `let y = ...`) accepts a visibility specifier governing cross-module reachability of the instance name. (§10.3)
003-34. Visibility specifiers attach to any named declaration: any name introduced into a scope may carry a specifier governing its cross-scope reach. (§10.3)
003-35. A `use` statement imports a name from a different module into the current file's scope so the file can refer to it unqualified: `use root::audio::Synthesizer`. (§10.4)
003-36. `use` statements are required only for cross-module references; sibling files in the same module need none. (§10.4)
003-37. A `use` statement is per-file; there is no mechanism to share imports across sibling files. (§10.4)
003-38. `use` has no visibility modifier; it controls only how the current file refers to other names. (§10.4)
003-39. A name brought in via `use` does not become a declaration in the importing file; it remains the original declaration in the original module under a shorter local reference. (§10.4)
003-40. The imported declaration's visibility governs whether a `use` is permitted: importing a `private` declaration from a different module is a compile error. (§10.4)
003-41. Importing a `shared` declaration works only within its package; importing a `public` declaration works from any package depending on the source package. (§10.4)
003-42. A `use` targets the module path of the declaration's folder, never the file within it. (§10.4)
003-43. A `use` may rename the imported name with `as`: `use root::audio::Synthesizer as Synth`. (§10.4)
003-44. Selection lists on `use` paths use parentheses: `use root::ops::(add, sub, mul)`. (§10.4.1)
003-45. A glob import brings in every visible name from the source: `use root::variants::*`. (§10.4.1)
003-46. Two glob imports bringing colliding names into the same scope are a compile error at the `use` site that introduces the second collision. (§10.4.1)
003-47. A `use` selection-list item may be a multi-segment path, carry its own `as` alias, or be a `*` glob: `use root::audio::(synth::Oscillator, Filter as Filt, fx::*)`. (§10.4.1)
003-48. There is no re-exporting `use`; exposing a declaration under another path requires an explicit re-declaration such as a `public alias type` or a wrapper function. (§10.4.2)
003-49. `alias type` declarations accept visibility specifiers under the same rules as other declarations: `public alias type Synthesizer = root::audio::internal::Synthesizer`. (§10.4.2)
003-50. `use` may appear only at file scope; a `use` inside a function body, block, or other inner scope is a compile error. (§10.4.3)
003-51. A type or constructor name is not a first-class value; `let x = path::TypeName` is not a value binding. (§10.4.3)
003-52. A file-scope `use … as` introduces a local short name for an imported type: `use root::audio::Synthesizer as synth`. (§10.4.3)
003-53. The cross-module use-and-reference graph must be acyclic; any cycle, binary (A→B→A) or longer, is rejected at compile time. (§10.4.4)
003-54. The cycle-rejection error identifies the cycle's members. (§10.4.4)
003-55. Type-reference cycles between sibling files of one module are permitted; the module's files are compiled as a single unit. (§10.4.4)
003-56. Initializer-reference cycles between sibling files (top-level const values, signal initial values, module-scope attr defaults) are forbidden and rejected at compile time. (§10.4.4)
003-57. The acyclicity rule governs the static reference graph only and is distinct from runtime reactive-graph cycle rules. (§10.4.4)
003-58. Top-level initializers (`const`, `signal`, placement-time-evaluated node/connection portions) run in topological order of the cross-module reference graph: if module A references module B, B initializes first. (§10.4.5)
003-59. Within a module, files initialize topologically by cross-file initializer references: if file A's initializer references a name from file B, B's initializer runs first. (§10.4.5)
003-60. Among declarations in the same file, the textually earlier one initializes first. (§10.4.5)
003-61. The topological initialization order is computed at compile time and fixed; a program never observes initialization in any other order. (§10.4.5)
003-62. Records and newtypes carry an independent constructor visibility as a parenthesized modifier on the type visibility keyword: `public(private) type Email`. (§10.5)
003-63. When the inner constructor specifier is omitted, constructor visibility defaults to the type's visibility. (§10.5)
003-64. The inner constructor specifier may never be more permissive than the outer type visibility: `private(public)` is a compile error. (§10.5)
003-65. With a `(private)` constructor, the type name is usable across the type's scope but `TypeName(...)` construction is unreachable outside the constructor's scope — the language's mechanism for construction-time invariants. (§10.5.1)
003-66. Enum visibility applies uniformly to the enum type and all its variants; there is no per-variant visibility specifier. (§10.6)
003-67. Each record field declares its own visibility independently: `private password_hash: string`. (§10.7)
003-68. A field's visibility may never exceed the enclosing type's; a `public` field on a `private` type is a compile error. (§10.7)
003-69. Access to a field from outside its visibility scope is a compile error at the access site. (§10.7)
003-70. `fulfill` blocks have no separate visibility specifier. (§10.8)
003-71. An implementation's effective visibility is `min(trait_visibility, type_visibility)` under `private < shared < public`: it is callable wherever both the trait and the type are visible. (§10.8)
003-72. The orphan rule operates on package-of-declaration, not visibility: a `fulfill` is legal if the trait or the type is declared in the current package, regardless of either's visibility level. (§10.9)
003-73. A `private` trait or type still counts as in-package for orphan purposes; a `fulfill` of a private trait for a foreign type is valid, accessible only inside the declaring module. (§10.9)
003-74. A call `x.f()` resolves `f` against names visible in the current scope; visibility filters which names are reachable from the call site. (§10.10)
003-75. Every cross-module reference is explicit: the name is brought into scope via `use` or the call uses a path-qualified form like `root::module::function_name(args)`. (§10.10)
003-76. Trait-method calls follow the same name-resolution rule with the additional reach constraint that the implementation's effective visibility is the minimum of the trait's and the type's. (§10.10)

## 004. Type System Core — 153 Rules

004-1. Placeholders are compile-time-only types standing for not-yet-resolved concrete types; they exist solely during type checking and are eliminated before code generation. (§2.1)
004-2. A placeholder attaches to a value, not a binding; a binding is a transparent alias with no type independent of its value: `let x = 5` carries an integer placeholder. (§2.1.1)
004-3. A placeholder is resolved to a concrete type at each use site — a position where the value participates in an operation requiring a concrete type. (§2.1.2)
004-4. Use sites include explicit type annotations, arguments to concretely-typed parameters, operands paired with concretely-typed operands, assignments to concretely-typed fields, and returns from concretely-typed functions. (§2.1.2)
004-5. Each use site resolves a placeholder independently; the same value may resolve to different concrete types at different sites: `let a: i32 = x` and `let b: i64 = x` from `let x = 5`. (§2.1.2)
004-6. Each independent resolution verifies the compile-time-known value fits the resolved target type. (§2.1.2)
004-7. Placeholder resolution at a use site considers all information available at that site, including other operands' types in the same expression and the value-fits check. (§2.1.2)
004-8. A placeholder's integer/float kind tags it for defaulting but does not prevent resolution to a compatible type of a different kind when the value fits exactly: `let f: f64 = x` with `x` bound to `5`. (§2.1.2)
004-9. A binding whose right-hand side has a concrete type carries that concrete type with no placeholder: `let y = some_function()` is concretely typed. (§2.1.3)
004-10. A use site that cannot resolve its placeholder produces a compile error at the use site, not propagated back to the binding. (§2.1.4)
004-11. Unused bindings require no placeholder resolution and produce no placeholder error, though they may produce a separate unused-binding warning. (§2.1.4)
004-12. A value used at multiple use sites demanding mutually incompatible concrete types is surfaced as errors at the conflicting use sites. (§2.1.4)
004-13. No runtime value has a placeholder type and no machine representation corresponds to a placeholder. (§2.1.5)
004-14. The compiler infers types for variables, function parameters, function return types, and generic type parameters using a bidirectional inference algorithm. (§2.2)
004-15. Inference treats every omitted annotation as a fresh placeholder, generates constraints from expression structure, and solves them to concrete types or trait-constrained type variables. (§2.2.1)
004-16. Inference errors are reported at the sites where constraints cannot be satisfied. (§2.2.1)
004-17. Generic function bodies are typechecked at definition time, not deferred to call sites. (§2.2.2)
004-18. Constraints inferred from a generic body's operations attach to the function's signature, and call sites must satisfy them: `fn lerp(a, b, c): a + (b - a) * c` requires `+`, `-`, `*`. (§2.2.2)
004-19. A function with omitted parameter types is generic; each omitted parameter type desugars to a distinct fresh generic parameter: `fn make_pair(a, b)` becomes `fn make_pair[T0, T1](a: T0, b: T1)`. (§2.2.3)
004-20. Unification of synthesized type parameters is a result of body inference, never part of the desugaring. (§2.2.3)
004-21. Implicit and explicit generic forms produce the same code and the same semantics; the choice is stylistic. (§2.2.3)
004-22. Mixed signatures are permitted: some parameter types explicit, others omitted, the omitted ones receiving fresh parameters. (§2.2.3)
004-23. Body operations resolve to trait methods and the trait becomes the parameter's constraint: `+` resolves to `Add::add`, where `Add` denotes `Add[Subject]`. (§2.2.4)
004-24. The compiler may collapse inferred constraint sets to umbrella traits when unambiguous: `Add & Sub & Mul & Neg & Zero & One` may become `Numeric`. (§2.2.4)
004-25. Generic arguments — type and const-generic alike — are supplied with turbofish syntax `::[…]` or omitted and resolved by the compiler. (§2.2.5)
004-26. Positional generic arguments follow declaration order, and a prefix may be supplied with trailing arguments omitted: `resize::[2](stream)`. (§2.2.5)
004-27. The wildcard `_` in a turbofish is a positional hole resolved exactly like an omitted argument, used to skip an interior parameter: `resize::[2, _, _](stream)`. (§2.2.5)
004-28. Named generic arguments use `Name = value` in any order, omitting the rest: `resize::[K = 2](stream)`. (§2.2.5)
004-29. Named generic arguments use `=`, not the `:` of named value arguments, because inside a generic list `:` separates a parameter from its type. (§2.2.5)
004-30. A single turbofish list is either all-positional (with optional wildcards and trailing omission) or all-named, never mixed. (§2.2.5)
004-31. An omitted, wildcarded, or unnamed generic argument resolves in order: inferred from value arguments (and, for a parameter bare in the return type, from the expected type), else its declared default, else a compile error requiring explicit supply. (§2.2.5)
004-32. The `::` prefix on a type-argument list disambiguates generic application from indexing; without it `foo[T](args)` would be ambiguous. (§2.2.5)
004-33. `_` in a type-annotation position denotes a type the compiler infers from context, resolved as a placeholder at its use site: `let r: Result[i32, _] = compute()`. (§2.2.6)
004-34. The type wildcard is permitted in any type-expression position: generic arguments, tuple components, annotated return types, and trait-bound positions where inference can resolve the constraint. (§2.2.6)
004-35. An uninferable type wildcard produces an error reported at the wildcard's location, identifying the inference failure and the missing context. (§2.2.6)
004-36. The type wildcard `_` is distinct from the pattern wildcard `_`; the parser disambiguates by syntactic position. (§2.2.6)
004-37. Generic functions are realized via monomorphization: each unique combination of concrete type arguments produces a separate specialized function. (§2.3)
004-38. There is no type erasure, no boxing, and no dynamic dispatch for static generic calls. (§2.3)
004-39. Users pay no runtime cost for generic abstraction beyond the cost of the specialized operations themselves. (§2.3)
004-40. Each unique tuple of concrete type arguments at a call site produces a distinct instantiation, even if the machine code happens to be similar: `lerp(i32, i32, i32)` and `lerp(i32, i32, f64)` are separate instantiations. (§2.3.1)
004-41. Backend codegen may deduplicate identical machine code as an optimization; the deduplication is semantically invisible. (§2.3.1)
004-42. Monomorphization is per-call-site across module boundaries: calls from different modules with different concrete types produce separate instantiations in each calling module. (§2.3.2)
004-43. Generic function bodies must be available, in source or intermediate representation, to every module that calls them; generic definitions are not closed binary units. (§2.3.2)
004-44. Polymorphic recursion — a recursive call requiring a different type instantiation than its caller — is rejected at compile time. (§2.3.3)
004-45. Direct same-type recursion is permitted and reuses the same instantiation. (§2.3.3)
004-46. The polymorphic-recursion ban is permanent, structural to monomorphization; cases that need it use explicit dynamic dispatch via `dyn`. (§2.3.3)
004-47. Dead code elimination operates per-instantiation: a generic with no call sites produces no output, and only the called type combinations are emitted. (§2.3.4)
004-48. The semantic unit for codegen is the instantiation, not the generic. (§2.3.4)
004-49. Trait method calls in monomorphized code resolve to direct calls to the concrete implementation, with no vtable, indirection, or runtime dispatch cost. (§2.3.5)
004-50. Coherence guarantees exactly one `fulfill` block per (trait, type) pair within the module graph. (§2.3.5)
004-51. `dyn` trait objects are the only path through which trait method calls incur runtime indirection. (§2.3.5)
004-52. A const-generic parameter may declare a default expression referencing other generic parameters of the same declaration: `const N: usize = A + B`. (§2.3.6)
004-53. A const-generic default expression is evaluated in the const evaluation context at instantiation time. (§2.3.6)
004-54. A const-generic default expression must produce a value of the declared parameter type; otherwise it is a compile error. (§2.3.6)
004-55. A default expression may reference only generic parameters that precede it in the parameter list; forward references are compile errors. (§2.3.6)
004-56. An omitted const-generic parameter takes its evaluated default at the call site: `merge(stream_a, stream_b)` yields `N = A + B`. (§2.3.6)
004-57. An explicit turbofish value overrides a const-generic default, subject to type-correctness: `merge::[T = Event, N = 1024](stream_a, stream_b)`. (§2.3.6)
004-58. Type-parameter and const-generic defaults resolve in left-to-right declaration order, each with access to the resolved values of preceding parameters. (§2.3.6)
004-59. The dependency graph among defaults must be a DAG, enforced by the compiler during instantiation. (§2.3.6)
004-60. A multi-line generic parameter list permits a trailing comma after its final parameter: `const N: usize = A + B,` precedes the closing `]` of `operator merge[...]`. (§2.3.6)
004-61. Binary-size mitigations (backend deduplication, outlining, opt-in `dyn`) are implementation levers that do not change the language's semantic model. (§2.3.7)
004-62. Compile-time evaluation is a semantic feature the language relies on, not an optimization; expressions are evaluated at compile time whenever possible. (§2.4)
004-63. Literals, constructions and operations over compile-time-known operands, and calls to pure functions with compile-time-known arguments are compile-time known. (§2.4.1)
004-64. A binding of a compile-time-known expression is compile-time known. (§2.4.1)
004-65. In `for x in iterable:` with compile-time-known `iterable`, `x` is compile-time known on each iteration and the body is reproduced once per element. (§2.4.1)
004-66. A range `a..b` is compile-time known iff both bounds are. (§2.4.1)
004-67. An array literal `[e1, …, eK]` is compile-time known iff every element is. (§2.4.1)
004-68. The compiler determines compile-time-knowability automatically; users do not annotate it for `let` bindings. (§2.4.1)
004-69. `let` is the general, immutable binding form; the compiler determines compile-time-knowability of its RHS automatically. (§2.4.1.1)
004-70. A `let` bound to a non-reactive expression is compile-time known and may be tree-shaken, inlined, or otherwise optimized. (§2.4.1.1)
004-71. A `let` bound to a reactive or runtime expression participates in the reactive graph and exists at runtime: `let z = read_sensor()`. (§2.4.1.1)
004-72. `const` asserts a binding is compile-time-only; the compiler enforces the assertion and guarantees no runtime existence: `const TAU = 2.0 * PI`. (§2.4.1.1)
004-73. A `const` binding may carry an explicit type annotation: `const MAX_ITEMS: usize = 1024`. (§2.4.1.1)
004-74. A `const` RHS must not involve any signal, derived value, external input, or reactive expression; violation is a compile error at the declaration site identifying the reactivity source. (§2.4.1.2)
004-75. A `const` occupies no runtime memory location: references from non-`const` code inline the value, and an unreferenced `const` does not appear in compiled output. (§2.4.1.2)
004-76. A `const` of type `string` is a compile-time-assigned index into the string-pool seed: static program-lifetime bytes, no dynamic allocation, no refcount traffic on the read path. (§2.4.1.2)
004-77. A `const` has no address; operations requiring a runtime address (passing by reference, storing a pointer, FFI sharing) are compile errors. (§2.4.1.2)
004-78. A type is `const`-eligible if all its values are fully representable at compile time with no dynamic allocation and no mutable runtime state; static program-lifetime representation is permitted. (§2.4.1.3)
004-79. All primitive types are `const`-eligible: `i8`–`i128`, `u8`–`u128`, `isize`, `usize`, `f32`, `f64`, `bool`, `char`, `string`, `never`. (§2.4.1.3)
004-80. Fixed-size arrays, records, enums (including payload-carrying), tuples, and newtypes are `const`-eligible when all their element, field, payload, component, or wrapped types are. (§2.4.1.3)
004-81. Heap-allocated collections (`Vec`, `HashMap`), signal-bearing or reactive types, types containing function references or closures with captured runtime state, and `dyn` trait objects are not `const`-eligible. (§2.4.1.3)
004-82. `const`-eligibility is checked at the declaration site; a `const` whose RHS produces a non-eligible type is rejected with an error identifying the offending type. (§2.4.1.3)
004-83. `const` is permitted at module top level, inside function bodies, and inside type, trait, node, and connection bodies. (§2.4.1.4)
004-84. Type-associated constants are accessed via path syntax: `Vec3::ZERO`. (§2.4.1.4)
004-85. `const` declarations follow the same visibility model as other declarations, defaulting to `shared`: `public const TAU = ...`. (§2.4.1.4)
004-86. A non-reactive `let` is effectively eligible for `const`-style optimization but unasserted and unenforced; it may or may not exist at runtime depending on observation. (§2.4.1.5)
004-87. A `let` bound to an expression using `const` values is itself compile-time known; no promotion to `const` is needed for downstream `const` use. (§2.4.1.5)
004-88. Tooling may suggest converting an eligible `let` to `const`, but the compiler never requires it. (§2.4.1.5)
004-89. Expressions involving signals or any value derived from a signal are not compile-time known. (§2.4.2)
004-90. Expressions involving external I/O, host-boundary calls, or any construct whose value is determined by the runtime environment are not compile-time known. (§2.4.2)
004-91. Runtime/reactive dependency propagates transitively: a call with a reactive argument produces a reactive result, and a binding to a reactive expression is itself reactive. (§2.4.2)
004-92. A compile-time-known value is checked to fit each demanded type; a non-fitting value is a compile error: `let x: u8 = 300` ✗. (§2.4.3)
004-93. Value-fits-type checking applies to any compile-time-known value however computed, including through arithmetic and pure calls: `let v: u8 = y + 100` with `y = 200` ✗. (§2.4.3)
004-94. Integer values require exact fit in the target type. (§2.4.3)
004-95. Float literal values fit any float type, rounded to nearest representable. (§2.4.3)
004-96. Integer-to-float fit follows the lossless conversion rules: `let x: f32 = 5` ✓. (§2.4.3)
004-97. Compile-time-known values can serve as type-level arguments; a const-generic parameter accepts a value rather than a type: `fn fill(arr: i32[fib(10) + 1])`. (§2.4.4)
004-98. Anything compile-time evaluable can appear in a type position requiring a value. (§2.4.4)
004-99. A negative integer literal `-N` is parsed as a single signed literal token for type-checking: `let x: i8 = -5` checks `-5` against `i8`'s range, not unary minus applied to `5`. (§2.4.5)
004-100. Runtime unary-minus rules apply unchanged to runtime values; only literal parsing is special. (§2.4.5)
004-101. The compiler tracks reactivity provenance through expressions and can identify the signal(s) a reactive value depends on. (§2.4.6)
004-102. Supplying a reactive expression where compile-time evaluation is required (a `const` RHS, a const-generic argument, an array length) is a compile error whose diagnostic names the source signal. (§2.4.6)
004-103. A reactive value in an ordinarily-typed binding is not an error on reactivity grounds; reactive values are checked by ordinary type-compatibility, and value-fits checking applies only to compile-time-known values. (§2.4.6)
004-104. When a reactive value produces an error, the diagnostic mentions signal provenance as context, but the underlying error is a type mismatch, not a fit-check failure. (§2.4.6)
004-105. Limits on compile-time evaluation (recursion depth, step count, memory) are implementation concerns; the compiler enforces configurable limits and reports which limit was exceeded and at what call site. (§2.4.7)
004-106. Compile-time floating-point evaluation uses the target's IEEE 754 format exactly; compile-time and runtime float computation of the same expression must be bit-identical. (§2.4.7)
004-107. A const-generic parameter carries a compile-time-known value and is declared with the `const` keyword in a generic parameter list: `type Buffer[T, const N: usize = 1024]`. (§2.5)
004-108. A const-generic argument fills a const-generic parameter at a use site — supplied, inferred, or defaulted — and may be written as an expression. (§2.5)
004-109. A const-generic parameter's declared type must be an integer type or `bool`. (§2.5.1)
004-110. Floating-point const-generic parameters are forbidden: `type Spinner[const ANGLE: f64]` ✗. (§2.5.1)
004-111. The float ban applies to type-level parameters only; a regular `const` of float type is unaffected. (§2.5.1)
004-112. When every dependency is known, a const-generic argument may be any compile-time-known expression of the parameter's type, evaluated to a concrete value before type-checking proceeds: `stream ring[BUF * 2] events: Event = ...`. (§2.5.2)
004-113. Concrete const-generic arguments admit the full compile-time vocabulary — integer arithmetic including division and modulo, comparisons, conditionals, and calls to pure functions: `fn recent(h: i32[fib(10) + 1])`. (§2.5.2)
004-114. Regular `const`s are admitted in const-generic arguments on the same footing as literals. (§2.5.2)
004-115. A reactive value may never flow into a const-generic argument; doing so is a compile error. (§2.5.2)
004-116. A concrete const-generic argument is checked against the parameter's type and bounds by value-fits-type checking. (§2.5.2)
004-117. A const-generic expression that still mentions an unbound parameter is symbolic; only a restricted, decidable set of operations is admitted in symbolic position. (§2.5.3)
004-118. A symbolic integer expression must be a polynomial over in-scope const-generic parameters and compile-time-known integers, built from `+`, `-`, `*` only: `RingStream[T, N * 2]`. (§2.5.3)
004-119. A parameter may multiply another parameter in a symbolic polynomial: `N * K`. (§2.5.3)
004-120. A symbolic boolean expression must use only `and`, `or`, `not`, and `is`/`is not` between booleans, over in-scope `bool` parameters and compile-time-known booleans: `Mode[A and B, A or B]`. (§2.5.3)
004-121. Integer `/` and `%` are rejected in symbolic const-generic position and permitted the instant every operand is concrete: `RingStream[T, N / 2]` ✗ while `N` is unbound. (§2.5.3)
004-122. Integer comparisons (`<`, `>`, `<=`, `>=`, `is`, `is not` on integers) are rejected in symbolic const-generic position: `Mode[N > 0, true]` ✗. (§2.5.3)
004-123. `if`/`match` and calls to user-defined functions are rejected in symbolic const-generic position. (§2.5.3)
004-124. All floating-point operations are rejected in symbolic const-generic position. (§2.5.3)
004-125. Two const-generic types are the same type iff their type arguments are pairwise identical and each pair of const-generic arguments has the same canonical form. (§2.5.4)
004-126. An integer-polynomial argument canonicalizes to a sum of monomials with integer coefficients, in a fixed total order: `RingStream[T, N + 1]` and `RingStream[T, 1 + N]` are the same type. (§2.5.4)
004-127. Like terms in an integer-polynomial argument are combined in canonical form: `2 * N` and `N + N` share `2·N`. (§2.5.4)
004-128. A boolean argument canonicalizes to a normal form (identical truth tables): `Mode[A and B, …]` and `Mode[B and A, …]` are the same type. (§2.5.4)
004-129. A concrete const-generic argument canonicalizes to its value. (§2.5.4)
004-130. Const-generic positions are invariant: no subtyping or coercion between differing canonical forms even when one value is provably larger; `RingStream[T, N]` is never assignable to `RingStream[T, N + 1]`. (§2.5.4)
004-131. A const-generic parameter is inferred only from a position where it appears as a bare parameter: `RingStream[T, N]`, not `RingStream[T, N + 1]`. (§2.5.5)
004-132. A bare occurrence in the return type supports inference when the expected type is known from context, e.g. a `let` annotation. (§2.5.5)
004-133. The compiler evaluates const-generic expressions once their parameters are known; it never solves them. (§2.5.5)
004-134. A parameter appearing only inside an expression (never bare) cannot be inferred and must be supplied or carry a default: `resize::[2](small)` supplies `K` of `N * K`. (§2.5.5)
004-135. The compiler does not back-solve a parameter from a result type: from `-> RingStream[T, N * K]` and expected `RingStream[T, 16]` it will not deduce `K`. (§2.5.5)
004-136. A parameter appearing both bare and inside an expression is inferred from the bare occurrence; the expression occurrence is then checked for consistency against that value. (§2.5.5)
004-137. The result of const-generic inference is checked against the parameter's bounds, if any. (§2.5.5)
004-138. Type and const-generic parameters may interleave in any order in a generic parameter list: `resize[const K: usize, T, const N: usize]`. (§2.5.5)
004-139. A const-generic parameter may be bounded by a `where` clause on the declaration — the same clause carrying trait bounds — attached to the signature before the body's `:`. (§2.5.6)
004-140. A `where` clause holds a comma-separated predicate list and may mix trait bounds and value bounds: `where T: Numeric, N >= 1`. (§2.5.6)
004-141. A bound predicate is a boolean expression over the const-generic parameters and compile-time-known values using `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=`, `is`, `is not` and `and`, `or`, `not`. (§2.5.6)
004-142. Bound-predicate comparison operands may use the full compile-time vocabulary — `/`, `%`, function calls, even on parameters — because predicates are only evaluated, never compared for type identity: `where N % 2 is 0`. (§2.5.6)
004-143. Bounds are checked at instantiation, once every parameter is concrete, using the compile-time evaluation rules including overflow rules. (§2.5.6)
004-144. A false bound predicate is a compile error at the instantiation site, naming the parameter values and the violated predicate: `window::[K = 32](buf16)` ✗ when `K <= N` and `N = 16`. (§2.5.6)
004-145. When a generic instantiates another generic with still-symbolic arguments, the bound check is deferred until those arguments become concrete during monomorphization. (§2.5.6)
004-146. The declaration site checks only that each predicate is a well-formed boolean over in-scope parameters, never that it holds. (§2.5.6)
004-147. The compiler does not prove bounds symbolically and does not use one declaration's bound to discharge another's obligation. (§2.5.6)
004-148. A body relying on a value relationship type-checks structurally regardless of any `where` clause; the obligation is discharged at instantiation alongside the declared bounds. (§2.5.6)
004-149. A matching `where` bound never changes whether a definition compiles; it documents the contract and yields a clearer, earlier instantiation error when violated. (§2.5.6)
004-150. A `where` clause on a `type` declaration sits between the generic parameter list and the body colon: `type EvenBuffer[T, const N: usize] where N % 2 is 0:`. (§2.5.6)
004-151. A `where` clause may stand alone on an indented line between a signature and its body colon: `where K <= N, N >= 1:`. (§2.5.6)
004-152. A const-generic default is used when the argument is neither supplied nor inferred and may itself be an expression referencing earlier parameters: `const N: usize = A + B`. (§2.5.7)
004-153. A const-generic default expression obeys the const-generic expression rules: symbolic where it references unbound parameters, concrete once they are known. (§2.5.7)

## 005. Traits — 229 Rules

005-1. A trait declares an interface — method signatures, associated types, and requirements — that types explicitly conform to and provide implementations for. (§3)
005-2. The trait system is nominal throughout: a type satisfies a trait only via an explicit conformance declaration, never by accidental structural match. (§3)
005-3. Two types with structurally identical method sets are distinct unless both have explicitly declared and implemented the same trait. (§3)
005-4. A trait is declared with the `trait` keyword; its body may declare method signatures, associated types, requirements on other traits, and defaults. (§3.1)
005-5. A trait declaration may be empty, yielding a marker trait: `trait Marker`. (§3.1)
005-6. A `satisfies` clause naming a marker trait is a declarative assertion about the type, checked by the compiler only for the trait's existence. (§3.1)
005-7. Trait methods are declared with `fn` inside the trait body, using the type-level identifier `Subject` for the implementing type: `fn eq(a: Subject, b: Subject) -> bool`. (§3.1.1)
005-8. `Subject` is bound during implementation: in `fulfill Eq for i32`, `Subject` resolves to `i32`. (§3.1.1)
005-9. Trait method signatures name their receiver parameter explicitly; there is no implicit receiver. (§3.1.1)
005-10. Trait methods may have any parameter list, including no `Subject` parameter at all (associated functions such as constructors). (§3.1.1)
005-11. The instance value `subject` is reserved for reactive context inside node and connection bodies and is not bound in trait method signatures. (§3.1.1)
005-12. A trait method signature may declare any parameter with the `own` keyword; the `own` declaration is part of the trait's contract and implementations cannot strengthen or weaken it. (§3.1.1)
005-13. An implementation must declare as `own` exactly the parameters the trait declares as `own` — no more, no fewer — enforced at the `fulfill` block. (§3.1.1)
005-14. Callers invoking a trait method follow the same `own`/`move` symmetry as free-function calls. (§3.1.1)
005-15. A trait may declare associated types with the `type` keyword in its body; each implementation binds the concrete value: `type Item`. (§3.1.2)
005-16. Associated types may be referenced in the trait's method signatures and in other associated-type expressions. (§3.1.2)
005-17. An associated type may declare a default value, which applies when an implementation does not bind it explicitly: `type Output = Subject`. (§3.1.2)
005-18. Bounds on associated types in generic constraints use where-clauses with `.` member-access notation: `where P.Item: Numeric`. (§3.1.2)
005-19. A where-clause may equate an associated type to a specific type, constraining it to be exactly that type: `where Iter.Source = Subject`. (§3.1.2)
005-20. Associated-type slots follow the borrow-default convention: under `type Name = T`, appearances of `Name` in the trait's method signatures are borrow-equivalent. (§3.1.2)
005-21. An implementation opts an associated type into owned semantics by writing `type Name = own T`; appearances of `Name` then carry owned semantics. (§3.1.2)
005-22. The associated-type borrow/own convention applies uniformly to user-defined and stdlib traits; no language-privileged path bypasses it. (§3.1.2)
005-23. A trait may provide a default implementation for any method by including a body in the trait declaration: `fn shout(value: Subject) -> string: greet(value).to_upper() + "!"`. (§3.1.3)
005-24. An implementation may override a trait's default method body by providing its own body in the `fulfill` block, or accept the default by omitting the method. (§3.1.3)
005-25. A trait declares super-trait requirements with the `requires` keyword: `requires Person`. (§3.1.4)
005-26. A type satisfies a trait only if it also satisfies every required trait; the compiler enforces this where `satisfies` is declared, rejecting it if a required trait is absent from the type's direct-or-transitive `satisfies` set. (§3.1.4)
005-27. A child trait may not redeclare a method already declared by any directly or transitively required trait; redeclaration is a compile error at the trait declaration site. (§3.1.4)
005-28. The no-redeclaration rule applies to signatures only; default bodies remain overridable at the fulfill site. (§3.1.4)
005-29. A child trait inherits required traits' signatures and default bodies and cannot re-provide a default; the trait hierarchy offers no alternative defaults at intermediate levels. (§3.1.4)
005-30. Every trait method — both signature and default body — has exactly one origin: the trait that originally declared it. (§3.1.4)
005-31. A single `requires` clause may name multiple traits comma-separated: `requires Numeric, IntDiv, Rem`. (§3.1.4)
005-32. A trait may declare a default concrete type used by the defaulting mechanism: `@default(i32) trait Integer`. (§3.1.5)
005-33. A trait's declared default fires when a use site is constrained solely by traits with declared defaults and nothing else pins the type. (§3.1.5)
005-34. A trait's declared default type must itself satisfy the trait; this is compiler-enforced. (§3.1.5)
005-35. The concrete syntax for declaring a trait default (annotation, dedicated keyword, or body clause) is unspecified; the normative decision is that defaults are declared on the trait, not built into the compiler. (§3.1.5)
005-36. Any use site that would require defaulting through a trait with no declared default is a compile error. (§3.1.5)
005-37. Trait-declared defaults are the only defaulting mechanism: there are no compiler-internal defaults, no module-level pragmas, and no use-site overrides via alternative defaulting paths. (§3.1.5)
005-38. When the default mechanism does not fire, the user resolves the type through explicit annotation only. (§3.1.5)
005-39. Traits may declare type parameters: `trait From[T]`. (§3.1.6)
005-40. Trait type parameters are part of the trait's identity: each trait instance (`From[i32]` vs `From[i64]`) is a distinct constraint and a distinct dispatch target. (§3.1.6)
005-41. A parent trait identity is the trait's declared name independent of generic arguments; several conformance and dispatch rules operate at parent-trait granularity. (§3.1.6)
005-42. A type may implement multiple trait instances of the same parent trait: `fulfill From[i32] for MyNumber` and `fulfill From[i64] for MyNumber` coexist. (§3.1.6)
005-43. A trait type parameter may declare a default, applied when the argument is omitted: `trait Add[Rhs = Subject]`. (§3.1.6)
005-44. A bare reference to a generic trait with defaulted type parameters resolves to the instance with all defaults applied, uniformly in `requires`, trait bounds, `satisfies`, and `fulfill` positions: `T: Add` is sugar for `T: Add[Subject]`. (§3.1.6.1)
005-45. In inferred constraints the compiler infers the all-defaults instance unless the operation's operand types force a cross-type form. (§3.1.6.1)
005-46. Bare-name default resolution is universal across all generic traits with default type parameters, not specific to operator traits. (§3.1.6.1)
005-47. A non-default trait instance is referenced by supplying explicit type arguments: `Add[i64]`. (§3.1.6.1)
005-48. Default type parameters are applied at name-resolution time; by code generation every reference is a fully-specified trait instance. (§3.1.6.1)
005-49. Traits may declare required members that implementing types must provide: `attr`, `const`, `derived`, `recurrent`, and `stream` declarations, and — for connection types — `from` and `to` endpoints. (§3.1.7)
005-50. A trait that declares any required cell or endpoint is kind-specific: a node trait, or a connection trait when it declares endpoints. (§3.1.7)
005-51. A trait that declares only methods and associated types is kind-agnostic and may be satisfied by any kind. (§3.1.7)
005-52. A kind-specific trait is usable as the instantiation contract of a `Type[…]` value: the receiver of a `Type[ThatTrait]` may rely on exactly the members the trait declares. (§3.1.7)
005-53. Required cells in a trait body are written with the same syntax they use in a node or connection body, with optional defaults: `attr enabled: bool = true`. (§3.1.7)
005-54. A connection trait may declare required endpoints with `from: Type` and `to: Type`: `from: Driver`. (§3.1.7)
005-55. A required member declared without a default must be supplied by the implementing type with a concrete value: `const kind: string = "@action/log"`. (§3.1.7)
005-56. A required member declared with a default may be omitted by the implementing type (the trait's default applies) or overridden by the type's own declaration: `attr enabled: bool = false`. (§3.1.7)
005-57. A node or connection type satisfies a trait with required cells only if every required declaration is present (or defaulted) in the type's body with matching name and type. (§3.1.7)
005-58. A trait carrying `from`/`to` endpoints — and any `Type[…]` bounded by it — is a connection contract the compiler can check at placement. (§3.1.7)
005-59. A trait declaring any required cell or endpoint cannot be satisfied by a record, enum, newtype, or primitive; the compiler rejects the `satisfies`. (§3.1.7)
005-60. A trait declaring a `from` or `to` endpoint cannot be satisfied by a node; the compiler rejects the `satisfies`. (§3.1.7)
005-61. A trait with required cells but no endpoints is satisfiable by either nodes or connections. (§3.1.7)
005-62. An implementing connection's `from`/`to` endpoint types must satisfy (not equal) the trait's required endpoint types. (§3.1.7)
005-63. A type declares conformance to a trait via a `satisfies` clause in its body: `satisfies Display, Hash`. (§3.2)
005-64. Implementations live in `fulfill` blocks, possibly in different files from the type, subject to the orphan rule. (§3.2)
005-65. `satisfies Trait` in a type body without a corresponding `fulfill Trait for Type` block reachable through the module graph is a compile error. (§3.2)
005-66. `fulfill Trait for Type` without a corresponding `satisfies Trait` in the type's body is a compile error. (§3.2)
005-67. The satisfies/fulfill pairing requirement is waived for traits with no methods and traits whose methods all have default bodies; these are automatically satisfied when all their required traits are satisfied. (§3.2)
005-68. A type's `satisfies` set must not contain two distinct parent-trait identities whose method names overlap; the compiler rejects the declaration. (§3.2.1)
005-69. The method-name-conflict diagnostic identifies the conflicting method name and the two source traits. (§3.2.1)
005-70. Different generic instantiations of the same parent trait do not conflict under the method-name-overlap rule: `satisfies From[i32], From[i64]` ✓. (§3.2.1)
005-71. Methods from same-parent instantiations are disambiguated at call sites by argument type or expected return type: `MyNumber::convert(5_i32)` resolves to `From[i32]::convert`. (§3.2.1)
005-72. The universal identity `From[T] for T` and a user-written `From[U] for T` are instantiations of the same parent `From` and do not conflict. (§3.2.1)
005-73. Effective method-set computation begins from an empty set and, for each directly-satisfied trait, computes its closure under the `requires` relation — the trait plus every trait reachable through any chain of `requires` clauses. (§3.2.1)
005-74. The effective method set is the union of the method declarations of all traits in all closures, each entry a (method-name, parent-trait-identity) pair. (§3.2.1)
005-75. Two effective-set entries sharing a method name but originating from different parent-trait identities cause the type declaration to be rejected. (§3.2.1)
005-76. Effective-set entries with the same method name and the same parent-trait identity (different generic instantiations) do not collide; dispatch among them is resolved by inference. (§3.2.1)
005-77. A method reached through multiple `requires` paths but originating from the same trait-method declaration is not a conflict; the diamond case is permitted. (§3.2.1)
005-78. The canonical workaround for wanting two traits with conflicting method names and different parent identities is the newtype pattern: separate newtype wrappers each satisfying one trait. (§3.2.1)
005-79. A `fulfill` block delivers a trait's implementation for a specific type: `fulfill Display for Person:`. (§3.3)
005-80. A `fulfill` block may live in any module subject to the orphan rule, not necessarily the trait's or the type's module. (§3.3)
005-81. Multiple `fulfill` blocks for the same (trait, type) pair are rejected by coherence: exactly one implementation exists per pair, reachable through the module graph. (§3.3)
005-82. Functions defined inside a `fulfill Trait for Type` block live in a (Trait, Type)-scoped namespace, not the enclosing module's free-function namespace. (§3.3)
005-83. One module may contain multiple `fulfill` blocks for different (trait, type) pairs whose methods share a name; these do not conflict. (§3.3)
005-84. A module may define a free function and a same-named method in a `fulfill` block; the two do not conflict at the definition site. (§3.3)
005-85. Grammar: `FulfillItem := 'fulfill' TypeExpr 'for' TypeExpr WhereClause? FulfillBody`. (§3.3)
005-86. A `fulfill` body holds one or more items, each an optionally annotated, doc-commented function declaration or an associated-type binding: `AssocTypeBinding := 'type' Ident '=' TypeExpr`. (§3.3)
005-87. `fulfill` is a reserved keyword. (§3.3)
005-88. Inside `fulfill` blocks, `Subject` is permitted as a synonym for the implementing type; the compiler substitutes it during type checking, and both forms produce identical signatures and compiled code. (§3.3.1)
005-89. In `fulfill` blocks, the trait's associated-type names may be written either with the trait's name (`Output`) or with the concrete type bound to them. (§3.3.1)
005-90. The receiver parameter name in trait method implementations is the implementer's choice. (§3.3.1)
005-91. A `fulfill` block binds the trait's associated types via `type Name = Concrete`: `type Output = i32`. (§3.3.2)
005-92. An associated type with a default in the trait declaration may be omitted in the `fulfill` block; the default applies. (§3.3.2)
005-93. An associated type without a default must be bound explicitly in the `fulfill` block. (§3.3.2)
005-94. Omitting an abstract method (one with no default body) in a `fulfill` block is a compile error. (§3.3.3)
005-95. A `fulfill` block may be conditional on its type parameters via a where-clause: `fulfill Display for Result[T, E] where T: Display, E: Display`. (§3.3.4)
005-96. A conditional implementation is available only when the type parameters satisfy the where-clause's required traits. (§3.3.4)
005-97. The compiler verifies a conditional implementation's where-clause conditions at every call site that requires the implementation. (§3.3.4)
005-98. A `where` clause may mix trait bounds and const-generic value bounds in one comma-separated list: `where T: Numeric, N >= 1`. (§3.3.4)
005-99. Trait bounds are checked where the implementation or call is resolved; const-generic value bounds are checked at instantiation against concrete values. (§3.3.4)
005-100. A `where` clause may carry associated-type equality constraints of the form `Path.Assoc = ConcreteType`. (§3.3.4)
005-101. A trait declaring no methods and no associated types — only `requires` clauses — is a pure-requirement trait. (§3.3.5)
005-102. A trait is automatically satisfied when all of its `requires` are satisfied and every method it declares has a default body. (§3.3.5)
005-103. An auto-satisfied trait needs no `fulfill` block and no `satisfies` clause on the type; the clause may still be written for documentation. (§3.3.5)
005-104. Auto-satisfied traits remain nominal in constraint checking: a bound like `T: Numeric` is checked against the trait's name, verifying `T`'s satisfied set includes everything the trait requires. (§3.3.5)
005-105. Auto-satisfaction is the only point of structural satisfaction in the trait system; any trait with one or more abstract methods requires explicit `satisfies` plus `fulfill`. (§3.3.5)
005-106. `fulfill` blocks have no visibility specifier; an implementation is visible exactly where the trait and the type are jointly visible — the intersection of their visibility scopes. (§3.3.6)
005-107. Under uniform function call syntax, a trait method is callable in three equivalent forms: `person.display()`, `display(person)`, `Display::display(person)`. (§3.4)
005-108. The conventional call form requires the function name to be in scope: `display(person)` needs `display` in scope. (§3.4)
005-109. Operator application uses the `|>` token and is not a function-call form. (§3.4)
005-110. `>>` is bitwise right shift only. (§3.4)
005-111. The trait-path form `Trait::method` requires no `use` import; the path itself satisfies the in-scope requirement for dispatch. (§3.4)
005-112. The trait-path form does not override visibility: the trait must still be visible at the call site under module visibility rules. (§3.4)
005-113. Resolution of a bare-name call `f(x)` or method-call `x.f()` prioritizes trait implementations over free functions. (§3.4.1)
005-114. The trait-impl search collects a candidate `T::f` for every trait `T` reachable in the current scope (imported or accessible by path) such that the receiver's type fulfills `T` and `T` declares a method named `f`. (§3.4.1)
005-115. Candidates from the same parent trait collapse into one logical method parameterized by the trait's generic arguments. (§3.4.1)
005-116. Disambiguation among generic instantiations of one parent trait uses call-site context — argument types, expected return type, explicit turbofish — exactly as for generic function dispatch. (§3.4.1)
005-117. A matching trait-impl candidate wins over a same-named in-scope free function, which is shadowed at that call site and callable only via path qualification: `some_module::f(x)`. (§3.4.1)
005-118. Within a winning parent-trait candidate, failure to resolve a unique generic instantiation by inference is a compile error at the call site asking for explicit disambiguation. (§3.4.1)
005-119. With no trait-impl candidate, resolution falls back to the current scope's free-function namespace, matching the first parameter type directly or via implicit widening. (§3.4.1)
005-120. Multiple free functions in scope under one local name cannot occur: free-function names are unique per module, and cross-module conflicts are resolved at import time, not call time. (§3.4.1)
005-121. When nothing matches, the compiler reports an unknown-method error carrying the receiver's type, the unmatched name, and any near-matches identified. (§3.4.1)
005-122. Path-qualified type-side dispatch is available: `Type::f(x)` selects the method of the `fulfill` block whose for-type is `Type`. (§3.4.1)
005-123. Bare-name dispatch among multiple instantiations of one parent trait selects the instantiation pinned by the argument's concrete type, using the same inference algorithm as generic functions. (§3.4.1.1)
005-124. When inference cannot uniquely determine the instantiation (e.g., the argument has a generic-parameter type), the compiler reports a call-site ambiguity error. (§3.4.1.1)
005-125. Explicit instantiation selection uses turbofish on the trait identity: `From::[T]::convert(v)`. (§3.4.1.1)
005-126. A `fulfill T for X` block participates in dispatch on `x: X` only when `T` is in scope; otherwise the candidate is invisible and resolution proceeds as if it did not exist. (§3.4.1.1)
005-127. `x.f::[T]()` is not a disambiguation form: its turbofish specifies generic type arguments, not the receiving trait. (§3.4.1.1)
005-128. A `where` bound may constrain a concrete type, not only a type parameter: `fn build[T](v: T) -> MyNumber where MyNumber: From[T]`. (§3.4.1.1)
005-129. Trait method calls in monomorphized code resolve to direct function calls. (§3.4.2)
005-130. The free-function/trait-impl namespace distinction exists only for call-site name resolution; a resolved call compiles to a direct call to one function identified by its fully-qualified path. (§3.4.2)
005-131. Traits compose into hierarchies via `requires` clauses. (§3.6)
005-132. The standard library pervasively uses fine-grained capability traits combined into umbrella traits. (§3.6)
005-133. A fine-grained trait declares exactly one method or one closely related method group: `trait Neg: fn neg(value: Subject) -> Subject`. (§3.6.1)
005-134. Umbrella traits combine fine-grained traits via `requires` and introduce no new methods. (§3.6.1)
005-135. `Neg` is required by `Signed` and `Float`, not by `Numeric`. (§3.6.1)
005-136. Unsigned integer types satisfy `Numeric` and `Unsigned` but not `Neg`. (§3.6.1)
005-137. `Ord` and `Eq` are standalone traits; no numeric umbrella trait requires them. (§3.6.1)
005-138. Built-in numeric types implement both `Ord` and `Eq`. (§3.6.1)
005-139. A function needing both arithmetic and ordering combines bounds explicitly: `T: Numeric & Ord`. (§3.6.1)
005-140. When multiple defaulted traits constrain one use site, the most-specific trait's default wins. (§3.6.2)
005-141. Trait `A` is more specific than `B` iff `A` transitively requires `B`: a `Float`-bound site defaults to `f64`, not `i32`. (§3.6.2)
005-142. Incomparable defaulted traits at one use site make defaulting ambiguous: compile error demanding an explicit annotation. (§3.6.2)
005-143. Coherence holds: every (trait, type) pair has exactly one implementation reachable through the module graph. (§3.7)
005-144. Coherence is enforced structurally via the orphan rule. (§3.7)
005-145. `fulfill Trait for Type` is permitted in module M iff `Trait` or `Type` is defined in M. (§3.7.1)
005-146. A `fulfill` whose trait and type are both foreign to the module is rejected at compile time. (§3.7.1)
005-147. The orphan rule admits no exception for private or non-exported implementations. (§3.7.1)
005-148. A generic `fulfill` must place at least one concrete local type in its trait-or-type part: `fulfill ForeignTrait[LocalType] for ForeignType` ✓. (§3.7.2)
005-149. A generic `fulfill` with no concrete local type is rejected: `fulfill ForeignTrait[T] for ForeignType` ✗. (§3.7.2)
005-150. Language-provided implementations are not subject to the orphan rule. (§3.7.3)
005-151. The fine-grained operator traits (`Add`, `Sub`, `Mul`, ...) are pre-implemented for the built-in numeric types. (§3.7.3)
005-152. User code cannot redefine the built-in numeric operator-trait implementations. (§3.7.3)
005-153. `fulfill From[T] for U` automatically provides `Into[U] for T`. (§3.7.3)
005-154. `TryFrom` implementations automatically provide the corresponding `TryInto` implementation. (§3.7.3)
005-155. The `From`-to-`Into` and `TryFrom`-to-`TryInto` auto-derivations apply uniformly to user-defined and stdlib pairs. (§3.7.3)
005-156. The identity conversion `From[T] for T` exists structurally for every type and is exempt from the orphan rule. (§3.7.3)
005-157. A user `fulfill From[T] for T` with identical source and target is a compile error; the auto-provided identity is the sole impl. (§3.7.3)
005-158. Stdlib provides `Copy` for primitive numerics, `bool`, `char`, `string`, `duration`, `instant`, tuples of Copy components, and `Range[T]` when `T: Copy`. (§3.7.3)
005-159. The orphan rule bars user redefinition of the stdlib built-in `Copy` impls. (§3.7.3)
005-160. `Eq`, `Ord`, `Hash`, `Clone`, `Display`, `Debug` exist for a tuple type at any arity iff every component type has the trait. (§3.7.3)
005-161. Tuple standard-trait derivation is component-by-component, like record `@derive`, generated per instantiation. (§3.7.3)
005-162. Tuple structural impls are exempt from the orphan rule. (§3.7.3)
005-163. In an `Option`-returning function `?` returns `None` directly; no `From`-conversion impl is involved for the `Option` absence case. (§3.7.3)
005-164. Stdlib auto-impls exempt from the orphan rule (such as the tuple `From` impls) live outside the user-writable `fulfill` space and cannot be user-overridden. (§3.7.3)
005-165. Language-privileged impls use the same trait/fulfill mechanism as user impls; no language path bypasses the trait system. (§3.7.3)
005-166. The language defines a small, closed category of marker traits with no methods and no associated types, enforced with compiler privilege. (§3.7.4)
005-167. Language-defined marker traits are not redeclarable: user code cannot define a same-named trait to reuse the privileged behavior. (§3.7.4)
005-168. Marker traits divide into opt-in and intrinsic sub-kinds, distinguished by how a type acquires membership. (§3.7.4)
005-169. Opt-in marker membership is acquired explicitly, via a `satisfies` clause or `@derive`. (§3.7.4)
005-170. The opt-in markers are `Copy` and `Circularity`. (§3.7.4)
005-171. `Copy` flags a type whose values are implicitly duplicated at every use site. (§3.7.4)
005-172. `Circularity` flags a connection type allowed to participate in topology cycles; the static cycle rule checks this flag. (§3.7.4)
005-173. Intrinsic marker membership is automatic and universal for its declaration kind, with no `satisfies` clause. (§3.7.4)
005-174. Intrinsic markers are not `@derive`-eligible: membership is automatic at declaration, leaving nothing to opt into. (§3.7.4)
005-175. Intrinsic marker membership cannot be opted out of. (§3.7.4)
005-176. The intrinsic markers are `Node` (every `node` type), `Connection` (every `connection` type), and `Effect` (every `effect` type). (§3.7.4)
005-177. Intrinsic markers work as generic bounds, in `&` composition, and as super-trait requirements: `[T: Node]`. (§3.7.4)
005-178. A node/connection/effect type in value position is carried by the `Type[...]` meta-type. (§3.7.4)
005-179. A node/connection/effect instance referenced through a marker bound is a borrow under ordinary borrow semantics. (§3.7.4)
005-180. Any language-defined marker trait may serve as a generic bound or `where`-clause constraint: `fn duplicate[T: Copy](value: T) -> (T, T)`. (§3.7.4)
005-181. `Drop` is not a marker trait: it carries the method `fn drop`. (§3.7.4)
005-182. User-defined empty traits are valid nominal tags but receive no compiler privilege beyond the usual `satisfies` check. (§3.7.4)
005-183. Adding a marker trait to either sub-kind is a language-level change; the category is not user-extensible. (§3.7.4)
005-184. The newtype pattern is the sanctioned orphan-rule workaround: wrap the foreign type locally, then `fulfill SomeForeignTrait for MyVec[T]`. (§3.7.5)
005-185. `@derive` on a type structurally generates the `fulfill` blocks for the named traits. (§3.8)
005-186. The structural-derivation traits are `Eq`, `Ord`, `Hash`, `Clone`, `Display`, and `Debug`. (§3.8.1)
005-187. Every opt-in language-defined marker trait is `@derive`-eligible. (§3.8.1)
005-188. The intrinsic markers `Node`, `Connection`, and `Effect` are not `@derive`-eligible. (§3.8.1)
005-189. `@derive(Copy)` performs the structural Copy-eligibility check — every field's type must be `Copy` — and generates no method body. (§3.8.1)
005-190. `@derive(Circularity)` is a bare opt-in declaration: no structural check, no method body. (§3.8.1)
005-191. Users cannot register new traits for `@derive`; the derivable set is fixed by the language. (§3.8.1)
005-192. Any trait outside the derivable set is implemented with a manual `fulfill` block. (§3.8.1)
005-193. `@derive(Eq)` on a record compares fields pairwise via each field type's own `Eq`. (§3.8.2)
005-194. `@derive(Ord)` on a record yields lexicographic ordering by field declaration order. (§3.8.2)
005-195. `@derive(Hash)` on a record combines each field's hash. (§3.8.2)
005-196. `@derive(Clone)` on a record structurally copies each field. (§3.8.2)
005-197. `@derive(Display)` on a record yields a default record-formatted string whose exact format is compiler-defined. (§3.8.2)
005-198. `@derive(Debug)` on a record yields a structural debug format. (§3.8.2)
005-199. `@derive(Eq)` on an enum compares variant tags, then payload fields pairwise on matching tags. (§3.8.2)
005-200. `@derive(Ord)` on an enum orders by variant declaration order, breaking ties by payload comparison. (§3.8.2)
005-201. The remaining enum derivations follow the same variant-by-variant structural pattern. (§3.8.2)
005-202. Newtype `@derive` may delegate to the underlying type or operate structurally, per the newtype's shape (§6.3 governs). (§3.8.2)
005-203. Derivation requires every field or payload type to satisfy the derived trait: `@derive(Eq)` on `type Foo: x: SomeType` needs `SomeType: Eq`. (§3.8.2)
005-204. A component type lacking the derived trait fails derivation with a compile error naming the offending component. (§3.8.2)
005-205. A manual `fulfill` block for a derived trait takes precedence, suppressing the derived impl for that (trait, type) pair. (§3.8.3)
005-206. After `@literal_suffix` registration the lexer recognizes `<NumericLiteral><suffix>` as a single token. (§3.9)
005-207. A suffixed literal resolves to a call of the registered constructor: `440hz` resolves to `from_hz(440)`. (§3.9)
005-208. The annotation form is `@literal_suffix("<suffix>", <constructor>)`. (§3.9.1)
005-209. A suffix is one or more identifier-continue characters (letters, digits, underscores, Unicode identifier characters): `μs`, `years_2k`. (§3.9.1)
005-210. The constructor argument is the unqualified name of an in-scope function or trait method. (§3.9.1)
005-211. Multiple `@literal_suffix` annotations may decorate one type, each registering a distinct suffix. (§3.9.1)
005-212. Duplicate (suffix, target-type) registrations within a scope are a compile error. (§3.9.1)
005-213. A suffix must resolve to exactly one (suffix, target-type) pair at any use site. (§3.9.1)
005-214. Two modules registering the same suffix for different types make any use site importing both a compile error. (§3.9.1)
005-215. There is no qualified-suffix disambiguation syntax in v1. (§3.9.1)
005-216. Reserved suffixes — every built-in numeric type name (`i8`, `i16`, `i32`, `i64`, `i128`, `u8`, `u16`, `u32`, `u64`, `u128`, `isize`, `usize`, `f32`, `f64`) and the built-in `duration` suffixes — cannot be registered; doing so is a compile error. (§3.9.1)
005-217. A suffix constructor takes exactly one parameter, of a concrete numeric type or bounded by `Numeric` or a sub-trait. (§3.9.2)
005-218. A suffix constructor returns the annotated type. (§3.9.2)
005-219. A suffix constructor must be pure: no side effects, no reactive cell reads. (§3.9.2)
005-220. A generic `Numeric`-bound constructor lets one registration serve both integer and float literals: `fn from_hz[N: Numeric](n: N) -> Frequency`. (§3.9.2)
005-221. Each (suffix, target-type) pair has exactly one registered constructor. (§3.9.2)
005-222. Registering multiple non-generic constructors for one (suffix, target-type) pair is a compile error. (§3.9.2)
005-223. The type checker resolves a suffix among registrations visible at the site; ambiguity or no match is a compile error. (§3.9.3)
005-224. The literal value is passed to the constructor; the call's return value is the literal expression's value. (§3.9.3)
005-225. Suffix resolution is entirely compile-time; no runtime dispatch is involved. (§3.9.3)
005-226. The built-in `duration` suffixes are `ns`, `us`, `μs`, `ms`, `s`, `min`, `h`, `d`. (§3.9.4)
005-227. Built-in duration suffixes are reserved and may not be re-registered for another type in the same scope. (§3.9.4)
005-228. `@literal_suffix` registrations follow normal name-visibility rules: visible in the defining module and to importers. (§3.9.5)
005-229. The built-in `duration` suffixes are globally visible. (§3.9.5)

## 006. Calls & Argument Forms — 31 Rules

006-1. Every call site chooses positional or named argument form per call, not per callee. (§3.5)
006-2. Named form pairs each argument with its parameter name as `name: value`: `clamp(value: temperature, lower: 0, upper: 100)`. (§3.5.1)
006-3. In named form argument order does not matter; the compiler matches arguments by parameter name. (§3.5.1)
006-4. In positional form arguments must appear in parameter declaration order. (§3.5.1)
006-5. A no-argument call is trivially both forms: `person.display()`. (§3.5.1)
006-6. Mixing positional and named arguments in one call is a compile error: `add(3, right: 4)` ✗. (§3.5.2)
006-7. The no-mixing rule applies to every invocation: free functions, trait methods, variant constructors, and all other calls. (§3.5.2)
006-8. The mixed-form diagnostic is reported at the call site and identifies which argument breaks the pattern. (§3.5.2)
006-9. Records are always constructed with named arguments; positional record construction is a compile error. (§3.5.3)
006-10. Tuples are always constructed positionally; named tuple construction is a compile error. (§3.5.3)
006-11. Newtypes are always constructed positionally with exactly one argument, the underlying value. (§3.5.3)
006-12. Free functions and trait methods accept either argument form at every call site. (§3.5.3)
006-13. Positionally-declared enum variants accept only positional argument form: `Some(T)`. (§3.5.3)
006-14. Named-declared enum variants accept both argument forms per call: `Rectangle(width: f64, height: f64)`. (§3.5.3)
006-15. Defaulted parameters may appear in any position, including before non-defaulted ones: `fn render(scale: f32 = 1.0, content: string)`. (§3.5.4)
006-16. In named form, defaulted parameters may be omitted and take their default: `greet(name: "Alice", suffix: "?")`. (§3.5.4)
006-17. In named form, non-defaulted parameters must still be supplied. (§3.5.4)
006-18. Positional form cannot skip a mid-list defaulted parameter; skipping requires named form: `render("hello")` ✗. (§3.5.4)
006-19. The defaulted-before-non-defaulted permission is uniform across functions, operators, and constructor invocations. (§3.5.4)
006-20. A positional call may omit trailing defaulted parameters, supplying a prefix in declaration order: `greet("Alice", "Hi")` leaves the third parameter at its default. (§3.5.4)
006-21. A method call `x.f(args)` always passes the receiver positionally; the argument-form rule governs only `args`. (§3.5.5)
006-22. The receiver's ownership follows the method signature like any other parameter, borrow-equivalent by default. (§3.5.5)
006-23. A receiver is consumed only when the method's first parameter is `own` and the caller writes `(move x).method(args)`. (§3.5.5)
006-24. Dot-call adds no implicit consume rule: `x.foo()` then `x.bar()` behaves like `foo(x)` then `bar(x)`. (§3.5.5)
006-25. The record-update `with` expression takes named-form field overrides: `p1 with name: "new", age: 30`. (§3.5.6)
