006-26. There is no positional `with` form. (§3.5.6)
006-27. Variant patterns may be positional or named, parallel to variant construction. (§3.5.7)
006-28. Mixing positional and named bindings within one pattern is a compile error. (§3.5.7)
006-29. Record patterns are always named. (§3.5.7)
006-30. Tuple patterns are always positional. (§3.5.7)
006-31. A record pattern is exhaustive — it must bind every field; an unneeded field is bound to `_` (`field: _`), and there is no rest or ellipsis token. (§3.5.7)

## 007. Numerics — 223 Rules

007-1. The built-in numeric primitive type set is fixed at fourteen types. (§4.1)
007-2. The signed integer types are `i8`, `i16`, `i32`, `i64`, `i128`, and `isize`. (§4.1)
007-3. The unsigned integer types are `u8`, `u16`, `u32`, `u64`, `u128`, and `usize`. (§4.1)
007-4. The floating-point types are `f32` (IEEE 754 single) and `f64` (IEEE 754 double). (§4.1)
007-5. `i128` and `u128` are first-class primitive types, not library types. (§4.1)
007-6. `isize` and `usize` are distinct platform-pointer-sized integer types. (§4.1)
007-7. `isize` is the array-length and index type. (§4.1)
007-8. `usize` is designated for FFI/ABI interop, byte-count contexts, and low-level memory layout. (§4.1)
007-9. `f16` and `f128` are not included in v1. (§4.1)
007-10. The stdlib defines `alias type byte = u8`, `short = i16`, `int = i32`, `long = i64`, `double = f64`. (§4.2)
007-11. `alias type` aliases are fully transparent — shared identity, no new trait impls, no conversion cost: `int` is `i32`. (§4.2)
007-12. No `float` alias for `f32` is provided; users write `f32` directly. (§4.2)
007-13. No stdlib alias is provided for `i128`, `u128`, `isize`, `usize`, `i8`, `u16`, `u32`, or `u64`. (§4.2)
007-14. The explicit-width names are the canonical primary identifiers and appear unaltered throughout the stdlib and documentation. (§4.2)
007-15. Users may define their own `alias type` aliases anywhere; the built-in aliases have no privileged status. (§4.2)
007-224. An `alias type` may declare generic parameters (type and/or const-generic) and expand to a parameterized use of the underlying type, staying transparent: `alias type Pair[T] = (T, T)`. (§4.2)
007-16. Numeric literals comprise integer literals (decimal, `0x`, `0o`, `0b`) and float literals (decimal point, exponent, or float suffix), specified in §4.3.1–§4.3.2. (§4.3)
007-17. Integer literals are written in decimal, hexadecimal (`0x`), octal (`0o`), or binary (`0b`): `0b1010_1100`. (§4.3.1)
007-18. Underscores are permitted between digits of a numeric literal as visual separators: `1_000_000`. (§4.3.1)
007-19. An integer literal may carry an explicit type suffix separated by an underscore: `255_u8`. (§4.3.1)
007-20. A numeric type suffix forces the literal's type, bypassing both the placeholder mechanism and the trait-level default. (§4.3.1)
007-21. A suffix naming a type the value does not fit is a compile error: `300_u8` ✗. (§4.3.1)
007-22. An unsuffixed integer literal carries the integer placeholder and resolves at the use site per §2.1, cross-kind permitted when the value fits exactly. (§4.3.1)
007-23. Leading zeros are permitted in a decimal literal and carry no octal meaning: `007` is `7`. (§4.3.1)
007-24. A float literal requires at least one of: a decimal point with digits, an exponent, or a float suffix: `1e6`. (§4.3.2)
007-25. A digit is required on each side of the decimal point; leading-dot forms are rejected: `.5` ✗. (§4.3.2)
007-26. Float literals may carry `_f32`/`_f64` type suffixes: `3.14_f32`. (§4.3.2)
007-27. An unsuffixed float literal carries the float placeholder, defaulting to `f64` per §3.1.5. (§4.3.2)
007-28. A float exponent is written `e` or `E` with an optional sign: `2.5e-3`. (§4.3.2)
007-29. A float suffix attaches only to decimal literals; a based literal absorbs valid suffix characters into its digit run, so `0x1_f32` is the integer `0x1F32`. (§4.3.2)
007-30. A numeric literal directly followed by an identifier (no underscore) lexes as one suffixed-literal token: `100ms`. (§4.3.3)
007-31. The type checker resolves an identifier suffix against the built-in suffixes and user-registered suffixes. (§4.3.3)
007-32. The built-in duration suffixes are `ns`, `us`, `μs`, `ms`, `s`, `min`, `h`, `d`, accepted on integer and float literals: `1.5s`. (§4.3.3)
007-33. User-defined literal suffixes registered via `@literal_suffix` resolve through the registered constructor function. (§4.3.3)
007-34. The underscore distinguishes suffix kinds: `5_i32` is a forced numeric type; `5ms` is a suffixed literal. (§4.3.3)
007-35. The `duration` suffixes are the language's only built-in identifier-suffix forms: `100ms`. (§4.3.3)
007-36. `true` and `false` are the two values of `bool`. (§4.3.4)
007-37. `bool` is not numeric and does not participate in the numeric trait hierarchy. (§4.3.4)
007-38. Character literals produce `char` (32-bit Unicode scalar value), which is not numeric: `'a'`. (§4.3.4)
007-39. Byte literals produce fully numeric `u8` values: `b'a'`. (§4.3.4)
007-40. Each numeric operator corresponds to one or more trait methods in the §4.9 trait hierarchy. (§4.4)
007-41. Binary `+`, `-`, `*`, `%` constrain operands to `Add`, `Sub`, `Mul`, `Rem` and produce the trait's associated `Output` type. (§4.4.1)
007-42. Unary `-` constrains its operand to `Neg` and produces the operand's own type. (§4.4.1)
007-43. The `/` operator accepts `Numeric` operands and always produces a `Float` result, even on uniformly-integer operands like `10_i32 / 5_i32`: `5 / 2` is `2.5`, not `2`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-44. The always-float rule governs `/` on numeric operands only; non-numeric primitives with their own `/` (e.g. `duration`) are exempt. (§4.4.1.1)
007-45. `/` promotion is a language-level rule at the operator: operands are verified `Numeric`, implicitly widened to float per §4.5, then `Div::div` dispatches on the float type. (§4.4.1.1)
007-46. `i8`/`u8`/`i16`/`u16` operands to `/` widen to `f32` when the other operand is `f32`, otherwise to `f64`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-47. `i32`/`u32` operands to `/` widen to `f64`, never `f32`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-48. `i64`/`u64` operands to `/` widen to `f64` under the §4.5.4 pragmatic exception: `5_i64 / 2_i64` is `2.5_f64`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-49. `/` on `i128`/`u128` operands is rejected; an explicit cast to float is required first. (§4.4.1.1)
007-50. When both `/` operands are integers and no context pins a float type, the result is a `Float`-placeholder value defaulting to `f64`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-51. `/` is the only operator that performs implicit kind-crossing promotion on uniformly-integer operands. (§4.4.1.1)
007-52. With mixed width and kind, the integer widens to the smallest float covering its range, then floats unify upward: `i64 + f32` yields `f64`. (§4.4.1.1)
007-53. `\` is the integer-division operator, defined on `Integer` operands with an `Integer` result via `IntDiv`: `5 \ 2` is `2`. (§4.4.1.2)
007-54. `\` applied to `Float` operands is a type error. (§4.4.1.2)
007-55. Float-input integer-output division is obtained only by explicit conversion via `T(value)` or `From`/`Into`. (§4.4.1.2)
007-56. The `\` token is reused: integer division at value level, escape character inside string/character literals; position disambiguates. (§4.4.1.2)
007-57. `%` accepts integer and float operands and produces a result of the operands' kind. (§4.4.1.2)
007-58. Unary `-` is defined on signed integers and floats only; applying it to an unsigned value is a compile-time type error. (§4.4.1.2)
007-59. Negative literals are exempt from the unary-minus-on-unsigned rule: `-N` parses as a single signed literal per §2.4.5. (§4.4.1.2)
007-60. `\` truncates toward zero and `%` takes the dividend's sign: `-5 \ 2` is `-2`, `-5 % 2` is `-1`. (§4.4.1.2)
007-61. Bitwise operators are integer-only; applying them to float values is a type error. (§4.4.2)
007-62. `&`, `|`, `^`, and unary `~` dispatch via `BitAnd`, `BitOr`, `BitXor`, `BitNot` and produce the operands' same integer type. (§4.4.2)
007-63. `<<` and `>>` dispatch via `Shl` and `Shr` and produce the same type as the left operand. (§4.4.2)
007-64. The language has no bit-reinterpret operator. (§4.4.2)
007-65. Float bit access uses the stdlib `to_bits`/`from_bits` pair: `f32` ↔ `u32`, `f64` ↔ `u64`. (§4.4.2)
007-66. `to_bits`/`from_bits` are ordinary stdlib behaviors over the `raw_read`/`raw_write` intrinsics. (§4.4.2)
007-67. A `to_bits`/`from_bits` round-trip lowers to a zero-cost bitcast. (§4.4.2)
007-68. `to_bits` is a bit reinterpret, not a value conversion: `(1.0_f32).to_bits()` is `1065353216`. (§4.4.2)
007-69. `&` and `|` carry type-level meanings elsewhere; inside value-level expressions they are bitwise operators, selected by positional context. (§4.4.2)
007-70. Bitwise `|` and operator-application `|>` are distinct tokens; `|>` is the loosest-binding operator while bitwise operators bind tighter than the logical operators, per the §4.4.7 precedence table. (§4.4.2)
007-71. `>>` shifts arithmetically (sign-extending) on signed types and logically (zero-extending) on unsigned types, dispatched via the type's `Shr` impl. (§4.4.2)
007-72. No `>>>` operator exists; `>>` has no other value-level meaning. (§4.4.2)
007-73. Operator precedence, loosest to tightest: `|>`; `or`; `and`; `not`; bitwise `|`/`^`/`&`; `..`; comparison; shifts `<<`/`>>`; additive; multiplicative; prefix `-`/`~`/`weak`; postfix `?`/`!`/`.`/`[]`/`()`; `::`. (§4.4.7)
007-74. Bitwise operators bind tighter than the logical operators but looser than comparison (the C convention): `a & b is c` parses as `a & (b is c)`. (§4.4.7)
007-75. The range operator `..` binds looser than arithmetic: `0..n + 1` parses as `0..(n + 1)`. (§4.4.7)
007-76. `not` binds looser than comparison and negates the whole comparison: `not a is b` parses as `not (a is b)`. (§4.4.7)
007-77. Binary operators are left-associative, comparison and `..` are non-associative, and prefix operators are right-associative. (§4.4.7)
007-78. Each arithmetic policy variant — wrapping `…%`, saturating `…|`, checked `…?` — binds at its base operator's tier (additive, multiplicative, or prefix negation). (§4.4.7)
007-79. Comparison operators `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=` produce `bool` and are defined on both integer and float kinds. (§4.4.3)
007-80. Mixed-kind comparisons widen per §4.5 before comparing. (§4.4.3)
007-81. Float comparison follows IEEE 754 NaN semantics: `NaN < x` is `false`. (§4.4.3)
007-82. Comparison chaining is rejected by the type system though the grammar admits it: `a < b < c` ✗. (§4.4.3)
007-83. Equality operators are the keyword forms `is` and `is not`, constraining operands to `Eq` and producing `bool`. (§4.4.4)
007-84. Symbolic `==` and `!=` are not equality operators; they are reserved against future use. (§4.4.4)
007-85. Mixed-kind equality widens per §4.5 before comparing. (§4.4.4)
007-86. Float equality via `is`/`is not` is permitted despite IEEE 754 hazards: `0.1 + 0.2 is not 0.3`. (§4.4.4)
007-87. Approximate float comparison is delegated to the stdlib, e.g. `approx_eq(a, b, epsilon)`. (§4.4.4)
007-88. In an expression mixing integer and float operands, the integer widens implicitly to the float type before the operation. (§4.4.5)
007-89. During generic-body inference per §2.2.2, each operator implies a fixed trait constraint on its operands and result per a normative mapping table. (§4.4.6)
007-90. The shift right operand may be any unsigned integer type narrower than or equal to `u32` (implicitly widened); other types require an explicit cast. (§4.4.6)
007-91. Wrapping and saturating operator forms constrain operands to the corresponding `Wrapping...`/`Saturating...` traits and produce the operands' type. (§4.4.6)
007-92. Checked operator forms constrain operands to the corresponding `Checked...` traits and produce `Option[T]`. (§4.4.6)
007-93. A bare trait name in an operator constraint denotes the default-parameter instance per §3.1.6: `Add` means `Add[Subject]`. (§4.4.6)
007-94. For operands of different types, an in-scope cross-type instance (`Add[T2] for T1`) may be inferred; otherwise the operand types must unify via §4.5 widening. (§4.4.6)
007-95. Inference attaches the union of operator-implied constraints across the body to the generic signature; call sites must satisfy it. (§4.4.6)
007-96. Umbrella traits may substitute for fine-grained constraint sets in reported signatures only when unambiguous; the underlying constraints remain fine-grained. (§4.4.6)
007-97. Implicit widening fires only when the conversion is provably lossless (one pragmatic exception, §4.5.4); all other conversions require explicit forms. (§4.5)
007-98. Same-signedness integer widening is implicit: `i8` widens to `i16`/`i32`/`i64`/`i128`/`isize`. (§4.5.1)
007-99. Unsigned-to-wider-signed widening is implicit (always representable): `u8` → `i16`. (§4.5.1)
007-100. Same-width signed/unsigned crossing requires an explicit cast: `i32` ↔ `u32`. (§4.5.1)
007-101. Signed-to-wider-unsigned conversion is never implicit: `i8` → `u16` ✗. (§4.5.1)
007-102. Integer narrowing is never implicit. (§4.5.1)
007-103. Integer-to-float widening is implicit exactly when the integer's full range fits the float's mantissa: widths ≤ 16 bits into `f32`, ≤ 32 bits into `f64`. (§4.5.2)
007-104. `i128`/`u128` never widen implicitly to any float type. (§4.5.2)
007-105. `f32` → `f64` widening is implicit. (§4.5.3)
007-106. `f64` → `f32` conversion requires an explicit cast. (§4.5.3)
007-107. `i64`/`u64` → `f64` is permitted as implicit widening despite possible loss of low-order bits above 2⁵³. (§4.5.4)
007-108. The `i64`/`u64` → `f64` case is the only deviation from strict lossless implicit widening. (§4.5.4)
007-109. Conversions outside the implicit-widening tables use explicit `T(value)` or, when fallible, `TryFrom`/`TryInto` returning `Result`. (§4.5.5)
007-110. Float-to-integer conversion is never implicit, in any direction. (§4.5.5)
007-111. Cross-type conversions involving user-defined types go through `From`/`Into`. (§4.5.5)
007-112. Mixed-kind `+`, `-`, `*`, `%` widen operands to a common type per §4.5, then dispatch the trait method on that type. (§4.5.6)
007-113. Every arithmetic operation has four variants — default trapping, wrapping, saturating, and checked. (§4.6)
007-114. Default arithmetic operators trap on overflow at runtime in all build modes; no debug-traps/release-wraps distinction exists. (§4.6.1)
007-115. The overflow-trap diagnostic identifies the operation, the operand values, and the source location. (§4.6.1)
007-116. Overflow traps cannot be caught as values. (§4.6.1)
007-117. Wrapping operators `+%`, `-%`, `*%`, `\%`, `%%`, and unary `-%` perform modular two's-complement arithmetic: `255_u8 +% 1 is 0_u8`. (§4.6.2)
007-118. `\%` wraps the sole integer-division overflow case, signed minimum divided by −1: `(-128_i8) \% (-1_i8) is -128_i8`. (§4.6.2)
007-119. Unary wrapping negation of a signed type's minimum yields the minimum itself without trapping (`-128_i8` negates to `-128_i8`). (§4.6.2)
007-120. `%%` (wrapping remainder) is defined for completeness. (§4.6.2)
007-121. No `/%` operator exists; `/` always produces `Float`, whose overflow follows IEEE 754. (§4.6.2)
007-122. `\%` defines no division-by-zero result; a zero divisor traps. (§4.6.2)
007-123. Saturating operators `+|`, `-|`, `*|`, `\|`, `%|`, and unary `-|` clamp to the destination type's range bounds on overflow: `255_u8 +| 1 is 255_u8`. (§4.6.3)
007-124. `\|` clamps signed minimum divided by −1 to the type's maximum: `(-128_i8) \| (-1_i8) is 127_i8`. (§4.6.3)
007-125. Unary saturating negation of a signed type's minimum yields the type's maximum (`-128_i8` negates to `127_i8`). (§4.6.3)
007-126. `%|` (saturating remainder) is defined for completeness. (§4.6.3)
007-127. No `/|` operator exists. (§4.6.3)
007-128. Saturating division by zero is not defined; the recoverable form is `\?`. (§4.6.3)
007-129. Checked operators `+?`, `-?`, `*?`, `\?`, `%?`, and unary `-?` return `Option[T]`: `Some(result)` or `None` instead of trapping. (§4.6.4)
007-130. `\?` returns `None` on overflow or division by zero. (§4.6.4)
007-131. `%?` returns `None` on overflow or a zero divisor. (§4.6.4)
007-132. `/?` widens integer operands per §4.4.1.1 and dispatches `CheckedDiv` on the float type, returning `Option[Float]`. (§4.6.4)
007-133. On float operands `/?` returns `None` when IEEE 754 would produce NaN or ±Infinity, e.g. `0.0/0.0`. (§4.6.4)
007-134. Compile-time constant overflow is a compile error regardless of operator variant: `const x: u8 = 200_u8 +% 100_u8` ✗. (§4.6.5)
007-135. Float overflow produces signed infinity (`f64::INFINITY`/`f64::NEG_INFINITY`). (§4.6.6)
007-136. Float underflow may produce subnormals or signed zero. (§4.6.6)
007-137. NaN propagates through float operations involving NaN operands. (§4.6.6)
007-138. Float operators have no wrapping or saturating variants. (§4.6.6)
007-139. Checked operators on floats are normatively defined, returning `None` when the operation produces NaN or infinity. (§4.6.6)
007-140. Integer division by zero traps at runtime. (§4.6.7)
007-141. No wrapping or saturating variant exists for division by zero. (§4.6.7)
007-142. Explicit numeric conversion uses call syntax on the target type: `T(value)`. (§4.7)
007-143. The `as` keyword is not a cast; it is reserved for naming — placement names and import aliases. (§4.7)
007-144. `T(x)` is the default conversion and traps at runtime on out-of-range values: `u8(x)` traps when `x` is `300_i32`. (§4.7.1)
007-145. `T%(x)` wraps modularly via `WrappingAs[T]`: `u8%(x)` is `44` when `x` is `300_i32`. (§4.7.1)
007-146. `T|(x)` clamps to `T`'s range bounds via `SaturatingAs[T]`: `u8|(x)` is `255` when `x` is `300_i32`. (§4.7.1)
007-147. `T?(x)` returns `Option[T]` via `CheckedAs[T]`, `None` on out-of-range: `u8?(x)`. (§4.7.1)
007-148. The policy suffix attaches to the target type immediately before the argument list: `u8%(x)`, never `u8(x)%`. (§4.7.1)
007-149. Cast-policy suffixed casts parse unambiguously because a type name is never a value operand, so `u8%`/`u8|`/`u8?` cannot read as binary operators. (§4.7.1)
007-150. On lossless widening conversions, `T(x)` is equivalent to implicit widening. (§4.7.2)
007-151. The `%`/`|`/`?` variants on lossless conversions are equivalent to `T(x)` and remain syntactically valid for generic code. (§4.7.2)
007-152. Float-to-integer conversion truncates toward zero. (§4.7.3)
007-153. `T%(x)` on out-of-range float-to-integer input is implementation-defined (typically truncation modulo the destination range). (§4.7.3)
007-154. `T|(x)` on float-to-integer input saturates to the destination's range bounds, treating NaN as 0. (§4.7.3)
007-155. `T?(x)` on float-to-integer input returns `None` for NaN, infinity, or out-of-range values. (§4.7.3)
007-156. Conversion forms desugar to trait methods callable via uniform call syntax: `x.wrapping_as::[u8]()` is equivalent to `u8%(x)`. (§4.7.4)
007-157. The `T(x)` family is the canonical user-facing cast syntax; the trait methods exist for trait-constrained generic code. (§4.7.4)
007-158. `T(value)` is numeric conversion when `T` is a built-in scalar: `f64(x)`. (§4.7.5)
007-159. Scalar `T(value)` also performs newtype extraction when the source is a newtype: `f64(meters)` yields the wrapped `f64`. (§4.7.5)
007-160. `T(value)` is ordinary construction when `T` is a user type: `Meters(5.0)`. (§4.7.5)
007-161. The conversion and construction roles never overlap: `T(value)` has exactly one meaning per `T`. (§4.7.5)
007-162. Conversions between user types go through `From`/`Into`/`TryFrom`/`TryInto`, not `T(value)`. (§4.7.5)
007-163. Special numeric operations are trait methods, callable via method-call, conventional, and trait-path syntax per §3.4. (§4.8)
007-164. `abs`, `min`, `max` are available on all `Numeric` types via the `Abs`, `Min`, `Max` traits. (§4.8.1)
007-165. `abs` on a signed type's minimum traps on overflow: `i32::MIN.abs()` traps. (§4.8.1)
007-166. `wrapping_abs` and `saturating_abs` method variants are provided. (§4.8.1)
007-167. Float `min`/`max` are NaN-propagating: if either operand is NaN, the result is NaN. (§4.8.1)
007-168. `min_or`/`max_or` are NaN-suppressing variants: the non-NaN operand when exactly one is NaN, NaN when both are. (§4.8.1)
007-169. Float-only operations comprise `sqrt`; `sin`/`cos`/`tan`/`asin`/`acos`/`atan`/`atan2`; `ln`/`log2`/`log10`/`log`; `exp`/`exp2`; `floor`/`ceil`/`round`/`trunc`; `is_nan`/`is_infinite`/`is_finite`/`is_normal`. (§4.8.2)
007-170. Each float-only operation has its own fine-grained trait, e.g. `Sqrt`, `Sin`, `Floor`. (§4.8.2)
007-171. No bare one-argument `log(x)` exists; natural log is `ln(x)`. (§4.8.2)
007-172. Arbitrary-base logarithm is two-argument with the base first: `log(base, x)`. (§4.8.2)
007-173. `floor`, `ceil`, `round`, `trunc` are defined only on floats. (§4.8.2)
007-174. Integer-domain rounding operations (e.g. ceiling division) are delegated to the stdlib, such as a `div_ceil` method. (§4.8.2)
007-175. `pow` splits into two traits: `IntPow` on `Integer` and `FloatPow` on `Float`. (§4.8.3)
007-176. `IntPow` takes integer base and exponent, yields an integer, and traps on overflow or negative exponent: `2.pow(-1)` ✗. (§4.8.3)
007-177. `FloatPow` takes a float base and any-numeric exponent (integers promote to float), yielding float: `(2.0_f64).pow(-1)` is `0.5`. (§4.8.3)
007-178. The typer selects the `pow` trait by the receiver's type: `2.0.pow(0.5)` uses `FloatPow`. (§4.8.3)
007-179. The `Integer` umbrella includes `IntPow`; the `Float` umbrella includes `FloatPow`. (§4.8.3)
007-180. Numeric constants are associated values on the concrete types, accessed via path syntax: `f64::PI`. (§4.8.4)
007-181. Float types provide `PI`, `E`, `TAU`, `LN_2`, `LN_10`, `INFINITY`, `NEG_INFINITY`, `NAN`; integer types provide `MIN` and `MAX`. (§4.8.4)
007-182. Numeric constants are `const` declarations per §2.4.1.1: no runtime storage, inlined at use sites. (§4.8.4)
007-183. The numeric trait hierarchy instantiates §3.6's fine-grained-plus-umbrella pattern. (§4.9)
007-184. Each operator has its own fine-grained trait whose method name matches the conventional operator name, e.g. `Add::add`. (§4.9.1)
007-185. The fine-grained set is normative as a pattern — one trait per capability — not as an exact list; the stdlib may add further fine-grained traits. (§4.9.1)
007-186. Binary arithmetic traits are generic over `Rhs` defaulting to `Subject` with associated `Output` defaulting to `Subject`: `fn add(a: Subject, b: Rhs) -> Output`. (§4.9.1)
007-187. `Div` is non-generic and declared on the `Float` umbrella only: `fn div(a: Subject, b: Subject) -> Subject`. (§4.9.1)
007-188. `CheckedDiv` is generic for consistency, but instances are auto-derived only for `Float` types where `Div` is implemented. (§4.9.1)
007-189. Checked arithmetic trait methods return `Option[Output]`: `fn checked_add(a: Subject, b: Rhs) -> Option[Output]`. (§4.9.1)
007-190. `Shl`/`Shr` trait methods take the shift count as `u32`: `fn shl(value: Subject, n: u32) -> Subject`. (§4.9.1)
007-191. The cast traits are destination-parameterized: `WrappingAs[T]`, `SaturatingAs[T]`, and `CheckedAs[T]` returning `Option[T]`. (§4.9.1)
007-192. `Zero` and `One` are nullary-constructor traits: `fn zero() -> Subject`. (§4.9.1)
007-193. `Eq` declares the single method `eq`; both `is` and `is not` flow through it. (§4.9.1)
007-194. `a is not b` desugars at parse time to `not (a is b)`; `is not` has no trait method of its own. (§4.9.1)
007-195. `Ord` is a methodless umbrella requiring `Lt`, `Le`, `Gt`, `Ge`, auto-satisfying once those four are satisfied. (§4.9.1)
007-196. `Le`, `Gt`, `Ge` each declare one defaulted method over `Lt::lt`/`Eq::eq` (e.g. `ge(a, b)` is `not lt(a, b)`) and auto-satisfy once their `requires` are met. (§4.9.1)
007-197. `Lt` and `Eq` are the only ordering-closure traits with abstract methods; fulfilling both cascades `Le`, `Gt`, `Ge`, and `Ord`. (§4.9.1)
007-198. `@derive(Ord)` generates the full fulfill-block set structurally; manual implementation needs only `fulfill Lt for X` and `fulfill Eq for X`. (§4.9.1)
007-199. `Ord` and `Eq` are standalone traits outside the numeric umbrellas, available to non-numeric types. (§4.9.1)
007-200. `IntDiv`'s dispatch method is named `intdiv`: `fn intdiv(a: Subject, b: Rhs) -> Output`. (§4.9.1)
007-201. The bitwise trait methods are the single-word `bitand`, `bitor`, `bitxor`, `bitnot`: `fn bitand(a: Subject, b: Subject) -> Subject`. (§4.9.1)
007-202. Umbrella traits combine fine-grained traits via `requires`, declare no methods, and are automatically satisfied when all required traits are satisfied. (§4.9.2)
007-203. `Numeric` requires `Add`, `Sub`, `Mul`, `Zero`, `One`, the wrapping/saturating/checked add, sub, and mul variants, and `Abs`, `Min`, `Max`. (§4.9.2)
007-204. `Integer` requires `Numeric`, `Rem`, `IntDiv`, the bitwise and shift traits, the wrapping/saturating int-div and rem variants, `CheckedIntDiv`, `CheckedRem`, and `IntPow`. (§4.9.2)
007-205. `Signed` requires `Integer`, `Neg`, `WrappingNeg`, `SaturatingNeg`, `CheckedNeg`. (§4.9.2)
007-206. `Unsigned` requires `Integer` only and does not require `Neg`. (§4.9.2)
007-207. `Neg` and its wrapping/saturating/checked variants are excluded from `Numeric`; they appear on `Signed` and `Float`. (§4.9.2)
007-208. The signed/unsigned distinction is exactly the presence or absence of `Neg` in a type's effective method set. (§4.9.2)
007-209. The umbrella defaults are `Numeric` → `i32`, `Integer` → `i32`, `Float` → `f64`, `Signed` → `i32`, `Unsigned` → `u32`. (§4.9.3)
007-210. The fourteen built-in numeric types auto-implement their fine-grained traits per §3.3; umbrella satisfaction follows transitively. (§4.9.4)
007-211. All integer types auto-implement the arithmetic, bitwise, shift, wrapping, saturating, and checked fine-grained traits, the cast traits, and `Zero`, `One`, `Abs`, `Min`, `Max`, `Ord`, `Eq`, `IntPow`. (§4.9.4)
007-212. Integer types do not implement `CheckedDiv`; it is float-only because `/` widens integers to float. (§4.9.4)
007-213. Among integer types, only signed ones implement `Neg`, `WrappingNeg`, `SaturatingNeg`, `CheckedNeg`. (§4.9.4)
007-214. Integer types satisfy `Integer`, `Numeric`, and `Signed` or `Unsigned` as appropriate. (§4.9.4)
007-215. Float types auto-implement `Add`, `Sub`, `Mul`, `Div`, `Rem`, `Neg`, the checked add/sub/mul/div/neg variants, the float-only operation and inspection traits, and `Zero`, `One`, `Abs`, `Min`, `Max`, `Ord`, `Eq`, `FloatPow`. (§4.9.4)
007-216. Floats do not implement the wrapping or saturating arithmetic traits. (§4.9.4)
007-217. Float `WrappingAs[T]` is implemented for integer and wider-float destinations, but not for narrower-float destinations. (§4.9.4)
007-218. Float `SaturatingAs[T]` and `CheckedAs[T]` are implemented for integer, narrower-float, and wider-float destinations. (§4.9.4)
007-219. Floats satisfy `Float` and `Numeric` but not `Signed`, which requires `Integer`. (§4.9.4)
007-220. Built-in numeric types implement same-type trait instantiations only: `fulfill Add[i32] for i32`, never `Add[i64] for i32`. (§4.9.4)
007-221. Cross-type arithmetic between built-in numeric types requires explicit conversion. (§4.9.4)
007-222. User-defined types may implement cross-type instantiations such as `Add[i32] for Distance`. (§4.9.4)
007-223. User-defined numeric-like types implement whichever fine-grained traits are appropriate; umbrella satisfaction follows. (§4.9.4)

## 008. Type Composition: `&`, `dyn`, `Type[...]` — 70 Rules

008-1. The `&` type-intersection operator appears in exactly three contexts: trait conjunction in generic bounds, trait intersection behind `dyn` at value position, and record intersection in `type` definitions. (§5)
008-2. In a generic parameter list or where-clause, `T: A & B` constrains `T` to types for which both `fulfill A for T` and `fulfill B for T` exist: `fn pick[T: Drivable & Insurable](item: T)`. (§5.1)
008-3. `&` in a generic bound is constraint conjunction, not a type expression. (§5.1)
008-4. A conjoined constraint is resolved statically at every use site; instantiations are monomorphized with no runtime dispatch cost. (§5.1)
008-5. A conjoined constraint set is checked at the call site for each concrete instantiation. (§5.1)
008-6. Constraint conjunction is commutative and associative: `A & B`, `B & A`, and `(A & B) & C` are equivalent constraint sets. (§5.1)
008-7. A trait may appear at value position — variable, parameter, field, or return type — only when wrapped in `dyn`. (§5.2)
008-8. A trait object dispatches method calls dynamically through a vtable. (§5.2)
008-9. A single-trait trait-object type is written `dyn Trait`: `let x: dyn Drivable = dyn some_value`. (§5.2.1)
008-10. A multi-trait trait-object type requires parentheses around the trait intersection: `dyn (Drivable & Insurable)`. (§5.2.1)
008-11. Without parentheses `dyn` binds tighter than `&`: `dyn Drivable & Insurable` parses as `(dyn Drivable) & Insurable`. (§5.2.1)
008-12. A trait object is not a valid operand of trait intersection, so `(dyn Drivable) & Insurable` is ill-formed. (§5.2.1)
008-13. `dyn` is mandatory at every trait-object value position; a bare trait type is rejected: `let x: Drivable` ✗ when `Drivable` is a trait. (§5.2.2)
008-14. A bare trait intersection at value position without `dyn` is likewise rejected: `let x: Drivable & Insurable` ✗ when both operands are traits. (§5.2.2)
008-15. Each method invocation on a trait object costs one indirect call through the vtable. (§5.2.3)
008-16. A trait object stores a vtable pointer adjacent to the value's data. (§5.2.3)
008-17. A trait is object-safe iff every method in its effective method set — its own plus those inherited through `requires` — is vtable-dispatchable and its associated types are bound at the `dyn` site. (§5.2.4)
008-18. Object safety is strict and whole-trait: a single non-dispatchable method makes the entire trait non-object-safe, with no per-method carve-out. (§5.2.4)
008-19. A method is vtable-dispatchable only if its first parameter is typed `Subject`: `fn make() -> Subject` lacks a receiver and is not dispatchable. (§5.2.4)
008-20. A method is vtable-dispatchable only if `Subject` appears nowhere besides the receiver — in no other parameter and not in the return type: `fn clone(value: Subject) -> own Subject` ✗. (§5.2.4)
008-21. A method is vtable-dispatchable only if it declares no type parameters of its own. (§5.2.4)
008-22. A trait with associated types is object-safe only when they are bound at the `dyn` site: `dyn Iterator[Item = i32]` ✓; bare `dyn Iterator` is a compile error. (§5.2.4)
008-23. A non-object-safe trait used in a `dyn` position is a compile error at the use site, naming the trait, the offending method, and the violated rule. (§5.2.4)
008-24. A trait whose `requires` closure includes a non-object-safe trait is itself non-object-safe. (§5.2.4)
008-25. The object-safety rules also govern the structural closure type `fn(P) -> R`, treated as a single-method callable with the closure value as receiver and concrete `P`/`R`. (§5.2.4)
008-26. There is no per-method escape hatch that admits a trait as `dyn` while marking individual methods unavailable on the object. (§5.2.4)
008-27. Guidance: to make a trait that bundles dispatchable and non-dispatchable methods usable as `dyn`, factor the non-dispatchable methods into a sibling trait — the dispatchable remainder is then object-safe, and the spec sanctions this factoring as the right design, not a workaround. (§5.2.4)
008-28. Erasing a concrete value to a trait object is explicit and never implicit, written with the `dyn` prefix on the value: `let shape: dyn Drawable = dyn circle`. (§5.2.5)
008-29. The target trait set of a `dyn` coercion is taken from context: the binding's annotation, the parameter type at a call, or the collection's element type. (§5.2.5)
008-30. A `dyn` coercion whose target trait set no context determines is a compile error requesting an annotation. (§5.2.5)
008-31. At the coercion site the value's concrete type must fulfill every trait in the target set. (§5.2.5)
008-32. At the coercion site every trait in the target set must be object-safe. (§5.2.5)
008-33. `dyn value` consumes the value into the trait object as a category-B storage operation — an implicit move, no `move` keyword. (§5.2.5)
008-34. For a `Copy` concrete type, `dyn value` copies the value instead of consuming it. (§5.2.5)
008-35. A `dyn` value owns its erased payload and is itself a real owner. (§5.2.5)
008-36. Position disambiguates `dyn`: in type position it denotes the trait-object type; in value position it is the coercion. (§5.2.5)
008-37. A `type` declaration whose right-hand side is a record-record intersection produces a new nominal record combining both operands' fields: `type InsuredCar = Car & Insured`. (§5.3)
008-38. A record-intersection type is nominally distinct from both operands; values are not implicitly assignable in either direction. (§5.3)
008-39. Converting between a record-intersection type and its operands requires explicit construction or a `From` impl. (§5.3)
008-40. Record intersection is a definitional combinator, not subtyping; the language has no nominal subtyping. (§5.3)
008-41. A same-named field with identical type and identical visibility in both operands merges into a single field. (§5.3.1)
008-42. A same-named field with different types in the two operands makes the intersection a compile error naming the field and the two types. (§5.3.1)
008-43. A same-named field with the same type but different visibility makes the intersection a compile error naming the field and the two visibility specifiers. (§5.3.1)
008-44. A record-intersection type inherits operand traits only via explicit `@derive`, which generates `fulfill` blocks delegating to the operand types' implementations: `@derive(Display, Hash)` on `type InsuredCar = Car & Insured`. (§5.3.2)
008-45. `alias type X = A & B` over traits is valid and names a constraint for bound positions: `fn process[T: X]` is equivalent to `fn process[T: A & B]`. (§5.4)
008-46. `alias type X = dyn (A & B)` is valid and names a dynamic-dispatch trait-object type. (§5.4)
008-47. `alias type X = A & B` over records is rejected at compile time; record intersection requires a `type` declaration to create the new identity. (§5.4)
008-48. Intersection is well-defined only within `{trait & trait}` and `{record & record}`; all cross-kind combinations and `Enum & Enum` are rejected at compile time. (§5.5)
008-49. Intersections involving tuples, function types, or primitive types are rejected; those kinds are not subject to intersection. (§5.5)
008-50. An invalid intersection is reported at the `&` expression with the operand kinds named. (§5.5)
008-51. There are no variance markers and no subtyping between generic instantiations: `Container[Cat]` and `Container[Animal]` are unrelated types. (§5.6)
008-52. Intersection of two distinct generic instantiations receives no special rule; the ordinary kind rules for the operands apply. (§5.6)
008-53. A trait conjunction that no type satisfies is not an error at the constraint declaration; it fails at call sites where no concrete type satisfies all conjuncts. (§5.6)
008-54. `Type[C]` is the type of a type value — a value whose value is a type satisfying the constraint `C`. (§5.7)
008-55. `dyn` lifts a trait to value position as an erased instance while `Type[…]` lifts it as the type itself: `dyn Drivable` is some Drivable value; `Type[Drivable]` is some Drivable type. (§5.7)
008-56. A trait constraint argument is the existential form: `Type[Drivable]` admits any type satisfying the trait. (§5.7.1)
008-57. A concrete or generic-parameter argument is the singleton form admitting exactly that type: `Type[PostCard]`, `Type[T]`. (§5.7.1)
008-58. `Type[…]` appears only in value position — attr, parameter, return value, `let`/`const` binding, or generic value argument. (§5.7.2)
008-59. `Type[…]` never appears in bound position; a generic bound is always a trait: write `[T: Drivable]`, never `[T: Type[Drivable]]`. (§5.7.2)
008-60. A type value is resolved at compile time and is never erased behind a vtable. (§5.7.3)
008-61. An existential `Type[C]` slot desugars to a compiler-introduced generic parameter: `attr item: Type[Drivable]` becomes `[~F: Drivable]` with `attr item: Type[~F]`. (§5.7.3)
008-62. Each supply site picks a concrete type and monomorphizes the enclosing type for it: `ListView | item=PostCard` infers `~F = PostCard`. (§5.7.3)
008-63. Two anonymous `Type[C]` slots desugar to two independent parameters and may hold different types. (§5.7.3)
008-64. Forcing two type-value slots to hold the same type requires a named parameter used in both: `[T: Drivable]` with `Type[T]` in two places. (§5.7.3)
008-65. Runtime selection among type values of the same slot type is allowed: a `match` may yield a `Type[C]`. (§5.7.3)
008-66. Runtime selection among different node structures is the role of `given` (§13.9.13), not of type values. (§5.7.3)
008-67. A type value may be stored in persistent slots: record fields, attrs, and cells. (§5.7.4)
008-68. Whatever a receiver does with a `Type[C]` value — construct it, place it — must be justified by what `C` guarantees. (§5.7.5)
008-69. A bare `Type[Node]` admits any node type but can only be held, passed, stored, and returned — not placed. (§5.7.5)
008-70. Placing a type value requires its constraint to be a trait declaring the instantiation interface: required attrs/consts/cells and, for connections, endpoints. (§5.7.5)

## 009. Records, Enums & Newtypes — 118 Rules

009-1. Records (product types), enums (sum types), and newtypes (wrapper types) are the three user-defined nominal compound types, with identity by name rather than structure. (§6)
009-2. All three compound kinds participate uniformly in the trait system. (§6)
009-3. A record is a nominal product type with a fixed set of named fields and carries data only. (§6.1)
009-4. Behavior associated with a record type is expressed via free functions and trait implementations, not record methods. (§6.1)
009-5. A record is declared as `type Name[generics]:` followed by a body of field declarations: `type Point[T]:` with fields `x: T` and `y: T`. (§6.1.1)
009-6. A field declaration gives a name and a type — any type expression. (§6.1.1)
009-7. A record's generic parameters are in scope within its field declarations. (§6.1.1)
009-8. A record body may contain at most one `satisfies` clause listing the traits the type promises to implement. (§6.1.1)
009-9. Every `satisfies`-listed trait must have a matching `fulfill` block reachable through the module graph, except pure-requirement umbrella traits, which are satisfied automatically when their requirements are. (§6.1.1)
009-10. A record may declare no fields; the bodiless `type Marker` is a zero-field nominal tag. (§6.1.1)
009-11. Record fields have no defaults; default-bearing construction uses a constructor function with default parameters (the smart-constructor pattern §10.5.1), not the record type. (§6.1.2)
009-12. A record value is constructed by calling the type name with named field arguments: `Window(title: "Main")`. (§6.1.3)
009-13. Named construction arguments may appear in any order. (§6.1.3)
009-14. Every field must be supplied at construction. (§6.1.3)
009-15. Supplying the same field twice at construction is a compile error. (§6.1.3)
009-16. Supplying an unknown field name at construction is a compile error. (§6.1.3)
009-17. Positional record construction is not supported. (§6.1.3)
009-18. Generic records require concrete type arguments at construction, inferred from the field arguments or given explicitly via turbofish: `Point::[i32](x: 1, y: 2)`. (§6.1.3)
009-19. A multi-line construction argument list permits a trailing comma after the final argument. (§6.1.3)
009-20. A record field is read with dot notation: `record.field_name`. (§6.1.4)
009-21. The compiler distinguishes field access from method call by the syntax following the dot. (§6.1.4)
009-22. Record fields are read-only and cannot be reassigned after construction; binding immutability applies transitively to fields. (§6.1.4)
009-23. The `with` expression produces a new record derived from a base, with selected fields overridden or merged from other records: `base with name: "new"`. (§6.1.5)
009-24. Single-line `with` lists merge sources and field overrides comma-separated: `base with other1, other2, name: "new"`. (§6.1.5)
009-25. Multi-line `with` puts field overrides in a colon-introduced indented body, optionally after merge sources: `base with other1:` then `name: "new"` lines. (§6.1.5)
009-26. Single-line and multi-line are the only two `with` surface forms; mixing them in one expression is a parse error. (§6.1.5)
009-27. The components of a `with` expression are evaluated left to right. (§6.1.5)
009-28. The result of `with` is always a new record of the base's type. (§6.1.5)
009-29. Merge sources and field overrides apply left-to-right, with later assignments winning on conflict. (§6.1.5)
009-30. A field set by no merge source or override keeps the base's value. (§6.1.5)
009-31. Every merge source must have the exact same type as the base; cross-type merge is a compile error: `car with insured_record` ✗. (§6.1.5)
009-32. In a reactive declaration context `with` gains per-field rules (§13.2.9.8): bare reactive names alias, reactive expressions become implicit derived cells, and non-reactive values become static fields. (§6.1.5)
009-33. Every override field name must exist in the base's type; overriding a non-existent field is a compile error. (§6.1.5)
009-34. Override values must be type-compatible with the field's declared type. (§6.1.5)
009-35. Each record field carries its own visibility specifier, independent of the type's and the constructor's visibility: `private password_hash: string`. (§6.1.6)
009-36. A field with no visibility specifier defaults to `shared` — readable within the package. (§6.1.6)
009-37. A field's visibility may not exceed the enclosing type's; a `public` field on a `private` type is a compile error. (§6.1.6)
009-38. Access to a field from outside its visibility scope is a compile error reported at the access site, not at the record's declaration. (§6.1.6)
009-39. A record constructor's visibility is controlled independently of the type's via the `outer(inner)` form: `public(private) type Email`. (§6.1.7)
009-40. With a private constructor the type name remains visible while `TypeName(...)` construction is unreachable outside the constructor's scope. (§6.1.7)
009-41. Constructor visibility may not exceed type visibility; an inner specifier more permissive than the outer is a compile error. (§6.1.7)
009-42. `@derive` on a record generates structural implementations for a fixed trait set: `@derive(Eq, Ord, Hash, Clone, Display, Debug)`. (§6.1.8)
009-43. Record derivation operates field-by-field: every field's type must itself satisfy the derived trait, else a compile error identifies the offending field. (§6.1.8)
009-44. Deriving `Ord` requires `Eq` to be available on the same type — derived in the same annotation or manually fulfilled; deriving `Ord` without `Eq` is a compile error identifying the missing dependency. (§6.1.8)
009-45. A record body cannot contain `fn` declarations; functions on a record live as free functions or `fulfill`-block methods. (§6.1.9)
009-46. An enum is a nominal sum type — a tagged union whose value is exactly one declared variant at any time. (§6.2)
009-47. An enum is declared with the `enum` keyword and a body of variant declarations: `enum Option[T]:` with variants `Some(T)` and `None`. (§6.2.1)
009-48. Variant names are PascalCase, like type names. (§6.2.1)
009-49. A variant payload may be declared positionally, types only: `Circle(f64)`. (§6.2.1)
009-50. A variant payload may be declared with named fields: `Rectangle(width: f64, height: f64)`. (§6.2.1)
009-51. A variant with no payload is a unit variant: `North`. (§6.2.1)
009-52. A single variant's payload declaration must be all-positional or all-named; mixing is a compile error: `Mixed(width: f64, f64)` ✗. (§6.2.1)
009-53. Different variants of one enum may use different payload forms independently. (§6.2.1)
009-54. An enum may declare no variants; a zero-variant enum is uninhabited — no values — a type-level bottom. (§6.2.1)
009-55. A variant payload field, named or positional, may carry a default applied when omitted at construction, like a function parameter: `Rectangle(width: f64 = 1.0, height: f64 = 1.0)`. (§6.2.1)
009-56. A variant with a defaulted payload field may omit it at construction, taking the default: `Shape::Rectangle(width: 10.0)` leaves `height` at `1.0`. (§6.2.1)
009-57. A positionally-declared variant can omit only trailing defaulted payload fields; positional construction cannot skip a mid-list default (§3.5.4). (§6.2.1)
009-58. A named-payload variant supports both named and positional forms at construction sites and in patterns: `Shape::Rectangle(10.0, 20.0)` ✓. (§6.2.1.1)
009-59. A positional-payload variant supports only the positional form: `Shape::Circle(radius: 5.0)` ✗. (§6.2.1.1)
009-60. Mixing named and positional arguments within one variant call is an error: `Shape::Rectangle(width: 10.0, 20.0)` ✗. (§6.2.1.1)
009-61. Adding names to a previously positional variant is a non-breaking change; both forms become valid. (§6.2.1.1)
009-62. Removing names from a previously named variant is a breaking change; named-form call sites stop compiling. (§6.2.1.1)
009-63. The stdlib declares `Option::Some`, `Result::Ok`, and `Result::Err` with positional payloads. (§6.2.1.2)
009-64. An enum's generic parameters are in scope within all variants' payload declarations. (§6.2.1.2)
009-65. An enum may include a `satisfies` clause parallel to records, requiring matching `fulfill` blocks. (§6.2.2)
009-66. Trait conformance applies to the enum as a whole, not per-variant; one implementation handles all variants and produces a uniform result type. (§6.2.2)
009-67. A variant value is constructed by naming the variant and supplying its payload arguments: `Shape::Circle(5.0)`. (§6.2.3)
009-68. By default every variant reference is path-qualified with the enum name via `::`: `Result::Ok`, `Direction::North`. (§6.2.3)
009-69. Unqualified variant names are unavailable by default and require explicit import: `use Result::(Ok, Err)` enables `Ok(42)`. (§6.2.3)
009-70. `use Enum::*` imports all of an enum's variants unqualified: `use Direction::*` enables bare `North`. (§6.2.3)
009-71. Selection lists in `use` paths are written with parentheses: `use Result::(Ok, Err)`. (§6.2.3)
009-72. Context disambiguates parentheses: after `::` they are a selection list; after a value expression they are a call. (§6.2.3)
009-73. Importing colliding variant names from two enums is an error at the `use` statements, not a call-site ambiguity: `use Direction::*` then `use Heading::*` ✗. (§6.2.3)
009-74. A generic enum's type arguments at variant construction are inferred from the payload arguments or the expected type: `let n: Option[i32] = Option::None`. (§6.2.3)
009-75. `match` is the canonical consumer of an enum value; each arm pairs a pattern with an expression. (§6.2.4)
009-76. Positional variant patterns bind payload fields in declaration order: `Rectangle(width, height)`. (§6.2.4)
009-77. Named variant patterns bind by field name with `field_name: bound_name`: `Rectangle(width: w, height: h)`. (§6.2.4)
009-78. Mixing positional and named bindings within one pattern is a compile error: `Rectangle(width, height: h)` ✗. (§6.2.4)
009-79. Named-form patterns require the variant to be declared with named payload fields: `Some(value: x)` ✗ for positionally-declared `Some(T)`. (§6.2.4)
009-80. Patterns nest for compound values: `(Ok(x), Err(e))`. (§6.2.4)
009-81. The wildcard pattern `_` matches without binding. (§6.2.4)
009-82. A catch-all pattern — a bare identifier with no constructor — matches any value and binds it. (§6.2.4)
009-83. A `match` must cover every variant of the matched enum and, for compound matches, every combination; exhaustiveness is verified at compile time. (§6.2.5)
009-84. A non-exhaustive `match` is a compile error identifying the unreached variants. (§6.2.5)
009-85. A catch-all arm (`_:` or a bare identifier) covers all remaining variants, making a match trivially exhaustive. (§6.2.5)
009-86. `match` is a value selector: it evaluates the scrutinee, selects one arm, evaluates that arm to a value, and discards the rest. (§6.2.5)
009-87. Selecting which node/connection subtree is exposed and kept live is the role of `given` (§13.9.13), not `match`. (§6.2.5)
009-88. `given` shares `match`'s arm shape and exhaustiveness rule, differing in operation: it builds all arms and freezes unselected ones rather than discarding them. (§6.2.5)
009-89. Visibility applies to an enum as a whole; all variants share the enum's visibility. (§6.2.6)
009-90. There is no per-variant visibility specifier. (§6.2.6)
009-91. Enums support `@derive` for the same fixed trait set as records, with derivation operating variant-by-variant. (§6.2.7)
009-92. Derived enum `Eq` compares variant tags and, for matching tags, payload fields pairwise. (§6.2.7)
009-93. Derived enum `Ord` orders variants by declaration order, with ties broken by payload comparison. (§6.2.7)
009-94. Derived enum `Hash` combines the variant tag and the payload fields. (§6.2.7)
009-95. Derived enum `Clone` structurally copies each variant's payload. (§6.2.7)
009-96. Derived enum `Display` and `Debug` output is a compiler-defined structural format. (§6.2.7)
009-97. Enum derivation requires every payload field type to satisfy the derived trait; failure is a compile error identifying the offending payload field. (§6.2.7)
009-98. A newtype is a wrapper type creating a new nominal identity over an existing type. (§6.3)
009-99. A newtype is declared with `type` and a body containing a `wraps` clause naming the underlying type: `type UserId:` with `wraps i64`. (§6.3.1)
009-100. A newtype may be generic: `type MyVec[T]:` wrapping `Vec[T]`. (§6.3.1)
009-101. A `wraps` body may include `satisfies` clauses and metadata but no field declarations; a body mixing `wraps` with fields is rejected. (§6.3.1)
009-102. A `wraps` declaration creates a new nominal identity, unlike `alias type`, which is transparent substitution: `UserId` is distinct from `i64`. (§6.3.1)
009-103. A newtype is constructed by calling its type name with the underlying value as a single positional argument: `UserId(42)`. (§6.3.2)
009-104. Newtype construction has no named-argument form and no multi-argument form. (§6.3.2)
009-105. The underlying value is extracted with the conversion form naming the wrapped type: `i64(id)` unwraps `UserId` to `i64`. (§6.3.2)
009-106. Numeric-to-`string` is not a `T(value)` conversion — `string(i64(id))` ✗ — onward conversion of an extracted value uses stdlib functions such as `to_string()`. (§6.3.2)
009-107. By default a newtype inherits no traits from its underlying type. (§6.3.3)
009-108. Newtype trait inheritance is opt-in via `@derive`: `@derive(Eq, Hash, Display)` on a `wraps string` newtype. (§6.3.3)
009-109. Each derived newtype trait gets a compiler-generated `fulfill` block delegating to the underlying type's implementation: with `@derive(Add)`, `Distance(1.0) + Distance(2.0)` is `Distance(3.0)`. (§6.3.3)
009-110. Operators between a newtype and a different type require manual implementation: `Distance + i32` is a compile error without a `fulfill Add[i32] for Distance` block. (§6.3.3)
009-111. The orphan rule permits such cross-type operator fulfills in the newtype's defining module. (§6.3.3)
009-112. `@derive` implicitly declares `satisfies` for its listed traits; no separate `satisfies` clause is written. (§6.3.3)
009-113. Manual `fulfill` blocks still require their corresponding `satisfies` clauses in the type body. (§6.3.3)
009-114. Deriving a trait the underlying type does not satisfy is a compile error at the annotation site: `@derive(Display)` over a non-`Display` underlying type ✗. (§6.3.3)
009-115. Deriving `Ord` on a newtype requires the underlying type to satisfy `Eq`. (§6.3.3)
009-116. A newtype's constructor visibility is independently controllable from its type visibility: `public(private) type Email:` with `wraps string`. (§6.3.4)
009-117. The smart-constructor pattern makes the type name publicly visible while restricting construction to the defining module, forcing every value-producing path through the constructor's visibility scope. (§6.3.4)
009-118. Implementing a foreign trait for a local newtype wrapping a foreign type satisfies the orphan rule: `fulfill SomeForeignTrait for MyVec[T]` where local `MyVec[T]` wraps `Vec[T]`. (§6.3.5)

## 010. Conversions — 45 Rules

010-1. Infallible user-defined conversions use the `From`/`Into` trait pair. (§7)
010-2. Fallible user-defined conversions use the `TryFrom`/`TryInto` trait pair. (§7)
010-3. The conversion-trait system is layered on the ordinary trait system and complements built-in numeric implicit widening and the `T(value)` conversion form. (§7)
010-4. `From[T]` declares `fn convert(value: T) -> Subject`. (§7.1)
010-5. `Into[T]` declares `fn into(value: Subject) -> T`. (§7.1)
010-6. `TryFrom[T]` declares an associated `type Error` and `fn try_from(value: T) -> Result[Subject, Error]`. (§7.1)
010-7. `TryInto[T]` declares an associated `type Error` and `fn try_into(value: Subject) -> Result[T, Error]`. (§7.1)
010-8. Each fallible conversion impl declares its own `Error` associated type, so different conversions can produce different error kinds. (§7.1)
010-9. `Into` and `TryInto` are sealed traits: usable in trait bounds and method dispatch but never implementable by users. (§7.2)
010-10. Whenever `From[T] for U` exists, the language auto-provides `Into[U] for T`. (§7.2)
010-11. Whenever `TryFrom[T] for U` exists, the language auto-provides `TryInto[U] for T` with the same `Error` associated type. (§7.2)
010-12. The ban on manually written `Into`/`TryInto` impls applies uniformly to user-defined and language-built-in `From`/`TryFrom` pairs. (§7.2)
010-13. Auto-derived `Into`/`TryInto` impls require no `satisfies` declaration on the source type. (§7.2)
010-14. The language auto-implements the identity conversion `From[T] for T` for every type. (§7.3)
010-15. A user-written `fulfill From[T] for T` with source and target identical is a compile error. (§7.3)
010-16. The identity self-conversion is required to be a no-op. (§7.3)
010-17. A `T: Into[U]` bound accepts a bare `U` via the identity conversion. (§7.3)
010-18. Structural impls such as the identity `From[T] for T` are exempt from the orphan rule. (§7.3)
010-19. User `fulfill From[T] for U` and `fulfill TryFrom[T] for U` obey the orphan rule with generic-parameter coverage: a concrete local type must appear in the source or destination position: `fulfill From[i64] for f64` ✗. (§7.4)
010-20. Local-type coverage through a generic argument satisfies the orphan rule for conversions: `fulfill From[Vec[MyType]] for SomeType` ✓ when `MyType` is local. (§7.4)
010-21. A conversion between two foreign types is written by wrapping one side in a local newtype and converting through it. (§7.4)
010-22. A synthesized `Into[U] for T` impl exists at the module of its `From[T] for U` impl and is bound by the same orphan rule. (§7.4)
010-23. Stdlib pre-populates `From` impls for every lossless numeric widening of §4.5: same-signedness integer widening, unsigned-to-wider-signed, `f32` → `f64`, and exact integer-to-float cases. (§7.5)
010-24. The pragmatic conversions `From[i64] for f64` and `From[u64] for f64` are included among the built-in `From` impls. (§7.5)
010-25. Built-in `TryFrom` impls cover numeric narrowing, signed/unsigned crossings, and lossy integer-to-float, each with an appropriate `Error` type. (§7.5)
010-26. Users cannot declare `From`/`TryFrom` impls between primitive types (orphan rule); stdlib uses no special path unavailable to user types. (§7.5)
010-27. For lossless numeric conversions, `U(x)` and `x.into::[U]()` produce the same result and both are valid. (§7.6)
010-28. For lossy numeric conversions, the `T(x)` variant family selects a value-level range policy (trap/wrap/saturate/optional) while `try_into` expresses trait-level fallibility via `Result[T, Error]`. (§7.6)
010-29. Newtype extraction `T(value)` bypasses the conversion-trait system entirely; the wrapped value is exposed directly. (§7.6)
010-30. `T(value)` does not apply to user-defined conversions on non-newtype types; those use `.into()`, `From::convert()`, or `.try_into()`. (§7.6)
010-31. User-defined `From` impls never enable implicit conversion; invocation is explicit: `let f: Fahrenheit = some_c.into()`. (§7.7)
010-32. The implicit-conversion surface is exactly the built-in lossless widenings of §4.5; user types never participate in implicit expression-level type adjustment. (§7.7)
010-33. Auto-derivation of `Into` from `From` is not an implicit conversion: the synthesized method still requires explicit call-site syntax. (§7.7)
010-34. Three explicit invocation forms cover all `From`/`Into` impls: `x.into::[U]()`, `Into::into(x)`, and `From::convert(x)`. (§7.8)
010-35. A bare value converts without any invocation only for built-in lossless widenings: `let x: f64 = 5_i32`. (§7.8)
010-36. The trait-path form works only when the target type is inferable from context: `let x: f64 = Into::into(5_i32)`; otherwise the turbofish method form is used. (§7.8)
010-37. Fallible conversion uses the same explicit invocation forms returning `Result`: `let r: Result[i32, _] = big_value.try_into::[i32]()`. (§7.8)
010-38. On failure, `?` propagates the failure value up the call stack, terminating the enclosing function early. (§7.9)
010-39. At a `?` site the source's error type must equal the enclosing function's error type or be convertible to it via `From`. (§7.9)
010-40. `expr?` evaluates to the source's success type at that site, bound locally or used inline. (§7.9)
010-41. `?` imposes no constraint between the source's success type and the enclosing function's return success type. (§7.9)
010-42. When source and destination error types are identical, `?` applies no conversion. (§7.9)
010-43. When the source error type converts via `From`, the compiler inserts the `From::convert` call automatically at the propagation site. (§7.9)
010-44. With no `From` relationship between error types, `?` is a compile error identifying the source and destination error types and the missing `From` impl. (§7.9)
010-45. The error-type rule is the only relationship `?` enforces: no implicit success-type coercion and no fallback through other trait machinery. (§7.9)

## 011. Error Handling — 94 Rules

011-1. Failure handling uses a two-track model — traps versus typed values — chosen at the operation site when code is written. (§8)
011-2. A failure encoded on one track cannot be silently converted to the other. (§8)
011-3. Trap-track failures represent bugs and invariant violations. (§8.1)
011-4. Arithmetic overflow on a default operator is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-5. Integer division by zero is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-6. An out-of-range `T(x)` conversion is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-7. An out-of-range array index is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-8. `abs` on the signed minimum is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-9. A negative integer exponent on an integer base is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-10. `unwrap` or `expect` on `Option::None` or `Result::Err` is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-11. Runtime stack overflow is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-12. Allocation failure is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-13. An explicit `panic` call is a trap-track failure. (§8.1)
011-14. Traps halt execution and produce diagnostics. (§8.1)
011-15. Traps are not catchable as values. (§8.1)
011-16. A non-exhaustive `match` is a compile error, never a runtime trap. (§8.1)
011-17. A deliberate runtime panic for an "unreachable" `match` case is written as an explicit catch-all arm calling `panic`. (§8.1)
011-18. Value-track failures flow through the type system as `Option[T]` (occurrence-only failure) and `Result[T, E]` (failure with contextual information). (§8.1)
011-19. The value track also comprises `?` short-circuit propagation, the user-implementable `Try` trait, `From`-conversion of failure types during propagation, and the `+?`-family arithmetic operator variants producing `Option`-typed results. (§8.1)
011-20. A trap never becomes a `Result::Err` value. (§8.1)
011-21. A `Result::Err` never aborts the program. (§8.1)
011-22. There is no `try`/`catch` mechanism for traps. (§8.1)
011-23. `panic` is a prelude built-in available unqualified in every scope. (§8.2.1)
011-24. `panic` has the signature `fn panic(message: string) -> never`. (§8.2.1)
011-25. Calling `panic` triggers an immediate trap carrying the supplied diagnostic message. (§8.2.1)
011-26. Because `panic` returns `never`, it may appear wherever a value of any type is expected: `None: panic("expected Some, got None")`. (§8.2.1)
011-27. `never` is a built-in primitive type with no values, spelled in lowercase. (§8.2.2)
011-28. `never` unifies with every type during type-checking: `if condition: 5 else: unreachable()` types as `i32`. (§8.2.2)
011-29. A firing trap prints a diagnostic naming the triggering operation (with operand values where available), the source location, and a stack trace. (§8.2.3)
011-30. After the trap diagnostic, the process exits. (§8.2.3)
011-31. No unwinding hook can intercept a trap and convert it to a value. (§8.2.3)
011-32. Diagnostics for user-triggered `panic` calls include the user-supplied message. (§8.2.4)
011-33. Trap-diagnostic formatting is implementation-defined; the normative requirement is information sufficient to identify what trapped, where, and through what call chain. (§8.2.4)
011-34. `Option` and `Result` are ordinary stdlib generic enums with no language-level special-casing of their identity. (§8.3)
011-35. `Option[T]` is the stdlib enum with variants `Some(T)` and `None`. (§8.3)
011-36. `Result[T, E]` is the stdlib enum with variants `Ok(T)` and `Err(E)`. (§8.3)
011-37. The `?` and error-conversion interactions are mediated by the stdlib `Try` trait, not by compiler knowledge of `Option`/`Result`. (§8.3)
011-38. `Option` and `Result` are discriminated with standard exhaustive `match`. (§8.3.1)
011-39. Ductus provides no `if let` and no check-and-unwrap sugar. (§8.3.1)
011-40. The postfix `?` operator dispatches through the stdlib trait `Try`. (§8.4)
011-41. `Try` declares `type Success`, `type Failure`, and `fn branch(value: Subject) -> TryBranch[Success, Failure]`. (§8.4)
011-42. `TryBranch[S, F]` is an enum with variants `Continue(S)` and `Break(F)`. (§8.4)
011-43. `Option[T]` fulfills `Try` with `Failure = ()`: `branch(Some(x))` is `Continue(x)`; `branch(None)` is `Break(())`. (§8.4)
011-44. `Result[T, E]` fulfills `Try` with `Failure = E`: `branch(Ok(x))` is `Continue(x)`; `branch(Err(e))` is `Break(e)`. (§8.4)
011-45. Any user-defined type may implement `Try` to make `?` available on it. (§8.4)
011-46. `expr?` desugars to a `match` on `Try::branch(expr)`: `Continue(value)` yields `value`; `Break(failure)` returns early — `Err(From::convert(failure))` in a `Result`-returning function (converting the inner error value) or `None` in an `Option`-returning function. (§8.4.1)
011-47. The postfix `!` operator unwraps an `Option[T]` to its `T`: `let car = target!`. (§8.4.2)
011-48. `!` never propagates a failure and never traps. (§8.4.2)
011-49. `opt!` compiles only when the compiler can prove `opt` is always `Some` at that point; otherwise it is a compile error. (§8.4.2)
011-50. A compiled `!` performs no runtime check and costs nothing at runtime. (§8.4.2)
011-51. `!` has no "trust me" escape that bypasses the compile-time proof. (§8.4.2)
011-52. The `!` provability rule is normative and closed, so the same programs are well-formed under every conforming compiler. (§8.4.2)
011-53. An `Option`-typed expression is provably `Some` iff every value that can reach it is a `Some(...)` construction or a qualifying `Handle` resolution. (§8.4.2)
011-54. A `Handle` resolution qualifies for `!` iff its topology-analysis candidate referents are all statically placed: non-`repeat`, non-dismountable graph entities. (§8.4.2)
011-55. Reaching values for `!` are computed over the expression's definitions and the data flow into it. (§8.4.2)
011-56. Any other provenance — a reaching `None` construction, a `Handle` with any dismountable candidate, an `Option` of unknown origin crossing a function boundary — makes `!` a compile error. (§8.4.2)
011-57. Conforming compilers accept exactly the closed `!` provability set: no implementation-specific extensions, no omissions. (§8.4.2)
011-58. A `Handle` into a `repeat` view is permanently non-provable for `!`, since a keyed scope is always dismountable. (§8.4.2)
011-59. Postfix value-expression `!` is distinct from the `!` cardinality sigil in `parts:`/`incoming:`/`outgoing:` type-cardinality position; the two never collide. (§8.4.2)
011-60. The compiler verifies the required error `From` impl at every `?` use site, rejecting with an error when no conversion path exists. (§8.5)
011-61. Using `?` on an `Option` inside a `Result`-returning function, or on a `Result` inside an `Option`-returning function, is a compile error. (§8.6)
011-62. The cross-track `?` ban is categorical: it holds even when `From` impls exist that could bridge the failure types. (§8.6)
011-63. Crossing failure tracks is explicit-only, via stdlib methods such as `option.ok_or(err)` and `result.ok()`. (§8.6)
011-64. Stdlib provides a standard method set on `Option` and `Result`; the specified list is non-exhaustive. (§8.7)
011-65. All standard `Option`/`Result` methods are stdlib free functions reached through uniform call syntax; the enum types carry no methods of their own. (§8.7)
011-66. `option.unwrap()`, `unwrap(option)`, and `std::option::unwrap(option)` are equivalent invocation forms. (§8.7)
011-67. Module-path qualification such as `std::option::unwrap(option)` disambiguates among multiple in-scope same-named free functions. (§8.7)
011-68. No type-qualified free-function form exists: `Option::unwrap(option)` ✗. (§8.7)
011-69. The `fn(T) -> U` parameters in stdlib method signatures are closure types accepting any signature-matching callable, monomorphized per call site. (§8.7)
011-70. `unwrap(value)` on `Option[T]` returns the contained `T` or traps on `None`. (§8.7.1)
011-71. `expect(value, msg)` on `Option` is `unwrap` with a caller-supplied trap message. (§8.7.1)
011-72. `unwrap_or(value, default)` on `Option` returns the contained value or the given default. (§8.7.1)
011-73. `unwrap_or_else(value, f: fn() -> T)` on `Option` returns the contained value or a computed default. (§8.7.1)
011-74. `map[U](value, f: fn(T) -> U) -> Option[U]` applies `f` to the success value. (§8.7.1)
011-75. `and_then[U](value, f: fn(T) -> Option[U]) -> Option[U]` chains optional computations. (§8.7.1)
011-76. `or_else(value, f: fn() -> Option[T]) -> Option[T]` supplies a fallback computation. (§8.7.1)
011-77. `ok_or[E](value, err: E) -> Result[T, E]` converts an `Option` to a `Result`, using `err` on `None`. (§8.7.1)
011-78. `is_some` and `is_none` are non-consuming `bool` discriminator predicates on `Option`. (§8.7.1)
011-79. `unwrap(value)` on `Result[T, E]` returns the success value or traps on `Err`. (§8.7.2)
011-80. `expect(value, msg)` on `Result` is `unwrap` with a caller-supplied trap message. (§8.7.2)
011-81. `unwrap_or(value, default)` and `unwrap_or_else(value, f: fn(E) -> T)` on `Result` return the success value or a given/computed default. (§8.7.2)
011-82. `map[U](value, f: fn(T) -> U) -> Result[U, E]` transforms the success value. (§8.7.2)
011-83. `map_err[F](value, f: fn(E) -> F) -> Result[T, F]` converts the error type. (§8.7.2)
011-84. `and_then[U](value, f: fn(T) -> Result[U, E]) -> Result[U, E]` chains fallible computations. (§8.7.2)
011-85. `or_else[F](value, f: fn(E) -> Result[T, F]) -> Result[T, F]` provides an error-recovery chain. (§8.7.2)
011-86. `ok(value) -> Option[T]` and `err(value) -> Option[E]` convert a `Result` to an `Option`, discarding the other arm. (§8.7.2)
011-87. `is_ok` and `is_err` are non-consuming `bool` discriminator predicates on `Result`. (§8.7.2)
011-88. The `Option`-vs-`Result` choice is a convention, not a language rule; the compiler accepts either signature. (§8.8)
011-89. Convention: use `Option[T]` when failure carries no information beyond occurrence and `Result[T, E]` when callers may inspect failure information. (§8.8)
011-90. Convention: when in doubt between `Option` and `Result`, prefer `Result`. (§8.8)
011-91. The reactive system uses the same two-track failure model as the rest of the language. (§8.9)
011-92. A trap inside a `derived` expression's computation propagates as a normal trap; the reactive system does not catch traps. (§8.9)
011-93. A `derived` whose expression has type `Result[T, E]` or `Option[T]` produces a reactive value of that type, consumed with standard `match` or `?`. (§8.9)
011-94. The reactive layer adds no error mechanism beyond the existing type system. (§8.9)

## 012. Strings, Tuples, Arrays & Time — 146 Rules

012-1. `string`, tuples, and fixed-size arrays `T[N]` are language-level compound types with dedicated syntax and language-specified behavior, not user-defined types. (§9)
012-2. `string` is a built-in primitive type at the same level as `i32` or `bool`, not a stdlib type with privileged literal syntax. (§9.1)
012-3. The lowercase keyword `string` is reserved. (§9.1)
012-4. The complete set of primitive non-numeric types is `bool`, `char`, `string`, `duration`, and `instant`; no other non-numeric primitives exist. (§9.1.1)
012-5. Byte sequences are `u8[N]` arrays; there is no dedicated byte-string primitive. (§9.1.1)
012-6. The language commits to exactly one string type, encoded as UTF-8; UTF-16 strings, ASCII-only strings, and unencoded byte strings are stdlib concerns. (§9.1.1)
012-7. The language commits to monotonic time only; wall-clock dates, calendar arithmetic, and timezones are stdlib concerns. (§9.1.1)
012-8. `char` is a Unicode scalar value — an integer in `0..=0xD7FF` ∪ `0xE000..=0x10FFFF`; the surrogate range `0xD800..=0xDFFF` is excluded. (§9.1.2)
012-9. The type system rejects `char` values outside the Unicode-scalar range at construction time, so every `char` value is always a valid scalar. (§9.1.2)
012-10. `char` has a fixed 32-bit representation per value. (§9.1.2)
012-11. Character literals use single quotes: `'a'`, `'\n'`, `'\u{1F600}'`, `'\x41'`. (§9.1.2)
012-12. A character literal contains exactly one Unicode scalar; multi-character literals are a compile error. (§9.1.2)
012-13. Character literals use the same escape conventions as string literals. (§9.1.2)
012-14. `From[char] for u32` converts every `char` to `u32` losslessly. (§9.1.2)
012-15. `TryFrom[u32] for char` yields `Err` for surrogate-range values and values above `0x10FFFF`. (§9.1.2)
012-16. `char` satisfies `Eq`, `Ord`, `Hash`, `Display`, `Debug`, and `Clone`. (§9.1.2)
012-17. `char` comparison and ordering follow numeric Unicode scalar value order. (§9.1.2)
012-18. `char` literals use the same escape set as strings (§9.1.3), so `'\''` and `'\"'` are both valid. (§9.1.2)
012-19. String literals take plain, raw, escape-sequence, and interpolation forms: `"hello world"`, `r"no \n escapes"`, `r#"with "quotes""#`, `"user {name} has {count} items"`. (§9.1.3)
012-20. The recognized string escape sequences are `\n`, `\r`, `\t`, `\0`, `\\`, `\"`, `\'`, `\{`, `\xHH`, and `\u{HHHHHH}`; `char` literals accept the same set except `\{`. (§9.1.3)
012-21. Every string-literal form produces a value of type `string`. (§9.1.3)
012-22. A plain string may contain literal newlines; the embedded newline becomes part of the value. (§9.1.3)
012-23. A raw string performs no escape processing and no interpolation; every character — backslashes, braces, newlines — is literal. (§9.1.3)
012-24. UTF-8 is the internal string encoding; the type system guarantees every string value is valid UTF-8, so no invalid-UTF-8 string can exist at runtime. (§9.1.4)
012-25. String constructors and conversions taking untrusted input either reject ill-formed input or return a fallible result. (§9.1.4)
012-26. Strings are not indexable — there is no `s[i]` operator; content access requires explicit view methods. (§9.1.5)
012-27. `s.bytes()` returns a sequence of raw `u8` values, indexable in O(1); the exact return type is a stdlib concern. (§9.1.6)
012-28. `s.chars()` returns a sequence of `char` values, iterable in O(n) total traversal because UTF-8 decoding is required; the exact return type is a stdlib concern. (§9.1.6)
012-29. `s.byte_len() -> isize` returns the length in bytes in O(1). (§9.1.6)
012-30. `s.char_count() -> isize` returns the number of Unicode scalars in O(n). (§9.1.6)
012-31. `s.slice(start: isize, end: isize) -> string` slices at validated character positions with cost O(end). (§9.1.7)
012-32. `s.byte_slice(start: isize, end: isize) -> string` slices at byte positions with O(1) cost per boundary. (§9.1.7)
012-33. `byte_slice` traps when a boundary lands mid-codepoint in a multi-byte UTF-8 sequence. (§9.1.7)
012-34. Both slicing methods return a new string value. (§9.1.7)
012-35. Invalid slice boundaries — a mid-codepoint byte index or an out-of-range position — trap at runtime. (§9.1.7)
012-36. Strings are immutable with no in-place mutation; every content-modifying operation returns a new string value: `let upper = s.to_upper()`. (§9.1.8)
012-37. The runtime may share immutable backing storage between string values; sharing is an implementation detail invisible to the user. (§9.1.8)
012-38. `+` concatenates strings via a stdlib `Add` implementation with both operands and result typed `string`: `"hello" + " " + "world"`. (§9.1.8)
012-39. The orphan rule prevents users from declaring their own `Add` for `string`. (§9.1.8)
012-40. Interpolation `{expr}` evaluates the expression and converts the result to `string` via the `Display` trait. (§9.1.9)
012-41. Interpolating a value whose type does not satisfy `Display` is a compile error at the interpolation site. (§9.1.9)
012-42. Interpolation expressions are arbitrary expressions (method calls, arithmetic, field access), not just bare identifiers: `"value: {amount * tax_rate}"`. (§9.1.9)
012-43. A literal brace in a plain string is written `\{`, which yields `{` and suppresses interpolation there. (§9.1.9)
012-44. Tuples are structurally typed: two tuples with the same component types in the same order are the same type: `(1, 2)` and `(3, 4)` are both `(i32, i32)`. (§9.2)
012-45. Tuples and closure types are the only two structural-typing carve-outs in the otherwise nominal type system. (§9.2)
012-46. The type expression `(T1, T2, ...)` denotes a tuple type directly; no type declaration is required. (§9.2)
012-47. Tuple field access uses zero-based numeric postfix syntax: `t.0`. (§9.2.1)
012-48. Tuple field indices must be integer literals; runtime indexing with a variable expression (`t.i`) is not permitted. (§9.2.1)
012-49. Tuple field access is bounds-checked at compile time: `t.3` on a 3-tuple is a compile error. (§9.2.1)
012-50. Tuple patterns are admitted in `let` bindings, `match` arms, and every other pattern position: `let (a, b, c) = (1, "hello", 3.14)`. (§9.2.2)
012-51. Tuple patterns nest to arbitrary depth: `let ((a, b), c) = ((1, 2), 3)`. (§9.2.2)
012-52. The wildcard `_` in a tuple pattern ignores a component without binding it: `let (x, _, z) = some_tuple`. (§9.2.2)
012-53. Tuple patterns are always positional; tuples have no field names and no named pattern form. (§9.2.2)
012-54. The unit type is `()`, with a single value also written `()`. (§9.2.3)
012-55. A function without a final expression returns the unit value implicitly. (§9.2.3)
012-56. The `-> ()` return annotation may be omitted: `fn print_hello():` equals `fn print_hello() -> ():`. (§9.2.3)
012-57. `()` is admitted in pattern position to match unit-typed values. (§9.2.3)
012-58. A 1-tuple requires a trailing comma: `(42,)` has type `(i32,)`; `(42)` is a parenthesized `i32`, not a tuple. (§9.2.4)
012-59. A tuple of arity two or more permits an optional trailing comma: `(1, 2,)` equals `(1, 2)`. (§9.2.4)
012-60. Generic parameters appear in tuple types via standard generic syntax: `fn swap[A, B](t: (A, B)) -> (B, A)`. (§9.2.5)
012-61. Each unique tuple-type instantiation produces its own specialized code under monomorphization. (§9.2.5)
012-62. Variadic generics — abstraction over tuples of arbitrary arity — are not part of Ductus; generics range over fixed-arity tuple component types only. (§9.2.5)
012-63. `fulfill SomeTrait for (T1, T2, ...)` is permitted iff the trait is local or at least one element type `Ti` is local: `fulfill MyTrait for (i32, string)` ✓. (§9.2.6)
012-64. A `fulfill` of a foreign trait for a tuple whose element types are all foreign is an orphan-rule error: `fulfill Display for (i32, string)` ✗. (§9.2.6)
012-65. The compiler provides `Eq`, `Ord`, `Hash`, `Clone`, `Display`, and `Debug` for tuples structurally at any arity by component-by-component derivation. (§9.2.6)
012-66. A tuple type has a derivable trait iff every component type has it; the implementation is generated per instantiation during monomorphization. (§9.2.6)
012-67. Structural tuple-trait derivation is automatic-when-eligible rather than opt-in, since tuples have no declaration site to annotate. (§9.2.6)
012-68. There is no arity cap on structural tuple-trait derivation and no hand-written per-arity stdlib impls. (§9.2.6)
012-69. Manual tuple `fulfill` blocks have no arity limit. (§9.2.6)
012-70. Any non-derivable trait is implemented for a specific tuple type with a manual `fulfill` block, subject to the orphan rule. (§9.2.6)
012-71. Tuple-to-record conversion is always explicit; tuples are structural and records nominal, and the compiler never converts implicitly between them. (§9.2.7)
012-72. Tuple-to-record conversion is written field-by-field or via a user `From` impl: `Vec3(x: t.0, y: t.1, z: t.2)`. (§9.2.7)
012-73. Arrays are fixed-size contiguous sequences of a single element type, and the element count is part of the type. (§9.3)
012-74. The dedicated array type syntax is `T[N]`: `i32[5]`. (§9.3.1)
012-75. There is no exposed canonical `Array[T, N]` type name; the underlying array representation is internal and not addressable in user code. (§9.3.1)
012-76. Multi-dimensional array types parse left-to-right: `T[N][M]` is an M-element array of `T[N]`. (§9.3.1)
012-77. Zero-length array types `T[0]` are valid. (§9.3.1)
012-78. An array is constructed with a bracketed element list `[e1, …, eK]`, producing a `T[K]` value of the unified element type: `[1, 2, 3]` is an `i32[3]`. (§9.3.1)
012-79. An empty array literal `[]` has size 0 and takes its element type from context: `let xs: i32[0] = []`. (§9.3.1)
012-80. `T[args]` in type position is interpreted by what `T` resolves to: a primitive or other non-generic type forms an array type (`i32[5]`); a generic type instantiates (`Vec[i32]`). (§9.3.2)
012-81. Array-vs-generic disambiguation depends only on the kind of `T`, never on the kind of the arguments; there is no parser-level ambiguity. (§9.3.2)
012-82. The array length type is `isize` — signed and platform-sized; there is no `usize` length type. (§9.3.3)
012-83. `usize` remains available for explicit use where a non-negative invariant is needed (allocation sizes, FFI); the language does not block this. (§9.3.3)
