012-84. `arr[i]` traps at runtime if `i < 0 || i >= length`. (§9.3.5)
012-85. When both index and length are compile-time known, out-of-range indexing is a compile error: `arr[10]` on `i32[5]` ✗. (§9.3.5)
012-86. Recoverable indexing uses stdlib methods such as `arr.get(i)` returning `Option[T]`, or a `?` indexing variant if one is provided. (§9.3.5)
012-87. The dynamic growable vector is a stdlib concern; only fixed-size arrays receive dedicated language syntax, and the vector's name and syntax are outside the specification. (§9.3.6)
012-88. Stdlib collections receive no special powers; their mutating operations are ordinary `own subject -> Subject` methods. (§9.3.6)
012-89. Whether a collection call mutates in place or yields a fresh value follows entirely from the caller's ownership, never from the type; there is no separate mutable-collection type. (§9.3.6)
012-90. The only primitive a stdlib collection rests on is the allocator intrinsic, available to any program; users can implement an equivalent collection with no privileged access. (§9.3.6)
012-91. A `usize` array index requires an explicit cast to `isize` (`arr[isize(n)]`); a same-width signed↔unsigned crossing is never implicit. (§9.3.4)
012-92. Index-widening legality is platform-dependent: an index fitting `isize` on a wider platform may need a cast on a narrower-`isize` one, so portable code casts proactively. (§9.3.4)
012-93. `duration` (a span of time) and `instant` (a point in monotonic time) are first-class lowercase primitive types. (§9.4)
012-94. The operators on `duration` and `instant` are language-defined primitives, not dispatched through the numeric `Div` trait machinery, which is why shapes like `duration / duration` -> `f64` are well-formed. (§9.4)
012-95. `duration` is internally i64 nanoseconds, giving a range of approximately ±292 years at single-nanosecond precision. (§9.4.1)
012-96. Negative durations are permitted. (§9.4.1)
012-97. The duration literal suffixes are `ns`, `us`, `μs` (microseconds), `ms`, `s`, `min`, `h`, and `d`: `500ns`, `250ms`, `1.5s`, `5min`, `2h`, `1d`. (§9.4.1.1)
012-98. Both integer and float numeric literals may carry duration suffixes. (§9.4.1.1)
012-99. Float duration literals convert to nanoseconds with rounding-to-nearest at compile time: `1.5s` is `1_500_000_000ns`. (§9.4.1.1)
012-100. Integer duration literals scale exactly. (§9.4.1.1)
012-101. Duration suffixes are reserved by the language; `@literal_suffix` may not re-register them in any scope. (§9.4.1.1)
012-102. `duration + duration` yields the sum as `duration`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-103. `duration - duration` yields the difference as `duration`, which may be negative. (§9.4.1.2)
012-104. `duration * Numeric` scales and yields `duration`; `Numeric * duration` is equally permitted. (§9.4.1.2)
012-105. `duration / Numeric` scales down and yields `duration`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-106. `duration / duration` yields the ratio as `f64`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-107. `duration % duration` yields the remainder as `duration`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-108. Unary negation `-duration` yields `duration`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-109. `<`, `<=`, `>=`, `>`, `is`, and `is not` on two durations yield `bool`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-110. The `Numeric` operand in duration scaling may be any integer or float type. (§9.4.1.2)
012-111. Duration multiplication by an integer is exact. (§9.4.1.2)
012-112. Duration division by an integer or float rounds to nearest at the nanosecond level: `2s / 3` is 666,666,667 ns. (§9.4.1.2)
012-113. Duration multiplication by a float rounds to nearest at the nanosecond level. (§9.4.1.2)
012-114. `duration / 0` with an integer-typed zero traps as integer division by zero. (§9.4.1.2)
012-115. `duration / 0.0` with a float-typed zero produces ±inf or NaN per IEEE 754, and converting that result back to the i64-nanosecond representation traps. (§9.4.1.2)
012-116. Dividing a duration by a zero-duration value traps, treated as the i64 zero-divisor case. (§9.4.1.2)
012-117. Checked variants `/?` and `%?` on durations return `Option[duration]`, or `Option[f64]` for `duration / duration`. (§9.4.1.2)
012-118. `duration + Numeric` and `Numeric + duration` are not defined; attempting them is a compile error. (§9.4.1.2)
012-119. `duration - Numeric` and `Numeric - duration` are not defined; attempting them is a compile error. (§9.4.1.2)
012-120. `duration * duration` is not defined; attempting it is a compile error. (§9.4.1.2)
012-121. Default duration arithmetic traps on overflow: a result that does not fit i64 nanoseconds aborts the process. (§9.4.1.3)
012-122. Checked duration variants `+?`, `-?`, `*?`, `/?`, `%?` return `Option[duration]`. (§9.4.1.3)
012-123. Construction of `duration` from raw integer/float values and conversion to numeric counts are stdlib concerns, not language built-ins. (§9.4.1.4)
012-124. Stdlib is expected to provide `duration::from_nanos(n)`, `from_micros(n)`, `from_millis(n)`, `from_secs(n)`, `from_minutes(n)`, `from_hours(n)`, and `from_days(n)`. (§9.4.1.4)
012-125. `d.as_nanos() -> i64` is lossless, nanoseconds being the internal representation. (§9.4.1.4)
012-126. `d.as_micros() -> i64`, `d.as_millis() -> i64`, and `d.as_secs() -> i64` truncate sub-unit components. (§9.4.1.4)
012-127. Stdlib is expected to provide float conversion variants such as `d.as_secs_f64() -> f64`, precision-bound by f64. (§9.4.1.4)
012-128. `instant` is a monotonic point in time — from a clock that never goes backward — measured against an implementation-defined epoch (typically program start or system boot). (§9.4.2)
012-129. `instant` has no literal syntax. (§9.4.2)
012-130. `instant` has no direct construction from raw nanoseconds and no operation exposing the underlying value as an absolute count. (§9.4.2)
012-131. Internally an `instant` is i64 nanoseconds since the implementation-defined epoch, exposed only via comparisons and difference operations. (§9.4.2)
012-132. `instant - instant` yields the elapsed time as `duration`. (§9.4.2.1)
012-133. `instant + duration` yields a future `instant`. (§9.4.2.1)
012-134. `instant - duration` yields a past `instant`. (§9.4.2.1)
012-135. `<`, `<=`, `>=`, `>`, `is`, and `is not` on two instants yield `bool`. (§9.4.2.1)
012-136. `instant + instant` is not defined. (§9.4.2.1)
012-137. `instant * Numeric` and `instant / Numeric` are not defined. (§9.4.2.1)
012-138. All direct arithmetic between `instant` and `Numeric` is not defined. (§9.4.2.1)
012-139. The language core provides no `instant` construction; stdlib is expected to provide `instant::now() -> instant`. (§9.4.2.2)
012-140. No conversion from `instant` to absolute values is provided. (§9.4.2.2)
012-141. Comparison and difference are the canonical operations on `instant`; no other introspection is provided. (§9.4.2.2)
012-142. A saved `instant` has no meaningful interpretation in a later run started from a different epoch; cross-run serialization of instants is not reliable. (§9.4.2.3)
012-143. Conversion of instants to and from a stable absolute representation for persistence is a stdlib concern, not provided by the language core. (§9.4.2.3)
012-144. `duration` and `instant` are i64-sized, satisfy the direct in-cell storage criteria, and may directly type `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, and `derived` declarations. (§9.4.3)
012-145. `Option`/`Result` wrappings of `duration`/`instant` store directly in a reactive cell when discriminant plus payload fits the platform atomic word, and otherwise use index-based pool storage. (§9.4.3)
012-146. The compiler chooses the cell-storage strategy; the source-level wrapped type is permitted on all platforms. (§9.4.3)

## 013. Ownership & Mutability — 243 Rules

013-1. Category A (value ownership: function call, function return, let-rebinding, for-loop iteration variable) defaults to borrow-equivalent: the callee or new binding gets read-only access and the source binding survives. (§11.1)
013-2. Category A consumption is opt-in, requiring `own` in the signature and `move` at the call site. (§11.1)
013-3. Category B (structural storage: record construction, indexed assignment, field assignment via `mut`, whole-value reassignment of `mut`, attr initialization with a value RHS at placement) consumes the RHS into the storage slot implicitly, with no `move` keyword. (§11.1)
013-4. Category C (reactive wiring: placement attribute assignment with a reactive RHS, connection arguments, attr/derived references in reactive expressions) binds names to reactive cells rather than stack-owned values; multiple aliases to one cell may coexist. (§11.1)
013-5. Category C operations are not §11 ownership operations; their values flow through the reactive system specified by §13 and §14. (§11.1)
013-6. Category D (reactive cell writes: `signal.write(v)`, `stream.emit(v)`, attr reassignment from a recurrent advance) consumes the written value into the cell implicitly, with no `move` keyword. (§11.1)
013-7. Category A is the only ownership category requiring an explicit call-site consumption marker. (§11.1)
013-8. Nothing outside a function body is mutable: module-level bindings, record fields, function parameters, and enum variants cannot be declared `mut`. (§11.1)
013-9. At every moment, every value has exactly one real owner. (§11.1)
013-10. Exception: `Signal[T]` parameters and reactive composite bindings name reactive cells, so multiple live aliases to the same cell may coexist without violating single ownership. (§11.1)
013-11. Materialization of a reactive composite (at the boundaries of §13.2.9.7) produces a concrete instance subject to standard single-ownership rules from that point on. (§11.1)
013-12. Single writer: a `mut` binding is the only path through which its underlying value may be mutated. (§11.1)
013-13. The single-writer rule is enforced entirely at compile time, with no runtime check. (§11.1)
013-14. Mutation is globally invisible: no caller can observe a callee's mutations except through the callee's declared return value. (§11.1)
013-15. Exactly two binding forms exist for runtime values: `let x = expr` (immutable) and `mut x = expr` (mutable, function bodies only). (§11.2)
013-16. A `let` binding name cannot be reassigned. (§11.2)
013-17. Field or element assignment through a `let` binding is a compile error. (§11.2)
013-18. A `mut` binding may be reassigned and its value mutated in place via indexed assignment, field assignment, or whole-value reassignment. (§11.2)
013-19. `mut` declarations are legal only inside function bodies (including nested block scopes); they are forbidden at module top level and inside type, trait, node, and connection bodies. (§11.2)
013-20. A `mut` declaration outside a function body is a compile error reported at the declaration site. (§11.2)
013-21. `const` and `mut` are mutually exclusive: `const` asserts compile-time-only and immutable, `mut` is necessarily runtime and mutable. (§11.2)
013-22. `let` is a function-body construct only; module scope contains no `let` bindings. (§11.2)
013-23. Module scope contains `const`, `signal`, `attr`, `derived`, and `recurrent` declarations. (§11.2)
013-24. The ownership rules of §11.3–§11.9 apply to function bodies and to parameter/return wiring at function boundaries, not to module-scope declarations. (§11.2)
013-25. Each use site of a top-level `const` reifies a fresh compile-time value. (§11.2)
013-26. Consts do not enter the ownership system: a const has no runtime owner to consume or transfer. (§11.2)
013-27. Const eligibility requires static representability of the type, not `Copy`-ness or non-heap-backedness: heap-backed `string` is const-eligible, represented as a static string-pool index. (§11.2)
013-28. Dynamic collections such as `Vec` and `HashMap` are not supported as const types. (§11.2)
013-29. Applying `move` to a const or passing a const to an `own` parameter is diagnosed as an error: consumption does not apply to compile-time-only values. (§11.2)
013-30. Every binding carries an initial value at its declaration — `let`, `mut`, top-level `let`/`const`, and `signal` alike; there is no uninitialized, implicitly-undefined, or implicitly-zero state. (§11.2)
013-31. Either binding form may shadow a prior binding of the same name in the same scope, making the prior binding inaccessible by that name from the shadow point forward: `let input = preprocess(input)`. (§11.2.1)
013-32. A `let` may shadow a `mut` and vice versa; the new binding's mutability is governed solely by its own declaration form. (§11.2.1)
013-33. The real owner of a value is the binding that holds its storage and is responsible for dropping it when its scope ends. (§11.3)
013-34. `let y = x` or `mut y = x` kills the name `x` (single-name-per-cluster); if `x` was the real owner, `y` becomes the new real owner: after `let w = v`, `print(v)` ✗. (§11.3)
013-35. `for x in v:` binds `x` as a borrow-equivalent alias rooted in each successive element of `v` for the body of one iteration. (§11.3)
013-36. `for own x in v:` consumes `v` and binds `x` as a real owner of each element. (§11.3)
013-37. Returning a real-owner local transfers its ownership directly to the call site's receiving binding. (§11.3)
013-38. Using a binding after its name has been killed is a compile error reported at the use site. (§11.3)
013-39. Rebinding moves no storage at runtime; renames are compile-time bookkeeping only. (§11.3)
013-40. Any binding, real owner or borrow-equivalent alias, may read its value without consuming it; reading needs no keyword and leaves ownership and cluster membership unchanged. (§11.3.1)
013-41. Reading comprises field access, indexed access, read-only pattern matching, built-in operator inspection (`is`, `<`), borrow-default method calls, and passing to borrow-default parameters. (§11.3.1)
013-42. Consuming comprises passing to an `own` parameter with explicit call-site `move`, returning a real-owner local, storing into a record field, tuple component, or enum payload (implicit move), and writing into a reactive cell (implicit move): `consume(move r)`. (§11.3.1)
013-43. Reassigning a `mut` binding is whole-value assignment: the new value is consumed implicitly (category B, no `move`) and the old value is dropped: `buf = make_other_buffer()`. (§11.3.2)
013-44. Reassignment is not shadowing: it modifies the existing binding rather than introducing a new one. (§11.3.2)
013-45. A binding going out of scope drops its value only if it is the cluster's current real owner; borrow-equivalent aliases never drop the underlying value. (§11.3.3)
013-46. Dropping a `Copy` value is a no-op. (§11.3.3)
013-47. Dropping a non-`Copy` value whose resources require cleanup invokes the type's drop behavior; user-defined drop is specified through the trait system per §14.7. (§11.3.3)
013-48. A cluster is the transitive closure of borrow-equivalent aliases rooted in a single real-owner binding, computed and tracked entirely at compile time. (§11.3.4)
013-49. Borrow-equivalent aliases arise from five sources: default parameters, `let`/`mut` rebinds of an alias, default for-loop iteration variables, default-convention function returns, and borrow-default trait associated-type slots. (§11.3.4)
013-50. A trait associated-type slot under the borrow-default convention is rooted in the enclosing method call's argument cluster. (§11.3.4)
013-51. A cluster's lifetime is the union of its members' scopes; the cluster ends when the root's scope ends or the root is consumed by `move`. (§11.3.4)
013-52. A borrow-equivalent alias cannot be stored in a record field, tuple component, enum payload, or indexed slot. (§11.3.4)
013-53. A borrow-equivalent alias cannot be written into a reactive cell (`signal.write`, `stream.emit`, recurrent advance, attr reassignment). (§11.3.4)
013-54. A borrow-equivalent alias cannot be captured by an escaping closure. (§11.3.4)
013-55. A cluster member may be returned under the default return convention, extending the cluster into the caller's scope. (§11.3.4)
013-56. While a cluster has any alias member beyond its root, the root's value cannot be moved (passed to an `own` parameter, returned, or reassigned into a different slot). (§11.3.4)
013-57. While a cluster has any alias member beyond its root, the root's value cannot be mutated (`mut` reassignment, indexed assignment, field assignment). (§11.3.4)
013-58. Once every alias goes out of scope, the cluster collapses to its root, which may again be moved or mutated. (§11.3.4)
013-59. A function-parameter borrow that does not escape into a `let` or for-loop binding is bounded by the call expression. (§11.3.4)
013-60. Rule (P): rebinding a borrow-equivalent alias (`let y = x`, `mut y = x`) kills the name `x` and transfers the borrow-equivalent role to the new binding: `let y = x` makes `y` an alias in the same cluster. (§11.3.5)
013-61. Under Rule (P) the source value is untouched: no data moves out of the cluster's root and no ownership transfer occurs at the value level. (§11.3.5)
013-62. Function return slots are transient binding sites that default to the borrow-equivalent convention, like parameter, let-rebinding, and trait associated-type slots. (§11.3.6)
013-63. A default-convention return rooted in an input makes the caller's result binding a borrow-equivalent alias in that input's cluster, extending the cluster across the call boundary: `let r = first(buf)` roots `r` in `buf`'s cluster. (§11.3.6)
013-64. While a default-returned alias is live, the rooted-in input may not be moved or mutated. (§11.3.6)
013-65. The compiler infers return rootedness from the function body's control flow. (§11.3.6)
013-66. When multiple inputs may contribute to a return, the result is rooted in the union of contributing clusters, extending move/mutation locks across all rooted-in inputs. (§11.3.6)
013-67. Declaring `-> own T` opts the return into anchoring, giving the caller a real owner independent of the inputs: `fn first_owned(v: Vec[Record]) -> own Record`. (§11.3.6)
013-68. Anchoring a `Copy`-typed return is a free implicit copy at the return site. (§11.3.6)
013-69. Anchoring a `Clone`-but-not-`Copy` return inserts an implicit `.clone()` at the return site; the user does not write it and the cost is exposed in the elaborated form and diagnostics. (§11.3.6)
013-70. Anchoring a return whose type is neither `Copy` nor `Clone` is a compile error. (§11.3.6)
013-71. Only storage sites (record fields, indexed slots, enum payloads, reactive cells) default to owned; every transient binding site defaults to borrow-equivalent. (§11.3.6)
013-72. There is no stdlib-privileged borrow-returning carve-out; element accessors such as `element_at(v: Vec[T], i: isize) -> T` are borrow-equivalent under the ordinary default convention. (§11.3.6)
013-73. A return-value alias may feed a category B/D storage site only if the function declares `-> own T` or the caller's binding is explicitly `.clone()`d. (§11.3.6)
013-74. `Copy` is a language-defined marker trait with no methods and no associated types: `trait Copy`. (§11.4)
013-75. Values of a `Copy` type are duplicated implicitly at every use site (assignment, argument passing, return) without ownership transfer; the original binding remains usable. (§11.4)
013-76. Non-primitive types opt into `Copy` via `@derive(Copy)` or explicit `satisfies Copy`; the two forms have identical semantics. (§11.4)
013-77. All primitive numeric types (`i8`–`i128`, `u8`–`u128`, `isize`, `usize`, `f32`, `f64`), `bool`, and `char` automatically implement `Copy`. (§11.4.1)
013-78. `string`, `duration`, and `instant` automatically implement `Copy` (`duration` and `instant` are i64-sized scalars). (§11.4.1)
013-79. A tuple implements `Copy` when all of its components are `Copy`. (§11.4.1)
013-80. `Range[T]` is `Copy` when `T: Copy`, via a conditional `Copy` impl provided by the stdlib. (§11.4.1)
013-81. Users may write conditional `Copy` impls of the same pattern for their own generic types. (§11.4.1)
013-82. Closure types automatically implement `Copy`; they capture only `Copy` values. (§11.4.1)
013-83. `@derive(Copy)` requires every field's type to be `Copy`; a non-`Copy` field fails derivation with a compile error identifying the offending field. (§11.4.2)
013-84. A newtype may derive `Copy` when its wrapped type is `Copy`. (§11.4.2)
013-85. For `Copy` types every operation that would otherwise transfer ownership instead produces an independent value: `let q = p` leaves `p` usable. (§11.4.3)
013-86. `Copy` duplication is conceptually value-by-value; the runtime mechanism (register copy, memcpy) is implementation-chosen while user-visible behavior is language-guaranteed. (§11.4.3)
013-87. A `mut` binding to a `Copy` type supports reassignment and field assignment exactly as for non-`Copy` types. (§11.4.4)
013-88. Mutation through one `mut` binding of a `Copy` value never affects other bindings, which hold independent copies: after `working.value = 5`, `original.value` is `0`. (§11.4.4)
013-89. `Clone` is the explicit deep-duplication trait, declared as `fn clone(value: Subject) -> own Subject`. (§11.5)
013-90. `clone` takes its parameter under the default borrow-equivalent convention and returns an independent owned copy; the source is not consumed. (§11.5)
013-91. `Clone` duplication requires an explicit `.clone()` call at every duplication site. (§11.5)
013-92. `Clone` is a derivable trait: `@derive(Clone)`. (§11.5.1)
013-93. Derived `Clone` on a record clones each field; every field's type must itself be `Clone`. (§11.5.1)
013-94. Derived `Clone` on an enum clones the payload of the active variant. (§11.5.1)
013-95. Every `Copy` type is auto-derived `Clone`; its `.clone()` returns the same result as direct use. (§11.5.2)
013-96. Heap-allocated structures (`Vec`, `HashMap`), arrays, and records containing them are `Clone` (when their fields support it) but not `Copy`. (§11.5.2)
013-97. Calling `.clone()` on a non-`Copy` value yields a second owned copy, allocating as the type requires: `let backup = buf.clone()`. (§11.5.3)
013-98. `Clone` is the trait the compiler invokes to anchor `-> own T` rooted returns; implementations are invoked implicitly at return sites in addition to explicit `.clone()` call sites. (§11.5.4)
013-99. `string` realizes its `Copy` semantics through refcounted shared backing: assigning, passing, or returning a string increments a refcount without copying bytes. (§11.6)
013-100. Dropping a `string` decrements the refcount and deallocates the backing when it reaches zero. (§11.6)
013-101. String duplication is constant-time regardless of string length: `let t = s` is constant-time. (§11.6)
013-102. Arrays are not `Copy`, regardless of element type or compile-time size. (§11.6.1)
013-103. A function parameter has exactly one of two ownership conventions: default borrow-equivalent (`fn f(v: T)`) or opt-in consumption (`fn f(own v: T)`). (§11.7)
013-104. A default parameter is a borrow-equivalent cluster member rooted in the caller's argument binding, with lifetime equal to the call expression. (§11.7.1)
013-105. A `Copy`-typed default parameter is a fresh independent value via implicit copy at the call boundary; the caller's binding behaves identically to the non-`Copy` case. (§11.7.1)
013-106. A default parameter is immutable. (§11.7.1)
013-107. Declaring a function parameter `mut` is a compile error: `fn process(mut buf: Vec[f32])` ✗. (§11.7.2)
013-108. `mut local = param` is legal when `param` is an `own` parameter: ownership transfers to `local` and `param`'s name is killed. (§11.7.3)
013-109. `mut local = param.clone()` on a default parameter produces a fresh real owner; the parameter's cluster is untouched. (§11.7.3)
013-110. `mut local = param` where `param` is a default (borrow-equivalent) parameter is a compile error. (§11.7.3)
013-111. For `Copy` parameter types, `mut local = param` and `mut local = param.clone()` are equivalent; the compiler flags the `.clone()` as redundant with a style lint. (§11.7.3)
013-112. An `own T` parameter consumes its argument; the caller's binding is inaccessible after the call: `let s = into_sorted(move v)` then `print(v)` ✗. (§11.7.4)
013-113. Inside the body an `own` parameter is a real owner: it may be `mut`-rebound, moved, stored in structural slots, or dropped at function exit. (§11.7.4)
013-114. `own` is grammatically a keyword preceding the binding name in parameter position and may mark any subset of parameters: `fn g(a: T, own b: U) -> V`. (§11.7.4)
013-115. `own` on a `Copy`-typed parameter is legal but redundant (the caller is unaffected); the compiler may report it with an optional style lint. (§11.7.4)
013-116. A trait method's `own` declarations are part of its signature contract: implementations must declare exactly the parameters the trait declares `own`, no more and no fewer. (§11.7.4)
013-117. The compiler maintains an elaborated form of every signature making conventions, cluster/return lifetime relationships, and anchoring points explicit: `fn f(v: T) -> T` elaborates to `fn f(borrow v: T) -> borrow_rooted_in(v) T`. (§11.7.5)
013-118. Borrow-rootedness of returns is inferred from the function body; `own` parameters and `-> own T` are written by the user in source. (§11.7.5)
013-119. The elaborated form is normative for diagnostic templates: every ownership-related error references elaborated-form entities rather than the user-facing surface. (§11.7.5)
013-120. A user may write the elaborated form explicitly in signatures; the explicit and default forms must be equivalent and the compiler verifies the mapping. (§11.7.5)
013-121. The unmarked call form `f(x)` always means no consumption: the caller's binding survives the call regardless of the callee's internal use of its alias. (§11.8)
013-122. There is no borrow sigil at call sites — no `&v` or `borrow(v)` surface syntax; the compiler inserts the borrow-equivalent aliasing automatically: `length(v)`. (§11.8.1)
013-123. Use-after-move is a compile error reported at the use site; the diagnostic identifies the `move` expression that consumed the binding. (§11.8.2)
013-124. Use-after-move detection is local: the callee's signature plus the call-site `move` identify the consumption without analyzing the callee's implementation. (§11.8.2)
013-125. `x.f(args)` is sugar for `f(x, args)`: the receiver is the first positional argument, follows the method's first-parameter convention, and the dot-call introduces no implicit ownership rule of its own. (§11.8.3)
013-126. Consuming a method receiver uses prefix parenthesization: `(move v).into_sorted()`. (§11.8.3)
013-127. Field access `x.field` and indexed access `x[i]` are not function calls and never consume, regardless of any signature. (§11.8.3)
013-128. Tightening a parameter from default `T` to `own T` makes every existing `move`-less call site a compile error requiring `move` to be added. (§11.8.4)
013-129. Loosening a parameter from `own T` to default `T` makes every existing `move` call site a compile error requiring `move` to be removed. (§11.8.4)
013-130. `move <l-value identifier>` is legal only as an immediate sub-expression of a function-call argument list: `f(move x)`, `g(a, move b, c)`. (§11.8.5)
013-131. `move` in a let-RHS is a parse error: `let y = move x` ✗. (§11.8.5)
013-132. `move` in a return expression is a parse error: `return move x` ✗. (§11.8.5)
013-133. `move` in a mut-RHS is a parse error: `mut z = move x` ✗. (§11.8.5)
013-134. `move` attached to a dotted expression is a parse error; prefix parenthesization is required: `move v.method()` ✗, `(move v).method()` ✓. (§11.8.5)
013-135. An `own` parameter whose call-site argument is a named binding lacking `move` is a compile error. (§11.8.5)
013-136. A call-site `move` whose corresponding parameter is not declared `own` is a compile error. (§11.8.5)
013-137. An rvalue argument or receiver (a temporary with no name, such as a call result or constructor expression) feeds an `own` parameter or `own` receiver by direct consumption with no `move`; `move` marks the consumption of a named binding only, and a temporary has no name to render inaccessible: `a.clone().push(move x)`. (§11.8.5)
013-138. The user-facing surface has no `&T` syntax; borrow-equivalent aliases are introduced only implicitly by the slot-default conventions. (§11.9)
013-139. The compiler's elaborated form exposes the inferred alias lifetimes for diagnostics and tooling. (§11.9)
013-140. Stdlib types use the same alias mechanism as user-defined types; there is no language-privileged carve-out for the standard library. (§11.9)
013-141. A borrow-equivalent alias may not be stored in a record field, enum variant payload, or tuple component (category B); compound types contain only owned values. (§11.9.1)
013-142. Storing a value derived from an alias requires declaring the producing function's return `-> own T` or an explicit `.clone()` to create a real owner. (§11.9.1)
013-143. A borrow-equivalent alias may not be stored in a reactive cell (category D): `signal.write`, `stream.emit`, attr reassignment, and recurrent advance require a real owner. (§11.9.1)
013-144. A borrow-equivalent alias may not be captured by an escaping closure. (§11.9.1)
013-145. Aliases may appear only in: default function parameters, `let`/`mut` rebindings of an alias source, default for-loop iteration variables, default-convention function returns, and borrow-default trait associated-type slots. (§11.9.1)
013-146. A default-parameter alias's lifetime is bounded by the call expression. (§11.9.1)
013-147. Alias lifetimes extend per site: let-rebind to the binding's scope, for-loop variable to the loop body, function return to the caller's scope (rooted in the inputs), associated-type slot to its use-site's scope. (§11.9.1)
013-148. `Handle[T]` is not an alias but a `Copy` value weakly designating a graph entity; it is exempt from every alias restriction and may be stored in cells, fields, payloads, or tuples. (§11.9.1)
013-149. A handle's resolution `Option[&T]` contains a borrow and is subject to storage categories B and D like any alias-bearing value. (§11.9.1)
013-150. While a cluster has any alias member, the root may not be moved — passed to an `own` parameter, returned, or reassigned into a different slot. (§11.9.2)
013-151. While a cluster has any alias member, the root may not be mutated — reassigned through `mut`, or assigned by index or field. (§11.9.2)
013-152. Once every alias goes out of scope the cluster collapses back to its root, which may again be moved or mutated. (§11.9.2)
013-153. Borrowing a binding in one sub-expression while consuming it in another sub-expression of the same expression is a compile error: `combine(v, extract(move v))` ✗. (§11.9.2)
013-154. The borrow/consume conflict is reported at the offending sub-expression. (§11.9.2)
013-155. Multiple borrow-equivalent aliases of one source may coexist; all are read-only: `compare(v, v)` ✓. (§11.9.3)
013-156. Each let-rebind within a cluster kills the prior name and creates a new alias of the same source (single-name-per-cluster). (§11.9.3)
013-157. Several distinct aliases of one source may be live across a single call expression: `read_two(y, items)` ✓. (§11.9.3)
013-158. A default (borrow-equivalent) parameter may be passed to another default-parameter function with no extra syntax; the alias chain extends: `count_elements(v)`. (§11.9.4)
013-159. A borrow-equivalent parameter cannot be consumed inside the function body: `consume_vec(move v)` ✗. (§11.9.4)
013-160. Returning a borrow-equivalent parameter under the default convention propagates its cluster to the caller as a borrow-equivalent alias with no `Clone` gate; an owned return requires `-> own T` anchoring (§11.3.6): `return v`. (§11.9.4)
013-161. Every `let`/`mut` rebinding of an alias inside a function body follows Rule (P): the new name aliases the same source and the old name dies: `let saved = v`. (§11.9.4)
013-162. A `Copy` value passed by the default convention behaves as a fresh independent copy: the caller's binding remains usable and the parameter's value cannot be observed by the caller. (§11.9.5)
013-163. The compiler may internally elide the alias machinery for `Copy` types where useful. (§11.9.5)
013-164. A function generic over `T: SomeTrait` declares its parameter under the default convention regardless of whether `T` is `Copy`; the compiler specializes per instantiation. (§11.9.5)
013-165. A closure is a `fn` literal — a `fn` declaration with the name omitted: `fn(params) -> ret: body`. (§11.10)
013-166. A closure literal's parameter and return types may be omitted when inferable from the expected closure type at the use site. (§11.10)
013-167. A closure body is a single expression or an indented block, exactly as for a named `fn`. (§11.10)
013-168. Closures capture by value: each captured value is stored inside the closure at the moment of definition. (§11.10)
013-169. Every value captured by a closure must have a `Copy` type; the capture is a snapshot at definition time: `fn(sample: f32): sample * gain` captures `gain` ✓. (§11.10.1)
013-170. Capturing a non-`Copy` value is a compile error: `fn(): sum(buf)` ✗ for `buf: Vec[f32]`. (§11.10.1)
013-171. Non-`Copy` values flow through closures as parameters under the default borrow-equivalent convention: `fn(b: Vec[f32]): sum(b)`. (§11.10.1)
013-172. A closure that consumes a non-`Copy` argument declares the parameter `own`: `fn(own b: Vec[f32]): into_sorted(move b)`. (§11.10.1)
013-173. A closure value is invoked with ordinary call syntax on its binding: `let total = closure(buf)`. (§11.10.1)
013-174. The compiler captures the minimal set of subvalues the closure body references: `fn(): contact.age + 1` captures only `contact.age`. (§11.10.2)
013-175. The `Copy` constraint applies to the captured subvalue's type, not the root binding's type. (§11.10.2)
013-176. Closures may capture from `let` bindings: `fn(): stable + 1` ✓. (§11.10.3)
013-177. Capturing from a `mut` binding is a compile error: `fn(): counter + 1` ✗ for `mut counter`. (§11.10.3)
013-178. The capture-must-be-`Copy` restriction applies only to the captured environment; closure bodies follow normal function-body rules (local `mut`, consuming calls, construction, iteration). (§11.10.4)
013-179. An escaping closure cannot capture borrow-equivalent aliases: cluster members are not `Copy` and an escaping closure may outlive the cluster root. (§11.10.5)
013-180. A closure escapes when it is stored, returned, or captured by another escaping closure; the compiler distinguishes escaping from non-escaping by closure-flow analysis. (§11.10.5)
013-181. A closure type is written `fn(P1, P2, …) -> R` and is inhabited by closure literals and free functions alike: `let d: fn(i32) -> i32 = double`. (§11.10.6)
013-182. Closure types are structural — defined by signature alone, with no declaration and no nominal identity — the second structural carve-out after tuples. (§11.10.6)
013-183. A bare parenthesized list `(T1, T2)` is a tuple type; only the `fn` prefix forms a function type. (§11.10.6)
013-184. `fn((T1, T2)) -> R` is a one-parameter function type taking a tuple, distinct from the two-parameter `fn(T1, T2) -> R`. (§11.10.6)
013-185. Parameter and return ownership conventions are part of a closure type: `fn(own T) -> own R` consumes its parameter and returns an owned value. (§11.10.6)
013-186. A callable inhabits a closure type only if its ownership conventions match the type's. (§11.10.6)
013-187. A closure value is itself `Copy`: read, passed, and duplicated freely, never consumed. (§11.10.6)
013-188. There is no consumed-once or mutable-capture closure variant; captures can be neither mutable nor non-`Copy`. (§11.10.6)
013-189. In parameter and return positions a closure type is monomorphized per concrete closure (distinct captures are distinct concrete types), behaving as an implicit generic with no indirection: `fn apply(f: fn(i32) -> i32, x: i32)`. (§11.10.6)
013-190. Holding closures of the same signature but different concrete types in one place requires erasure to `dyn fn(P) -> R`, paying vtable indirection: `Vec[dyn fn(Event) -> ()]`. (§11.10.6)
013-191. A closure type exposes one operation — its call — with the closure value as the erased receiver and concrete `P`/`R`, satisfying every object-safety rule of §5.2.4. (§11.10.6)
013-192. Every closure type is object-safe without exception; `dyn fn(P) -> R` is always well-formed. (§11.10.6)
013-193. Coercion from `fn(P) -> R` to `dyn fn(P) -> R` is governed by §5.2.5. (§11.10.6)
013-194. A closure cannot be the value type of a `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, or `derived` cell. (§11.10.6)
013-195. A `mut` binding may carry an explicit type annotation: `mut handlers: Vec[dyn fn(Event) -> ()] = Vec::new()`. (§11.10.6)
013-196. Field assignment through a `mut` root binding is permitted: `r.field = new_value`. (§11.11)
013-197. Indexed assignment through a `mut` root binding is permitted: `arr[5] = 1.5`. (§11.11)
013-198. Field and indexed assignment desugar to operator-trait method calls. (§11.11)
013-199. The exact assignment traits (`FieldAssign`, `IndexAssign`, or analogous) are stdlib concerns specified outside the language spec. (§11.11)
013-200. The assignment desugaring preserves the single-writer invariant: no other binding to the same underlying value may exist while the mutation occurs. (§11.11)
013-201. The RHS of an indexed, field, or whole-value assignment is consumed implicitly into the storage slot — no `move` keyword: `arr[5] = v` kills `v`'s name. (§11.11)
013-202. Implicit RHS consumption also applies to record construction and to reactive cell writes (category D). (§11.11)
013-203. Function-call argument positions (category A) are the only places where consumption requires the explicit `move` keyword. (§11.11)
013-204. The RHS of a storage assignment must be a real owner or a `Copy` value; a borrow-equivalent alias as RHS is a compile error. (§11.11)
013-205. The alias-as-RHS diagnostic suggests `.clone()` or restructuring to source the value from a real owner. (§11.11)
013-206. Reading a field or element from any binding, `let` or `mut`, is unrestricted. (§11.11)
013-207. A `mut` binding may be reassigned entirely; the previous value is dropped: `buf = make_other_buffer()`. (§11.11.1)
013-208. Whole-value reassignment moves the new value into the binding, consuming it from its source. (§11.11.1)
013-209. Index, field, and whole-value assignments mutate a value the `mut` binding already owns and are in place by construction. (§11.11.2)
013-210. The consume-and-produce form `fn f(own subject, …) -> Subject` is the canonical way a type exposes mutation under single ownership, for stdlib and user types alike. (§11.11.2)
013-211. The language has no mutable-reference parameter. (§11.11.2)
013-212. When a consume-and-produce call's subject is uniquely owned and the result is rebound to the same location with no other reference to the consumed binding in between, the compiler reuses the input's storage with no copy: `v = (move v).push(move x)`. (§11.11.2)
013-213. In-place storage reuse is a guarantee of the ownership model, deterministic because both conditions are statically decidable — not a heuristic optimization. (§11.11.2)
013-214. The §12.7.2 iterator optimization is the in-place reuse rule applied to the for-loop's internal binding. (§11.11.2)
013-215. A consume-and-produce call whose value must outlive the call produces a fresh value, leaving the original intact. (§11.11.2)
013-216. Keeping a value while obtaining a transformed copy requires an explicit `.clone()` first; there is no hidden allocation: `let b = a.clone().push(move x)`. (§11.11.2)
013-217. A method call on a `mut` binding whose receiver parameter is `own` and which returns `Subject` may omit the receiver move and rebind: `v.m(args)` desugars to `v = (move v).m(args)`. (§11.11.3)
013-218. The receiver sugar covers only the receiver; an `own` non-`Copy` argument still requires an explicit `move`: `v.push(move item)`. (§11.11.3)
013-219. `Copy` arguments in the sugared form need no `move`: `v.push(3)`. (§11.11.3)
013-220. The receiver sugar applies only to `mut` bindings and only when the receiver parameter is declared `own`. (§11.11.3)
013-221. A default (borrow-equivalent) receiver call is an ordinary non-consuming call and is never rewritten by the sugar. (§11.11.3)
013-222. A record, enum, or newtype may itself be `Copy` if it carries `@derive(Copy)` and all its fields/payloads are `Copy`. (§11.12)
013-223. Mutability is a property of the binding, never of the type; a type cannot declare itself mutable. (§11.12)
013-224. A record's field is assignable in place through a `mut` binding to the containing record, provided the field's type permits assignment: `r.field = v`. (§11.12)
013-225. An enum variant's payload and a newtype's wrapped value have no in-place place form and are never assigned through the binding; a `mut` enum or newtype is updated only by reassigning the whole value to a freshly constructed one, per the whole-value reassignment of §11.11.1: `state = Active(p2)`. (§11.12)
013-226. The record-body ban on `fn` declarations does not restrict mutation; any holder of a `mut` binding to a record may assign its fields. (§11.12)
013-227. The `public(private)` constructor pattern restricts only initial construction; a holder of a `mut` binding may still mutate the fields, subject to field visibility. (§11.12.1)
013-228. Restricting post-construction mutation is done with field-level visibility (`private field_name: T`), which prevents external code from naming the field in an assignment. (§11.12.1)
013-229. Trait method signatures follow the same ownership conventions as free functions — default borrow-equivalent, `own` opt-in: `fn into_sorted(own value: Subject) -> Subject`. (§11.13)
013-230. Trait dispatch is unaffected by ownership semantics; whether a method consumes or borrows is determined solely by the trait method's signature. (§11.13)
013-231. Implementations cannot strengthen or weaken `own`: a trait-declared `own` parameter must be `own` in every `fulfill`, and a default parameter must stay default. (§11.13)
013-232. A `fulfill` block's signature is checked structurally against the trait's signature. (§11.13)
013-233. Trait objects (`dyn T`) may invoke methods declared with any ownership convention. (§11.13)
013-234. In trait-object calls, the alias lifetime of a default-convention receiver remains call-scoped, as for direct calls. (§11.13)
013-235. A `derived` (and analogous) expression body is pure-evaluated: it runs as a pure function of its inputs each time the inputs change. (§11.14)
013-236. A `derived` body may invoke ordinary functions that use `mut` internally; the body's produced value enters the reactive graph. (§11.14)
013-237. A value bound into the reactive system as a signal, derived, or reactive store becomes external state, no longer owned by any single function's scope. (§11.14)
013-238. Reactive values are updated only through the reactive system's defined update mechanisms, never through `mut` assignment. (§11.14)
013-239. Reactive cell writes — `signal.write(v)`, `stream.emit(v)`, attr reassignment from a recurrent advance — are category D: the RHS is consumed implicitly, with no `move` written. (§11.14)
013-240. Reactive wiring (category C) — placement attribute assignment from a reactive RHS, connection arguments referencing attrs or signals — does not consume and produces multiple aliases to the same cell. (§11.14)
013-241. The category-B/C/D distinction at placement attribute assignment is type-directed: a reactive-typed RHS produces wiring; a value-typed RHS is consumed into the attr's slot. (§11.14)
013-242. Function parameters flowing into reactive declarations follow `own`/`move` like any other category A consumption. (§11.14)
013-243. The reactive boundary is one of the "global" scopes referenced by §11.1's principles; value crossing is fully specified in §13.12. (§11.14)

## 014. Iteration & Loops — 166 Rules

014-1. The language's iteration constructs are integer ranges, the `for` loop in default and `for own` forms, the `while` loop, `break` and `continue`, loop expressions with `else:`, and the `Iterator`, `Iterable`, and `IntoIterable` traits. (§12)
014-2. A `for` loop obtains an iterator through `Iterable` (default form) or `IntoIterable` (`for own` form), then produces successive values through `Iterator`. (§12.1)
014-3. All iteration dispatches through the trait protocol; no type has built-in iteration logic specific to it. (§12.1)
014-4. `for` and `while` loops are expressions whose value is determined by the body's `break` expressions and an optional `else:` clause. (§12.1)
014-5. A loop is an expression in every position: its type follows §12.6's value-shaping rules whether or not the value is consumed; using a loop where its value is unused does not coerce it to `()` or create a separate statement form. (§12.1)
014-6. Loop bodies are ordinary function-body fragments: they may mutate `mut` bindings declared inside or outside the loop and perform indexed and field assignment per §11's ownership rules. (§12.1)
014-7. The range expression syntax is `start..end` over integer expressions: `0..10`. (§12.2)
014-8. Ranges are half-open, exclusive on the upper bound: `0..10` contains 0 through 9. (§12.2)
014-9. There is no inclusive-range form (`..=`) in v1; iteration through `N` is written `start..(N + 1)`. (§12.2)
014-10. A range has type `Range[T]` where `T` is the bounds' integer type: `let r = 0..1024` is `Range[i32]` via the default integer placeholder. (§12.2)
014-11. Both range bounds must have the same integer type; mixed-kind operands require explicit conversion. (§12.2)
014-12. Ranges are ordinary values that may be bound, passed, and returned: `fn process_range(r: Range[i32]) -> isize`. (§12.2)
014-13. `Range[T]` implements both `Iterable` and `IntoIterable`. (§12.2)
014-14. `Range[T]` is `Copy`, so the default and `for own` forms are functionally indistinguishable on ranges. (§12.2)
014-15. Range iteration yields successive integers starting at `start` and stopping before `end`. (§12.2)
014-16. Ranges have no step parameter in v1; iteration always advances by one, and striding is written as explicit arithmetic: `let actual_i = i * 2`. (§12.2.1)
014-17. Range bounds are evaluated once at range construction; later mutation of variables used in the bound expressions does not affect the range: after `mut n = 10; let r = 0..n; n = 100`, `r` still iterates 0..10. (§12.2.2)
014-18. Range bound expressions must produce integer values. (§12.2.2)
014-19. Overflow in a range bound expression traps at range construction per §4.6.1. (§12.2.2)
014-20. A range with `start >= end` is empty and yields no iterations: `for i in 5..3:` runs the body zero times. (§12.2.3)
014-21. Negative range bounds are valid when `T` is signed: `for i in -10..10:` yields -10 through 9. (§12.2.3)
014-22. A negative range-bound literal with unsigned `T` is rejected by the value-fits check of §2.4.3. (§12.2.3)
014-23. `for x in v:` is the default, borrow-equivalent form: the source survives the loop. (§12.3)
014-24. `for own x in v:` is the consuming form: the source is consumed at loop entry and is not accessible to the surrounding scope afterward. (§12.3)
014-25. The iteration source expression is evaluated exactly once, at loop entry. (§12.3.1)
014-26. The default form constructs its iterator by invoking `Iterable::iterator(v)`, passing `v` borrow-equivalently so `v` becomes a cluster source. (§12.3.1)
014-27. Under the default form the iterator is held in an internal `mut` binding, and both the iterator and the source are live throughout the loop. (§12.3.1)
014-28. Each default-form iteration step calls `Iterator::next(iter, v)`, threading the source through every call and returning `(Option[Item], NewIter)`. (§12.3.1)
014-29. A default-form yielded `Item` is borrow-equivalent rooted in the source's cluster. (§12.3.1)
014-30. After each `next` call the loop's internal iterator binding is reassigned to the returned `NewIter`. (§12.3.1)
014-31. A `Some(value)` result from `next` binds `value` to the iteration variable and runs the body. (§12.3.1)
014-32. A `None` result from `next` exits the loop. (§12.3.1)
014-33. When a default-form loop exits — natural completion, `break`, or enclosing function return — the iterator is dropped and the cluster's iteration alias collapses. (§12.3.1)
014-34. After a default-form loop, the source is unchanged and remains owned by its original binding. (§12.3.1)
014-35. The `for own` form invokes `IntoIterable::consuming_iterator(move v)`, consuming the binding and moving the underlying value into the iterator. (§12.3.1)
014-36. Each `for own` iteration step calls `Iterator::next(iter, ())`, supplying unit as the source per the `Iter.Source = ()` constraint. (§12.3.1)
014-37. Under `for own`, each yielded `Item` is an owned value moved out of the iterator's storage, and the iteration variable is a real owner. (§12.3.1)
014-38. When a `for own` loop exits, the iterator is dropped and elements not yet yielded are dropped per their `Drop` semantics. (§12.3.1)
014-39. The iteration variable is bound fresh on each iteration as an immutable `let`-style binding of the iterated `Item` type; reassigning it in the body is a compile error. (§12.3.2)
014-40. `for mut x in iterable:` is a parse error; the iteration variable cannot be declared `mut`. (§12.3.2)
014-41. A mutable per-iteration value is obtained by rebinding inside the body: `mut local = x`. (§12.3.2)
014-42. The iteration variable's type is `Iter::Item`, where `Iter` is `Iterable::Iter` under the default form and `IntoIterable::Iter` under `for own`. (§12.3.3)
014-43. Under the default form with a `Copy` element type, the iteration variable is an implicit Copy of the current element: `for sample in buf:` with `buf: f32[1024]` gives `sample: f32`. (§12.3.3)
014-44. Under the default form with a non-`Copy` element type, the iteration variable is a borrow-equivalent alias rooted in the source's current element. (§12.3.3)
014-45. A default-form borrow-equivalent iteration variable cannot be consumed into ownership (Rule P): `consume(move r)` ✗. (§12.3.3)
014-46. Binding a default-form iteration alias to a new name creates a new alias in the same cluster and kills the old name: `let saved = r`. (§12.3.3)
014-47. Under `for own` with a non-`Copy` element type, each iteration moves one element out of the consumed source's storage and the body has full ownership of it: `destinations = (move destinations).push(move r)`. (§12.3.3)
014-48. The body executes in a fresh nested scope each iteration; bindings declared inside the body are dropped at the end of every iteration. (§12.3.4)
014-49. The iteration variable is in scope only within the loop body. (§12.3.4)
014-50. Bindings declared outside the loop are in scope inside the body, persist across iterations, and can be mutated if declared `mut`: the accumulator `total = total + sample`. (§12.3.4)
014-51. Consuming an outer binding inside a loop body is a compile error because later iterations would use the already-moved value: `consume(move v)` in a loop body ✗. (§12.3.5)
014-52. The loop use-after-move check is conservative: every `move` of an outer binding inside a loop body is reported as a potential use-after-move at the move site. (§12.3.5)
014-53. Guidance: to consume a value inside a loop body, `.clone()` it per iteration, restructure to consume after the loop, or move it into a single-iteration loop. (§12.3.5)
014-54. Under the default form, the owner may not move or mutate the iterated source while the loop is active: `v[0] = 5` inside `for x in v:` ✗ (live cluster member). (§12.3.6)
014-55. Iteration aliases collapse when the loop exits, after which the owner may freely move or mutate the source. (§12.3.6)
014-56. Under `for own`, referencing the source binding inside the body is a use-after-move error, not a cluster conflict, because the binding was consumed at loop entry. (§12.3.6)
014-57. A `for` loop is compile-time-unrolled iff its iterable is compile-time known per §2.4.1; otherwise it runs at runtime. (§12.3.7)
014-58. There is no syntactic marker selecting unrolling — no `const for`, no `inline for`, no modifier; the iterable alone decides the kind. (§12.3.7)
014-59. Unrolling reproduces the body once per element, binding the iteration variable to the corresponding compile-time value. (§12.3.7)
014-60. An unrolled body participates in ordinary §2.4.1 propagation: expressions depending only on the iteration variable and other compile-time values are themselves compile-time known. (§12.3.7)
014-61. Unrolled loops do not dispatch through `Iterable` or `IntoIterable`; element binding happens at compile time, not by calling `next`. (§12.3.7)
014-62. Exactly two iterable forms produce compile-time-known iteration in v1: a range with both bounds compile-time known, and an array literal whose every element expression is compile-time known. (§12.3.7)
014-63. Iterating over the elements of a tuple and iterating over a list of types are not provided. (§12.3.7)
014-64. Loops over variable-extent types run at runtime: `Vec[T]`, `SmallVec[T, N]`, `RingBuf[T, N]`, `String`/`s.chars()`, `HashMap`, and ranges with runtime bounds. (§12.3.7)
014-65. A pure function with a `for` in its body has one source; whether that loop unrolls is decided at each call site by what flows into the iterable expression. (§12.3.7)
014-66. A function that requires its loop to unroll expresses the requirement in its signature via a const-generic parameter: `fn process[const N: usize](buf: T[N]):`. (§12.3.7)
014-67. A const-generic parameter is compile-time known at every call site, so an internal `for i in 0..N` over it is guaranteed to unroll. (§12.3.7)
014-68. There is no body-internal assertion form for requiring compile-time iteration; lifting the parameter to a const-generic is the only mechanism. (§12.3.7)
014-69. A loop is runtime iff its iterable is runtime; there is no silent flip between compile-time and runtime based on context. (§12.3.7)
014-70. A `for` in a node type's `expose:` block or in a placement body is auto-enforced compile-time per §13.1's static-graph rule; a runtime iterable there is a compile error. (§12.3.7)
014-71. Runtime-varying child cardinality in a placement uses `repeat` (§13.5.4), not `for`; the compile-time `for` covers only const-generic-parameterized, per-instance-fixed multiplicity. (§12.3.7)
014-72. `while condition: body` repeatedly evaluates the condition and runs the body so long as the condition is true. (§12.4)
014-73. A `while` condition must produce a value of type `bool`. (§12.4.1)
014-74. The condition is re-evaluated before each iteration, including the first; a loop whose condition is `false` at entry never executes its body. (§12.4.1)
014-75. There is no separate `loop` keyword; `while true:` is the idiomatic loop-forever form. (§12.4.2)
014-76. The move-inside-loop rule of §12.3.5 applies to `while` bodies and equally to the condition expression. (§12.4.3)
014-77. `break` exits the innermost enclosing loop. (§12.5)
014-78. `continue` skips the rest of the current iteration and advances the innermost enclosing loop to its next iteration. (§12.5)
014-79. `break expr` carries a value that becomes the loop's expression value. (§12.5.1)
014-80. Plain `break` is equivalent to `break ()`. (§12.5.1)
014-81. A loop body mixing plain `break` with `break value` of a non-unit value is a type error. (§12.5.1)
014-82. `continue` never produces a value; it is a control-flow statement only. (§12.5.2)
014-83. There is no label syntax in v1; `break` and `continue` always target the innermost enclosing loop. (§12.5.3)
014-84. Guidance: to exit nested loops, use a flag variable or extract the inner loop into a function that returns early. (§12.5.3)
014-85. A `break` or `continue` outside a loop is a parse error. (§12.5.4)
014-86. A loop may carry an optional `else:` clause attached to its body. (§12.6.1)
014-87. The `else:` expression is evaluated exactly when the loop completes naturally: iteration exhausts for `for`, or the condition becomes false for `while`. (§12.6.1)
014-88. The `else:` clause is not evaluated when the loop exits via `break` or via an enclosing function return. (§12.6.1)
014-89. A loop's `else:` clause is written at the loop head's indentation, dedented from the body rather than nested inside it. (§12.6.1)
014-90. A loop with no `break value` and no `else:`, whose natural completion is reachable, has type `()`. (§12.6.2)
014-91. A loop with no `break value` and an `else:`, whose natural completion is reachable, has the type of the `else:` expression. (§12.6.2)
014-92. A loop with `break value` sites and no `else:`, whose natural completion is reachable, has type `Option[T]`. (§12.6.2)
014-93. In a break-value loop without `else:` whose natural completion is reachable, each `break value` is auto-wrapped to `Some`; the user writes the bare value: `break item.id` yields `Some(item.id)`. (§12.6.2)
014-94. In a break-value loop without `else:`, natural completion produces `None`. (§12.6.2)
014-95. A loop with both `break value` sites and an `else:` has type `T`, the unified type of all break sites and the `else:` expression. (§12.6.2)
014-96. All `break value` expressions in one loop body must produce values of the same type or be unifiable, together with the `else:` expression when present: `break 42` vs `break "hello"` ✗. (§12.6.3)
014-97. A failed unification among `break value` sites and the `else:` clause is reported as a type error at the conflicting break or else site. (§12.6.3)
014-98. If every path through the body diverges (`break value`, `return`, `panic`, or other diverging operation), the loop's natural completion is unreachable. (§12.6.4)
014-99. When a loop's natural completion is provably unreachable (§12.6.4), the natural-completion arm unifies away via `never` (§8.2.2) and a loop with `break value` sites has type `T`, not `Option[T]`, regardless of any `else:`; this collapse is required, not implementation-discretionary. (§12.6.4)
014-100. A loop with no `break value` and provably-unreachable natural completion (e.g. `while true:` with no `break`) yields no value on any path and has type `never`. (§12.6.4)
014-101. The loop's expression value is determined by `break value` and `else:` only; `continue` does not contribute to it. (§12.6.5)
014-102. The stdlib `Iterator` trait declares `type Item`, `type Source = ()`, and `fn next(own iter: Subject, source: Source) -> (Option[Item], Subject)`. (§12.7)
014-103. `Iterator::next` consumes the iterator and returns the next item — `None` when iteration is complete — together with the advanced iterator, which the caller binds for the next call. (§12.7)
014-104. `Item` and `Source` are borrow-equivalent associated-type slots per §3.1.2; consuming semantics require opting in via `type Item = own T` or `type Source = own T`. (§12.7)
014-105. The associated-type slot convention is uniform across all traits; stdlib types get no special treatment. (§12.7)
014-106. `Source` is the external value the iterator reads from on each `next` call; the choice of pattern is the iterator author's, expressed by declaring `Source`. (§12.7)
014-107. Self-contained iterators (Range, counters, infinite generators) hold all state internally, use the default `type Source = ()`, and ignore the unit argument on each `next` call. (§12.7)
014-108. Source-bearing iterators hold only cursor state and declare `type Source = TheCollection`, receiving the collection borrow-equivalently on every `next` call. (§12.7)
014-109. Linear-ownership compilation triggers when the iterator type is statically known (monomorphized), the binding is a single `mut` location, and `NewIter` is rebound to that same location in a single statement with no other reference to the consumed binding. (§12.7.2)
014-110. The for-loop's internal desugaring satisfies the linear-ownership conditions by construction: one statement calls `next`, destructures the returned tuple, and rebinds the iterator location. (§12.7.2)
014-111. Under linear-ownership compilation, `next` receives a pointer to the iterator's state, mutates the cursor in place, and returns only the item; the consumed and returned iterator are the same memory location and no copy occurs. (§12.7.2)
014-112. Linear-ownership compilation of `Iterator::next` is a required property of conforming implementations, not an optional optimization. (§12.7.2)
014-113. The linear-ownership rule is the for-loop instance of the general consume-and-produce storage-reuse guarantee of §11.11.2. (§12.7.2)
014-114. A self-contained `Iterator` implementation declares `type Source = ()` — or omits it for the default — and its `next` ignores the source parameter. (§12.7.3)
014-115. An `Iterator` implementation advances its cursor by rebinding the consumed iterator to a local `mut` (per §11.7.3), mutating it, and returning it alongside the item: `mut local = iter`. (§12.7.3)
014-116. An iterator cannot hold a borrow-equivalent reference to its collection: record fields are owned (§11.11), so a source-bearing iterator carries only a cursor. (§12.7.4)
014-117. The `source` parameter of `next` aliases the caller's collection borrow-equivalently for the duration of one call. (§12.7.4)
014-118. An iterator reached through `Iterable` dispatch (source-bearing, default form) may not declare `type Item = own T`; owned items are exclusive to the consuming `IntoIterable` path (`Source = ()`, `for own`). (§12.7.4)
014-119. The stdlib `Iterable` trait declares `type Iter: Iterator` and `fn iterator(value: Subject) -> Iter` with `where Iter.Source = Subject`. (§12.8)
014-120. The `where Iter.Source = Subject` constraint binds the iterator's source type to the iterable itself, so the for-loop supplies the original value as the source on each `next` call. (§12.8)
014-121. `Iterable::iterator` takes its parameter by the default borrow-equivalent convention; the returned iterator is rooted in the value's cluster. (§12.8)
014-122. The `Iterable` construction method is named `iterator`, not `iter`, per the full-name convention. (§12.8.1)
014-123. The iterator returned by `Iterable::iterator` has a lifetime bounded by the for-loop expression. (§12.8.2)
014-124. A type implements `Iterable` by declaring `type Iter` and an `iterator` method; `for x in d:` then dispatches to the implementation automatically. (§12.8.3)
014-125. The stdlib `IntoIterable` trait declares `type Iter: Iterator` and `fn consuming_iterator(own value: Subject) -> Iter` with `where Iter.Source = ()`. (§12.9)
014-126. `IntoIterable::consuming_iterator` declares its parameter `own`: the source is consumed at the call and the returned iterator owns the source's storage. (§12.9)
014-127. The `where Iter.Source = ()` constraint binds the iterator's source to unit; the iterator reads from its own internal state, not an external parameter. (§12.9)
014-128. The `IntoIterable` construction method is named `consuming_iterator`, signalling ownership transfer per the full-name convention. (§12.9.1)
014-129. Under `IntoIterable`, each `next` call on a non-`Copy` `Item` physically moves one element out of the iterator's internal storage. (§12.9.2)
014-130. Under `IntoIterable`, each `next` call on a `Copy` `Item` yields a copy of the element. (§12.9.2)
014-131. When a consuming iterator is dropped at loop exit — including early exit via `break` or enclosing return — remaining unyielded elements are dropped and the underlying buffer is released. (§12.9.3)
014-132. The exact `Drop` mechanism for non-`Copy` elements is specified in §14.7. (§12.9.3)
014-133. For `Copy` types, drop is a no-op. (§12.9.3)
014-134. A type implements `IntoIterable` by declaring `type Iter` and a `consuming_iterator` method; `for own x in d:` then dispatches automatically, consuming `d`. (§12.9.4)
014-135. A user-defined type may implement `Iterable`, `IntoIterable`, both, or neither. (§12.9.5)
014-136. If a type implements only `Iterable`, the `for own` form on it is a compile error. (§12.9.5)
014-137. If a type implements only `IntoIterable`, the default `for` form on it is a compile error. (§12.9.5)
014-138. There is no reclaim after consumption: once `consuming_iterator(move v)` is called, `v` is consumed and its elements are either yielded to the body or dropped with the iterator. (§12.9.5)
014-139. Guidance: a caller that needs the source after iteration uses the default `for` form, or restructures to consume-and-rebuild through a transforming function. (§12.9.5)
014-140. Stdlib provides `Iterable` and `IntoIterable` implementations for built-in iterable types using the same mechanism available to user code; no language privilege applies to stdlib iterators. (§12.10)
014-141. Stdlib collections such as `Vec[T]` and `HashMap[K, V]` implement both traits with container-specific iterator types following the source-bearing pattern of §12.7.4. (§12.10)
014-142. Stdlib iteration implementations obey the orphan rule (§3.7): user code cannot redefine `Iterable` for `Vec`. (§12.10)
014-143. Arrays `T[N]` implement both `Iterable` and `IntoIterable`; the user picks the form at the call site. (§12.10.1)
014-144. While an array is iterated under the default form, indexed writes are forbidden per §11.9.2: `arr[i] = v` ✗. (§12.10.1)
014-145. Indexed reads of an array are allowed during default-form iteration: `arr[i]` ✓. (§12.10.1)
014-146. Ranges and their iterators are stack-allocated; range iteration has no heap overhead. (§12.10.2)
014-147. A conforming implementation must compile monomorphized loops over built-in iterables to machine code equivalent to hand-written indexed loops; this is a required property, not an optimization. (§12.11)
014-148. The required loop code has no heap allocation, no iterator-object lifecycle overhead, and no per-iteration function-call cost: the cursor is held in registers and `next` is inlined. (§12.11)
014-149. The iteration variable may be a pattern that destructures each yielded value: `for (id, name) in pairs:`. (§12.12.1)
014-150. Iteration-variable patterns follow the pattern rules of §6.2.4 and §9.2.2. (§12.12.1)
014-151. Refutable patterns are not permitted as iteration variables; the pattern must bind successfully for every yielded value. (§12.12.1)
014-152. There is no `for pattern if guard in iterable:` inline-filter form; filtering is written with `continue` in the body. (§12.12.1)
014-153. Trait method bodies, including default bodies in trait declarations (§3.1.3), may contain loops under the standard mutation and ownership rules. (§12.12.2)
014-154. A generic function body containing a loop is type-checked at definition per §2.2.2. (§12.12.3)
014-155. A generic loop's iterable type must satisfy `Iterable` (default form) or `IntoIterable` (`for own` form) at the call site for each monomorphization. (§12.12.3)
014-156. Associated-type constraints use `.` member-access notation: `fn total[T: Iterable](source: T) -> T.Iter.Item`. (§12.12.3)
014-157. A `derived` expression's body may contain loops; each evaluation of the derived runs the loop fresh. (§12.13)
014-158. A loop's local mutations inside a derived body are not observable outside the evaluation; only the derived's final value enters the reactive graph. (§12.13)
014-159. The collection or range iterated in a derived body may itself be reactive; each update of that value re-evaluates the derived and re-runs the loop. (§12.13)
014-160. A `while` condition may depend on reactive values, but reactive updates do not interrupt an in-progress loop iteration. (§12.13)
014-161. An empty iterable (e.g. `0..0` or an empty container) produces no iterations: the body does not execute and the expression value follows the §12.6.2 table. (§12.14.1)
014-162. An iterator whose `next` always returns `Some(_)` produces an infinite loop; there is no language-level prevention, and responsibility lies with the iterator implementation. (§12.14.2)
014-163. `break` inside the body is the user's mechanism for terminating a loop over a never-completing iterator. (§12.14.2)
014-164. `Iterator::next` is pure in the type-system sense; the cursor advance across invocations (via consume-and-return) does not violate purity. (§12.14.3)
014-165. Iterators must not perform externally observable I/O. (§12.14.3)
014-166. Externally-driven sequences belong to the reactive system's signals (§13); iterators are for collection traversal. (§12.14.3)

## 015. Reactive Model & Principles — 41 Rules

015-1. Ordinary computation is pure and immutable; change is confined to two contexts: local mutation within a function body and the reactive system. (§13)
015-2. The reactive expression forms are `observe` and `where`. (§13)
015-3. The reactive composition constructs are `node`, `connection`, parts, `operator`, and `effect`. (§13)
015-4. A reactive graph is built declaratively from `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, `derived`, `node`, and `connection` declarations. (§13.1)
015-5. Placement syntax constructs reactive instances. (§13.1)
015-6. Composition is structural: the reactive graph's shape is known at compile time. (§13.1)
015-7. The set of instances — signals, attrs, recurrents, nodes, connections — is created at startup and fixed for the lifetime of the runtime instance. (§13.1)
015-8. Hot reload is an exception to the fixed instance set: it replaces the program source and applies a diff atomically. (§13.1)
015-9. `repeat` is the explicit dynamic-lifecycle construct: it materializes and drops keyed scopes per a runtime source. (§13.1)
015-10. Conditional activation never adds or removes instances: a gated instance is constructed unconditionally and merely frozen while inactive, never unmounted. (§13.1)
015-11. Gates govern only activation; runtime-varying existence is exclusively `repeat`'s domain. (§13.1)
015-12. Topology — the wiring among existing instances — may change at runtime. (§13.1)
015-13. A connection's `to` may be a reactive node reference that re-points over time; the connection instance persists across re-pointing. (§13.1)
015-14. Re-pointing a connection's destination creates or destroys no instance; only the wiring moves. (§13.1)
015-15. A function or cell can only yield a node that was already placed, so every node reference resolves within the statically-known instance set. (§13.1)
015-16. Endpoint typing and topology-cycle analysis are performed on the candidate topology — every node type a `to` could resolve to — not on a single frozen wiring. (§13.1)
015-17. Reactive expressions (derived declarations, attr default expressions, recurrent expressions) are pure expressions over signal, attr, recurrent, and derived values. (§13.1)
015-18. Reactive expressions contain no `mut` bindings, no loops, and no statement-level imperative constructs. (§13.1)
015-19. Imperative work needed by a reactive expression is delegated to a call of a pure function, which may use `mut` internally. (§13.1)
015-20. Signal and attr writes mark dependent cells dirty without immediate recomputation. (§13.1)
015-21. `runtime.commit()` evaluates the dirty set in topological order. (§13.1)
015-22. `runtime.commit()` advances triggered recurrent cells per their expressions in lockstep. (§13.1)
015-23. `runtime.commit()` atomically publishes the new snapshot. (§13.1)
015-24. Writes accumulate between commits; one commit processes their union. (§13.1)
015-25. derived↔derived expression cycles are forbidden. (§13.1)
015-26. Recurrent self-reference and recurrent↔recurrent cross-reference are permitted in reactive expressions. (§13.1)
015-27. A topology cycle among nodes via connections is valid only if it traverses at least one connection type satisfying the `Circularity` trait. (§13.1)
015-28. Reactive cells (signal, attr, recurrent, derived) may hold values of any type. (§13.1)
015-29. The runtime stores a cell's value directly in-cell when it fits the platform atomic word, otherwise via index-based pool storage. (§13.1)
015-30. Imperative data structures (`Vec`, `HashMap`, etc.) are first-class as values held inside reactive cells via pool storage. (§13.1)
015-31. Cell content is governed by ordinary type rules; the reactive system governs composition and propagation. (§13.1)
015-32. `fn` is pure compute and is reactive-transparent. (§13.1)
015-33. `operator` is a stateful reactive transform from cells to cells, pure with respect to outside reality. (§13.1)
015-34. `node` and `connection` carry topology; the runtime traverses the graph via `expose:`. (§13.1)
015-35. `stream` is the append-only reactive primitive for event-shaped flows. (§13.1)
015-36. `effect` is the host-interpreted bridge for outside-world alignment, using the `desired:`/`observed:` reconciliation model. (§13.1)
015-37. The reconciliation model subsumes request/response, long-lived resources, fire-and-forget, and event-stream shapes under one primitive. (§13.1)
015-38. Hosts may interpret nodes of specific types but are not obliged to use nodes for outside-world alignment. (§13.1)
015-39. A new effect type is added by declaring an `effect`; the host registers a reconciler for that effect type. (§13.1)
015-40. A new topological participant is added by declaring a `node`; the host extends its interpreter for traversed node types. (§13.1)
015-41. There is no built-in clock or tick primitive; hosts declare their own signal and write it at their own cadence: `signal tick: i64 = 0`. (§13.1.1)

## 016. Reactive Declarations (Cells) — 276 Rules

016-1. There are exactly six reactive declaration kinds — `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, `derived`, `const`, `stream` — distinguished by who controls the value and how it changes. (§13.2)
016-2. `signal`, `attr`, `recurrent`, and `derived` declare value-shaped reactive cells. (§13.2)
016-3. `stream` declares an event-sequence-shaped reactive cell. (§13.2)
016-4. `const` declares a compile-time constant. (§13.2)
016-5. `signal name: Type = initial` declares a writable reactive cell whose initial value is supplied at the declaration: `signal mouse_x: i32 = 0`. (§13.2.1)
016-6. After construction, a signal is written only through the host API. (§13.2.1)
016-7. Ductus source has no syntactic form for assigning to a signal. (§13.2.1)
016-8. Signals are reserved for externally-fed reactive inputs; they are never computed by Ductus source. (§13.2.1)
016-9. A signal may be declared only at module level or in an effect's `observed:` block; it may not be declared in a node body, connection body, `repeat` template, or operator body. (§13.2.1)
016-10. A module-level signal is one cell shared program-wide; all references read the same cell. (§13.2.1)
016-11. Signal semantics — host-written, not source-assignable, reactive — are identical at both declaration sites: module level and effect `observed:`. (§13.2.1)
016-12. A signal's scope determines only instance multiplicity and how the host addresses the signal when writing. (§13.2.1)
016-13. `attr` declares a writable reactive cell that is per-instance of its enclosing node or connection type. (§13.2.2)
016-14. Attrs are written only through the host API or at placement time. (§13.2.2)
016-15. An attr with a default (`attr name: Type = default`) may be overridden at placement but need not be: `attr method: string = "GET"`. (§13.2.2)
016-16. An attr without a default (`attr name: Type`) must receive a value at every placement of the enclosing type: `attr url: string`. (§13.2.2)
016-17. Omitting a defaultless attr's value at placement is a compile error. (§13.2.2)
016-18. A required attr's placement value may come via the attribute clause, a flag, or the `/expr` slot when the attr is the type's `default attr`. (§13.2.2)
016-19. A default expression is evaluated to provide the initial value when an instance is constructed without an explicit value for that attr. (§13.2.2)
016-20. An attr default may reference previously-declared attrs of the same enclosing node or connection type; declaration order is significant: `attr resonance: f32 = cutoff_hz / 1000.0`. (§13.2.2)
016-21. An attr default may be a constant expression, an expression over other declared attrs, or an expression over signals visible in scope. (§13.2.2)
016-22. At placement, a defaulted attr may be overridden by supplying a value: `Filter f1 | cutoff_hz=500.0`. (§13.2.2)
016-23. A defaultless attr's cell receives the placement-supplied value at construction and is reactive from then on, exactly as if the value were a default. (§13.2.2)
016-24. A node or connection type may designate one attr as its positional default by prefixing the declaration with `default`: `default attr message: string`. (§13.2.2.1)
016-25. A `default attr` is a regular attr in every respect (writable, overridable at placement, may carry a default value) plus being the target of positional `/expr` at placement. (§13.2.2.1)
016-26. The positional and named forms are equivalent and interchangeable: `Log /"Hello World"` is `Log | message="Hello World"`. (§13.2.2.1)
016-27. At most one `default attr` per type; declaring two is a compile error. (§13.2.2.1)
016-28. Only `attr` declarations may carry the `default` marker; `recurrent`, `derived`, `const`, and `signal` cannot. (§13.2.2.1)
016-29. `/expr` binds the placed type's default attr uniformly for nodes and connections: `Drives/0.8: some_car`. (§13.2.2.1)
016-30. A connection's destination is supplied separately in the placement body; it is not an attr and is never targeted by `/expr`. (§13.2.2.1)
016-31. `derived name: Type? = expression` declares a read-only reactive value defined by an expression: `derived is_aggressive: bool = risk_tolerance > 0.7`. (§13.2.3)
016-32. A `derived`'s type annotation is optional when inferable from its expression, paralleling `recurrent` and `stream`: `derived skill_factor = f32(expertise_level) / 10.0`. (§13.2.3)
016-33. A derived re-evaluates, under the lazy-batched rules, when any signal, attr, recurrent, or derived its expression reads changes. (§13.2.3)
016-34. A derived's expression is pure: no `mut`, no loops, no statements. (§13.2.3)
016-35. Reads of signals, attrs, recurrents, and other deriveds inside a derived expression create reactive dependencies. (§13.2.3)
016-36. Derived expressions may include arithmetic and comparison operations on reactive and non-reactive values, field accesses and indexed reads, `match`, and `if`/`else`. (§13.2.3)
016-37. Function calls are permitted in derived expressions; functions are reactive-transparent. (§13.2.3)
016-38. Closure construction is permitted in derived expressions; the closure captures values at construction time. (§13.2.3)
016-39. A derived's dependency set is its provenance: the reactive cells its expression reads, including transitively through function calls. (§13.2.3)
016-40. When any cell in its dependency set changes, a derived becomes dirty and recomputes at the next commit. (§13.2.3)
016-41. A derived may be declared at module level (one shared cell), node level (per-instance), or connection level (per-instance per-connection). (§13.2.3.1)
016-42. Module-level deriveds are referenced by bare name, with no `here::` prefix. (§13.2.3.1)
016-43. A module-level derived's initial value may reference only cells visible at module scope, per the topological-init rule. (§13.2.3.1)
016-44. `recurrent[N]? name: Type? = expression` declares a per-instance reactive cell whose expression may reference its own past values. (§13.2.4)
016-45. The optional `[N]` declares the cell's self-history depth, bounding `name.past(k, fallback)` accesses. (§13.2.4)
016-46. `[N]` must be a compile-time-known positive `usize`: a literal, a `const`, or a const-generic parameter. (§13.2.4)
016-47. Omitting `[N]` defaults the self-history depth to `[1]`, leaving only `.previous` accessible. (§13.2.4)
016-48. A recurrent's name is a snake_case identifier. (§13.2.4)
