016-49. A recurrent's type annotation is optional when inferable from the expression's result type and the fallbacks given to `.previous`/`.past`. (§13.2.4)
016-50. A recurrent's expression defines the cell's value at every moment; prior self-values are readable only via `.previous(fallback)`/`.past(k, fallback)`. (§13.2.4)
016-51. `.previous(fallback)`/`.past(k, fallback)` return the fallback while the accessed cell has had fewer than `k` commits: on the first commit `counter.previous(0)` is `0`. (§13.2.4)
016-52. A fallback's type must match the accessed cell's element type. (§13.2.4)
016-53. By default, a gated-off recurrent holds its last committed value and resumes from pre-gap history on reactivation. (§13.2.4)
016-54. `@reset_on_reopen` on a recurrent clears the cell's self-history and input-history when the gating predicate transitions false→true. (§13.2.4)
016-55. After a `@reset_on_reopen` clear, the next evaluation's `.previous`/`.past` return their fallbacks, exactly as at initialization. (§13.2.4)
016-56. `@reset_on_reopen` fires only on the gate false→true transition; mere absence of triggers never resets history. (§13.2.4)
016-57. On a gated stream consumer (an operator or derived reading a stream), `@reset_on_reopen` resets the consumer's cursor on resume to the current head, discarding the gap's backlog. (§13.2.4)
016-58. While frozen, a `@reset_on_reopen` consumer of a gate-policy stream source releases its buffer hold, neither pinning the buffer nor back-pressuring producers. (§13.2.4)
016-59. `@reset_on_reopen` acts only on gated instances; on a never-gated instance it never fires and is harmless, not an error. (§13.2.4)
016-60. Triggers are implicit: a recurrent re-evaluates whenever any cell it references — other than via `.previous`/`.past` on itself — commits a new value: `counter.previous(0) + step_value` re-evaluates on `step_value`. (§13.2.4)
016-61. A recurrent whose expression contains only self-references evaluates once and freezes; this is valid behavior, not an error. (§13.2.4)
016-62. A trigger that contributes no value to the expression (e.g., a pure clock signal) requires an explicit `observe` arm. (§13.2.4)
016-63. Explicit triggers are written by making the recurrent's expression an `observe` block. (§13.2.4)
016-64. `@reset_on_reopen` is a decorator written on its own line directly above the `recurrent` declaration it modifies. (§13.2.4)
016-65. Recurrents re-evaluating in one commit advance in lockstep: each triggered recurrent's expression reads the previous-committed values of all recurrent cells, including other triggered ones. (§13.2.4.1)
016-66. All recurrents advanced in a pass commit together at the end of the pass; none sees another's just-advanced value within the pass. (§13.2.4.1)
016-67. A recurrent's own `.previous`/`.past` always return previously-committed values; the value being computed in the current commit is never visible through them. (§13.2.4.1)
016-68. Recurrents whose expressions did not re-evaluate in a pass do not advance and retain their existing values. (§13.2.4.1)
016-69. A re-evaluated recurrent's new value is a pure function of the previous-committed values and the inputs received during the pass. (§13.2.4.1)
016-70. `attr` cells change only when the host writes via `runtime.write_attr`; the runtime never advances them automatically. (§13.2.4.2)
016-71. The host cannot directly write a recurrent cell at runtime; control is indirect, via the signals and attrs the recurrent's expression reads. (§13.2.4.2)
016-72. `cell.previous(fallback)` is sugar for `cell.past(1, fallback)`. (§13.2.4.3)
016-73. Self-history `name.past(k, fb)` reads the recurrent's own value `k` commits ago, with `k` bounded by the declared `[N]` depth (default 1): `recurrent[2] fib: i32 = fib.past(2, 0) + fib.past(1, 1)`. (§13.2.4.3)
016-74. Self-history access with `k > N` is a compile error. (§13.2.4.3)
016-75. Input history: `.past(k, fb)` on any reactive cell referenced in the expression reads that input's value `k` commits ago: `input.past(2, 0.0)`. (§13.2.4.3)
016-76. The compiler allocates per-input history equal to the maximum `k` observed in `.past` calls on that input. (§13.2.4.3)
016-77. Inputs never accessed via `.past` add no history overhead. (§13.2.4.3)
016-78. Recurrent history and stream-recurrent history are the same mechanism; signals contribute commits where streams contribute events. (§13.2.4.3)
016-79. History depth `k` must be a compile-time-known positive `usize` (literal, `const`, or const-generic parameter); runtime or reactive values are rejected. (§13.2.4.3)
016-80. Each `.previous`/`.past` access is an ordinary function call. (§13.2.4.3)
016-81. Multiple `.previous`/`.past` calls on the same cell with different fallbacks are independent; each returns its own fallback when history is absent. (§13.2.4.3)
016-82. Bare references to the recurrent's own name in its expression are a compile error. (§13.2.4.3)
016-83. Non-self cells are read by bare name for their current values; `.previous`/`.past` on those names access their history. (§13.2.4.3)
016-84. A cell's commit fires downstream re-evaluation only when its value differs from its previously-committed value. (§13.2.4.4)
016-85. Writing an unchanged value to a signal does not fire its dependents. (§13.2.4.4)
016-86. Value-change gating applies uniformly to signal commits, attr writes, recurrent advancements, and derived re-evaluations. (§13.2.4.4)
016-87. A recurrent that re-evaluates to the same value as its previous commit propagates no change downstream. (§13.2.4.4)
016-88. Fire-on-every-event is expressed via a monotonically increasing counter signal; each increment changes the value, so dependents trigger every time. (§13.2.4.4)
016-89. A recurrent may be declared at module level (one shared cell), node level (per-instance), or connection level (per-instance per-connection). (§13.2.4.5)
016-90. Module-level recurrents are referenced by bare name, with no `here::` prefix. (§13.2.4.5)
016-91. Multiple recurrents may share one expression evaluation via a tuple declaration; the tuple type annotation is optional when inferable, as in the scalar form: `recurrent (name1, name2): (Type1, Type2)? = tuple_expression`. (§13.2.4.6)
016-92. A tuple-coupled declaration creates one independent cell per component, each named and typed individually. (§13.2.4.6)
016-93. The tuple expression must return a tuple of the same shape and types as the declared cells. (§13.2.4.6)
016-94. All tuple cells advance atomically from a single evaluation; shared computation runs once, not once per cell. (§13.2.4.6)
016-95. Each tuple cell has its own self-history accessor: `mean.previous(0.0)`, `variance.previous(1.0)`. (§13.2.4.6)
016-96. The optional `[N]` on a tuple-coupled recurrent applies to every cell; all share the same depth. (§13.2.4.6)
016-97. A tuple cell is read by its individual name, bare or `here::`-anchored. (§13.2.4.6)
016-98. Lockstep holds across a tuple: during evaluation each cell's `.previous`/`.past` returns its previous-committed value. (§13.2.4.6)
016-99. In the per-commit DAG, a tuple-coupled recurrent contributes one evaluation node with one output edge per cell, not independent evaluation nodes. (§13.2.4.6)
016-100. A recurrent's expression may be an `observe` expression whose arms supply trigger sets and per-arm update expressions: `on reset: 0`. (§13.2.4.7)
016-101. The active arm's expression may use `.previous`/`.past` to access the recurrent's history, like any recurrent body. (§13.2.4.7)
016-102. Trigger sets in a recurrent's `observe` may be filtered explicitly via `where`. (§13.2.4.7)
016-103. Recurrent cells may hold dynamic-size types as well as fixed-size types. (§13.2.4.8)
016-104. Dynamic-size cell types include `Vec[T]` (persistent, structural sharing), `SmallVec[T, N]` (inline up to N, then heap), and `RingBuf[T, N]` (fixed-capacity ring buffer). (§13.2.4.8)
016-105. Cell storage and cost details are delegated to the cell-types-and-storage rules of §13.12.4. (§13.2.4.8)
016-106. A recurrent expression returns a new value of the declared type; the runtime handles allocation and snapshot management transparently. (§13.2.4.8)
016-107. Source code never mutates a cell in place; the functional builder API (`.with(value)`, `+`) returns new collection values. (§13.2.4.8)
016-108. A `[N]` depth allocates `N` history slots per cell; for dynamic-size types total memory is proportional to `N * average_value_size`. (§13.2.4.8)
016-109. The compiler may warn when the static history-size product exceeds a configurable threshold. (§13.2.4.8)
016-110. `const name: Type = value` declares a compile-time constant associated with a node or connection type: `const kind: string = "@action/log"`. (§13.2.5)
016-111. A `const` is not reactive and not per-instance: its value is identical for every instance of the declaring type and fixed at compile time. (§13.2.5)
016-112. A const's right-hand side must be evaluable at compile time; it may reference other consts (same-type or top-level), literals, and any compile-time-evaluable expression. (§13.2.5.1)
016-113. A const's right-hand side may not reference reactive cells (signals, attrs, recurrents, deriveds). (§13.2.5.1)
016-114. A const occupies no reactive cell and does not participate in dirty propagation. (§13.2.5.1)
016-115. Consts may hold complex values — records, arrays, tuples, nested structures — because the single-cell reactive-buffer constraint does not apply to them. (§13.2.5.1)
016-116. Placement bodies cannot override a const; attempting to set a const at placement is a compile error. (§13.2.5.1)
016-117. The host API has no `write_const`; consts are immutable for the runtime's lifetime. (§13.2.5.1)
016-118. Inside the declaring type's reactive expressions, a const is read instance-level by bare name or anchored as `here::<const>`. (§13.2.5.2)
016-119. A const is readable through an instance reference as `<instance>.<const>` from function bodies or other instances' bodies. (§13.2.5.2)
016-120. A const is readable at type level without any instance as `<TypeName>::<const>`: `Log::type`. (§13.2.5.2)
016-121. Type-level const reads work through generic type parameters: `T::type` inside `fn tag_for[T: Action](_: T) -> string`. (§13.2.5.2)
016-122. Within a node or connection body, a const's value expression may reference previously-declared consts of the same body, in declaration order. (§13.2.5.3)
016-123. A const referencing a later-declared const of the same body is a compile error. (§13.2.5.3)
016-124. Initialization has exactly two phases: compile-time resolution of consts, then a startup pass for reactive cells. (§13.2.6)
016-125. The startup pass initializes all reactive cells in topological order over their init-time read dependencies, with no separate serialized steps for signals, attrs, recurrents, and deriveds. (§13.2.6)
016-126. Top-level consts resolve in declaration order at compile time; their values are embedded in the compiled artifact with no runtime computation. (§13.2.6)
016-127. Per-type consts resolve at compile time and are never allocated reactive cells. (§13.2.6)
016-128. The init-time dependency graph has one node per reactive cell, with edges from each cell to the cells its initial-value expression reads. (§13.2.6)
016-129. Signals evaluate their `= initial` expression during the startup pass. (§13.2.6)
016-130. An attr is initialized when its containing instance is placed. (§13.2.6)
016-131. An attr whose placement supplies an explicit value — via the attribute clause, a flag, or `/expr` for the default attr — evaluates and stores that value. (§13.2.6)
016-132. An attr with no placement-supplied value evaluates its declared `= default` expression. (§13.2.6)
016-133. An attr with neither a declared default nor a placement value is a compile error caught before startup. (§13.2.6)
016-134. Recurrents evaluate their expression for the first time during the startup pass. (§13.2.6)
016-135. During startup evaluation, `.previous(fallback)` and `.past(k, fallback)` return their fallback values, since no committed history exists yet. (§13.2.6)
016-136. An init-time read of a `Signal[T]` cell returns the cell's value at the topological-init evaluation point — a snapshot at startup. (§13.2.6)
016-137. The snapshot-at-startup read semantic also applies to attrs and signals whose initial expressions read other reactive cells. (§13.2.6)
016-138. After startup, a recurrent re-evaluates when any non-self reference in its expression commits a new value. (§13.2.6)
016-139. Deriveds evaluate their expression body during the startup pass. (§13.2.6)
016-140. `when` predicates are evaluated within the same topological order as other cells, establishing each instance's initial gate state at startup. (§13.2.6)
016-141. An instance whose `when` evaluates false at the end of startup begins inactive, its other cells holding their just-computed initial values. (§13.2.6)
016-142. An initial-value expression may read any reactive cell visible in scope, regardless of declaration kind; the topological sort resolves ordering with no artificial kind-order constraint. (§13.2.6)
016-143. Init-time dependency cycles are compile errors; init time has no notion of a previous commit to break them. (§13.2.6)
016-144. Within a node body, an attr's default or a recurrent's expression may reference previously-declared cells of the same body. (§13.2.6)
016-145. The compiler may permit forward references within a body when the init dependency graph is well-defined. (§13.2.6)
016-146. At type-declaration time, attr defaults and recurrent expressions may reference same-instance cells (bare name or `here::X`), same-type consts, module-level cells, and compile-time-evaluable expressions. (§13.2.6)
016-147. Cross-instance references are resolved only at placement time, never at type declaration. (§13.2.6)
016-148. A recurrent's self-history fallback expressions (`.previous(fallback)`, `.past(k, fallback)`) are evaluated in the same reference context as the recurrent's own expression. (§13.2.6)
016-149. A trap during any initial evaluation (signal initializer, attr default, first recurrent commit, initial derived evaluation) aborts the process; startup traps have no recovery path. (§13.2.6)
016-150. An initial value referencing a cell on another instance adds cross-instance edges to the init dependency graph. (§13.2.6)
016-151. Init cycles spanning instances are compile errors at the same severity as within-instance init cycles, identifying the participating instances and cells. (§13.2.6)
016-152. Ductus source has no syntactic form for assigning to a signal, attr, recurrent, derived, or const after declaration; source expressions only read them. (§13.2.7)
016-153. Host-driven writes occur only through `runtime.write_signal`, `runtime.write_attr`, and `runtime.transaction`. (§13.2.7)
016-154. The host cannot directly write recurrents, deriveds, or consts at runtime; influence is indirect via signals and attrs. (§13.2.7)
016-155. Placement-time initial values may set attrs and recurrents; consts are not settable at placement. (§13.2.7)
016-156. The runtime itself writes a derived's output cell when it evaluates the derived's expression. (§13.2.7)
016-157. The runtime itself commits a recurrent's computed value at the end of the commit cycle. (§13.2.7)
016-158. The no-source-level-write rule applies uniformly to all six declaration kinds. (§13.2.7)
016-159. `Cell[T]` is the umbrella type over all reactive cells; the value cells are the concrete types `Signal[T]`, `Derived[T]`, and `Recurrent[T, N]`, whose value type is `T`. (§13.2.8)
016-160. An `attr` is a placement-written `Signal[T]`: its value is supplied by the placing parent (§13.8.2), reference-passable as a read-only `Signal[T]`. (§13.2.8)
016-161. Streams are reactive cells but are not value cells (not `Signal[T]`/`Derived[T]`/`Recurrent[T, N]`). (§13.2.8)
016-162. `Cell[T]` is the umbrella over all reactive cells: `Signal[T]`, `Derived[T]`, `Recurrent[T, N]`, and `Stream[T]`. (§13.2.8)
016-163. `Signal[T]` is a first-class type usable in parameter positions, return types, and generic arguments. (§13.2.8)
016-164. `signal X = init` declares a host-writable `Signal[T]`, written via `runtime.write_signal`. (§13.2.8)
016-165. `derived X = expr` declares a `Derived[T]` whose value the runtime keeps consistent with its inputs. (§13.2.8)
016-166. `recurrent[N]? X: T = expression` declares a `Recurrent[T, N]` with self-history via `.previous(fallback)` and `.past(k, fallback)`. (§13.2.8)
016-167. The keyword `signal` and the type `Signal[T]` both name the host-writable value cell (`attr` is also a `Signal[T]`); `derived` and `recurrent` are the distinct types `Derived[T]` and `Recurrent[T, N]`. (§13.2.8)
016-168. An operator value parameter is typed `Cell[T]`, binding to any reactive value cell at instantiation and allocating internal state tied to that cell. (§13.2.8)
016-169. An operator's value output is a computed `Derived[T]`; the return type names the concrete produced type, not an umbrella. (§13.2.8)
016-170. A function parameter declared `x: T` receives a cell argument's current value, with reactive transparency. (§13.2.8)
016-171. A function parameter declared `s: Cell[T]` receives the cell reference itself. (§13.2.8)
016-172. Value-vs-reference cell passing has no call-site syntactic difference; resolution is by the function's declared parameter type. (§13.2.8)
016-173. A `Cell[T]` received as a parameter is read-only; no source-level form writes through a cell reference. (§13.2.8)
016-174. Generic functions and operators abstract over the value type via `Cell[T]`: `operator passthrough[T](source: Cell[T]) -> Cell[T]`. (§13.2.8)
016-175. Standard trait bounds apply to the value type: `Cell[T: Numeric]` requires `T` to satisfy `Numeric`. (§13.2.8)
016-176. Field access on a value cell holding a record (e.g. `Signal[Record]`) inside a derived projects the field, but the consumer re-evaluates whenever the record value changes in any field. (§13.2.8)
016-177. A reactive composite is a record, tuple, or fixed-array binding whose fields or elements are independently reactive. (§13.2.9)
016-178. Reactive composites are constructed in any reactive declaration context by binding individual fields or elements to reactive sources, static values, or reactive expressions. (§13.2.9.1)
016-179. A record literal mixing reactive and static fields forms a reactive composite: `derived A = PeakResult(some_property: signal_a, some_regular_property: 15)`. (§13.2.9.1)
016-180. A tuple literal mixing reactive and static elements forms a reactive composite: `derived t = (signal_a, 15, signal_b)`. (§13.2.9.1)
016-181. A fixed-array literal mixing reactive and static elements forms a reactive composite: `derived arr: f32[4] = [signal_a, signal_b, 0.0, signal_c]`. (§13.2.9.1)
016-182. Reactive-composite forms apply equally in `attr` declarations on node and connection instances. (§13.2.9.1)
016-183. Reactive-composite use in `signal` and `recurrent` declarations is constrained by their host-write and expression-evaluation semantics respectively. (§13.2.9.1)
016-184. A reactive composite is a structural grouping with no outer cell of its own. (§13.2.9.2)
016-185. When a constituent cell updates, only that field is dirty; consumers reading other fields through the same composite are not invalidated. (§13.2.9.2)
016-186. A composite field bound to a bare reactive name (signal/attr/derived/recurrent) becomes an alias to that cell; no new cell is allocated. (§13.2.9.3)
016-187. A composite field bound to a reactive expression becomes an implicit derived cell. (§13.2.9.3)
016-188. A composite field bound to a literal or compile-time constant is static: no cell, embedded constant. (§13.2.9.3)
016-189. A composite field bound to a non-reactive value expression is static, evaluated once at startup. (§13.2.9.3)
016-190. An aliased field is its target cell for all purposes — cell identity, hot reload, reload-time type changes: with `some_property: signal_a`, `A.some_property` is `signal_a`. (§13.2.9.3)
016-191. An implicit derived field allocates a fresh cell whose ID is its composite-field path, with dependency edges added to the graph specification: `some_property: signal_a * 2 + signal_b`. (§13.2.9.3)
016-192. Implicit derived field expressions evaluate under rules identical to an explicit `derived` declaration of the same path. (§13.2.9.3)
016-193. Static field values are embedded in the composite's lowered representation and contribute no cell entries to the graph specification. (§13.2.9.3)
016-194. Composite cell paths use `<binding>.<field_name>` for records and `<binding>.<index>` for tuples and fixed arrays: `A.some_property`, `t.0`, `arr.3`. (§13.2.9.4)
016-195. Aliased fields contribute no new cell entries to the graph specification; the alias target's existing entry is referenced and hot-reload identity follows the target. (§13.2.9.4)
016-196. The composite binding name itself (`A`, `t`, `arr`) is a naming prefix, not a cell; it does not appear as a standalone cell in the graph specification. (§13.2.9.4)
016-197. Composite field access reads the corresponding cell, or returns the embedded constant for static fields: `let r: i32 = A.some_regular_property` yields `15`. (§13.2.9.5)
016-198. Whole-composite reads — passing to a composite-typed parameter, returning from a function, or binding to a composite-typed `let` — do not allocate a snapshot. (§13.2.9.5)
016-199. Field reads of a composite-typed parameter resolve through to the caller's underlying cells: in `fn report(p: PeakResult)` called as `report(A)`, `p.some_property` is `A.some_property`. (§13.2.9.5)
016-200. A `let` whose declared or inferred type is the composite's type aliases the same underlying cells; reads through it resolve to live current cell values, not a binding-time snapshot: `let q = p`. (§13.2.9.6)
016-201. Standard scalar auto-deref still applies to single-cell reads from a composite: `let v: f32 = A.some_property`. (§13.2.9.6)
016-202. A composite binding names runtime-held cells, not stack-owned data, so multiple live aliases to the same composite may coexist without violating single ownership. (§13.2.9.6)
016-203. A materialized composite is a concrete record/tuple/array instance subject to standard ownership rules from the point of materialization on. (§13.2.9.6)
016-204. Reactive composites stay live across Ductus source except at exactly three materialization boundaries: non-reactive collection storage, FFI/host handoff, and serialization/persistence. (§13.2.9.7)
016-205. Pushing a composite into a non-reactive container stores a concrete snapshot of current per-field values; later cell changes do not propagate to the stored element: `vec.push(A)`. (§13.2.9.7)
016-206. Any value crossing into the host via the Host API is materialized; host code never sees reactive cells. (§13.2.9.7)
016-207. Hot-reload state save, debug dumps, and any explicit serialization path materialize composites to concrete values. (§13.2.9.7)
016-208. The `with` expression applies to reactive composites without syntactic change; overrides in a reactive declaration context follow the per-field binding rules: `derived A2 = A with some_other_property: signal_c`. (§13.2.9.8)
016-209. A `with` result is a new reactive composite binding with its own per-field reactive shape; the base composite is unchanged. (§13.2.9.8)
016-210. Each `with`-produced binding has its own cell IDs for any fields that contribute cells. (§13.2.9.8)
016-211. A `with` override RHS is interpreted (alias, implicit derived, or static) by the binding form receiving the result, not by RHS syntax alone. (§13.2.9.8)
016-212. A plain `let` of a `with` result that does not flow into a reactive declaration yields a concrete value, reading reactive RHS cells at evaluation time. (§13.2.9.8)
016-213. Reactive composites are data-only: no placement, no node/connection topology participation, no lifecycle beyond their declaration, and no `recurrent` or behavioral content. (§13.2.9.9)
016-214. A reactive composite's type is the underlying record, tuple, or fixed-array type: `PeakResult`, `(f32, i32, f32)`, `f32[4]`. (§13.2.9.10)
016-215. There is no `reactive` type qualifier; the type system does not distinguish reactive composites from non-reactive values of the same type. (§13.2.9.10)
016-216. Reactivity is a property of the binding (recorded in the graph specification) and its per-field cells, not of the type. (§13.2.9.10)
016-217. A function over a composite type works identically with a reactive binding or a concrete value; live-vs-snapshot access is determined by the caller's context, not the function signature. (§13.2.9.10)
016-218. A node or connection type is carried as a value via the `Type[...]` meta-type, the mechanism for an attr template slot deferring which kind a receiver places. (§13.2.10)
016-219. Node and connection instances are never values; the type value is the storable stand-in. (§13.2.10)
016-220. A `Type[...]` slot is filled by naming a type in value position, and the receiving node later places it: `ItemHost host | item_type=PostItem`. (§13.2.10)
016-221. A `Type[...]` slot's constraint is an ordinary trait bound that doubles as the instantiation contract: the receiver may feed exactly the inputs the bound trait declares as required members. (§13.2.10)
016-222. A `Type[...]` value carries no partial application; shipping a pre-configured node requires defining or parameterizing a type that bakes the configuration in. (§13.2.10)
016-223. Any node or connection type satisfying constraint `C` is a valid value for a `Type[C]` slot. (§13.2.10.1)
016-224. A bare `Type[Node]` admits any node type but is not placeable, since it carries no input contract. (§13.2.10.1)
016-225. A `Type[SomeTrait]` whose trait declares required attrs/cells (and, for connections, endpoints) is placeable against exactly that contract. (§13.2.10.1)
016-226. `default attr` plays no special role in `Type[...]` slots; a receiver may set a placed type's default attr with the `/expr` form when the constraint guarantees it exists. (§13.2.10.1)
016-227. A `Type[...]` value held in an attr is a type, not an allocated instance; cells are allocated only when the receiving node places it. (§13.2.10.2)
016-228. Placing a held type produces cells with paths under the receiving node's path (e.g., `host.item_type.<field>`), or one set per iteration when placed under a loop or `repeat`. (§13.2.10.2)
016-229. Dropping the receiving node drops the cells of the types it placed. (§13.2.10.2)
016-230. A `Type[...]` attr is an attr-shaped singular template slot, not a child-placement mechanism; cardinality, list semantics, and per-instance scoping belong to `parts:` with placement-time children and keyed scopes. (§13.2.10.2)
016-231. A `Type[...]` value's structure cannot be read or evaluated in user expressions beyond what its constraint guarantees; it is consumed by receivers that place it. (§13.2.10.3)
016-232. A `Type[...]` attr cannot be `mut` and cannot be written from Ductus source after the attr is set. (§13.2.10.3)
016-233. References a placed type captures (e.g., to the receiver's exposed attrs via its placement name) are bound by reference and re-read on per-commit re-evaluation. (§13.2.10.3)
016-234. Cell allocation for a placed type happens once at placement, preserving the static node set of the static-graph rule. (§13.2.10.3)
016-235. Generic constraints on a `Type[...]` slot behave as standard generic bounds. (§13.2.10.3)
016-236. A value conditional may select among `Type[C]` values of the same slot type — `match` yielding a `Type[C]` chooses one type, which the receiver places once, unlike `given`, which builds every arm's subtree. (§13.2.10.3)
016-237. `observe` is a reactive expression form that selects an active arm by which trigger most recently fired and evaluates that arm's expression reactively. (§13.2.11)
016-238. An observe arm is an `on` clause listing one or more trigger cells, an optional `where` filter, and a colon followed by the arm expression: `on T3 where C: expr_filtered`. (§13.2.11.1)
016-239. A `default:` arm has no `on` clause; its expression supplies the observe's value while no `on` arm has yet activated. (§13.2.11.1)
016-240. All observe arm expressions must produce the same type, as in `match` expressions. (§13.2.11.1)
016-241. An observe expression's value is a `Cell[T]` whose concrete reactive type (a value cell or `Stream[T]`) is determined by the use context. (§13.2.11.1)
016-242. An `observe` expression is written as `observe:` followed by an indented block of `on`-clause arms and an optional `default:` arm. (§13.2.11.1)
016-243. An arm becomes a selection candidate when any cell in its `on` trigger set commits a new value (signal) or emits an event (stream). (§13.2.11.2)
016-244. An arm's candidacy is filtered by its `where` clause when present. (§13.2.11.2)
016-245. When multiple arms are candidates in the same commit, the first candidate in declaration order wins, mirroring `match` semantics. (§13.2.11.2)
016-246. The winning arm becomes the active arm; a subsequent commit selecting a different arm changes the active arm. (§13.2.11.2)
016-247. While an arm is active, its expression is fully reactive: any referenced cell is dependency-tracked and re-evaluates the arm — without requiring the `on` trigger to re-fire. (§13.2.11.3)
016-248. An active arm's `on` trigger is also one of its reactive references, so re-firing the trigger re-evaluates the arm. (§13.2.11.3)
016-249. When a different arm activates, the previous arm's references stop being tracked and the new arm's references become active. (§13.2.11.3)
016-250. An observe's value can change in a commit where no `on` trigger fires; arm selection and intra-arm reactivity are independent concerns. (§13.2.11.3)
016-251. A parenthesized multi-cell trigger set activates its arm when ANY listed cell fires — logical OR: `on (T1, T2, T3): expr`. (§13.2.11.4)
016-252. All cells of a multi-cell trigger set are also reactive references of the arm while it is active. (§13.2.11.4)
016-253. A `default:` arm, when present, must be the last arm in declaration order; a `default:` arm preceding an `on` arm is a compile error. (§13.2.11.5)
016-254. In a signal context, the `default:` arm is required when every `on` arm's trigger set consists entirely of stream cells. (§13.2.11.5)
016-255. The `default:` arm is optional when at least one `on` arm has a signal in its trigger set. (§13.2.11.5)
016-256. A signal's initial value counts as its first emission for observe arm selection. (§13.2.11.5)
016-257. At startup, the first signal-triggered arm in declaration order activates and supplies the observe's value. (§13.2.11.5)
016-258. In a stream context, the `default:` arm is always optional; the stream may begin empty. (§13.2.11.5)
016-259. In a value-cell context — a `signal`, `derived`, or `recurrent` binding — an observe produces the matching value-cell type (`Signal[T]`, `Derived[T]`, or `Recurrent[T, N]`). (§13.2.11.6)
016-260. In a stream context, an observe produces the concrete stream type, `RingStream[T, N]` or `GateStream[T, N]`, per the stream context's policy and capacity. (§13.2.11.6)
016-261. Type mismatch across observe arms is a compile error. (§13.2.11.6)
016-262. `observe` may be the RHS of a `derived`, `signal`, `recurrent`, `recurrent[N] stream`, or `stream` declaration. (§13.2.11.7)
016-263. `observe` may appear as a sub-expression inside a larger reactive expression, and anywhere a `Cell[T]` value is valid. (§13.2.11.7)
016-264. `observe` may be a function-call argument; its reactive dependencies propagate through the reactive-transparent call site. (§13.2.11.7)
016-265. Inside a recurrent declaration, observe arm expressions may access the enclosing recurrent's self-history via `.previous(fallback)` / `.past(k, fallback)`. (§13.2.11.7)
016-266. An arm's `on` clause may carry a trailing `where` filter restricting activation: `on tick where counter.previous(0) < 100: counter.previous(0) + 1`. (§13.2.11.8)
016-267. The arm-level `where` is the general stream filter, producing a filtered trigger `T where C` that the arm treats as an ordinary trigger cell. (§13.2.11.8)
016-268. `T where C` emits only when `T` itself fires AND `C` evaluates true at that moment, sampling cells `C` references at their current values. (§13.2.11.8)
016-269. A change to a cell referenced by `C` between `T` emissions does not make the filtered trigger emit. (§13.2.11.8)
016-270. An active arm whose `where` later evaluates false stays active; `where` gates selection at trigger-emission moments, not continued activeness. (§13.2.11.8)
016-271. An active arm is supplanted only when a different arm's (possibly filtered) trigger emits and that arm wins declaration-order selection. (§13.2.11.8)
016-272. `Derived[T]` is the type produced by a `derived` declaration: a reactive value computation with no self-history. (§13.2.8)
016-273. `Recurrent[T, N]` is the type produced by a `recurrent[N]` declaration: a reactive value computation carrying `N` steps of self-history. (§13.2.8)
016-274. `Derived[T]` is the degenerate zero-history case of `Recurrent[T, N]` (`N = 0`). (§13.2.8)
016-275. A reactive value expression combining one or more reactive cells always produces a `Derived[T]`. (§13.2.8)
016-276. Value-reading operator and function parameters are typed `Cell[T]` (the umbrella); a `Stream[T]` has no current value, so it is excluded at the read site rather than by the signature. (§13.2.8)

## 017. Nodes & Parts — 260 Rules

017-1. A node is a reactive entity: it holds values (attrs, recurrents), computes values (deriveds), and communicates with other nodes through typed connections. (§13.3.0)
017-2. Each node type is a nominal type whose body declares its members; each placement of a node type creates an instance with its own cells. (§13.3.0)
017-3. Containment places sub-nodes inside a parent as parts; the parent owns the parts and their lifetimes are bound to the parent's. (§13.3.0)
017-4. Connections are typed directed links carrying their own reactive content, not passive pointers. (§13.3.0)
017-5. The reactive graph is the running structure of all node instances and the connections between them; once constructed, its shape is fixed. (§13.3.0)
017-6. Reactivity does not separate records from nodes — a record's fields can themselves be reactive (a reactive composite); placement and structure do. (§13.3.0)
017-7. A record is a value that can exist anywhere in a program; a node exists only as a placed graph instance with its own graph identity, topology, lifecycle, and runtime-managed per-instance cells. (§13.3.0)
017-8. A node type is declared `node TypeName[GenericParams]?:` with an indented body. (§13.3.1)
017-9. A node body may contain `satisfies`, `parts:`, `incoming:`, `outgoing:`, `when:`, `expose:`, `const`, `attr`, `default attr`, `recurrent[N]?`, `derived`, `stream`, and `effects:` items. (§13.3.1)
017-10. All node body items are optional; a node with no attrs, deriveds, parts, or connections is legal. (§13.3.1)
017-11. A node has at most one `default attr` (positional default): `default attr name: Type = default`. (§13.3.1)
017-12. A node declares trait conformance with a `satisfies` clause: `satisfies Drivable`. (§13.3.2)
017-13. Trait methods for a node are implemented in `fulfill` blocks: `fulfill Displayable for Driver:`. (§13.3.2)
017-14. Functions on node instances are free functions taking the node type as a parameter, callable via uniform call syntax. (§13.3.2)
017-15. The optional `parts:` clause constrains placement-time children: `parts: [dynamic] Type1 [cardinality]?, ...`. (§13.3.3)
017-16. A node with no `parts:` clause accepts child instances of any node type. (§13.3.3)
017-17. Without a `parts:` clause, the node body has no bulk access to children — only named access to children the body itself can see. (§13.3.3)
017-18. With a `parts:` clause, only children whose types appear in the listed set are accepted. (§13.3.3)
017-19. Part cardinality is enforced at placement: `parts: Oscillator+, Filter [=1], Amplifier?`. (§13.3.3)
017-20. The `parts:` clause places no instances itself; it is the node's caller-facing part signature, bounding only what a caller supplies. (§13.3.3)
017-21. A node's own emitted placements (compile-time `for` or `repeat`) are never counted against `parts:` and never appear in the `parts` namespace. (§13.3.3)
017-22. Self-recursion is legal: a node may have parts of its own type when `parts:` is omitted or its own type appears in the clause. (§13.3.3)
017-23. Self-recursive placements terminate: each placement is an explicit user act, and the compiler walks finite placement trees, not type recursions. (§13.3.3)
017-24. A part type with no sigil and no bracket has cardinality `0..` (zero or more, unlimited). (§13.3.3.1)
017-25. The `?` sigil means `0..=1` (optional): `Amplifier?`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-26. The `+` sigil means `1..` (at least one): `Oscillator+`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-27. The `!` sigil means exactly one, shorthand for `[=1]`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-28. The bracket form `[=N]` means exactly N: `Filter [=1]`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-29. The bracket form `[N..=M]` means between N and M, inclusive on both ends. (§13.3.3.1)
017-30. The bracket form `[N..]` means at least N with no upper bound. (§13.3.3.1)
017-31. The bracket form `[..=M]` means up to M with lower bound 0. (§13.3.3.1)
017-32. A part type carries at most one cardinality specifier — sigil or bracket, not both; duplicate specifiers are a compile error. (§13.3.3.1)
017-33. Sigils attach directly to the type name with no intervening whitespace: `Part?`, `Part+`, `Part!`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-34. Bracket forms permit an optional space before the bracket: `Part[=1]` and `Part [=1]` are both valid. (§13.3.3.1)
017-35. The `dynamic` prefix on a listed type marks runtime-varying supply mode, orthogonal to count: `parts: Header, dynamic Post`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-36. `dynamic` is mutually exclusive with every cardinality specifier: `dynamic Post+` ✗. (§13.3.3.1)
017-37. Runtime cardinality checks do not exist in the language. (§13.3.3.1)
017-38. An unmarked part type is static: supplied only by explicitly written placements, with the count known per placement site at compile time. (§13.3.3.1)
017-39. A `dynamic` part type may additionally be fed by a caller's `repeat`. (§13.3.3.1)
017-40. The `dynamic` marker has the same meaning and exclusivity rule in `incoming:` and `outgoing:` clauses. (§13.3.3.1)
017-41. For a static part type, `parts.<NodeType>` is a read-only array of node references whose length is an implicit const-generic fixed per placement site. (§13.3.3.2)
017-42. The declared cardinality supplies compile-time bounds `min..max` that every placement site's static supply must satisfy. (§13.3.3.2)
017-43. Indexing `parts.<NodeType>[i]` is legal iff `i < min_cardinality`: under `parts: Oscillator+`, `parts.Oscillator[0]` ✓, `[1]` ✗. (§13.3.3.2)
017-44. `for o in parts.<NodeType>:` always works, unrolling as a fixed-extent array with each site's actual length. (§13.3.3.2)
017-45. Whether codegen emits straight-line copies or a counted loop over the known list is an implementation choice. (§13.3.3.2)
017-46. `parts.<NodeType>` is in placement order — a stable filter of the caller's written placement sequence that selects, never reorders. (§13.3.3.2)
017-47. Every projection of the placement sequence in the language preserves placement order. (§13.3.3.2)
017-48. Elements of `parts.<NodeType>` are references, so the value is transient under the ordinary borrow rules: usable in loops, indexing, and calls; storable nowhere. (§13.3.3.2)
017-49. For a `dynamic` part type, `parts.<NodeType>` is a reactive cell, not an array; none of the static array forms apply. (§13.3.3.2)
017-50. There is no whole-namespace parts iteration: bare `parts` is an exposition reference, not a value, and per-type access is the only body-side bulk form. (§13.3.3.2)
017-51. A node type may emit child instances directly via a compile-time `for` written in its `expose:` block; the exposition is the only place structural output is emitted. (§13.3.3.3)
017-52. A type-emitted `for` body is a placement-body block: any number of part and/or connection placements per iteration, under the ordinary clause-ordering and self-delimiting rules. (§13.3.3.3)
017-53. A type-emitted `for` is compile-time-unrolled; its placements become children of the type itself, materialized by every instance at instantiation: `for i in 0..VOICE_COUNT: Oscillator | freq=base_freq(i)`. (§13.3.3.3)
017-54. Type-emitted placements appear at the loop's written position in the exposition, with each iteration's loop variable substituted at compile time. (§13.3.3.3)
017-55. The iterable of a type-emitted `for` must be compile-time-known; a runtime iterable is a compile error pointing at the iterable, with no new diagnostic class. (§13.3.3.3)
017-56. A const-generic parameter may drive the emission count, making multiplicity a property of each instantiation: `node OscBank[const N: usize]:` then `OscBank[16] sixteen_bank`. (§13.3.3.3)
017-57. A node's own emissions need no `parts:` entry; a type may emit children while declaring no `parts:` clause at all. (§13.3.3.3)
017-58. Type-emitted children are internals: never counted against `parts:` cardinality and never in the `parts` namespace, which holds caller-supplied children only. (§13.3.3.3)
017-59. Across hot reload, a changed compile-time iterable follows path-based cell identity: unchanged fully-qualified paths keep state, new loop indices allocate fresh, dropped indices are released per the standard removal rule. (§13.3.3.3)
017-60. A type-emitted `for` expands once at type elaboration and applies uniformly to every instance. (§13.3.3.3)
017-61. A placement-body `for` expands at each placement site and is caller supply: its placements count against the receiving type's `parts:` cardinality and appear in `parts.<NodeType>`. (§13.3.3.3)
017-62. A type-emitted `for` may place connections sourced from the enclosing instance; they are self-sourced — exempt from `outgoing:`, subject to endpoint typing, and engaging at their written position in the unrolled sequence. (§13.3.3.3)
017-63. A caller supplies a `dynamic` part type with `repeat` in the placement body: `repeat post in posts keyed by post.id: Post / post`. (§13.3.3.4)
017-64. Feeding an unmarked (static) part type with a `repeat` is a compile error at the feeding site, naming the receiving clause. (§13.3.3.4)
017-65. For a `dynamic` type, `parts.<NodeType>` is a reactive cell whose value is the current keyed, ordered collection of supplied children, updating as children mount and dismount. (§13.3.3.4)
017-66. The dynamic parts cell is in source order: the written interleaving, with each feeding `repeat`'s scopes expanded in place. (§13.3.3.4)
017-67. The dynamic parts cell's raw value is transient under the borrow rules: it cannot be bound, stored, or returned. (§13.3.3.4)
017-68. The dynamic parts cell is consumed in exactly two ways: operators for values, `repeat` for structure. (§13.3.3.4)
017-69. An operator over the dynamic cell yields an ordinary reactive value, storable and readable: `derived titles = parts.Post |> map(fn(p): p.title)`. (§13.3.3.4)
017-70. The per-element closure of an operator over the cell receives each element reference transiently and must produce plain values. (§13.3.3.4)
017-71. `repeat` over the dynamic cell is ordinary `repeat` over an ordinary reactive collection source: `repeat p in parts.Post:`. (§13.3.3.4)
017-72. Keys are carried: each dynamically supplied element brings the key its feeding `repeat` derived, so scope identity is stable end-to-end with no `keyed by` written. (§13.3.3.4)
017-73. The carried key is the first applicable path in standard key derivation; an explicit `keyed by` keeps its ordinary precedence. (§13.3.3.4)
017-74. An explicitly written (static) placement of a `dynamic` type carries a stable, path-derived placement-site key. (§13.3.3.4)
017-75. `for` cannot iterate a dynamic parts cell: `for` is compile-time and the set is not a compile-time fact. (§13.3.3.4)
017-76. Indexing a dynamic parts cell is rejected: `parts.<NodeType>[i]` ✗ — a positional index into a keyed, changing set is not a stable identity. (§13.3.3.4)
017-77. Key-addressing a dynamic parts cell is rejected: `parts.<NodeType>[key]` ✗ — keys belong to the supplier, and the receiving type cannot know or name them. (§13.3.3.4)
017-78. A bulk `parts` reference in `expose:` carries dynamic children like static ones: each feeding `repeat`'s scopes appear at the repeat's written position, mounting and dismounting under Model B. (§13.3.3.4)
017-79. When every supplier at a placement site is provably static, the compiler may unroll that site's `repeat` and operator applications; the developer still writes against the dynamic contract. (§13.3.3.4)
017-80. The same marker, cell model, and consumption rules apply to `dynamic` connection types in `incoming:` and `outgoing:`. (§13.3.3.4)
017-81. `incoming:` and `outgoing:` list the connection types in which instances may participate as endpoints, with optional cardinality: `incoming: [dynamic] ConnType [cardinality]?, ...`. (§13.3.4)
017-82. `incoming` connections target the node (it is the `to` endpoint); `outgoing` connections originate from it (it is the `from` endpoint). (§13.3.4)
017-83. `incoming:` bounds what others may direct at the node; `outgoing:` bounds what a caller placing the node may originate from it. (§13.3.4)
017-84. Connection cardinality syntax is identical to parts cardinality, and bare defaults to unlimited `0..`: `outgoing: Drives [=1], MaintainedBy?`. (§13.3.4)
017-85. A connection belongs to a node's incoming set as a static fact when both its presence and its destination are fixed at compile time. (§13.3.4)
017-86. Membership stops being a static fact in exactly two ways: the connection is materialized per `repeat` key (existence varies), or its destination is a reactive reference or `Handle` (target varies). (§13.3.4)
017-87. An unmarked (static) `incoming:` type admits only connections whose membership is a static fact. (§13.3.4)
017-88. Static incoming cardinality is checked by exact local arithmetic: the compiler counts, per instance, the statically present caller-placed and self-sourced static-destination connections directed at it. (§13.3.4)
017-89. A self-sourced connection into an own part counts at the part and, being always present, satisfies the part's lower bound. (§13.3.4)
017-90. `incoming.<ConnType>[i]` for `i` below the guaranteed minimum is backed exactly by the statically present connections. (§13.3.4)
017-91. A connection whose membership is not a static fact requires `dynamic <ConnType>` in the `incoming:` clause of every node type in its candidate envelope. (§13.3.4)
017-92. The candidate envelope is: for a dynamic destination, every type the reference's static type admits; for a repeat-materialized connection, its destination's type. (§13.3.4)
017-93. The dynamic-membership check runs at the connection placement site and names both sites on failure. (§13.3.4)
017-94. A `dynamic` incoming type carries no cardinality and has no bound to check; its membership is runtime data tracked reactively. (§13.3.4)
017-95. Caller-written `repeat` fan-out of connections sourced from a placed instance requires `outgoing: dynamic <ConnType>` on the instance's type. (§13.3.4)
017-96. A merely re-pointing destination does not dynamize the source side: the connection statically exists at its source; only its target moves. (§13.3.4)
017-97. Body access to connections is `incoming.<ConnType>` and `outgoing.<ConnType>`, bare or with the explicit anchor `here::incoming.<ConnType>`. (§13.3.4.1)
017-98. The member namespaces `parts`, `incoming`, and `outgoing` share one model, each with a static face and a dynamic face. (§13.3.4.1)
017-99. A static connection type reads as a read-only array of connection references with const-generic length bounded by the declared cardinality. (§13.3.4.1)
017-100. Indexed connection access is legal iff the index is below the guaranteed minimum: under `outgoing: Drives [=1]`, `outgoing.Drives[0]` ✓. (§13.3.4.1)
017-101. `for c in outgoing.<ConnType>:` iterates a static connection array, unrolling at compile time. (§13.3.4.1)
017-102. A `dynamic` connection type reads as a reactive cell of the current member set, consumed only by operators and `repeat`: `incoming.Send |> map(fn(c): c.gain * c.from.value)`. (§13.3.4.1)
017-103. A derived over a dynamic connection cell re-evaluates when members mount or dismount and when any member's read cells change. (§13.3.4.1)
017-104. `repeat` over a dynamic connection cell mounts one scope per current member, mounting and dismounting with it: `repeat c in incoming.Send: Ack: c.from`. (§13.3.4.1)
017-105. `incoming:` and `outgoing:` declare which connections a node participates in as an endpoint; they establish none. (§13.3.4.2)
017-106. A node type establishes self-sourced connections — every instance the `from` endpoint — by placing them inline in its structural sequence as exposition entries, directly or within an exposition-level `for` or `repeat`. (§13.3.4.2)
017-107. There is no separate clause for self-sourced connections; each is written at the position where it engages. (§13.3.4.2)
017-108. A self-sourced placement uses ordinary connection placement syntax; the source is always the enclosing instance. (§13.3.4.2)
017-109. Self-sourced destinations must be nameable from the type's scope; exactly three forms are admissible: an own part, a module-level instance, or a `Handle`-typed attr. (§13.3.4.2)
017-110. An own-part destination — a named part or `parts.X[i]` within the guaranteed minimum — is static: it counts as static supply at the part and can satisfy the part's `incoming:` lower bounds. (§13.3.4.2)
017-111. A module-level-instance destination is a fixed shared target and is static. (§13.3.4.2)
017-112. A `Handle`-typed attr destination gives per-instance parameterized wiring, configured at placement: `attr wheel: Handle[Drivable]` with entry `Drives: wheel`. (§13.3.4.2)
017-113. A self-sourced connection through a handle freezes while the handle resolves to `None`. (§13.3.4.2)
017-114. With a handle destination, membership at the destination is not a static fact: every node type the handle's type admits must declare `incoming: dynamic <ConnType>`. (§13.3.4.2)
017-115. A node type's `parts:`, `incoming:`, and `outgoing:` clauses are its caller-facing signature; its internals — every placement the type itself makes — are not bound by them and never appear in the signature namespaces. (§13.3.4.2)
017-116. Internals are checked only by connection endpoint typing and topology-cycle analysis. (§13.3.4.2)
017-117. A node is the caller of its own parts: internal wiring terminating at an own part counts against the part's `incoming:` budget, the same budget external callers draw from. (§13.3.4.2)
017-118. At a module-level destination, every placed instance of the source type contributes one connection to the target's `incoming:` accounting. (§13.3.4.2)
017-119. Destination contributions that are static facts count toward a static `incoming:` type's exact arithmetic. (§13.3.4.2)
017-120. A destination contribution that is not a static fact — source instances materialized by `repeat`, or a `Handle` destination — requires the target to declare `incoming: dynamic` for that connection type, with the error naming both sites. (§13.3.4.2)
017-121. A self-sourced connection is exempt from the source node's `outgoing:` clause, which governs caller-originated connections only. (§13.3.4.2)
017-122. A self-sourced connection is not exempt from endpoint typing: its `from`/`to` types must match the connection's declaration. (§13.3.4.2)
017-123. Self-sourced connections are a node-type concept: connection bodies remain minimal glue with no exposition of their own; a connection needing further structure is modeled with nodes. (§13.3.4.2)
017-124. The total cardinality of a node — caller-placed plus internal — is not tracked; a clause cardinality constrains only the caller-facing side, and no `[=N total]`-style annotation is specified. (§13.3.4.2)
017-125. A node may declare generic parameters in the standard `[T, U, ...]` form: `node Buffer[T: Numeric]:`. (§13.3.5)
017-126. Node generic parameters are in scope within the body's attr, recurrent, derived, const, parts, and connection declarations: `parts: BufferSlot[T]`. (§13.3.5)
017-127. Each instantiation with a different concrete type argument produces a distinct node type with its own cells, monomorphized by the standard rules. (§13.3.5)
017-128. A node body contains no `fn` declarations: nodes declare structure and reactive content; behavior lives as free functions or trait `fulfill` blocks. (§13.3.6)
017-129. A node instance is owned by the graph position that places it: placement is ownership. (§13.3.6.1)
017-130. The only ways to bring a node into the graph are placement syntax and a `repeat` declaration, both creating an owned node at a known graph path. (§13.3.6.1)
017-131. A name bound to a node — at top level, inside another node, or as a function parameter or return — holds a reference obeying ordinary borrow semantics, not the node itself. (§13.3.6.1)
017-132. A borrow cannot be placed: a node-bound name cannot be re-placed (no double placement). (§13.3.6.1)
017-133. A function cannot create or place a node: placement is a declaration-only form unavailable in function bodies, and functions only receive and return borrows. (§13.3.6.1)
017-134. A function may take and return node references, usable for reading cells and as connection endpoints, never for placing. (§13.3.6.1)
017-135. A node reference cannot be stored in a cell, record field, enum payload, or tuple. (§13.3.6.1)
017-136. The storable, non-owning designation of a node instance is `Handle[…]`; persistent wiring between instances is a connection; a node type held as a value is `Type[…]`. (§13.3.6.1)
017-137. The factory pattern is parameterizing the type (generics/const-generics) and placing at the call site: `Synth[8] my_synth`. (§13.3.6.1)
017-138. Deferring which node type is placed is done by passing a `Type[…]` value. (§13.3.6.1)
017-139. Connection and effect instances obey the same ownership rule: brought in only by the language's placement/instantiation syntax, held only by borrow, never stored as values; their types travel via `Type[…]`. (§13.3.6.1)
017-140. Operators are instantiated only via operator syntax and are carried via the structural type `operator(…) -> U`. (§13.3.6.1)
017-141. The graph-ownership rule is normative: conformant compilers enforce it at type-check time. (§13.3.6.1)
017-142. `Handle[T]` is a `Copy` value, freely placed in cells, fields, tuples, and enum payloads, that weakly designates a graph entity. (§13.3.6.2)
017-143. A handle never keeps its referent alive: no reference count, no reachability; entity lifetime is governed solely by the owning graph position or `repeat` scope. (§13.3.6.2)
017-144. A handle may outlive its referent and then resolves to absent. (§13.3.6.2)
017-145. `Handle[T]` is kind-scoped: `T` must be a node, connection, or effect type, or a trait that `requires` one of the `Node`/`Connection`/`Effect` markers. (§13.3.6.2)
017-146. There are no handles to records, tuples, or other plain values; one handles the value's home (the node or cell it lives in) instead. (§13.3.6.2)
017-147. A handle has no `.resolve()` method; resolution is transparent and type-directed by the position the handle occupies. (§13.3.6.2)
017-148. In a `Handle[T]`-typed position, a handle denotes the inert handle value; no graph entity is read and nothing becomes reactive. (§13.3.6.2)
017-149. In a read or elimination position — `match`, `?`, `!`, or where `&T` is expected — a handle denotes its resolution `Option[&T]`: `Some(&entity)` while live, `None` once gone. (§13.3.6.2)
017-150. A handle resolution read is reactive: it joins the reader's provenance and re-fires when the referent mounts or dismounts. (§13.3.6.2)
017-151. `Option[&T]` contains a borrow and is unstorable (the transient resolved view); `Handle[T]` contains no borrow and is the storable carrier. (§13.3.6.2)
017-152. A handle is produced by the prefix operator `weak` on a node/connection/effect reference: `weak some_car`. (§13.3.6.2)
017-153. Where a `Handle[T]`-typed position receives a reference directly, the `weak` coercion is inserted automatically. (§13.3.6.2)
017-154. There is no `.handle()` method and no handle sigil; `weak` is the one explicit spelling. (§13.3.6.2)
017-155. A handle is eliminated through exactly three forms, the ordinary `Option` eliminators: `match`, `?`, and `!`: `let s = target?.speed`. (§13.3.6.2)
017-156. `!` on a handle asserts liveness and is a compile error unless liveness is provable. (§13.3.6.2)
017-157. A handle is concretely a graph slot plus a generation stamp; a slot reused by a different entity fails the stamp comparison, so a stale handle resolves to `None`, never to the wrong entity. (§13.3.6.2)
017-158. Because mount and dismount flip its resolution, a handle read is a dynamic dependency. (§13.3.6.2)
017-159. A handle obtained by key from a `repeat` view designates the keyed scope, not one mount: when the same key reappears in the source, a handle that resolved `None` resolves `Some` again. (§13.3.6.2)
017-160. `expose:` declares the node type's structural output: the ordered list of placements the runtime traverses on encountering an instance of the type. (§13.3.7)
017-161. Exposition entries are node placements and connection placements; their order is semantic — the order of traversal and connection engagement. (§13.3.7)
017-162. The canonical node clause order is `satisfies` → `parts:` → `incoming:` → `outgoing:` → `when:` → `expose:` → cell declarations → `effects:`. (§13.3.7)
017-163. The `expose:` body is an ordered list of placements with the same syntax as inline child placements elsewhere. (§13.3.7.1)
017-164. A type-bulk entry exposes the full list of supplied parts of one type in placement order: `parts.SomeA`. (§13.3.7.1)
017-165. A named-part entry exposes one specific named child: `osc1`. (§13.3.7.1)
017-166. A wrapper entry places a node-internal type containing supplied parts as its own children; wrappers are declared nodes accepting children via their own `parts:` clause. (§13.3.7.1)
017-167. A connection entry places a self-sourced connection with full placement syntax — `ConnType: dest`, optional `/expr`, attribute clause, inline `when` — at its engagement position. (§13.3.7.1)
017-168. A positioning reference `outgoing.<ConnType>` (or `outgoing.<ConnType>[i]` under the guaranteed minimum) engages caller-supplied connections at that position, overriding the anchoring fallback. (§13.3.7.1)
017-169. A positioning reference carries no destination, so it never collides with the `ConnType: dest` placement shape. (§13.3.7.1)
017-170. Bare `incoming.<ConnType>` as an exposition entry is a compile error: engagement order belongs to the source's traversal, and a destination cannot reposition another node's connection. (§13.3.7.1)
017-171. The bare-`incoming.<ConnType>` exposition-entry error points at the expression-position idioms instead: `incoming.X[i].from` as a back-connection destination, or `repeat c in incoming.X: ...`. (§13.3.7.1)
017-172. Iteration entries emit type-internal structure in place: a compile-time `for` unrolls at its written position; a `repeat` mounts its keyed scopes there. (§13.3.7.1)
017-173. Lowercase-keyword iteration entries never collide with `Name:` entries; the casing rule is normative. (§13.3.7.1)
017-174. Entry kind follows name resolution: a connection type makes the entry a connection placement (destination follows), a node type a node placement (body follows), and a `Type[…]` binding follows its constraint's kind. (§13.3.7.1)
017-175. A single name never denotes both a node type and a connection type in one scope: type declarations of every kind share one namespace per scope. (§13.3.7.1)
017-176. An individual exposition entry may carry the inline `when` modifier for single-placement gating. (§13.3.7.1)
017-177. An exposition entry may be a `when` block or a `given` block; each arm body is a list of exposition entries, and the runtime exposes the active arm and freezes the rest (Model B). (§13.3.7.1)
017-178. No exposition-only control-flow syntax exists; conditional exposition reuses the general gate constructs. (§13.3.7.1)
017-179. The position directly under a `when`/`given` block header is arm position, admitting only variant labels or boolean guard expressions; connection entries appear only inside arm bodies, so arm labels never collide with `Name: dest` entries. (§13.3.7.1)
017-180. With `expose:` omitted, exposition defaults to `expose: parts`: all supplied placements in declaration order — parts and caller-placed connections in written interleaving, feeding `repeat` scopes expanded in place. (§13.3.7.2)
017-181. The default exposition covers caller-supplied content only; a type that emits internal structure necessarily writes `expose:` explicitly. (§13.3.7.2)
017-182. A no-`parts:` node with no `expose:` exposes its caller-supplied children and connections as-is via the default `expose: parts`; the exposition is empty only when no such content is supplied. (§13.3.7.2)
017-183. The reserved field `.exposition` returns the ordered list of placements — node and connection entries — the instance currently exposes: `instance.exposition`. (§13.3.7.3)
017-184. External readers and the runtime see identical exposition content. (§13.3.7.3)
017-185. Inside the node body, bare `exposition` is the same list. (§13.3.7.3)
017-186. `exposition` is read-only: its declaration is fixed by the type's `expose:` clause and the placer's supplied content, with no imperative mutation. (§13.3.7.3)
017-187. The current exposition varies at runtime only through declared constructs: gate arms flipping, `repeat` scopes mounting and dismounting, dynamic supply arriving and leaving. (§13.3.7.3)
017-188. The runtime traverses what `expose:` produces, not the `parts:` clause directly. (§13.3.7.4)
017-189. `parts:` is the supply half (accepted child types with cardinality, filled by placement-time child placements); `expose:` is the output half (the ordered structural output). (§13.3.7.4)
017-190. `parts:` exists solely to feed `expose:`: externally supplied children exist to become the node's structural output. (§13.3.7.4)
017-191. An explicit `expose:` may select a subset of the supplied parts; an omitted part has no structural output and is not a compile error. (§13.3.7.4)
017-192. Traversal proceeds in entry order: node entries by structural descent, connection entries by engagement. (§13.3.7.4)
017-193. A connection placement is an exposition entry positioned in the ordered sequence alongside node entries; its position states where in the node's structure the connection takes effect. (§13.3.7.5)
017-194. A connection entry engages when traversal reaches its position, after the entries before it: `Plays: next` after `Chord/'F#'`. (§13.3.7.5)
017-195. Presence is participation: an exposition entry's connection is reactively live whether or not traversal has reached it. (§13.3.7.5)
017-196. Effects are never exposition entries: effects are declared only in the `effects:` clause, never in `expose:`. (§13.3.7.5)
017-197. A node entry is traversed by structural descent: the runtime enters the placement and walks its exposition. (§13.3.7.6)
017-198. A connection entry is engaged: the runtime takes up the connection and begins interpreting the connected subgraph. (§13.3.7.6)
017-199. What engagement means is domain semantics carried by the specific connection type and interpreted by the runtime/host; it is not a language keyword, and the language and IR carry no sequencing flag for it. (§13.3.7.6)
017-200. One connection type may carry await-like meaning and another parallel meaning; the host interprets each connection type as it interprets the node types it traverses. (§13.3.7.6)
017-201. The language fixes only that a connection first engages when its turn comes in the structural (entry) order of the exposition — distinct from per-commit topological evaluation order. (§13.3.7.6)
017-202. Engagement is orthogonal to reactive liveness: unengaged and already-passed connections, and everything downstream, keep computing; traversal interprets the graph and does not power it. (§13.3.7.6)
017-203. Freezing comes only from explicit declared conditions — `when`/`given` gates and an unresolved dynamic destination — never implicitly from traversal position. (§13.3.7.6)
017-204. A frozen connection entry is not engaged: traversal passes over it, consistent with a gated-off exposition entry not being exposed. (§13.3.7.6)
017-205. Freezing removes an entry from engagement; engagement never affects freezing. (§13.3.7.6)
017-206. Terminology is fixed: activation refers to gate-state, engagement to traversal reaching an entry; a connection can be live yet unengaged, or frozen and unengageable. (§13.3.7.6)
017-207. The `effects:` clause is the sole site where an effect may be instantiated; module level, operator bodies, connection bodies, and function bodies are illegal hosts. (§13.3.8)
017-208. `expose:` declares topology (what the node is); `effects:` declares side effects (what it does); the two never mix. (§13.3.8)
017-209. Each `effects:` entry instantiates an effect via a `|>` pipe chain: `url |> fetch`. (§13.3.8)
017-210. An effect entry is named (`f = url |> fetch`, binding the instance value) or anonymous (`label |> log`, run for its side effect only). (§13.3.8)
017-211. A named effect's name is in scope for the whole node body regardless of clause order. (§13.3.8)
017-212. A named effect's cells are read elsewhere by flat access: `derived spinner: bool = f.in_flight`. (§13.3.8)
017-214. The `effects:` clause admits `when`, `given`, and `repeat` with effect entries in place of node placements; arm and loop bodies are lists of effect entries. (§13.3.8)
017-215. Arm headers use `:` and named effect entries use `=`, so the two never collide. (§13.3.8)
017-216. Gating freezes the inactive arm and suspends its effects (Model B). (§13.3.8)
017-217. Effects are local-only: never passed into a node from outside — there are no effect parts and no effect parameters. (§13.3.8)
017-218. A caller influences a node's effects only through data in (supplied signals/attrs/streams) and streams out (the parent consumes the child's exposed outgoing stream in the parent's own `effects:`). (§13.3.8)
017-219. One named effect may take another's observed cell as its piped input: `b = a.response |> persist`. (§13.3.8)
017-220. Operators in an effect pipe chain remain pure transformations of output; the effect itself is hosted in `effects:`. (§13.3.8)
017-221. A program-global effect lives in the `effects:` clause of a top-level node instance, since module level is not an effect host. (§13.3.8)
017-222. Instantiating an effect elsewhere in the node body — a free `f = url |> fetch` or `derived x = url |> effect` — is a compile error directing the author to `effects:`. (§13.3.8)
017-223. "Part" is a role, not a separate type: a part is a child node instance placed inside a parent node at construction time. (§13.4.0)
017-224. A part is contained within a parent instance and addressable only through that parent: `parent.osc1`, `parent.parts.Oscillator[0]`. (§13.4.0)
017-225. A node placed at module top level is an independent instance, reachable only by its module-scope name or through connections. (§13.4.0)
017-226. Guidance: a child is placed as a part when the parent conceptually owns it, when the parent’s reactive expressions aggregate over the child instances, or when the compositional structure is part of the parent’s identity. (§13.4.0)
017-227. Guidance: an instance is placed at top level when it stands on its own and participates in the graph through connections rather than containment. (§13.4.0)
017-228. A parent declares the child types it accepts via its `parts:` clause with optional cardinality; the specific instances appear via placement. (§13.4.0)
017-229. Runtime structural traversal goes through the `expose:` clause, not through `parts:` directly. (§13.4.0)
017-230. Parts the exposition does not include are not traversed by the runtime and do not contribute to structural descent. (§13.4.0)
017-231. Non-exposed parts remain queryable via the host API and addressable within the parent's own reactive expressions. (§13.4.0)
017-232. Type-bulk access `parts.<NodeType>` is available only when `<NodeType>` appears in the parent's `parts:` clause. (§13.4.1)
017-233. For a static (unmarked) part type, `parts.<Type>` is a read-only array of node references with const-generic length bounded by the declared cardinality, in placement order. (§13.4.1)
017-234. A static `parts.<Type>` array is indexable under the guaranteed minimum cardinality. (§13.4.1)
017-235. For a `dynamic` part type, `parts.<Type>` is a reactive cell consumed via operators or `repeat`. (§13.4.1)
017-236. A `dynamic` part type's membership is runtime data by declaration, tracked reactively. (§13.4.1)
017-237. Named individual access — bare `<name>`, or `paramName.<name>` from outside the node body — reaches a specific part by its placement-time name. (§13.4.1)
017-238. Part names are assigned in the placement body and are visible wherever the placement scope is known. (§13.4.1)
017-239. Named individual access is available with or without a `parts:` clause. (§13.4.1)
017-240. There is no whole-namespace (heterogeneous) access over `parts`: per-type access is the only bulk form. (§13.4.1)
017-241. A node without a `parts:` clause has no bulk parts access at all. (§13.4.1)
017-242. Inside the parent's own type body (its `derived` and `recurrent` expressions) only type-bulk access is available; placement names are not visible at the type-declaration level. (§13.4.1)
017-243. Named part access is available in function bodies receiving a specific instance: `c.osc1.output` with `c: Composite`. (§13.4.1)
017-244. Named part access is available in other instances' placement bodies that reference the named instance. (§13.4.1)
017-245. Within a placement body, lines after a part's naming may reference the just-named part by name. (§13.4.1)
017-246. Static type-bulk and named access are compile-time resolved: the compiler knows every statically supplied part's identity, type, and placement-name per site. (§13.4.1)
017-247. A function receiving the parent node as a parameter may iterate a part type with `for` via the parameter name (no implicit receiver): `for o in s.parts.Oscillator`. (§13.4.2)
017-248. The part-iteration bind has the concrete part type in each iteration. (§13.4.2)
017-249. There is no heterogeneous `for p in c.parts` over all declared part types: aggregate behavior over several part types is written per type and combined explicitly. (§13.4.2)
017-250. A uniform stored representation across part types uses `dyn Trait`, not heterogeneous parts iteration. (§13.4.2)
017-251. Part iteration is not a special construct: `c.parts.Oscillator` is a fixed-extent array, so `for` over it unrolls by the ordinary rule of §12.3.7 to one reference per declared part. (§13.4.2)
017-252. When a function called from a reactive expression iterates parts, each part reactive cell it reads contributes to the calling expression's dependency set. (§13.4.3)
017-253. When any one contributing part cell changes, the dependent derived becomes dirty. (§13.4.3)
017-254. Dependency tracking is provenance-based: reactive cells read by an expression are tracked transitively through function calls. (§13.4.3)
017-255. A node may contain at most one part of each name. (§13.4.4)
017-256. Multiple parts of the same type with different names are permitted, subject to the declared cardinality. (§13.4.4)
017-257. Statically supplied parts are not added or removed at runtime, except via hot reload. (§13.4.4)
017-258. Parts of a `dynamic` type mount and dismount with their feeding `repeat`'s key set. (§13.4.4)
017-259. A `dynamic` part type is not `for`-iterable, not indexable, and not key-addressable from the receiving body. (§13.4.4)
017-260. Operators and `repeat` are the only consumers of a `dynamic` part type. (§13.4.4)
017-261. Type-bulk and named individual access coexist on the same instance: `c.parts.Oscillator[0]` (indexed, legal under `+`) and `c.parts.Filter[0]` (legal under `[=1]`) alongside named `c.osc_a`. (§13.4.5)

## 018. Keyed Scopes & `repeat` — 138 Rules

018-1. Every conformant runtime exposes the three keyed-scope operations — `scope_obtain`, `scope_drop`, `scope_evaluate` — underlying the language's dynamic-scope reactive constructs. (§13.5)
018-2. Each instantiation of a keyed-scope template is backed by its own state cells. (§13.5)
018-3. The user-facing surface of the keyed-scope mechanism is `repeat`, materializing one scope per element yielded by a reactive iterable source. (§13.5)
018-4. A scope-managing construct's bound template is fixed at compile time; the scope operations parameterize only the key. (§13.5.1)
018-5. The keyed-scope primitive instantiates one scope per unique key; how a construct derives keys from its source elements is the construct's own choice (`repeat`: one key per element). (§13.5.1)
018-6. `scope_obtain(key)` returns the scope for `key`, allocating from the construct's per-template pool if absent. (§13.5.1)
018-7. A newly allocated scope initializes the template's state cells to their declared initial values. (§13.5.1)
018-8. `scope_drop(key)` invokes `Drop` on scope `key`'s state cells in reverse declaration order and returns the pool slot. (§13.5.1)
018-9. `scope_evaluate(key)` evaluates the template's deriveds and any recurrents eligible to fire within scope `key`'s state context. (§13.5.1)
018-10. During `scope_evaluate(key)`, bare references and `here::` inside the template body resolve to scope `key`'s cells. (§13.5.1)
018-11. References to the enclosing instance's cells inside a template body resolve to that instance's cells per the §13.7 scope chain. (§13.5.1)
018-12. The construct's elaboration is responsible for sequencing the operations: `scope_obtain` for new keys, `scope_evaluate` for active keys, `scope_drop` for keys no longer active. (§13.5.1)
018-13. Each scope-managing construct has its own keyed pool. (§13.5.1.1)
018-14. A construct's pool element shape is the template's state-shape. (§13.5.1.1)
018-15. A construct's pool index space is the construct's key domain. (§13.5.1.1)
018-16. Scopes are independent: no cell sharing across keys. (§13.5.1.1)
018-17. Pools follow the §14.3.3 extensible-pool model, growing as keys are added and shrinking as keys are dropped, subject to the runtime's pool-management policy. (§13.5.1.1)
018-18. A template's state-shape is the set of stateful cells it declares: `attr` and `recurrent` declarations in its body. (§13.5.2)
018-19. `derived` declarations are not part of the state-shape: they are pure functions of state cells, the loop binding, and the enclosing scope's cells. (§13.5.2)
018-20. `const` declarations are static and not state. (§13.5.2)
018-21. When a template's state-shape is empty, the runtime allocates no per-key cell pool; the per-key cell handling in `scope_obtain(key)`/`scope_drop(key)` is a no-op, but placement materialization is not (§13.5.4). (§13.5.2)
018-22. A `repeat` body places child instances and declares no cells, so its own state-shape is empty and it takes the no-pool fast path for its own storage; its placed children still persist per key via the placement machinery and §13.5.3 cell-identity paths. (§13.5.2)
018-23. For a stateless template, `scope_evaluate(key)` evaluates the deriveds against the loop binding and the enclosing scope's cells without any per-key state context. (§13.5.2)
018-24. The compiler determines state-shape at compile time and statically selects between the pool and no-pool case per construct instantiation. (§13.5.2)
018-25. When state-shape is non-empty, per-key state cells participate in hot reload per §13.15.2's cell-identity rules. (§13.5.3)
018-26. A scope cell's path is `<enclosing_path>.<key>.<template_field>`. (§13.5.3)
018-27. `<enclosing_path>` is the fully-qualified path of the enclosing instance — the node, placement, or effect containing the scope-managing construct. (§13.5.3)
018-28. Scope keys are required to be stringifiable primitives; for `repeat` the bound is the `StringifiableKey` trait. (§13.5.3)
018-29. The key value itself serves as the scope's path component. (§13.5.3)
018-30. Hot-reload changes to a template's body follow the standard reload-safe/reload-unsafe rules of §13.15.4, applied uniformly across all live keys. (§13.5.3)
018-31. `repeat` declares one reactive scope per element of a runtime reactive source. (§13.5.4)
018-32. `repeat` materializes scopes via the keyed-scope primitive: `scope_obtain` on key emergence, `scope_drop` on key disappearance, `scope_evaluate` per active key per commit. (§13.5.4)
018-33. `repeat` desugars directly to the §13.5.1 primitive; the runtime sees no machinery distinct from it. (§13.5.4)
018-34. `repeat` is the construct for runtime-varying existence: presence varies by mounting and dropping scopes as the source's key set changes, with state reclaimed on drop. (§13.5.4)
018-35. Conditional-activation gates never add or remove instances; they only freeze an unconditionally-constructed instance while it is inactive. (§13.5.4)
018-36. The `repeat` clause order is fixed: `repeat <bind> [at <index>] in <source> [as <view>] [keyed by <key-expr>]:` followed by an indented body. (§13.5.4.1)
018-37. A `repeat` source is a reactive collection cell: `Signal[I]` for some `I: Iterable`, or a `dynamic` namespace cell (`parts.<T>`, `incoming.<C>`, `outgoing.<C>`). (§13.5.4.1)
018-38. A `dynamic` namespace cell's value is a language-provided keyed, ordered collection — always iterable. (§13.5.4.1)
018-39. The standard library fulfills `Iterable` for `Vec[T]`, `T[N]` (any const N), `HashSet[T]`, and `HashMap[K, V]`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-40. User types may fulfill `Iterable` to participate as `repeat` sources. (§13.5.4.1)
018-41. `Stream[T]` is not a valid `repeat` source: an event source has no current snapshot and no key set to diff. (§13.5.4.1)
018-42. Driving `repeat` from a stream requires first projecting the stream into a collection-valued cell (e.g. folding events into a `Vec`/`HashMap`) and repeating over that. (§13.5.4.1)
018-43. `<bind>` is a bare identifier or a tuple-destructuring pattern per §12.12.1 — the same destructuring grammar the for-loop's iteration variable accepts. (§13.5.4.1)
018-44. `HashMap[K, V]`'s iterator yields `(K, V)` pairs. (§13.5.4.1)
018-45. `<bind>` is typed as the iterator's element type after move-promotion — a real owner, not the borrow-equivalent alias `Iterable::iterator` yields under the default `type Item = T` convention. (§13.5.4.1)
018-46. Move-promotion converts the cluster-member alias into a real owner scoped to one `scope_evaluate` invocation. (§13.5.4.1)
018-47. Each `scope_evaluate` runs in isolation against the source signal's current committed snapshot, which is read-only. (§13.5.4.1)
018-48. The runtime hands each `scope_evaluate` a unique pointer to its element; no other code can read or write that element during the scope's execution. (§13.5.4.1)
018-49. Using the bind within the scope is zero-copy: move-promotion is a static compiler reinterpretation, not a runtime operation. (§13.5.4.1)
