018-50. A `Copy` bind persists into cells or fields by copy as usual, with no effect on the source. (§13.5.4.1)
018-51. A non-`Copy` bind cannot be moved out of its storage: the element lives in the source's immutable committed snapshot and is re-read on the next commit. (§13.5.4.1)
018-52. Persisting a non-`Copy` bind into category B/D storage materializes a copy at that point, the same materialization as §13.2.9.7 boundaries. (§13.5.4.1)
018-53. Attrs, connection arguments, and other placement targets in a `repeat` body see owned types: borrow-equivalent aliases do not leak into attribute or argument positions through `repeat`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-54. The `repeat` body is an indented placement-body block per §13.8.3, with §13.8.9's clause ordering and §13.8.10's whitespace-separation and self-delimiting rules. (§13.5.4.1)
018-55. A scope body may contain any number of placements per key — nodes, connections, nested structure, or connections only; there is no one-placement-per-key requirement. (§13.5.4.1)
018-56. Each scope's placements join the enclosing structural sequence at the `repeat`'s written position, in source order. (§13.5.4.1)
018-57. In an `effects:` clause, the `repeat` body is a list of effect entries instead of placements. (§13.5.4.1)
018-58. `at <index>` binds a bare identifier to each element's 0-based enumeration index (`usize`) in iterator order. (§13.5.4.1)
018-59. The `at` index is ordinary per-iteration data: it updates whenever elements reorder. (§13.5.4.1)
018-60. `as <view>` binds a bare identifier naming the repeat's keyed view, addressable by key from the surrounding body — for an exposition-level `repeat`, the whole node type body. (§13.5.4.1)
018-61. Key derivation follows a strict ordered precedence; the compiler picks the first applicable path, leaving no call-site ambiguity. (§13.5.4.1)
018-62. Key path 1: an explicit `keyed by <key-expr>`, evaluated with the bind in scope, always wins when present. (§13.5.4.1)
018-63. An explicit `keyed by` result must be a `StringifiableKey`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-64. `StringifiableKey` comprises `i8`–`i64`, `u8`–`u64`, `bool`, `char`, and `string`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-65. Key path 2 (carried key): when the source is a `dynamic` namespace cell, each element arrives with the key its supplier derived for it, and that key is used. (§13.5.4.1)
018-66. Statically written elements of a `dynamic` namespace carry placement-site keys. (§13.5.4.1)
018-67. The carried-key path applies only to `dynamic` namespace sources. (§13.5.4.1)
018-68. Key path 3 (`Keyed` trait): if the element type fulfills the stdlib `Keyed` trait, the key is `Keyed::key(element)`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-69. `Keyed` is a stdlib trait of shape `type Key: StringifiableKey` plus `fn key(value: Subject) -> Key`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-70. A type fulfills `Keyed` for at most one `Key` (standard trait coherence), so the `Key` associated type is uniquely determined. (§13.5.4.1)
018-71. Key path 4 (stringifiable element): if the element type is itself a `StringifiableKey`, the element value is the key. (§13.5.4.1)
018-72. When no key-derivation path applies, compilation fails with an error directing the user to fulfill `Keyed` for the element type or add `keyed by <expr>`. (§13.5.4.1)
018-73. The `Keyed` path always wins over the stringifiable-element path when both apply, e.g. a newtype fulfilling both. (§13.5.4.1)
018-74. `repeat` re-evaluates only when its source is dirty; the runtime then reads the source's current committed value and iterates it via `Iterable::iterator`. (§13.5.4.2)
018-75. At each re-evaluation, every element's key is derived per §13.5.4.1's ordered selection. (§13.5.4.2)
018-76. At each re-evaluation, the new key set is diffed against the previous key set. (§13.5.4.2)
018-77. Keys in `old ∩ new` carry over: their scopes are preserved, and `<bind>` is updated to the new element. (§13.5.4.2)
018-78. Keys in `old − new` are dropped: `scope_drop(key)` releases the per-key cells. (§13.5.4.2)
018-79. Keys in `new − old` are added: `scope_obtain(key)` initializes the per-key cells per the state-shape. (§13.5.4.2)
018-80. For each key in iterator order, the runtime updates `<bind>` — and the `at` index, when present, to the element's 0-based position — and calls `scope_evaluate(key)`. (§13.5.4.2)
018-81. Two distinct elements deriving the same scope key in one evaluation: the first in iterator order wins the scope and bind, later duplicates are dropped, and the runtime raises a non-fatal warning; it never traps. (§13.5.4.2)
018-82. Reordering source elements without changing the key set performs no scope allocations or drops; only iteration order changes, and surviving scopes observe their new `at` indices. (§13.5.4.2)
018-83. Unordered iterables (`HashSet[T]`, `HashMap[K, V]`) are diffed by key identity; iteration order is whatever the underlying iterator emits and does not affect scope identity. (§13.5.4.2)
018-84. An `attr` is a reactive cell valid as a `repeat` source: reads of a collection-typed attr in the body yield a `Signal` of the collection (e.g. `Signal[Vec[VoiceConfig]]`), satisfying the source-type requirement with the collection supplying the iterator inside the `Signal`. (§13.5.4.3)
018-85. A key reappearing across a hot reload — source edit or program rerun — preserves its scope's state, identified by the same `<enclosing>.<key>.<cell>` path. (§13.5.4.4)
018-86. For a stateless template (empty state-shape), no per-key cells are allocated and the path machinery is bypassed. (§13.5.4.4)
018-87. Source mutations across a reload drive the same key-set diff as a runtime mutation: scopes whose keys disappear are dropped; scopes whose keys appear are allocated fresh. (§13.5.4.5)
018-88. Body changes across a reload — the template's placements and their attrs — apply uniformly to all existing scopes. (§13.5.4.5)
018-89. Changes to key derivation — the `keyed by` expression or a depended-on `Keyed::key` body — are reload-safe: recomputed against current inputs without a restart. (§13.5.4.5)
018-90. When a key-derivation change makes an element produce a different key, the old key's scope state is dropped and the new key's scope is freshly allocated: per-scope state is identified by key, not element identity. (§13.5.4.5)
018-91. `repeat` follows the commit-time-recompute model: signals carry current value, not deltas, so a dirty source is re-iterated and re-keyed in full. (§13.5.4.6)
018-92. The per-commit floor is O(N) iterate + O(N) key derivation + O(N) hash-diff against the previous key set, with N the current element count. (§13.5.4.6)
018-93. Scope add/remove costs O(K + K') on top of the floor — K keys removed, K' keys added — each invoking `scope_drop` or `scope_obtain`. (§13.5.4.6)
018-94. Per-scope evaluation costs the template body times the number of live keys. (§13.5.4.6)
018-95. A pure reorder performs zero scope-management work: keys carry across reorderings, with no `scope_obtain` or `scope_drop` invoked. (§13.5.4.6)
018-96. A pure reorder still pays the per-commit floor: reorder does not shortcut detecting that the key set is unchanged. (§13.5.4.6)
018-97. A clean commit (source not dirty) costs zero work: `repeat` does not re-iterate. (§13.5.4.6)
018-98. A stateless template allocates no per-key cells; the per-commit floor still applies. (§13.5.4.6)
018-99. There is no delta-driven `repeat` variant over a structural-delta reactive shape; `repeat` always re-iterates and re-keys the current value. (§13.5.4.6)
018-100. Programs that do not use `repeat` incur no runtime cost from the template-scope machinery. (§13.5.4.6)
018-101. `repeat` is admitted in `expose:` blocks: scopes become internal children of every instance of the enclosing node, materialized at instantiation, tracking the source, at the `repeat`'s written position in the exposition. (§13.5.4.7)
018-102. Exposition-level `repeat` children are internals, never counted against signature clauses. (§13.5.4.7)
018-103. `repeat` is admitted in placement bodies: scopes become children of that specific placement (caller supply). (§13.5.4.7)
018-104. Caller-supplied `repeat` node placements feed the receiving type's `dynamic`-marked part types. (§13.5.4.7)
018-105. Caller-supplied `repeat` connection placements sourced from the placed instance require `outgoing: dynamic` on its type. (§13.5.4.7)
018-106. `repeat` is admitted in `effects:` clauses: each scope materializes one effect-bearing instance per element. (§13.5.4.7)
018-107. Per-element effects from an `effects:`-clause `repeat` suspend, resume, and tear down with the element key. (§13.5.4.7)
018-109. `repeat` is rejected in function bodies — functions produce values, not reactive structure — by the same rule as `derived`/`recurrent`/`stream`. (§13.5.4.7)
018-110. `repeat` is rejected in both of an effect's blocks (`observed:` and `desired:`); per-element dynamic I/O is a node's `effects:`-clause `repeat`, one effect instance per element. (§13.5.4.7)
018-111. `repeat` is rejected in operator bodies: dynamic-scope materialization inside operators is not in scope for v1. (§13.5.4.7)
018-112. `repeat` is rejected in connection bodies; dynamic-scope structure belongs in node bodies, placement bodies, or `effects:` clauses. (§13.5.4.7)
018-113. `repeat` is rejected in trait and `fulfill` blocks, which declare behavior, not graph structure. (§13.5.4.7)
018-114. In each rejected context, the diagnostic identifies the misplaced `repeat` and points at the appropriate target context. (§13.5.4.7)
018-115. `<key-expr>` must be reactive-pure — no reactive dependencies beyond `<bind>` — guaranteeing key stability across evaluations. (§13.5.4.8)
018-116. The same reactive-purity rule applies to the `Keyed::key` method body when implicit keying goes through the `Keyed` trait. (§13.5.4.8)
018-117. A `repeat` body may not close over the enclosing scope's mutable state beyond §13.12.3's closure-snapshot semantics. (§13.5.4.8)
018-118. Nested `repeat` constructs are permitted; each nested level's scopes hang off the outer scope's path. (§13.5.4.8)
018-119. The source iterator must terminate at each evaluation; bounded-at-commit-time collections satisfy this. (§13.5.4.8)
018-120. No compiler termination check is mandated for user `Iterable` implementations; they are trusted. (§13.5.4.8)
018-121. An iterator whose `next` never returns `None` hangs the iterate phase; this is a programmer error against the trait's intended use. (§13.5.4.8)
018-122. The same key reached through different element values across commits identifies the same scope: the value is carried in `<bind>` and may change, while the key fixes scope identity. (§13.5.4.8)
018-123. Carried keys diff identically to derived keys: with a `dynamic` namespace source the diff compares the carried key set across commits, so an element keeps its scope across reorderings and value changes. (§13.5.4.8)
018-124. The `at` index never derives the key implicitly: it is excluded from the implicit key-derivation paths (`Keyed` trait and stringifiable element). (§13.5.4.8)
018-125. Positional identity requires explicit opt-in by writing `keyed by <index>`; there is no implicit path to index keying. (§13.5.4.8)
018-126. Without `as`, a `repeat` materializes its scopes anonymously: nothing in the surrounding body can name a particular child. (§13.5.4.9)
018-127. `as <view>` binds a keyed view: a lookup table over the scopes the `repeat` currently holds, addressed by scope key. (§13.5.4.9)
018-128. Names are scope entries, not instance members: a scope exposes its placements through their names, so addressing a child goes through the placement name, not an instance. (§13.5.4.9)
018-129. `<view>[<key>]` selects the scope for `<key>`. (§13.5.4.9)
018-130. `<view>[<key>].<name>` yields a key-addressed `Handle[T]` for the placement named `<name>` via `as <name>` at its site, with `T` that placement's node type: `posts_view[selected].avatar`. (§13.5.4.9)
018-131. Every named placement in the body is addressable through the view at any nesting depth. (§13.5.4.9)
018-132. The view table is flat: it maps names, not paths. (§13.5.4.9)
018-133. `as`-names must be unique within one `repeat` body, across nesting; a duplicate is a compile error. (§13.5.4.9)
018-134. Anonymous placements are unaddressable through the view; leaving a placement unnamed keeps it private to its scope. (§13.5.4.9)
018-135. A view lookup is a weak, storable `Handle` (§13.3.6.2): `<view>[k].<name>` resolves to `Some(&node)` while key `k`'s scope is mounted and to `None` otherwise. (§13.5.4.9)
018-136. A view handle is never provably live: `<view>[k].<name>!` is always a compile error; eliminate with `match` or `?`. (§13.5.4.9)
018-137. A key that leaves and later returns remounts the same scope, so a stored view handle resumes resolving `Some` when its key comes back. (§13.5.4.9)
018-138. Nested `repeat`s each own a separate `as` view and do not flatten into the outer one. (§13.5.4.9)
018-139. A nested view is scoped to its parent key; cross-level addressing composes view by view rather than through one global table. (§13.5.4.9)

## 019. Connections — 75 Rules

019-1. A connection is a directional, typed link between two node instances. (§13.6.0)
019-2. Connections are first-class entities: each has its own identity. (§13.6.0)
019-3. A connection carries its own reactive content — attrs, recurrents, deriveds — per-relationship state belonging to the relationship rather than to either endpoint: `Drives` holding `attr aggressiveness`. (§13.6.0)
019-4. A connection type may satisfy traits. (§13.6.0)
019-5. Every connection has a source — the `from` endpoint — and a destination — the `to` endpoint. (§13.6.0)
019-6. A caller-placed connection participates in the source node's outgoing surface declared via `outgoing:`. (§13.6.0)
019-7. A self-sourced connection sits outside the source's `outgoing:` surface. (§13.6.0)
019-8. Caller-placed and self-sourced connections both count at the destination node's `incoming:` surface. (§13.6.0)
019-9. The `from` endpoint is the enclosing instance, fixed structurally by where the connection is placed; it does not vary at runtime. (§13.6.0)
019-10. The `to` endpoint is a node reference and may be a reactive value that re-points among existing nodes at runtime. (§13.6.0)
019-11. When `to` re-points, the connection instance persists and retains its per-relationship state; only its target moves. (§13.6.0)
019-12. Re-pointing neither creates nor drops a node — a connection never owns its endpoints. (§13.6.0)
019-13. A node declares which connection types callers may wire to and from it via `incoming:` and `outgoing:` clauses, with optional cardinality constraints and the `dynamic` supply-mode marker. (§13.6.0)
019-14. Connection instances appear at placement, not in `incoming:`/`outgoing:` declarations. (§13.6.0)
019-15. A connection placement is an exposition entry at a position in its source's ordered structural sequence. (§13.6.0)
019-16. A connection's exposition position is where it engages during traversal. (§13.6.0)
019-17. Every statically placed connection exists from construction; only a dynamic destination's target may move. (§13.6.0)
019-18. A connection's exposition entry belongs to the source's exposition, yet the connection is owned by no single endpoint. (§13.6.0)
019-19. A node cannot store a direct reference to another node instance: `attr target: SomeNode` ✗. (§13.6.0)
019-20. A node-instance reference is a borrow, and a borrow may not be stored in a cell. (§13.6.0)
019-21. Storing a node type rather than an instance — a template slot — uses `Type[…]`. (§13.6.0)
019-22. A connection declares its endpoint types in exactly one of three forms: single, cartesian, or pairs. (§13.6.1)
019-23. Mixing endpoint forms is a compile error: `pairs:` alongside `from:`+`to:` ✗. (§13.6.1)
019-24. Single form requires `from:` exactly once and `to:` exactly once: `from: Driver` / `to: Drivable`. (§13.6.1.1)
019-25. A connection body admits `when:` (activation predicate), `const`, `attr`, `default attr`, `recurrent[N]`, `derived`, and `stream policy[N]` declarations. (§13.6.1.1)
019-26. A connection `const` is a per-type compile-time constant. (§13.6.1.1)
019-27. Connection `attr`, `recurrent`, `derived`, and `stream` members are per-instance. (§13.6.1.1)
019-28. A connection type may declare at most one `default attr`. (§13.6.1.1)
019-29. At placement, `/expr` targets the connection's default attr. (§13.6.1.1)
019-30. A placement's destination endpoint is supplied in the placement body, never via `/expr`. (§13.6.1.1)
019-31. `from` and `to` are endpoint slots, not attributes; placement attribute syntax (`name=value`, flags) does not target them. (§13.6.1.1)
019-32. In single form, `from` and `to` resolve inside the body directly to the endpoint instances at their concrete types: `to.top_speed`. (§13.6.1.1)
019-33. Cartesian form lists multiple from-types and/or to-types; every from×to combination is a valid placement: `from: Person, Company` with `to: Vehicle, Property`. (§13.6.1.2)
019-34. A cartesian body is specialized per from×to combination by a required, statically resolved `match`: `match (from, to):`. (§13.6.1.2)
019-35. The cartesian `match` must be exhaustive over the statically-known from×to product — a compile-time check. (§13.6.1.2)
019-36. Within each cartesian arm, `from` and `to` are concretely typed: `(Person(p), Vehicle(v)):`. (§13.6.1.2)
019-37. Cartesian form introduces no combined runtime value and no new type former. (§13.6.1.2)
019-38. Pairs form lists explicit `From -> To` combinations; only listed pairs are valid placements: `pairs:` with `Driver -> Vehicle`. (§13.6.1.3)
019-39. In pairs form, endpoints are accessed only via `pair`, matched per declared pair: `match pair: (Driver(d), Vehicle(v)): ...`. (§13.6.1.3)
019-40. In pairs form, `from` and `to` are not independently accessible. (§13.6.1.3)
019-41. Listing the same `From -> To` pair twice is a compile error. (§13.6.1.3)
019-42. Asymmetric pair counts are legal — pair uniqueness, not type count, governs: `A -> X`, `A -> Y`, `B -> Y` ✓. (§13.6.1.3)
019-43. A pairs-form body is uniform across pairs; attrs/deriveds cannot vary by pair. (§13.6.1.3)
019-44. Differing per-pair content requires a separate connection type per pair. (§13.6.1.3)
019-45. `from`, `to`, and `pair` are bound at the connection's placement, which specifies the source (the enclosing instance) and the destination. (§13.6.2)
019-46. The `from` binding is fixed for the connection's lifetime. (§13.6.2)
019-47. When the destination is a reactive selection or a `Handle`, `to` follows whichever node it currently resolves to. (§13.6.2)
019-48. Body reads of `to.*` re-evaluate when the destination re-points — a dynamic dependency on the current target. (§13.6.2)
019-49. Inside a connection body, `from` and `to` are never `Option`; they are the live endpoint instances directly. (§13.6.2)
019-50. A `Handle` destination (read type `Option[&N]`) is unwrapped at the boundary: while it resolves to `Some`, the connection is active and the body sees the contained node as `to`. (§13.6.2)
019-51. While a `Handle` destination resolves to `None`, the connection freezes and its body does not run at all. (§13.6.2)
019-52. The freeze is the unwrap: connection body code never handles destination absence. (§13.6.2)
019-53. Gates and the unresolved-destination condition are the only switches on a connection's reactive liveness. (§13.6.2)
019-54. Traversal position never switches a connection's liveness: an unengaged or already-passed connection keeps computing. (§13.6.2)
019-55. `from` and `to` are body-internal, visible only inside the connection type's own body; there is no external `some_conn.to` access. (§13.6.2)
019-56. A connection does not surface its endpoints or its activation as readable fields. (§13.6.2)
019-57. An outside observer reads a connection's own cells (attrs, deriveds), which hold their last committed value even while the connection is frozen. (§13.6.2)
019-58. Liveness-awareness about a node is the job of a `Handle`, not of a connection's surface. (§13.6.2)
019-59. A connection may declare generic parameters: `connection Contains[T]:` with `from: Container[T]`, `to: T`. (§13.6.3)
019-60. A connection's generic parameters scope over its `from`, `to`, attrs, recurrents, and deriveds. (§13.6.3)
019-61. Each unique instantiation of a generic connection is a distinct connection type. (§13.6.3)
019-62. A connection body contains no `fn` declarations. (§13.6.4)
019-63. Functions on connections are free functions taking the connection type, dispatched via uniform call syntax. (§13.6.4)
019-64. Trait methods on connections are implemented in `fulfill` blocks. (§13.6.4)
019-65. A connection body contains no `repeat` declarations. (§13.6.4)
019-66. Dynamic-scope structure belongs in `expose:` blocks, placement bodies, or effect `desired:` blocks, never in connection bodies. (§13.6.4)
019-67. A connection body hosts no effect instantiations. (§13.6.4)
019-68. Effects are instantiated only in a node's `effects:` clause. (§13.6.4)
019-69. A connection holds no outside-world resource of its own. (§13.6.4)
019-70. `Circularity` is a language-defined marker trait with no methods: `trait Circularity`. (§13.6.5)
019-71. A connection type opts into participating in topology cycles by declaring `satisfies Circularity`. (§13.6.5)
019-72. Every topology cycle in the construction-time node graph must traverse at least one connection whose type satisfies `Circularity`. (§13.6.5)
019-73. A cycle consisting only of non-`Circularity` connections is a compile error. (§13.6.5)
019-74. Satisfying `Circularity` has no effect beyond opting the connection type into cycle participation; that is its sole purpose. (§13.6.5)
019-75. Which connection types satisfy `Circularity` is domain-defined. (§13.6.5)

## 020. Name Resolution in Reactive Bodies — 36 Rules

020-1. Name resolution proceeds outward through enclosing scopes, innermost first — standard lexical scoping. (§13.7)
020-2. A node or connection body is an ordinary scope; its members are in scope within the body's reactive expressions. (§13.7)
020-3. There is no receiver concept: a bare name binds to the nearest enclosing scope that declares it. (§13.7)
020-4. Two explicit scope anchors exist: `here::x` resolves in the current scope, `module::x` in the module top level. (§13.7)
020-5. Routine member access uses a bare name or `here::`; `subject` is reserved for the value role. (§13.7)
020-6. Within a node or connection body, the scope chain innermost-first is: local bindings, the instance body scope, the module top-level scope. (§13.7.1)
020-7. Local bindings comprise `let` bindings and `for`-loop variables inside a reactive expression. (§13.7.1)
020-8. The instance body scope comprises `attr`/`recurrent`/`derived`/`stream` cells and the reserved fields `from`, `to`, `incoming`, `outgoing`, `pair`, `parts`, `exposition`. (§13.7.1)
020-9. The module top-level scope comprises module-level `signal`, `derived`, `recurrent`, `stream`, `const`, and `let` declarations. (§13.7.1)
020-10. A bare name resolves to the nearest scope in the chain that declares it: `local_gain * master_gain` reads a member and a module cell anchor-free. (§13.7.1)
020-11. `here::x` resolves `x` in the current scope, bypassing inner local bindings and ignoring same-named module-level declarations. (§13.7.2)
020-12. `here::` is meaningful in any scope (function, operator, trait, node/connection body), always denoting the binding in that very scope rather than an outer one. (§13.7.2)
020-13. Type-level positions (trait declarations, `fulfill` blocks) use the `Subject` alias for the subject type, not `here::`. (§13.7.2)
020-14. Bare and `here::`-anchored references resolve to the same cell when there is no collision: `expertise_level` and `here::expertise_level`. (§13.7.2)
020-15. `module::x` resolves `x` in the enclosing module's top-level scope, bypassing body and local scopes: `on module::tick:`. (§13.7.3)
020-16. `module::` reaches only the current module's top level; cross-module access uses the module-path mechanism. (§13.7.3)
020-17. A bare name declared in both the instance body scope and the module top-level scope is ambiguous — a compile error: `derived a: f32 = gain` ✗. (§13.7.4)
020-18. The body/module collision is resolved by anchoring: `here::gain` selects the member, `module::gain` the module-level cell. (§13.7.4)
020-19. Local bindings (`let`, `for`-vars) shadow outer scopes normally; the ambiguity error applies only to the body-vs-module collision. (§13.7.4)
020-20. The reserved fields `from`, `to`, `incoming`, `outgoing`, `pair`, `parts`, and `exposition` are instance-body-scope members resolving by bare name in expression position: `incoming.ShowsCount[0].count`. (§13.7.5)
020-21. Reserved-field keywords carry clause-header meaning only in declaration position (statement level, trailing colon: `from:`). (§13.7.5)
020-22. In expression-operand position, a reserved-field keyword denotes the corresponding instance field: `from.expertise_level`. (§13.7.5)
020-23. Reserved words cannot be declared as cell names, so reserved fields never collide with user names. (§13.7.5)
020-24. `here::` remains available as the explicit form for reserved fields: `here::from`, `here::incoming`. (§13.7.5)
020-25. `in` is not a reserved instance field; it serves solely as the `for`-loop separator: `for x in incoming.ShowsCount`. (§13.7.5)
020-26. A cell reference — bare, `here::`-anchored, or `module::`-anchored — joins the reactive dependency graph: `derived x: f32 = y + 1` dirties `x` when the resolved `y` changes. (§13.7.6)
020-27. Dependency edges are emitted per instance: body-scope references resolve to that specific instance's cells, never another instance's. (§13.7.6)
020-28. `module::` references resolve to the single shared module-level cell. (§13.7.6)
020-29. `subject` is the instance value, available in node/connection bodies for handling the whole instance as a value: `total_output(subject)`. (§13.7.7)
020-30. `subject.some_method()` and `some_method(subject)` are the same call — dispatch is by first argument and the dot is sugar, not a receiver binding. (§13.7.7)
020-31. There is no implicit receiver: the instance is always explicit, as `subject` in a body or as a named parameter in a `fulfill`-block function. (§13.7.7)
020-32. A type's "methods" are ordinary scoped functions taking the instance as a developer-named first parameter: `fn display(value: Subject)`. (§13.7.7)
020-33. `Subject` is the type-level alias for the subject type, usable only in type positions in trait declarations and `fulfill` blocks. (§13.7.7)
020-34. `here` denotes the current (innermost) scope as a namespace, usable only as the left side of `::`; it is not a value and has no `.` form. (§13.7.7)
020-35. `module` denotes the enclosing module namespace, usable only as the left side of `::`; it is not a value and has no `.` form. (§13.7.7)
020-36. The four reserved identifiers never overlap in role: `subject` is a value usable in expression positions, `Subject` a type usable in type positions, `here` and `module` namespaces usable only as the left side of `::`. (§13.7.7)

## 021. Placement — 143 Rules

021-1. Placement is the syntax for instantiating nodes, parts, and connections into a concrete reactive graph. (§13.8)
021-2. Placement is distinct from value construction of records, which uses constructor syntax. (§13.8)
021-3. A top-level placement creates a named instance of a node type at module scope: `Driver john_doe`. (§13.8.1)
021-4. A top-level placement line is the type name, then the instance name, then optional attribute settings, then an optional `:` introducing a body of child placements: `Driver john_doe | expertise_level=10:`. (§13.8.1)
021-5. A top-level placement is a declaration that names its subject positionally — type then name, with no marker between them. (§13.8.1)
021-6. A top-level placement is mandatorily named. (§13.8.1)
021-7. The `as` name marker is optional in a top-level placement; `Driver john_doe` and `Driver as john_doe` have identical meaning. (§13.8.1)
021-8. Instance names are unique within their declaring scope; two top-level placements with the same name in one module is a compile error. (§13.8.1)
021-9. Attribute settings never appear in a placement body; they live on the placement's main line or its aligned continuation lines. (§13.8.2)
021-10. A single-line placement with attrs uses one leading `|` followed by one or more `name=value` settings separated by whitespace: `Counter c1 | count=100`. (§13.8.2)
021-11. Multi-line attribute layout keeps the first attribute on the placement's main line; later attributes continue on lines indented exactly to the first attribute's column, with no further `|`. (§13.8.2)
021-12. A placement with attrs and children writes attributes inline (or via aligned continuation) and then `:` introducing the body: `Driver john_doe | expertise_level=10:`. (§13.8.2)
021-13. A placement attribute setting must name a cell declared as an `attr` on the placed type; setting any other identifier — `signal`, `recurrent`, `derived`, or `const` — is a compile error. (§13.8.2)
021-14. A placement attribute value must match the attr's declared type, subject to the standard widening rules. (§13.8.2)
021-15. A placement attr RHS whose provenance contains no reactive cell is consumed into the attr's storage slot at instantiation as an implicit move (§11.1 category B), with no `move` keyword required: `Server as srv | port=8080`. (§13.8.2.1)
021-16. A placement attr RHS that references reactive cells (signals, attrs, recurrents, deriveds) creates an implicit derived bridging the source cells to the target attr, so the attr reactively tracks the sources: `Display as d1 | label=format(c1.count)`. (§13.8.2.1)
021-17. Reactive placement wiring is §11.1 category C under the reactive-binding exception: the placement is not an ownership operation and the source cell is not consumed. (§13.8.2.1)
021-18. A reactive placement value synthesizes a derived in the parent's scope; when any cell in the expression's provenance changes, the synthesized derived re-evaluates and the target attr updates. (§13.8.2.1)
021-19. A value placement evaluates the RHS once and stores the result into the attr's slot. (§13.8.2.1)
021-20. The compiler determines the binding category from the expression's provenance set: any reference to a reactive cell makes the expression reactive (category C); otherwise it is a value expression (category B). (§13.8.2.1)
021-21. A `const` reference in a placement RHS contributes no reactive provenance; a const-only RHS is a category-B value binding, not category-C reactive wiring. (§13.8.2.1)
021-22. The value-vs-reactive distinction at placement is type-directed and requires no syntactic marker. (§13.8.2.1)
021-23. `const` declarations are not settable at placement; attempting to set a const at placement is a compile error. (§13.8.2.2)
021-24. `signal` declarations are not settable at placement: the declared initial value applies at construction and subsequent values come through the host API. (§13.8.2.2)
021-25. Recurrents are not settable at placement: a recurrent's value is fully defined by its expression and self-history fallbacks, with no separate initial value to override. (§13.8.2.2)
021-26. Per-instance variation of recurrent behavior is expressed via attrs the recurrent reads, which are settable at placement: `Counter c1 | start_value=100 step=5`. (§13.8.2.2)
021-27. The inline attribute forms (`name=value` / `name` / `!name`) and the flag form target the same underlying attr cells; setting the same attr via two mechanisms is a duplicate-set compile error. (§13.8.2.2)
021-28. Reactive bindings apply only to the `name=value` inline attribute form; the flag form has no expression slot and always sets a literal boolean, so reactive bindings do not apply to flags. (§13.8.2.2)
021-29. A type's declared `default attr` is additionally settable via the positional `/expr` form, uniformly for nodes and connections. (§13.8.2.2)
021-30. Connection destinations are supplied in the placement's body, never via `/expr`. (§13.8.2.2)
021-31. An attr not set at placement takes its declared default. (§13.8.2.2)
021-32. Consts always have their type-declared value. (§13.8.2.2)
021-33. The body of a placement — the indented block introduced by `:` — is reserved exclusively for child placements: parts and connections. (§13.8.3)
021-34. A child placement naming a node type listed in the parent's `parts:` clause is a part and creates an instance of that type as a child of the parent. (§13.8.3)
021-35. A nested placement is named with the `as` marker, which is required in nested position: `Pin as out1`. (§13.8.3)
021-36. A nested part may be anonymous: bare `Pin` is a valid unnamed part placement. (§13.8.3)
021-37. A part's placement-time name is visible by bare name to other placements in the same placement body regardless of order, including ones written earlier or later: a sibling connection can reference `out1`. (§13.8.3)
021-38. A named part is accessible outside the parent type, in function bodies receiving the parent instance, as `chip_b.out1` — in parallel with the type-bulk form `chip_b.parts.Pin[i]`. (§13.8.3)
021-39. A named part is accessible by qualified path from other instances' placement bodies that reference the parent instance: `chip_b.out1`. (§13.8.3)
021-40. Placement-time part names are not available inside the parent's own type body; there, `parts.<NodeType>[i]` or `parts.<NodeType>` iteration is used. (§13.8.3)
021-41. Cardinality declared in the parent's `parts:` clause is enforced at placement; violations are compile errors at the placement site. (§13.8.3)
021-42. A placement body may contain a `for` loop over a compile-time-known iterable; it unrolls at compile time into one child placement per iteration: `for i in 0..VOICE_COUNT:` with body `Oscillator | freq=base_freq(i)`. (§13.8.3.1)
021-43. Placement-body `for` preserves the static-graph principle: every part is determined at compile time. (§13.8.3.1)
021-44. `for` in a placement body is static supply: its unrolled placements count against the receiving type's cardinality and appear in `parts.<NodeType>`, exactly like individually written placements. (§13.8.3.1)
021-45. `repeat` in a placement body is dynamic supply, legal only against the receiver's `dynamic`-marked types. (§13.8.3.1)
021-46. A placement-body `for` whose iterable is not compile-time-known is a compile error pointing at the iterable; no new diagnostic class is introduced — the static-graph rule itself enforces it. (§13.8.3.1)
021-47. The non-compile-time-iterable diagnostic identifies the iterable and cites the runtime source via reactivity-provenance machinery when the runtime-ness flows from a reactive value. (§13.8.3.1)
021-48. Anonymous parts produced by an unrolled placement loop are addressable via `parts.<NodeType>[i]` from function bodies receiving the parent instance and via bare `parts.<NodeType>` inside the parent's type body. (§13.8.3.1)
021-49. Runtime-varying child multiplicity uses `repeat`, not `for`; placement-body `for` is for multiplicity parameterized by a compile-time-known value but fixed per instance. (§13.8.3.1)
021-50. Multiplicity intrinsic to the type — every instance materializing the same children — is declared in the node body (§13.3.3.3); the placement-body `for` is for loops intrinsic to a specific placement. (§13.8.3.1)
021-51. A connection placement creates a directional connection and is written inside the source instance's body. (§13.8.4)
021-52. The source of a connection placement is always the immediately enclosing instance — determined positionally, with no prefix or sigil. (§13.8.4)
021-53. The enclosing-instance source rule is uniform across nesting depth: the depth at which the connection appears does not change how the source is determined. (§13.8.4)
021-54. A connection placement's position among its sibling placements is its engagement position: the connection engages when traversal reaches that point, after the siblings written before it. (§13.8.4)
021-55. Position-is-engagement holds regardless of who places the connection — a caller in a placement body, or the type itself in its exposition, including exposition-level `for` and `repeat` bodies. (§13.8.4)
021-56. A caller-supplied connection's position survives the type's exposition by anchoring: it anchors to its nearest preceding sibling placement and engages immediately after that sibling wherever the exposition carries it — through a bulk reference, a by-name entry, or a wrapper. (§13.8.4)
021-57. A connection anchored to a `repeat` block anchors to the block as a unit and engages after the block's expanded scopes. (§13.8.4)
021-58. A connection written before any sibling anchors to the body start and engages before the node's first exposed entry. (§13.8.4)
021-59. Under the default `expose: parts`, anchoring reduces to traversing the full interleaved sequence as written. (§13.8.4)
021-60. Anchoring is the fallback: an explicit `expose:` that references caller-supplied connections directly via `outgoing.<ConnType>` overrides the anchor for the referenced connections. (§13.8.4)
021-61. A caller-placed connection's type must match a type listed in the source instance's `outgoing:` clause or in the type's traits' contributions. (§13.8.4)
021-62. A self-sourced connection placed in the source type's own exposition is exempt from `outgoing:` matching but not from endpoint typing. (§13.8.4)
021-63. A connection's destination is supplied in the placement's body as a node reference: a bare identifier, any (possibly reactive) expression yielding a node reference, or a `Handle[N]` read as `Option[&N]`. (§13.8.4)
021-64. A reactive or `Handle` destination makes membership at the destination a runtime fact: every node type in the reference's candidate envelope must declare `incoming: dynamic <ConnType>`. (§13.8.4)
021-65. On a connection placement, `/expr` sets the connection's `default attr` and the attribute clause sets named attrs; neither targets the destination. (§13.8.4)
021-66. A placement-level `when` modifier may gate a connection instance; it appears between `/Expr` and the attribute clause, before the body's `:`: `Cascade when filter.signal_active: next_filter`. (§13.8.4)
021-67. A placement-level `when` predicate evaluates in the scope of the enclosing source instance, not the connection being placed, whose scope is unavailable because the connection is not yet constructed. (§13.8.4)
021-68. Gating on a connection's own attrs requires a type-level `when:` clause inside the connection type's body, not a placement-level `when`. (§13.8.4)
021-69. The placement-level `when` predicate names the enclosing source instance directly: inside `my_app`'s body, `WiresTo when my_app.debug_enabled: monitor`. (§13.8.4)
021-70. A connection is owned by neither endpoint; it runs between two instances. (§13.8.4.1)
021-71. A connection is initiated from its source and terminated at its destination; "source" and "destination" are the canonical terms, and "owner" is not. (§13.8.4.1)
021-72. Re-pointing a connection's destination reference moves the connection's target without affecting either node's lifetime. (§13.8.4.1)
021-73. A connection type may be supplied as a value (`Type[…]`) and placed, exactly as a node type can. (§13.8.4.2)
021-74. Placing a supplied connection type binds the connection's `from` to the placing (enclosing) instance and takes its `to` at the placement site: `link_kind: the_car`. (§13.8.4.2)
021-75. Placement-scope instance names are visible across the whole enclosing placement scope regardless of order; a placement may reference a sibling placed later: `link_kind: the_car` with `the_car` placed afterward. (§13.8.4.2)
021-76. A supplied connection type is constrained by a trait carrying its endpoints, so the compiler checks both endpoints before the concrete type is known. (§13.8.4.2)
021-77. `outgoing: C` with `C` a connection-type parameter admits exactly the supplied connection type. (§13.8.4.2)
021-78. Attrs of a placed supplied connection type are set with the attribute clause, exactly as for a named connection. (§13.8.4.2)
021-79. The `/expr` slot appears immediately after the placed type name, any flags, and the optional `as` name, and before the attribute clause. (§13.8.5)
021-80. `/expr` is the placement's positional argument: it targets the placed type's `default attr`, whether the placed type is a node or a connection. (§13.8.5)
021-81. Using `/expr` on a type without a declared `default attr` is a compile error. (§13.8.5)
021-82. An unparenthesized `/expr` is restricted to a single atom — a literal, identifier, or path: `C/4`, `Filter/cutoff_default`. (§13.8.5)
021-83. A compound `/expr` must be parenthesized: `C/(base * 2)`. (§13.8.5)
021-84. On a connection placement, `/expr` sets the connection type's declared `default attr` while the destination is supplied in the body: `Drives/0.8: some_car`. (§13.8.5.1)
021-85. A connection destination is a node reference whose type satisfies the connection's `to:` clause. (§13.8.5.1)
021-86. A connection destination is a reference to an existing node, not a placement: an inline placement spec is not a valid destination — the target is placed first, then pointed at. (§13.8.5.1)
021-87. Any expression yielding a node reference is admissible as a destination, including a function return: `Drives: pick(mode, some_car, other_car)`. (§13.8.5.1)
021-88. A connection endpoint is a borrow; because a borrow cannot be stored in a cell, a destination that must persist or re-point across time is supplied as a `Handle[N]`, whose read is `Option[&N]`. (§13.8.5.1)
021-89. A `Handle` destination points at the contained node while the resolution is `Some` and the connection freezes while it is `None`. (§13.8.5.1)
021-90. A destination that varies at runtime makes the connection's `to` endpoint dynamic: the connection re-points among existing nodes as the reference changes. (§13.8.5.1)
021-91. Every node a destination reference could yield belongs to the statically-known instance set — a function or cell cannot create a node — so the destination always resolves within that set. (§13.8.5.1)
021-92. The compiler checks the `to:` constraint against the destination reference's type, so every possible target is well-typed. (§13.8.5.1)
021-93. Topology-cycle analysis for a dynamic destination ranges over the node types the reference type admits. (§13.8.5.1)
021-94. Each node type a dynamic destination reference admits must declare `incoming: dynamic <ConnType>`. (§13.8.5.1)
021-95. A reactive selection is what makes a destination move; a compile-time-fixed selection resolves to one node and the membership is a static fact. (§13.8.5.1)
021-96. On a node placement, `/expr` sets the type's `default attr` and the expression must match that attr's declared type: `Log /"Hello World"`. (§13.8.5.2)
021-97. A node-placement `/expr` is equivalent to the inline attribute form naming the default attr: `Log /"Hello World"` equals `Log | message="Hello World"`. (§13.8.5.2)
021-98. A node placement body contains zero or more child placements — parts and connections — with multiple children allowed. (§13.8.6)
021-99. A connection placement body — the indented block or inline single-line form after `:` — contains exactly one node reference (the destination): no child placements, no inline placement specs, no attr settings, no multiple values. (§13.8.6)
021-100. `to` and `from` are reserved as endpoint slots inside connection type bodies and cannot be used as attr names on connections. (§13.8.6)
021-101. `to` and `from` do not appear in connection placement bodies. (§13.8.6)
021-102. The parser distinguishes attribute settings from placements lexically: attribute settings follow a single leading `|` (or aligned continuation) in `name=expr` form; placements use the placement clause form. (§13.8.6)
021-103. An attribute clause follows the TypeRef (and optional flags, instance name, and `/expr`) on the same line, introduced by exactly one leading `|`. (§13.8.7)
021-104. After the leading `|`, attributes are separated by whitespace; intermediate `|` characters between attributes are not permitted. (§13.8.7)
021-105. `name=value` sets attribute `name` to expression `value`: `Sensor s1 | gain=0.5`. (§13.8.7)
021-106. A bare `name` in the attribute clause sets boolean attribute `name` to `true`: `Sensor s1 | active`. (§13.8.7)
021-107. `!name` in the attribute clause sets boolean attribute `name` to `false`: `Sensor s1 | !calibrated`. (§13.8.7)
021-108. Parentheses may be used freely around attribute values for grouping or disambiguation: `Sensor s1 | gain=(base + offset)`. (§13.8.7)
021-109. Multi-line attribute continuation is purely a formatting variant and does not change semantics. (§13.8.7)
021-110. Setting the same attribute twice on one placement is a duplicate-set compile error, parallel to record-field duplicate-set. (§13.8.7)
021-111. Attribute settings target attrs declared on the placed type directly or inherited via satisfied traits. (§13.8.7)
021-112. The bare `name` and `!name` forms require the attr to be of type `bool`; using them on a non-boolean attr is a compile error. (§13.8.7)
021-113. A flag is a single non-letter character adjacent to the placed type's TypeRef with no intervening whitespace, aliasing a boolean attribute of that type: `Pin' p1`. (§13.8.8)
021-114. Flags apply uniformly to node and connection placements: `WiresTo'! as my_wire: chip_b.in1`. (§13.8.8)
021-115. Multiple flags may appear as a run after the TypeRef: `Component?* c1`. (§13.8.8)
021-116. Flags are declared on attr declarations via the `@flag('c')` annotation: `@flag('!')` on `attr reverse_polarity: bool = false`. (§13.8.8)
021-117. The `@flag` annotation argument is a `char` literal. (§13.8.8)
021-118. Only boolean attrs may carry `@flag`; `@flag` on a non-boolean attr is a compile error. (§13.8.8)
021-119. The permitted flag characters are exactly `'`, `!`, `?`, `*`, `+`, `^`, `~`, `@`, `$`. (§13.8.8.1)
021-120. Flag characters are non-letter characters outside identifier syntax; `#`, being a valid identifier character, is excluded from the flag set. (§13.8.8.1)
021-121. Within a type's effective attribute surface — its own attrs plus those inherited via satisfied traits — each flag character must be unique. (§13.8.8.2)
021-122. Two attrs claiming the same flag character is a compile error at the type declaration site, identifying both attrs. (§13.8.8.2)
021-123. At a placement site, each flag character in the run resolves to the boolean attr it aliases and sets that attr to `true`. (§13.8.8.3)
021-124. There is no flag form for setting `false`; overriding a default-`true` attr to `false` uses the inline `!name` form. (§13.8.8.3)
021-125. In placement position, a non-letter character immediately following the TypeRef path with no intervening whitespace is a flag-run opener. (§13.8.8.4)
021-126. A flag character that doubles as a token elsewhere carries its other meaning in any non-placement position: `'` opens a char literal, `?` is postfix Try, `@` is the annotation prefix, `!` is the attribute-false marker. (§13.8.8.4)
021-127. A boolean attr may be set via at most one mechanism per placement — flag form or inline `name`/`!name`/`name=value`; using two on the same attr is a compile error: `Pin' p1 | reverse_polarity=false` ✗. (§13.8.8.5)
021-128. The two-mechanism duplicate uses the same diagnostic class as duplicate-set for inline attributes. (§13.8.8.5)
021-129. A placement's inline parts have the fixed order TypeRef, flags run, `as` name, `/Expr`, `when` predicate, attribute clause, `:` body — each element after TypeRef optional: `Drives'! as my_drive / 0.8 | enhanced_handling: some_car`. (§13.8.9)
021-130. The inline `when` clause gates the placement; its predicate is a boolean expression in placement scope. (§13.8.9)
021-131. When `/Expr` is absent, `when` slots immediately after whichever preceding element is present: `Logger when debug_enabled`. (§13.8.9)
021-132. The inline `when` is the single-placement modifier; `when` blocks and `given` blocks are not inline-parts modifiers — they appear as standalone entries at `expose:`/body level, each owning an indented arm body, outside the inline ordering. (§13.8.9)
021-133. A `when` predicate containing an unparenthesized `:` must be parenthesized to avoid colliding with the body-introducer `:`. (§13.8.9)
021-134. Multiple placements may appear on one line separated by whitespace; there is no comma separator (and no semicolon): `C/4 G'/4 A`. (§13.8.10)
021-135. Same-line multi-placement requires each placement to be self-delimiting: its end is determined without lookahead into the next placement. (§13.8.10)
021-136. A bare type, including any flag run, is self-delimiting: `C`, `G'`, `Pin'!`. (§13.8.10)
021-137. A single-atom `/expr` is self-delimiting: `C/4`, `Filter/cutoff_default`. (§13.8.10)
021-138. An `as` name is self-delimiting, consuming exactly one identifier: `G as a  rest  C`. (§13.8.10)
021-139. A placement carrying an open expression — a `when` predicate, an attribute clause, or a compound `/expr` — is not self-delimiting and must be parenthesized or placed on its own line: `(Sensor as s1 | gain=0.5) (Sensor as s2 | gain=0.7)`. (§13.8.10)
021-140. An unparenthesized open-expression placement sharing a line silently parses as a different construct under the greedy grammar; no placement-specific diagnostic is mandated. (§13.8.10)
021-141. Naming via `as` never requires parentheses in same-line multi-placement; parenthesizing a named placement is a readability convention, not a rule. (§13.8.10)
021-142. A placement introducing its own children body via `:` owns the rest of its line and the following indented block; it cannot share its line with sibling placements. (§13.8.10)
021-143. A `:`-bearing placement may carry inline children on its own line when no sibling placements share the line: `SomePart: Child1 Child2 Child3`. (§13.8.10)

## 022. Conditional Activation (Gates) — 119 Rules

022-1. A gate is a predicate or discriminant that conditions whether a node instance, a connection instance, or an exposed subtree is active. (§13.9)
022-2. Gates are the structural conditional layer, distinct from the value conditionals `if`/`else`/`match`. (§13.9)
022-3. A value conditional evaluates its scrutinee, selects one arm, discards the rest, and yields a value. (§13.9)
022-4. A structural conditional constructs all of its arms and freezes the unselected ones, determining which subtree is live. (§13.9)
022-5. `if`/`else`/`match` remain value selection everywhere they appear, including inside `derived` and `recurrent` expressions, and never gate structure. (§13.9)
022-6. Exactly four surfaces gate structure: type-level `when:`, the placement modifier `when`, the `when` block, and the `given` block. (§13.9)
022-7. Gates gate propagation, never existence: a gated instance is constructed unconditionally under the static-graph rule. (§13.9)
022-8. A gated-off instance is frozen: its cells hold their values and resume when the gate reopens. (§13.9)
022-9. Gates never unmount or reconstruct an instance; runtime-varying existence is exclusively the domain of `repeat`. (§13.9)
022-10. The compiler reasons about the graph under the assumption that any gate may open or close at any commit. (§13.9)
022-11. Routing is not a host concern; gate routing lives in the source, not in the host. (§13.9)
022-12. A `when` predicate is, for reactive evaluation, a derived expression of type `bool`, subject to the same purity, provenance, cycle-detection, and recurrent-read rules as any derived. (§13.9.1)
022-13. The expression forms accepted in a `when` predicate are identical to those accepted in any derived. (§13.9.1)
022-14. Activation is never surfaced as a readable cell or field; source code cannot read gate state as a value. (§13.9.1)
022-15. Being gated off is observable only structurally (the freeze) and, for effects, via the `suspend`/`resume` reconciler hooks delivered to the host. (§13.9.1)
022-16. Activation means gate-state and is distinct from engagement, which is traversal reaching a connection's position in the exposition order. (§13.9.1)
022-17. A gated-off (frozen) connection entry is not engageable; this is the only interaction between gate-state and traversal. (§13.9.1)
022-18. A `when` predicate evaluates in the scope of the construct it modifies: a type-level `when: from.is_emitting` reads the body's own scope. (§13.9.1)
022-19. A node or connection body may declare a `when:` predicate as a schema member in colon form: `when: weight > 0.0`. (§13.9.2)
022-20. At most one `when:` clause is permitted per type; multiple `when:` declarations in one body are a compile error. (§13.9.2)
022-21. Traits cannot declare or require a `when` predicate; a `when:` clause in a trait declaration is a compile error. (§13.9.2)
022-22. A placement may attach a colon-less `when` modifier that introduces or overrides a gate for that one instance: `Logger l1 when debug_enabled`. (§13.9.3)
022-23. Connection placements accept the `when` modifier: `ShowsCount when unread_count > 0: d1`. (§13.9.3)
022-24. Parts placed inside a parent's body carry `when` clauses with the same grammar as top-level placements: `Logger as l2 when my_app.verbose`. (§13.9.3)
022-25. In inline-parts ordering `when` sits after `/Expr` (if present) and before the attribute clause: `Filter f1 / "low-pass" when dsp_mode is DspMode::Realtime | gain=0.5`. (§13.9.3)
022-26. When `/Expr` is absent, the `when` clause follows whatever placement element precedes it. (§13.9.3)
022-27. A `when` predicate must have type `bool`; a non-bool predicate is a compile error. (§13.9.4)
022-28. Beyond the `bool` requirement, `when` predicates follow normal expression scope rules with no special restrictions. (§13.9.4)
022-29. A type-level predicate may reference the type's own cells by bare name plus anything visible at the type's declaration scope (module signals, consts, imports). (§13.9.4)
022-30. A connection's type-level predicate may additionally reference `from`, `to`, and `pair`. (§13.9.4)
022-31. A placement-level predicate may reference the full placement scope: siblings, parent attrs, top-level cells, and any identifier resolvable at that point. (§13.9.4)
022-32. A placement-level `when` totally replaces the type-level `when` for that instance; the two predicates are never conjoined or stacked: `Pulse when my_app.debug_audio: l1`. (§13.9.5)
022-33. A placement needing both predicates must combine them explicitly in the placement-level form: `Pulse when d1.is_emitting and my_app.debug_audio: l1`. (§13.9.5)
022-34. A placement with no `when` modifier inherits the type-level `when`, if any. (§13.9.5)
022-35. A placement with neither a type-level nor a placement-level `when` is unconditional: always active. (§13.9.5)
022-36. A gate predicate may reference cells of the gated instance itself: `when: weight > 0.0` against the instance's own `attr weight`. (§13.9.6)
022-37. A cyclically self-referential gate predicate evaluates against the gated cells' previously-committed values from the prior commit. (§13.9.6)
022-38. A self-conditional gate whose predicate reads a same-instance `recurrent` cannot reopen once closed; recurrent-based self-gates are inherently one-shot latches. (§13.9.6)
022-39. A self-gate reading an `attr` or `signal` can reopen, because input cells keep receiving values from outside the gated subtree. (§13.9.6)
022-40. The gating runtime model is Model B: frozen-when-gated, snap on activation. (§13.9.7)
022-41. The runtime evaluates gate state at edge level on each propagation cycle. (§13.9.7)
022-42. A gate-true edge propagates normally. (§13.9.7)
022-43. Gated subgraphs do no work; a permanently-gated node costs exactly the evaluation of its `when` predicate. (§13.9.7)
022-44. A construct freezes under exactly two conditions: its gate evaluating false, or, for a connection, a dynamic destination resolving to `None`. (§13.9.7)
022-45. An unresolved dynamic destination behaves as gate-false, and resolution returning `Some` behaves as the false-to-true flip, including its propagation event. (§13.9.7)
022-46. Traversal position never freezes a construct. (§13.9.7)
022-47. Incoming connections still deliver into a gated node's input cells; input cells stay live. (§13.9.7)
022-48. A gated node's `when` predicate re-evaluates whenever any cell in its provenance set changes. (§13.9.7)
022-49. A gated node's recurrents do not advance or re-evaluate; they hold their last committed value. (§13.9.7)
022-50. Input changes during a gated period that would have triggered a recurrent are lost: triggers are not queued and gate-open does not replay them. (§13.9.7)
022-51. A recurrent does not recompute at the open snap; it advances only on its next trigger during an active period. (§13.9.7)
022-52. A recurrent's self-history is preserved across a gated gap; the first post-gap evaluation reads pre-gap `.previous`/`.past` values. (§13.9.7)
022-53. A recurrent carrying `@reset_on_reopen` has its history cleared at gate-reopen, so the first post-gap evaluation reads fallbacks. (§13.9.7)
022-54. A gated node's deriveds do not recompute in the steady gated state; they hold their last committed value. (§13.9.7)
022-55. Deriveds read by the `when` predicate stay current while gated: the runtime keeps live the minimum subgraph needed for predicate evaluation. (§13.9.7)
022-56. A gated node's outputs do not propagate; its outgoing connections do not deliver to their destinations. (§13.9.7)
022-57. A gated connection does not propagate at all; its destination receives nothing through that connection. (§13.9.7)
022-58. A false-to-true predicate flip is a propagation event: the frozen deriveds re-evaluate against current upstream state in topological order. (§13.9.7)
022-59. Gated-period values are never replayed at gate-open; they are recomputed as of now, and downstream sees a single jump from frozen to current value. (§13.9.7)
022-60. The gate primitive performs no smoothing of open-snap discontinuities; smoothing is delegated to the parameter system. (§13.9.7)
022-61. On a true-to-false flip the subtree freezes: its cells hold their last values and stop recomputing. (§13.9.7)
022-62. At gate-close the runtime fires the `suspend` reconciler hook for every effect in the newly-frozen subtree, at the commit boundary. (§13.9.7)
022-63. Only effects need close-time handling; the `suspend`/`resume` protocol concerns effects exclusively, freezing being complete for pure nodes and connections. (§13.9.7)
022-64. A gated connection receives no `suspend`, because effects cannot be instantiated in connection bodies. (§13.9.7)
022-65. An effect gated by a `when`/`given` block inside an `effects:` clause freezes and receives `suspend`/`resume` by the same protocol as any gated instance. (§13.9.7)
022-66. An instance is effectively active iff its own gate and every ancestor gate on the path from the root are open. (§13.9.7)
022-67. Closing any ancestor gate transitively freezes every descendant and suspends every effect within it, regardless of the descendants' own gate states. (§13.9.7)
022-68. Reopening an ancestor restores each descendant to its own gate state; a descendant whose own gate is false stays frozen. (§13.9.7)
022-69. Cell reads on gated subgraphs always return a defined value of type `T`, never `Option[T]`. (§13.9.7)
022-70. Reads on a gated instance return frozen values: the last value committed during an active period, or the initial value if never active. (§13.9.7)
022-71. The reactive dependency graph is built independent of gate state; gates suspend propagation at runtime and never remove edges from the static graph. (§13.9.8)
022-72. In per-commit DAG construction, gated edges contribute no dirty propagation to destination output-affecting cells but do contribute to input cells and `when`-predicate provenance. (§13.9.8)
022-73. Gates do not relax the `Circularity` rule: a cycle through `Link` declared `when: false` still requires a `Circularity`-satisfying connection type. (§13.9.9)
022-74. The cycle constraint must hold across all reachable gate configurations; a gated edge is structurally still an edge. (§13.9.9)
022-75. The compile-time cycle check is sound because gated instances are suspended, never removed: every reachable gate configuration is a subset of one static edge set. (§13.9.9)
022-76. A dynamic-destination connection stays inside the compile-time cycle check: its candidate edges are a statically-known set and `Circularity` must hold for every candidate target. (§13.9.9)
022-77. A dynamic destination re-points within a fixed instance set; it creates and drops nothing. (§13.9.9)
022-78. Adding, removing, or modifying a `when` predicate is a reload-safe change; the predicate is structural metadata, not cell identity. (§13.9.10)
022-79. On reload the new predicate participates in the next commit, and cells retain their values. (§13.9.10)
022-80. A reloaded predicate takes effect prospectively only; states that would have differed historically are not recomputed retroactively. (§13.9.10)
022-81. The diagnostic classes for `when` clauses are normative; concrete wording is implementation-defined. (§13.9.11)
022-82. Diagnostic class: non-bool `when` predicate. (§13.9.11)
022-83. Diagnostic class: multiple `when:` clauses in a single type body. (§13.9.11)
022-84. Diagnostic class: `when:` in a trait declaration. (§13.9.11)
022-85. Diagnostic class: unresolved reference in a `when` predicate, surfaced as a standard name-resolution failure in `when`-clause context. (§13.9.11)
022-86. Diagnostic class: cycle through `when` provenance; gate predicates participate in cycle detection identically to deriveds. (§13.9.11)
022-87. The `when` block selects which placements or children are active by boolean condition; all arms are constructed, the live arm propagates, the rest freeze. (§13.9.12)
022-88. The `when` block appears wherever reactive structure is declared — `expose:` clauses, node bodies, placement bodies, `effects:` clauses, effect `desired:` blocks — never as an inline modifier. (§13.9.12)
022-89. Block arms hold whatever entries their context admits: placements at `expose:` and in bodies, effect entries in `effects:`, desired cells in `desired:`. (§13.9.12)
022-90. Each arm body is its own scope: arm-local names nest under the arm and do not collide across arms. (§13.9.12)
022-91. Cells declared in a `desired:` arm are nested and exempt from the flat-namespace uniqueness rule of effect desired blocks. (§13.9.12)
022-92. Simple form: `when cond:` introduces a then-body of placements, and an optional sibling `otherwise:` block supplies the else-body. (§13.9.12)
022-93. In the simple form, while `cond` is true the then-body is active and the `otherwise:` body frozen; when false the roles reverse. (§13.9.12)
022-94. A `when cond:` block with no sibling `otherwise:` gates its then-body alone: active while `cond` holds, frozen otherwise, with no alternative. (§13.9.12)
022-95. Multi-way form: a `when:` header with no condition takes `guard: body` arms plus an optional `otherwise:` arm: `cond_a: ArmA`. (§13.9.12)
022-96. Multi-way arms are tried in declaration order; the first arm whose guard holds is active and all others are frozen. (§13.9.12)
022-97. Every line at the arm indent of a multi-way `when:` block is a guard arm, never a connection entry; connection placements appear only inside arm bodies. (§13.9.12)
022-98. Guards are arbitrary boolean expressions, using the same predicate vocabulary as any `when` predicate. (§13.9.12)
022-99. A `when:` block is an open selector that is never exhaustiveness-checked; its `otherwise:` fallback is optional — if no guard holds and there is no `otherwise:`, no arm is live. (§13.9.12)
022-119. In a `when:` or `given` block the fallback arm — `otherwise:` for `when:`, `default:` for `given:` — must be the last arm; a non-last fallback is a compile error. (§13.9.12)
022-100. Switching the active arm of a `when` block runs the close pass on the deactivating arm and the open snap on the activating arm. (§13.9.12)
022-101. Placements in all arms of a `when`/`given` block in a node or placement body count toward `parts:` cardinality: two arms each placing one `Oscillator` violates `parts: Oscillator [=1]`. (§13.9.12)
022-102. At `expose:` level, block arms do not consume `parts:` cardinality: exposition references already-placed parts and constructs only wrapper placements. (§13.9.12)
022-103. A `when`/`given` block owns its indented body and occupies its own lines; it cannot share a line with sibling placements. (§13.9.12)
022-104. `given` gates among constructed arms by the current variant of a sum-typed scrutinee: `given mode:` with arm `Realtime: RealtimeChain`. (§13.9.13)
022-105. `given` is the structural counterpart of value `match`: same arm shape, but `given` builds all arms and freezes the inactive ones instead of discarding. (§13.9.13)
022-106. `given <scrutinee>:` introduces variant-pattern arms written exactly as value-`match` arms: bare `Pattern: body` with no per-arm keyword. (§13.9.13)
022-107. Inside a `given` block every line at the arm indent is an arm; connection entries appear only within arm bodies, never at arm position. (§13.9.13)
022-108. Each `given` arm body is itself a list of placements indented under the arm. (§13.9.13)
022-109. `given` is admitted in the same contexts as the `when` block, including `effects:` clauses, where each arm body is a list of effect entries. (§13.9.13)
022-110. In an `effects:` clause an arm header `Variant:` (colon form) never collides with a named effect entry `name = …` (equals form). (§13.9.13)
022-111. `given` arms bind variant payloads exactly as `match` patterns do: `Active(session):` binds `session`. (§13.9.13)
022-112. A bound payload is a live reactive projection of the scrutinee's current payload, not a snapshot taken at selection time. (§13.9.13)
022-113. The active `given` arm re-evaluates when its bound payload changes. (§13.9.13)
022-114. At a variant flip the deactivating arm freezes at its last value and does not re-evaluate against the new scrutinee; its payload projection never reads a payload the current variant lacks. (§13.9.13)
022-115. At a variant flip the newly-active arm projects the current variant's payload; the flip follows the standard gate-close and gate-open semantics. (§13.9.13)
022-116. A `given` block is a closed selector: it must be exhaustive over the scrutinee's variants, checked by the `match` exhaustiveness rules. (§13.9.13)
022-117. A `default:` arm in `given` is permitted only as an explicit catch-all that suppresses the exhaustiveness obligation, as in value `match`. (§13.9.13)
022-118. Selecting a type value with `match` over `Type[…]` and gating structure with `given` are both legal: `match` chooses one type placed once; `given` builds every arm and switches which is live. (§13.9.13)

## 023. Reactive Evaluation & Commit — 56 Rules

023-1. The reactive runtime exposes exactly two state operations: writes (signal/attr), which stage new values without evaluation, and commit, which evaluates and atomically publishes a new consistent snapshot. (§13.10)
023-2. A write call (`runtime.write_signal`, `runtime.write_attr`, or any write inside `runtime.transaction`) stages the new value; the staged value is not yet visible to observers. (§13.10.1)
023-3. No derived recomputation and no recurrent advancement happens at write time. (§13.10.1)
023-4. Staged writes accumulate until the next `runtime.commit()`; only the net change from the previous commit matters. (§13.10.1)
023-5. Dirty bits are determined at commit time, not per write. (§13.10.1)
023-6. A write sequence ending with a cell's value equal to its previous-committed value produces no dirty bit and fires no triggers, regardless of intermediate values: `write_signal(x_id, 1); write_signal(x_id, 0); commit()` dirties nothing. (§13.10.1)
023-7. Write staging and net-revert semantics are identical inside and outside a transaction. (§13.10.1)
023-8. Commit step 1 computes the dirty set: a writable cell (signal, attr) is dirty iff its staged value differs from its previously-committed value. (§13.10.2)
023-9. A reactive expression (derived, recurrent, or `observe` arm trigger set) re-evaluates when a referenced cell is dirty; value-change semantics are operationalized as current-commit value ≠ previous-commit value. (§13.10.2)
023-10. Dirty propagation extends to all derived cells transitively dependent on dirty roots. (§13.10.2)
023-11. Dirty propagation extends to all recurrents whose triggers fired this commit. (§13.10.2)
023-12. After dirty-set computation, no new dirty bits are added during the rest of the commit cycle. (§13.10.2)
023-13. Commit step 2 computes evaluation order by topologically sorting the per-commit DAG. (§13.10.2)
023-14. The per-commit DAG's nodes are the dirty derived expressions plus each recurrent whose expression became dirty this commit. (§13.10.2)
023-15. A recurrent contributes one DAG node per cell, or one node per tuple group for tuple-coupled recurrents. (§13.10.2)
023-16. A recurrent wrapped in `observe` contributes the whole observe expression as its DAG node, with arm selection deferred to evaluation. (§13.10.2)
023-17. In the per-commit DAG, recurrent self-history reads (`.previous`/`.past`) are treated as inputs at their previous-committed values, which breaks reactive cycles. (§13.10.2)
023-18. Reads of deriveds, signals, attrs, and streams follow normal dependency edges within the commit. (§13.10.2)
023-19. A dependency edge whose gate predicate evaluates false does not propagate to destination outputs. (§13.10.2)
023-20. Commit step 3 evaluates each DAG node in topological order by invoking its behavior through the runtime ABI. (§13.10.2)
023-21. During commit evaluation, signal and attr reads resolve to the current staged values (most recent writes since the previous commit). (§13.10.2)
023-22. During commit evaluation, a derived read resolves to the this-commit value if that derived was already evaluated this commit, and to its previously-committed value if it is not in the dirty set. (§13.10.2)
023-23. Recurrent self-history reads (`.previous`/`.past`) always resolve to previous-committed values (lockstep). (§13.10.2)
023-24. Derived behaviors write their results into the pending snapshot. (§13.10.2)
023-25. Recurrent expression results are held aside, invisible to in-pass evaluation, until the recurrent-advancement step. (§13.10.2)
023-26. `observe` arm selection stage one (activation): the candidate arms are those whose trigger set fired this commit and whose `where` filter, if present, passes. (§13.10.2)
023-27. `observe` arm selection stage two: the first candidate arm in declaration order wins, and its expression evaluates to produce the observe's value for the commit. (§13.10.2)
023-28. Non-selected candidate arms' expressions are not evaluated. (§13.10.2)
023-29. Commit step 4 writes the values computed during evaluation into the recurrent cells; after this step, recurrent reads return the newly-advanced values. (§13.10.2)
023-30. Commit step 5 atomically publishes the just-committed values as the visible snapshot, so observers' subsequent reads return the new state. (§13.10.2)
023-31. Commit step 6 clears the dirty bits. (§13.10.2)
023-32. Writes during a commit are forbidden. (§13.10.2)
023-33. When two same-level DAG nodes have no dependency between them, the tiebreaker is deterministic source declaration order: the cell declared earlier evaluates first. (§13.10.3)
023-34. For same-level cells across different node instances, the tiebreaker is placement order at construction time. (§13.10.3)
023-35. Recurrent expressions across multiple recurrent cells evaluate in lockstep, with no observable internal ordering between them. (§13.10.3)
023-36. `runtime.transaction` batches multiple writes into one logical change: `runtime.transaction(|tx| { tx.write_signal(a_id, new_a) })`. (§13.10.4)
023-37. Writes within a transaction accumulate as staged values applied atomically at transaction close. (§13.10.4)
023-38. A panic during a transaction closure follows trap-track semantics: the process aborts, with no rollback. (§13.10.4)
023-39. Nested transactions are flattened: only the outermost `runtime.transaction` commits, and inner `runtime.transaction` calls are no-ops with respect to commit. (§13.10.4)
023-40. All writes since the outer transaction's start are committed together at the outer close. (§13.10.4)
023-41. `tx.abort()` rolls back the transaction's accumulated writes, restoring staged values to their pre-transaction state, while the closure returns normally. (§13.10.4)
023-42. `tx.abort()` is the only rollback path. (§13.10.4)
023-43. Transaction close applies the staged writes; evaluation and visibility happen only at the next `runtime.commit()`. (§13.10.4)
023-44. Outside transactions, each individual `runtime.write_*` call behaves as its own one-write transaction. (§13.10.4)
023-45. A dynamic dependency is one whose depended-on cell identity can change between commits. (§13.10.5)
023-46. Handle resolution — individually or in its collective dynamic-namespace form — is the only source of dynamic dependency in the language. (§13.10.5)
023-47. A read through a `Handle` reaches whichever entity the handle currently resolves to, and the dependency flips on re-point, mount, and dismount. (§13.10.5)
023-48. The cell or expression producing a dynamic target reference (e.g. a connection's `to`) is itself part of the reading body's provenance. (§13.10.5)
023-49. When a dynamic target re-points, deriveds reading through it (e.g. a connection's `to.*` reads) are marked dirty and re-evaluate in the next commit against the new target's cells. (§13.10.5)
023-50. While a connection's `to` designates a particular node, the connection's reads subscribe to that node's cells; on re-point the subscription is re-established against the new target, and a write to the old target no longer dirties the connection. (§13.10.5)
023-51. The same two-part mechanism (reference as dependency; referent cells as dependencies only while selected) governs every handle read, including node-body deriveds reading `repeat`-view handles. (§13.10.5)
023-52. A dismount flips a handle's resolution to `None` and drops the referent-cell subscription. (§13.10.5)
023-53. Dynamic namespace cells are the collective form of handle resolution: an operator over `incoming.<C>` subscribes to the membership cell itself and, per current member, to the cells its per-element fn reads. (§13.10.5)
023-54. A mount or dismount re-establishes dynamic-namespace member subscriptions exactly as a re-point does. (§13.10.5)
023-55. Dynamic dependencies do not alter per-commit evaluation order: within any one commit every handle resolution is fixed, so that commit's dependency graph is static. (§13.10.5)
023-56. The candidate set of possible handle targets is statically known, which keeps topology-cycle analysis a compile-time check. (§13.10.5)

## 024. Cycle Handling — 27 Rules

024-1. Cycles are handled at two distinct layers with separate rules: reactive expression cycles between reactive cells, and topology cycles between node instances via connection placements. (§13.11)
024-2. The compiler builds the reactive dependency graph by walking every `derived` body and every recurrent expression body, recording the set of reactive cells each reads. (§13.11.1)
024-3. Reactive-dependency edges run from each read cell to the reading expression's output cell. (§13.11.1)
024-4. Signal, attr, derived, and recurrent reads all contribute reactive-dependency edges. (§13.11.1)
024-5. The reactive dependency graph is the basis for the per-commit DAG constructed at each commit. (§13.11.1)
024-6. A cycle consisting only of derived-to-derived edges is a compile error (instantaneous cycle): derived `a.x` reading `b.y` while `b.y` reads `a.x` is rejected. (§13.11.2)
024-7. A recurrent's expression may read the recurrent's own previous value: `on t: x + 1`. (§13.11.2)
024-8. A recurrent's expression may read another recurrent cell's previous value: `on t: other.value`. (§13.11.2)
024-9. A static cycle passing through a recurrent is legal: a derived may read a recurrent whose expression reads that derived back, since each commit the derived sees the recurrent's last-committed value. (§13.11.2)
024-10. The per-commit DAG is formed by treating every recurrent read as an input valued at the previous commit's result, which breaks all valid reactive cycles. (§13.11.3)
024-11. Reads OF a recurrent cell from any expression context — derived bodies, recurrent expressions, `where` guards — always return the previous-committed value. (§13.11.3)
024-12. Reads FROM a `where` guard (the cells the guard's own expression reads) are not previous-commit inputs: the guard evaluates in topological order during commit evaluation (step 3), reading the freshly-recomputed this-commit values, to determine whether its arm fires. (§13.11.3)
024-13. A recurrent cell on a cycle behaves as a one-commit delay element: reads see the previous-committed value regardless of what its expression computes this commit. (§13.11.4)
024-14. Delay-element behavior is fully specified at the language level; the runtime requires no per-implementation convention beyond the `recurrent` declaration. (§13.11.4)
024-15. A topology cycle is a cycle in the topology graph and is analyzed at compile time over every possible wiring. (§13.11.5)
024-16. Topology-graph nodes are placed instances; each placed connection contributes a directed edge from its `from`-endpoint instance to its `to`-endpoint instance, labeled with the connection type. (§13.11.5)
024-17. A connection whose destination is a dynamic reference contributes candidate edges to each node type the reference's static type admits, analyzed over node types as a sound over-approximation of their instances. (§13.11.5)
024-18. Candidate-envelope inference is by static type: a concrete node type contributes itself, a trait contributes every node type satisfying it (including via a supertrait), and the bare `Node` bound contributes all node types. (§13.11.5)
024-19. A topology cycle is a sequence of distinct directed edges returning to its starting node. (§13.11.5)
024-20. Every topology cycle in the construction-time node graph must traverse at least one connection whose type satisfies `Circularity`; cycles consisting only of non-`Circularity` connections are compile errors. (§13.11.5)
024-21. A dynamic reference can only resolve to an already-placed node of one of its envelope's types, so the candidate set is statically known. (§13.11.5)
024-22. Every node type in a connection's candidate envelope must declare `incoming: dynamic` for that connection type (the membership-marker rule). (§13.11.5)
024-23. The compile-time cycle check verifies that every cycle in the candidate graph traverses a `Circularity` connection, so no reachable wiring can form a non-`Circularity` cycle. (§13.11.5)
024-24. Candidate-edge cycle analysis is sound but conservative in proportion to the width of the candidate set. (§13.11.5)
024-25. `Circularity` is a marker trait whose semantic effect is domain-defined. (§13.11.5)
024-26. Connection types that imply simultaneous source-destination activation should not satisfy `Circularity`. (§13.11.5)
024-27. Topology-cycle validation and reactive-expression-cycle validation are independent passes; either kind of cycle can exist without the other. (§13.11.5)

## 025. Reactivity Boundary & Cell Storage — 66 Rules
