025-1. The reactivity boundary determines which expressions become reactive and which remain ordinary computation. (§13.12)
025-2. The compiler computes for each expression a provenance set: the reactive cells (signals, attrs, recurrents, derived results) it reads, including transitively through function calls and field accesses. (§13.12.1)
025-3. An expression is reactive iff its provenance set is non-empty. (§13.12.1)
025-4. A derived's dependency set, used by dirty-bit propagation, is decided from its provenance set. (§13.12.1)
025-5. Diagnostics rejecting reactivity where compile-time values are required give precise provenance blame (which cell makes the value reactive, and where). (§13.12.1)
025-6. A provenance set is normally static; the sole exception is a read through handle resolution, where the identity of the depended-on cells changes on re-point, mount, or dismount. (§13.12.1)
025-7. Under handle resolution, the resolution itself is in the provenance, and a referent's cells are in the provenance only while that referent is resolved (a member's cells only while it is a member). (§13.12.1)
025-8. A function body is not itself reactive: it takes parameters as ordinary values and returns ordinary values. (§13.12.2)
025-9. Reactivity emerges at the call site: a reactive expression calling `some_fn(signal_a, signal_b)` includes `signal_a` and `signal_b` in its provenance set. (§13.12.2)
025-10. When a signal argument changes, the containing reactive expression re-evaluates and re-runs the function with the new concrete values; the function never observes the signal itself. (§13.12.2)
025-11. Reactive transparency applies to `fn` declarations only; operators are not reactive-transparent and allocate cells per instantiation. (§13.12.2)
025-12. The call-site distinction is by callee declaration kind: `some_fn(my_signal)` evaluates per-emission, while `some_op(my_signal)` instantiates an operator with internal state. (§13.12.2)
025-13. A parameter declared with a cell type (`Cell[T]`) binds to the cell reference itself rather than to its current value: `fn some_fn(s: Cell[T])`. (§13.12.2)
025-14. Per-emission versus cell-reference parameter binding is distinguished solely by the declared parameter type, with no call-site syntactic difference. (§13.12.2)
025-15. A function whose body directly reads a reactive cell passes that provenance to its return value, so callers gain the directly-read cells: `shifted(base_value)` reading signal `global_offset` yields provenance `{base_value, global_offset}`. (§13.12.2.1)
025-16. Provenance analysis is transitive through function calls; module-level globals read by called functions are included. (§13.12.2.1)
025-17. When a function branches on its arguments, the provenance contributions of all branches are computed independently and unioned: branch A reading X and branch B reading Y contributes `{X, Y}` to callers. (§13.12.2.2)
025-18. Branch-union provenance is a conservative over-approximation that may cause unnecessary re-evaluation but never under-tracks dependencies. (§13.12.2.2)
025-19. A closure constructed with a reactive value in scope captures the value at construction time as a snapshot, not the live cell: `fn(x: f32): x > current_threshold` captures the snapshotted `f32`. (§13.12.3)
025-20. Calling a closure later does not observe changes made to the source signal after capture. (§13.12.3)
025-21. Closures are not reactive: they do not participate in the dependency graph. (§13.12.3)
025-22. Reactive cells (signal, attr, recurrent, derived) accept any value type. (§13.12.4)
025-23. The compiler determines each cell's storage strategy from the value type's size and shape. (§13.12.4)
025-24. A type whose size is at most the platform's atomic word width (typically 8 bytes) is stored directly in the cell. (§13.12.4)
025-25. Publishing a direct-storage cell at commit requires no allocation, no refcount, and no pool. (§13.12.4)
025-26. A backend may store 9–16-byte types across two consecutive cells where the platform supports it, otherwise such types fall back to index-based storage; this is a backend choice, not a language requirement. (§13.12.4)
025-27. `Result[T, E]` and `Option[T]` store directly when discriminant plus maximum payload fits the atomic word: `Option[i32]` is direct (1-bit tag + 4 bytes). (§13.12.4)
025-28. A type larger than the atomic word is stored as a one-word pool index into a per-type pool: the cell holds the index and the value lives in the pool. (§13.12.4)
025-29. Dynamically-sized types (`string`, `Vec[T]`, `HashMap[K, V]`, and other heap-allocated dynamic-size collections) always use index-based storage. (§13.12.4)
025-30. `string` cells use the string pool; other dynamic types use per-type pools generated by the compiler. (§13.12.4)
025-31. Pool-entry refcounting manages lifetime: when the cell is overwritten and no consumer holds a reference, the pool slot is released. (§13.12.4)
025-32. Pools are per-type, each sized at runtime construction from the IR. (§13.12.4)
025-33. Committing an index-stored cell publishes the one-word pool index unchanged. (§13.12.4)
025-34. Writing a pool-stored cell allocates or reuses a pool slot, copies the value in, and stages the index — work proportional to the value's size plus a pool acquire. (§13.12.4)
025-35. Reading a pool-stored cell resolves the pool index to reach the value. (§13.12.4)
025-36. Real-time hot paths (audio DSP, animation) should prefer cell types that fit direct storage, since pool storage involves allocation and refcounting. (§13.12.4)
025-37. Direct storage has zero overhead versus plain atomic reads and writes. (§13.12.4)
025-38. Index storage adds an indirection on read and a pool allocation on write. (§13.12.4)
025-39. A direct-stored value narrower than the cell width occupies one cell with appropriate padding or extension. (§13.12.4)
025-40. The stdlib provides three dynamic-size collection types usable as reactive cell types — `Vec[T]`, `SmallVec[T, N]`, `RingBuf[T, N]` — each using index-based pool storage. (§13.12.4)
025-41. The dynamic-collection cost model is normative: implementations must achieve the documented complexity bounds. (§13.12.4)
025-42. `Vec[T]` is a persistent trie with structural sharing across versions: append/push O(log32 n), read by index O(log32 n). (§13.12.4)
025-43. `SmallVec[T, N]` stores up to N elements inline and uses the heap beyond: append O(n) bounded, read by index O(1). (§13.12.4)
025-44. `RingBuf[T, N]` is a fixed-capacity ring that drops the oldest element when full: append O(1), read by index O(1). (§13.12.4)
025-45. In `SmallVec[T, N]` and `RingBuf[T, N]`, `N` is a const-generic slot-count parameter supplied as a value at use sites, not a second type parameter: `SmallVec[i32, 8]`. (§13.12.4)
025-46. A const-generic parameter is marked by the `const` keyword in the declaration: `type SmallVec[T, const N: usize]`. (§13.12.4)
025-47. Generic parameter lists use commas throughout, never semicolons. (§13.12.4)
025-48. `Vec[T]` is the default collection for unbounded growth. (§13.12.4)
025-49. `vec.with(value)` returns a new `Vec[T]` with `value` appended, preserving the no-mutation rule. (§13.12.4)
025-50. `vec + value` is the operator form equivalent to `vec.with(value)`. (§13.12.4)
025-51. A recurrent's expression returns the new collection value, and the runtime publishes it as the cell's next committed value. (§13.12.4)
025-52. Collection implementation strategies (persistent trie, inline+heap, fixed ring) must be observably indistinguishable from always-returns-new semantics; sharing and in-place optimization are runtime concerns transparent at the language level. (§13.12.4)
025-53. If user code never references `Vec[T]`, `SmallVec[T, N]`, or `RingBuf[T, N]`, the runtime cost is zero: the reactive state buffer remains a flat fixed-size table and the extensible pool machinery is not exercised. (§13.12.4)
025-54. Binary size grows only when the dynamic collection types are used. (§13.12.4)
025-55. `dyn` trait objects are not permitted as reactive cell types in v1. (§13.12.4)
025-56. Functions and closures are not permitted as reactive cell types in v1. (§13.12.4)
025-57. For collections of reactive things, composition via parts — a parent node with N parts of the same child type, each holding its own attrs and deriveds — is the canonical mechanism. (§13.12.4)
025-58. Collection-typed reactive cells work via pool storage, but each write rebuilds or replaces the whole collection — suited to batch updates, not fine-grained mutation. (§13.12.4)
025-59. A fixed-extent cell type (`T[N]`, tuples, records) has compile-time-known layout: every element's offset within the cell, or within the pool slot for sizes above the word, is determined at compile time. (§13.12.4)
025-60. A `for` iterating a fixed-extent cell's value compile-time-unrolls to element reads at known offsets — no runtime loop counter, bounds check, or per-iteration dispatch: `for x in buf.value():` over `Cell[f32[64]]`. (§13.12.4)
025-61. Direct-storage fixed-extent cells are read with no indirection; index-storage ones with one indirection (resolving the current pool index) plus compile-time-known offsets. (§13.12.4)
025-62. A fixed-extent cell's slot is pre-allocated at runtime construction (directly or as a fixed-size pool slot); the runtime writes the new value into reserved capacity (double-buffered / index-flipped) so the previous-committed value stays readable until the atomic publish, which is a constant-time index flip — no per-emission allocation, realloc, or resize. (§13.12.4)
025-63. A reactive value cannot be used where a compile-time-known value is required. (§13.12.5)
025-64. A reactive array size is a compile error: `i32[some_signal]` ✗. (§13.12.5)
025-65. A reactive value flowing into a const-generic argument position is a compile error: `Buffer[some_signal]` ✗. (§13.12.5)
025-66. A `const` declaration whose right-hand side is reactive is a compile error. (§13.12.5)

## 026. Reactive Error Handling — 12 Rules

026-1. The two-track failure model applies uniformly to reactive contexts. (§13.13)
026-2. A derived or recurrent expression that traps during evaluation — arithmetic overflow under default operators, division by zero, out-of-range array index, or explicit `panic` — aborts the process. (§13.13.1)
026-3. The runtime does not isolate traps within behavior invocations. (§13.13.1)
026-4. There is no errored-cell sentinel state at the runtime level. (§13.13.1)
026-5. There is no `catch_unwind` boundary and no continuation past a trap. (§13.13.1)
026-6. Recoverable failures in reactive code use value-track errors: declare the derived's type as `Result[T, E]` or `Option[T]` and produce `Err(...)` or `None` explicitly for failure cases. (§13.13.2)
026-7. Downstream reactive expressions propagate value-track errors through `?` or `match`. (§13.13.2)
026-8. Arithmetic that may overflow but should fail recoverably uses the checked operator variants (`+?`, `-?`, ...), whose `Option[T]` results flow through the type system. (§13.13.2)
026-9. Where an `Option` is provably `Some` — most often a `Handle` whose referents are all statically placed — postfix `!` unwraps it with no runtime check and no propagation. (§13.13.2)
026-10. Postfix `!` is a compile error wherever the compiler cannot prove the `None` case impossible. (§13.13.2)
026-11. The reactive evaluation context does not modify trap semantics: a behavior that traps aborts the process, the same as a free-function trap. (§13.13.3)
026-12. The language provides no hidden recovery mechanism in reactive contexts; graceful handling requires value-track errors. (§13.13.3)

## 027. Runtime Interface — 122 Rules

027-1. The runtime interface is the normative contract every backend must satisfy — the abstract counterpart to the IR the compiler emits. (§13.14)
027-2. The shape of the runtime interface is normative; the host-language binding is implementation-defined. (§13.14)
027-3. A runtime is a transactional store of reactive cells with snapshot isolation. (§13.14)
027-4. Deriveds are materialized views of the runtime's transactional store. (§13.14)
027-5. Side effects are reconciled at the commit boundary. (§13.14)
027-6. The core verbs `write`/`push` stage external input into the runtime. (§13.14)
027-7. The core verb `commit` settles all dependents glitch-free, advances the version, and publishes a consistent snapshot. (§13.14)
027-8. The core verbs `acquire_snapshot` + `read` observe the latest published snapshot. (§13.14)
027-9. The core verb `transaction` groups writes atomically. (§13.14)
027-10. The core verb `register_reconciler` installs effect hooks. (§13.14)
027-11. The core verb `teardown` shuts the reactive graph down. (§13.14)
027-12. The runtime calls behaviors and reconciler hooks back into host code. (§13.14)
027-13. A backend advertises optional capabilities beyond the mandatory core; a host queries what a runtime supports rather than assuming the union. (§13.14)
027-14. The observability and cadence cell hints bias how a backend stores cells. (§13.14)
027-15. A runtime need only honor the verbs' observable contract; the mechanism is its own choice. (§13.14)
027-16. Startup step 1 loads the IR (per §15.4). (§13.14.1)
027-17. Startup step 2 allocates cell storage (per §14.3). (§13.14.1)
027-18. Startup initializes all reactive cells and evaluates all `when` predicates in topological order over their init-time read dependencies, per §13.2.6's startup pass rules. (§13.14.1)
027-19. At startup, signal cells receive their declared initial values. (§13.14.1)
027-20. At startup, attr cells receive their declared defaults or placement-supplied values. (§13.14.1)
027-21. At startup, recurrent cells evaluate their expressions for the first time, with `.previous`/`.past` returning their fallback values since no history exists. (§13.14.1)
027-22. At startup, derived cells are computed by evaluating their expression bodies. (§13.14.1)
027-23. At startup, stream cells begin empty. (§13.14.1)
027-24. At startup, `when` predicates are evaluated to determine each instance's initial gate state. (§13.14.1)
027-25. Placement-level `when` predicates evaluate alongside type-level `when:` predicates in the same topological startup pass. (§13.14.1)
027-26. At startup, a placement-level `when` overrides the type-level predicate and evaluates in its placement scope rather than the type's own scope. (§13.14.1)
027-27. Startup initialization is not separated into per-declaration-kind phases; the topological sort alone determines order. (§13.14.1)
027-28. The final startup step performs the first commit, publishing the initial snapshot; observers' subsequent `acquire_snapshot` calls return real data. (§13.14.1)
027-29. The runtime is "constructing" until the first commit and "live" only after it completes. (§13.14.1)
027-30. Observer reads before the first commit return a sentinel or block, per implementation choice. (§13.14.1)
027-31. In steady state, host writes (`write_signal`, `write_attr`, `transaction`) only stage values and mark dirty; no evaluation runs at write time. (§13.14.1)
027-32. In steady state, `runtime.commit()` settles dirty cells, advances recurrents, publishes a new snapshot for observers, and then fires reconciler hooks (which observe the just-published snapshot, §13.14.9). (§13.14.1)
027-33. Shutdown step 1 stops accepting new signal/attr writes. (§13.14.1)
027-34. Shutdown step 2 drains any in-flight commit: the current commit, if running, completes. (§13.14.1)
027-35. Shutdown step 3 drops reactive cells in reverse-of-construction order per §14.7's Drop rules: connections before their endpoint instances; within an instance, attrs, recurrents, and deriveds in reverse declaration order. (§13.14.1)
027-36. During shutdown, each live effect receives reconciler `teardown`. (§13.14.1)
027-37. Shutdown step 4 drops top-level signals. (§13.14.1)
027-38. Shutdown step 5 drops string pool entries (per §14.5). (§13.14.1)
027-39. Shutdown step 6 releases cell storage. (§13.14.1)
027-40. After shutdown the runtime is terminated; subsequent observer reads return a sentinel. (§13.14.1)
027-41. `runtime.write_signal` is one overloaded call dispatched by arity: `write_signal(signal_id, v)` (module-level) or `write_signal(instance_id, signal_id, v)` (per-instance). (§13.14.2)
027-42. A `write_signal` call is synchronous and inexpensive: it records the pending value and marks dependents dirty. (§13.14.2)
027-43. No evaluation runs at `write_signal` time; evaluation happens at the next `commit`. (§13.14.2)
027-44. The module-level `write_signal` form targets a module-scope signal, for which one cell exists for the entire program. (§13.14.2)
027-45. The per-instance `write_signal` form targets an effect's `observed:` signal on one effect instance; each effect instance has its own observed cells and the write targets that one cell. (§13.14.2)
027-46. Both `write_signal` arities must be called from the runtime's driving context — the single context permitted to write, evaluate, and commit. (§13.14.2)
027-47. Other threads write only indirectly, by enqueueing requests for the driving context to apply — a host-application concern. (§13.14.2)
027-48. Signal IDs and instance IDs are stable, assigned at compile time, and obtained from the IR. (§13.14.2)
027-49. `runtime.write_attr(instance_id, attr_id, value)` stages a value for one instance's attr cell, behaving identically to per-instance `write_signal`: synchronous, staged-only, dirty propagation, no evaluation. (§13.14.3)
027-50. `write_attr` applies to attrs on node instances and connection instances alike; both instance kinds share one ID space. (§13.14.3)
027-51. `runtime.commit()` performs the complete commit operation of §13.10.2: it settles all dirty deriveds and recurrent expressions glitch-free in topological order and advances recurrents. (§13.14.4)
027-52. `commit` fires `suspend`/`resume` for effects whose effective activation changed. (§13.14.4)
027-53. `commit` is the sole point at which staged writes become observable, publishing a new consistent snapshot. (§13.14.4)
027-54. `commit` is synchronous, runs on the driving context, and returns when the commit completes. (§13.14.4)
027-55. Settling work in a commit is bounded by the dirty set (deriveds and recurrents with fired triggers). (§13.14.4)
027-56. The cost of making a new snapshot visible is backend-defined and not asserted to be constant. (§13.14.4)
027-57. A commit with no dirty cells is idempotent: a fresh snapshot is published with identical values. (§13.14.4)
027-58. The host chooses the commit cadence (per audio block, frame, or event); the runtime imposes none. (§13.14.4)
027-59. `runtime.transaction` provides atomic grouping of writes: `runtime.transaction(|tx| { tx.write_signal(a_id, new_a) })`. (§13.14.5)
027-60. A transaction's closure executes synchronously; its writes stage atomically at closure completion. (§13.14.5)
027-61. The full rules for transaction atomicity, panic-on-abort, nesting flattening, and `tx.abort()` rollback are those specified in §13.10.4; `runtime.transaction` is their invocation form. (§13.14.5)
027-62. Transactions give atomicity of grouped writes only — settlement and publication of the staged writes happen at the next `runtime.commit()`. (§13.14.5)
027-63. `runtime.acquire_snapshot() -> Snapshot` returns a consistent snapshot of the latest committed state. (§13.14.6)
027-64. Every cell read from one snapshot is coherent with every other; no torn or cross-cell-inconsistent reads occur. (§13.14.6)
027-65. Snapshot reads are wait-free. (§13.14.6)
027-66. A snapshot remains valid until the observer next acquires one; a later call obtains a newer snapshot if a commit occurred in the interim. (§13.14.6)
027-67. Multiple snapshots may be held concurrently; acquiring or holding a snapshot never blocks a `commit`. (§13.14.6)
027-68. `runtime.register_reconciler(effect_type_name, reconciler)` registers a host-side reconciler for one effect type. (§13.14.7)
027-69. `register_reconciler` must be called during a pre-live window — initial boot before the runtime goes live, or a hot reload's pre-live phase before the reload reaches the live state; registrations once live and outside a reload window are rejected. (§13.14.7)
027-70. The `effect_type_name` argument is a string matching the name of an `effect` declaration in the loaded source. (§13.14.7)
027-71. The *create* hook fires when a new effect instance enters the live graph; it receives the instance ID, current parameter values, and initial `desired:` values, and returns opaque reconciler-side instance state. (§13.14.7)
027-72. The *update* hook fires when any parameter or `desired:` cell of an existing instance becomes dirty; it receives the instance ID, the instance state, and current parameter/desired values. (§13.14.7)
027-73. The *update* hook writes its alignment outcome into observed cells via `runtime.write_signal` or `runtime.push_stream`. (§13.14.7)
027-74. The *teardown* hook fires when an effect instance leaves scope; it receives the instance ID and instance state and releases external resources. (§13.14.7)
027-75. The *suspend* hook fires when the instance's enclosing subtree gates off (effective activation goes false); its contract is to release the external resource while preserving the reconciler-side instance state. (§13.14.7)
027-76. What "release" means under *suspend* (close, cancel, flush, or keep warm) is the reconciler's decision; the runtime guarantees only that the signal is delivered. (§13.14.7)
027-77. The *resume* hook fires when the enclosing subtree gates back on; it re-acquires the external resource from the preserved instance state. (§13.14.7)
027-78. `suspend` then `resume` is a round-trip that does not reinitialize instance state; only `teardown` followed by a fresh `create` does. (§13.14.7)
027-79. The host-language binding of the reconciler interface is implementation-defined. (§13.14.7)
027-80. Normatively, the five reconciler hooks must be invokable by the runtime at the commit boundary, with per-instance state managed by the host between invocations. (§13.14.7)
027-81. For a generic effect, reconciler registration is per-effect-type-per-concrete-instantiation; the `effect_type_name` carries a mangled suffix encoding the resolved type parameters. (§13.14.7)
027-82. Generic-effect instantiations without a registered reconciler are detected at startup and cause the runtime to refuse the live transition. (§13.14.7)
027-83. If the program declares an effect type with no registered reconciler, startup fails with a diagnostic naming the effect type and the runtime does not enter the live state. (§13.14.7)
027-84. `runtime.push_stream` pushes a value into a stream cell, dispatched by arity: `push_stream(instance_id, stream_id, v)` (per-instance) or `push_stream(stream_id, v)` (module-level). (§13.14.8)
027-85. `push_stream` serves host-side reconcilers writing effect `observed:` stream cells and host code producing into top-level streams whose source is host-defined. (§13.14.8)
027-86. A push to a `ring` stream always succeeds; on a full buffer the oldest unconsumed event is overwritten and `dropped_total` increments. (§13.14.8)
027-87. A push to a `gate` stream succeeds only if a slot is free; on failure `rejected_total` increments. (§13.14.8)
027-88. A `gate` push returns a `bool` indicating success or failure; the host decides how to handle rejection. (§13.14.8)
027-89. A push is dirty-tracked: stream consumers become dirty and the pushed event becomes visible to them at the next commit. (§13.14.8)
027-90. The per-instance `push_stream` form targets a stream cell scoped to one specific effect or node instance; `stream_id` identifies the stream cell on that instance's type. (§13.14.8)
027-91. The module-level `push_stream` form targets a top-level, module-scope stream cell. (§13.14.8)
027-92. The runtime maintains a one-to-one correspondence between live effect instances and reconciler-side instance states. (§13.14.9)
027-93. Hooks align with the instance lifecycle: `create` on scope entry, `teardown` on scope exit, `update` on dirty parameters/`desired:` during a commit, `suspend` on gate-off, `resume` on gate-on. (§13.14.9)
027-94. Only scope death triggers `teardown`, which drops the reconciler-side instance state. (§13.14.9)
027-95. A suspended instance whose scope dies receives `teardown` directly, with no preceding `resume`. (§13.14.9)
027-96. A reconciler must tolerate `teardown` arriving while the instance is suspended; teardown subsumes the release the suspend already performed. (§13.14.9)
027-97. The runtime never delivers `resume` to an instance that is leaving scope. (§13.14.9)
027-98. Reconciler hooks fire at the commit boundary: after the commit publishes its snapshot and before the next commit begins. (§13.14.9)
027-99. Hooks run on the runtime's driving context; reconcilers must not block it with long-running operations (dispatch to host-managed workers and write results back via the interface). (§13.14.9)
027-100. Reconciler writes into observed cells via `runtime.write_signal`/`runtime.push_stream` are dirty-tracked normally: they stage and become visible to program-side observers on the next commit. (§13.14.9)
027-101. Reconcilers are expected to be idempotent in the reconciliation sense: applying the same desired state twice produces the same alignment outcome. (§13.14.9)
027-102. The runtime may invoke `update` with unchanged desired values when a commit dirties an instance for unrelated reasons; reconcilers must not produce duplicate side effects in that case. (§13.14.9)
027-103. The reconciler's host-side state is the canonical record of what has already been done; `desired:` cells describe only what should be true. (§13.14.9)
027-104. The runtime delivers `suspend` only on a gate-close transition and `resume` only on a gate-open transition, never repeated for the same transition. (§13.14.9)
027-105. A reconciler should treat a redundant `suspend` on an already-released resource, or a redundant `resume` on an already-acquired one, as a no-op. (§13.14.9)
027-106. A `suspend` may arrive for an instance whose `desired:` already implied no resource; it is then a no-op on the resource and serves only to mark the instance frozen. (§13.14.9)
027-107. Reconciler errors are reported to the program through the effect's `observed:` cells, typically an `error: Signal[Option[E]]` cell. (§13.14.9)
027-108. Reconcilers do not panic the runtime; reconciler-internal errors are domain errors expressed through the value track. (§13.14.9)
027-109. A reconciler panic is a host bug; the runtime's behavior is then implementation-defined (typically propagate to the host thread, mark the instance failed, write an error sentinel to a declared observed error cell). (§13.14.9)
027-110. The runtime invokes each behavior by its content-addressed id with the current values of its input cells and receives the output; it never reaches inside. (§13.14.10)
027-111. Behavior inputs are passed per the behavior's declared ownership modes. (§13.14.10)
027-112. The behavior callback and the reconciler hooks are the whole of what the runtime calls back into host code. (§13.14.10)
027-113. `runtime.teardown()` runs the shutdown sequence: drains any in-flight commit, delivers reconciler `teardown` to every live effect, drops all cells in reverse-construction order, and releases storage; afterward the runtime is terminated. (§13.14.10)
027-114. The mandatory core comprises `write`/`push`, `commit`, `acquire_snapshot`/`read`, `transaction`, `register_reconciler`, `teardown`, the behavior ABI, and the reconciler hooks. (§13.14.11)
027-115. Using a capability the runtime does not advertise is a load-time error, not silent misbehavior. (§13.14.11)
027-116. The cross-thread observation capability permits `acquire_snapshot`/`read` from threads other than the driving context, honoring the `cross_thread_*` observability classes. (§13.14.11)
027-117. A single-threaded runtime omits the cross-thread observation capability. (§13.14.11)
027-118. `confined` cells never require the cross-thread observation capability. (§13.14.11)
027-119. The hot reload capability exposes `runtime.reload(diff)`, applying an IR diff to the live graph: cells/scopes matched by path with state preserved, changed behaviors swapped by content-hash, added/removed nodes mounted/unmounted. (§13.14.11)
027-120. The persistence capability lets the runtime serialize and restore live graph state across runs. (§13.14.11)
027-121. The real-time bounds capability honors the `realtime` cadence hint with bounded, non-blocking observation. (§13.14.11)
027-122. The `observability` and `cadence_hint` cell fields are inputs to capability negotiation: the runtime selects storage that honors them or rejects a graph it cannot serve. (§13.14.11)

## 028. Hot Reload — 74 Rules

028-1. The runtime supports hot reload of the reactive graph when the host provides updated source code (per §14.8). (§13.15)
028-2. Before any runtime-side reload action, the new source must compile under the full type system and reactive-system rules. (§13.15.1)
028-3. If reload compilation fails for any reason — including dangling references to removed nodes — the reload is rejected and the runtime continues the previously-loaded version unaffected. (§13.15.1)
028-4. Reactive cells are identified across reloads by fully-qualified declaration path — module path, instance name, cell name: `audio.synth_a.osc_1.frequency`. (§13.15.2)
028-5. Anonymous or duplicated sibling placements get an ordinal suffix `:N` — the zero-based declaration-order index among same-type siblings at the same nesting depth. (§13.15.2)
028-6. A cell with the same fully-qualified path and same type in old and new source is the same cell; its value is preserved across reload. (§13.15.2)
028-7. A cell present only in the old source is a removal: it is dropped during reload. (§13.15.2)
028-8. A cell present only in the new source is an addition: a new cell is allocated and initialized per the new source's declared initial value or default. (§13.15.2)
028-9. A cell present in both sources with a different type is treated as removal of the old plus addition of the new. (§13.15.2)
028-10. `@reset_on_reopen` is not part of cell identity; adding or removing it across a reload preserves the cell's value and history. (§13.15.2)
028-11. A `@reset_on_reopen` change takes effect at the next gate-open. (§13.15.2)
028-12. Reload is performed atomically on the runtime's driving context. (§13.15.3)
028-13. Reload step 1 receives the precompiled IR diff; compilation and the §13.15.1 validation gate are host-side preconditions completed before `reload(diff)` is called, and on a malformed diff the reload is rejected with runtime state unchanged. (§13.15.3)
028-14. Reload step 2 acquires a reload lock and pauses acceptance of new signal/attr writes; host requests queue. (§13.15.3)
028-15. Reload step 3 lets any in-flight commit complete, ensuring a between-commits state. (§13.15.3)
028-16. Reload step 4 computes the old/new graph diff: surviving cells (same path, same type), additions, removals. (§13.15.3)
028-17. Reload step 5 allocates and initializes added cells per the new source. (§13.15.3)
028-18. Reload step 6 invokes Drop on removed cells in reverse-declaration order: connections before endpoint instances; within an instance, attrs, recurrents, and deriveds in reverse declaration order. (§13.15.3)
028-19. Reload step 7 updates the behavior table: new content-addressed IDs registered, absent behaviors deregistered, unchanged IDs carried over. (§13.15.3)
028-20. Reload step 8 recomputes the value of each derived whose behavior body changed (different content-addressed ID) from current inputs. (§13.15.3)
028-21. A derived whose body is unchanged keeps its value across reload. (§13.15.3)
028-22. Reload step 9 commits the reloaded state. (§13.15.3)
028-23. Reload step 10 releases the reload lock, resumes signal/attr writes, and applies queued writes to the new state. (§13.15.3)
028-24. Changes to `when` predicates — added, removed, or modified — are reload-safe; the predicate is structural metadata, not cell identity, and cells retain their values across the reload. (§13.15.3)
028-25. A changed `when` predicate takes effect prospectively at the next commit; historical gate state is not recomputed. (§13.15.3)
028-26. Cell-storage layout changes that would relocate live cells are not hot-reloadable and require a full-runtime restart. (§13.15.4)
028-27. Operator signature changes (parameters added, removed, or retyped; return type changed) require restart of the affected operator instances. (§13.15.4)
028-28. Internal cell type changes within an operator body require restart of the affected operator instances. (§13.15.4)
028-29. Any change to the text of a cell's `= initial` expression requires restart of the affected operator instances; the criterion is textual and decidable, not a semantic-equivalence judgment. (§13.15.4)
028-30. For operator-level changes, per-instance restart suffices: affected instances are recreated while the rest of the runtime continues without restart. (§13.15.4)
028-31. Stream element-type changes (an incompatible structural change to `T`) require per-instance restart of the affected stream cells and their consumers. (§13.15.4)
028-32. Stream policy changes (`ring` ↔ `gate`) require per-instance restart of the affected stream cells and their consumers. (§13.15.4)
028-33. Stream capacity changes require per-instance restart of the affected stream cells and their consumers. (§13.15.4)
028-34. Effect parameter-signature changes (added, removed, retyped, or reordered parameters) require per-instance restart of the affected effect instances. (§13.15.4)
028-35. Effect `desired:` or `observed:` cell type changes require per-instance restart of the affected effect instances. (§13.15.4)
028-36. Stream cell policy or capacity changes within an effect require per-instance restart of the affected effect instances. (§13.15.4)
028-37. On per-instance restart of effect instances, the host's reconciler receives a teardown call for the affected instances. (§13.15.4)
028-38. Implementations detect reload-unsafe changes during the diff phase and either reject the reload or schedule the appropriate restart (full-runtime or per-instance). (§13.15.4)
028-39. The runtime diagnoses which class of reload-unsafe change occurred. (§13.15.4)
028-40. Stream cell identity across reloads follows the same fully-qualified declaration path rule as other reactive cells. (§13.15.5)
028-41. A stream's buffer is preserved across reload iff its type signature is byte-identical: structurally identical element type, same policy, same capacity literal. (§13.15.5)
028-42. When any of element type, policy, or capacity differs across reload, the buffer resets to empty and all consumer cursors restart at the empty position. (§13.15.5)
028-43. Changes to a stream's `= source` expression are reload-safe and never affect buffer preservation. (§13.15.5)
028-44. Across a source-expression change, producers push into the same buffer and previously buffered events remain available to consumers until overwritten by policy. (§13.15.5)
028-45. A stream declared `@reset_on_reload` always resets its buffer across reload, regardless of type-signature match: `@reset_on_reload stream ring[1024] events: LogEntry = source`. (§13.15.5)
028-46. A consumer's cursor is preserved across reload when the consuming operator or derived instance is itself preserved per its own identity rule. (§13.15.5)
028-47. A consumer added in the new source starts at the current head, observing only events arriving after the reload. (§13.15.5)
028-48. A removed consumer's cursor is dropped. (§13.15.5)
028-49. The synthesized observation cells (`pending_count`, `pressure`, `is_full`, `dropped_total`, `rejected_total`, `last_overflow_at`) survive reload with the buffer when the type signature matches, and reset when the buffer resets. (§13.15.5)
028-50. Effect instance identity across reloads follows the operator identity rule: enclosing scope, effect type name, and argument bindings, tolerating positional moves within the same scope. (§13.15.6)
028-51. Within a preserved effect instance, a `desired:`/`observed:` `Signal[T]` cell is preserved when its type matches and reset to its initial value on type change. (§13.15.6)
028-52. Within a preserved effect instance, `Stream[T]` cells are preserved per the stream reload rules. (§13.15.6)
028-53. Adding a new cell to `desired:` or `observed:` is reload-safe; the new cell is initialized fresh. (§13.15.6)
028-54. Changing the initial-value expression of a `desired:`/`observed:` Signal cell is reload-safe: existing committed values persist and the new expression applies only to fresh instances. (§13.15.6)
028-55. Changing a parameter-derived `desired:` cell's derivation expression is reload-safe: the cell re-evaluates on the next commit with the new logic. (§13.15.6)
028-56. Changing effect visibility, generic-parameter bounds, or other declaration-level metadata that does not affect cell shape or parameter signature is reload-safe. (§13.15.6)
028-57. Effect parameter-signature changes are reload-unsafe and require per-instance restart per §13.15.4. (§13.15.6)
028-58. Cell type changes in `desired:` or `observed:` are reload-unsafe and require per-instance restart per §13.15.4. (§13.15.6)
028-59. Stream policy or capacity changes within an effect are reload-unsafe and require per-instance restart per §13.15.4. (§13.15.6)
028-60. Per-instance effect restart invokes the reconciler's teardown hook, constructs the new instance under the new declaration, then fires the create hook for it. (§13.15.6)
028-61. Renaming an effect type is removal of the old type plus addition of a new one: old instances are torn down and new-type instances are constructed fresh. (§13.15.6)
028-62. The reactive system's source semantics are realized through two backend-agnostic contracts — the compiler-emitted IR and the runtime interface — each construct mapping naming a contract, not a mechanism. (§13.16)
028-63. Reactive cells (signal, attr, recurrent, derived) map to graph-IR cells; the runtime stores their values and keeps them current across commits. (§13.16)
028-64. Stream cells map to graph-IR `stream` primitives carrying element type, policy, capacity, cursors, and observation cells. (§13.16)
028-65. Recurrent streams add history depth and per-input lookback to the `stream` primitive. (§13.16)
028-66. How a runtime buffers stream events is a backend concern. (§13.16)
028-67. Effect instances map to graph-IR `effect` primitives: groupings of standard cells plus a reconciler reference. (§13.16)
028-68. Per-instance reconciler state is host-managed and lives outside reactive state. (§13.16)
028-69. Reactive evaluation maps to the commit: apply staged writes, settle deriveds and recurrents glitch-free in topological order, fire reconciler hooks, publish a consistent snapshot for observers. (§13.16)
028-70. Which thread or threads perform the commit, and how the snapshot is published, are backend concerns. (§13.16)
028-71. Behaviors invoked during commit — derived, recurrent, gate, and effect-`desired` bodies — conform to the behavior ABI: invoked by content-addressed id with input-cell values, pure and stateless. (§13.16)
028-72. Host-registered reconcilers are dispatched at the commit boundary; `suspend`/`resume` fire there on gate-close/gate-open of an effect's enclosing subtree, driven by the runtime's internally-computed effective activation. (§13.16)
028-73. The IR carries the structural information the runtime needs: cells and dependency edges, gates, streams, effects, and behavior references. (§13.16)
028-74. Source-level hot reload applies an IR diff to the live runtime: cells matched by path with state preserved, changed behaviors swapped by content-hash, added/removed nodes mounted/unmounted. (§13.16)

## 029. Operators — 123 Rules

029-1. An operator is a reusable, cell-allocating reactive transformation declared with the `operator` keyword. (§13.17)
029-2. An operator takes `Cell[T]` inputs, optionally plus non-reactive value parameters, and produces an output of any type, exposed to callers as a reactive cell. (§13.17)
029-3. An operator allocates internal reactive cells (recurrents and/or deriveds) per instantiation. (§13.17)
029-4. `fn` is reactive-transparent: it takes value parameters, returns values, and allocates no cells. (§13.17)
029-5. `operator` is not reactive-transparent: it takes cell references, allocates internal cells, and is wired into the reactive graph at its call site. (§13.17)
029-6. A stateless operator wraps a pure projection over a source cell: `operator double(source: Cell[f32]) -> Derived[f32]` with body `source * 2`. (§13.17.1)
029-7. A stateful operator allocates `recurrent` state per instantiation. (§13.17.1)
029-8. Each instantiation of a stateful operator creates fresh internal cells; multiple call sites do not share state. (§13.17.1)
029-9. An operator declaration follows the form `operator name[GenericParams]?(params...) -> T: body`. (§13.17.2)
029-10. An operator name is a snake_case identifier. (§13.17.2)
029-11. An operator's declared return type may be any type — a value type, a record/tuple (plain or with cell fields), or an explicit `Cell` type (`Signal[T]`/`Derived[T]`/`Recurrent[T, N]`/`Stream[T]`); the output is exposed to callers as a reactive cell. (§13.17.2)
029-12. An operator parameter may be any value: cell-bound `name: Cell[T]`, value `name: T`, or function- or operator-typed (carried structurally, §13.17.13). (§13.17.3)
029-13. A cell-bound parameter binds to a reactive cell at instantiation; the operator tracks that cell's changes over time via the reactive engine. (§13.17.3)
029-14. Inside the body, a cell-bound parameter is treated as a cell of value type `T`; references read the cell's current value. (§13.17.3)
029-15. A value parameter is snapshotted at instantiation; the value is fixed for the lifetime of that operator instance. (§13.17.3)
029-16. Value-parameter snapshotting is structurally a category-B storage operation with implicit-move semantics; no `move` keyword is required. (§13.17.3)
029-17. Default values are allowed on value parameters: `rate: f32 = 0.1`. (§13.17.3)
029-18. Default values on `Cell[T]` parameters are not allowed in v1; a stdlib helper constructing a constant cell substitutes when needed. (§13.17.3)
029-19. Defaulted-before-non-defaulted parameter ordering follows the general rule of §3.5.4, uniform across functions, operators, and constructors; operators have no special-case relaxation. (§13.17.3)
029-20. A literal passed to a `Cell[T]` parameter is wrapped as an implicit constant signal cell, a compile-time-fixed `Signal[T]` value. (§13.17.3)
029-21. Literal wrapping costs one cell per literal at the call site, folded by the compiler when possible. (§13.17.3)
029-22. A cell passed to a `Cell[T]` parameter binds directly. (§13.17.3)
029-23. An argument passed to a value parameter `T` is evaluated and snapshotted. (§13.17.3)
029-24. Where a context requires `T` and the expression is a value cell, the compiler implicitly inserts a read of the cell's current value: `derived doubled: f32 = s * 2.0`. (§13.17.3.1)
029-25. An auto-dereferenced cell read is recorded as a provenance dependency, making the surrounding expression reactive on changes to that cell. (§13.17.3.1)
029-26. Auto-deref applies wherever a value cell flows into a `T` position: arithmetic operands, `T`-typed function arguments, attr initial-value expressions, derived bodies, recurrent expressions. (§13.17.3.1)
029-27. Auto-deref does not apply where the context expects `Cell[T]` directly — operator parameters, `Cell[T]`-typed function parameters, `|>` LHS — there the cell reference binds without dereferencing. (§13.17.3.1)
029-28. Value-cell auto-deref is a compile-time mechanism with no runtime cost beyond the cell read itself. (§13.17.3.1)
029-29. The permitted operator body items are `recurrent` declarations (with all §13.2.4 extensions), `derived` declarations, `let` bindings, and a final expression. (§13.17.4)
029-30. An operator-body `let` right-hand side evaluates in a reactive context: reads of cell-bound parameters return current values, and the binding recomputes whenever any read cell changes. (§13.17.4)
029-31. An operator-body `let` binding behaves as a synthesized derived for dependency-tracking purposes. (§13.17.4)
029-32. `attr` declarations are forbidden in operator bodies; per-instance configuration is expressed via parameters. (§13.17.4)
029-33. Side-effecting statements are forbidden in operator bodies. (§13.17.4)
029-34. `repeat` declarations are forbidden in operator bodies; operator state is fixed-shape. (§13.17.4)
029-35. Effect instantiations are forbidden in operator bodies. (§13.17.4)
029-36. An effect composing through an operator in a pipe chain is hosted in the enclosing node's `effects:` clause, never inside the operator: `url |> fetch |> render`. (§13.17.4)
029-37. The final expression's type must match the operator's declared return type, which may be any type (a value type, a record/tuple, or an explicit `Cell`). (§13.17.4)
029-38. For a final expression of value type `T`, the compiler synthesizes a derived cell holding its value and exposes that cell as the instance's output. (§13.17.4)
029-39. A final expression that is already a `Cell[T]` (a named recurrent, derived, or stream in the body) is the output directly, with no synthesis. (§13.17.4)
029-40. Whatever an operator's body returns — a value type, a record/tuple (plain or with cell fields), or an explicit `Cell` — the output is exposed to callers as a single reactive cell via implicit wrap; the caller neither knows nor cares which. (§13.17.5)
029-41. Event-producing operators (e.g. `to_stream`, `filter`, `merge`) return `Stream[T]`: `operator changes[T](source: Cell[T]) -> Stream[T]`. (§13.17.5)
029-43. Multiple outputs are returned as a record or tuple — either a plain record/tuple (form 1, exposed as a single `Derived` of the record) or one whose fields are themselves cells (form 2); both are valid and respect static memory. (§13.17.5)
029-126. An operator's value-typed output is implicitly wrapped into a reactive cell via the `Cell[T]`≅`T` relationship (§13.2.8): a `-> f32` return is the reactive output's value type, carried as `Derived[f32]`, never a non-reactive snapshot. (§13.17.5)
029-127. A record whose fields are cells (form 2) may itself be wrapped in a cell: the inner cells are part of the record's fixed compile-time shape (no dynamic allocation, static memory preserved) and the outer wrap is an inert passthrough that projections auto-deref through. (§13.17.5)
029-44. Consumers project fields of a record-valued output via reactive expressions: `derived just_peak: f32 = result.peak`. (§13.17.5)
029-45. Field-level reactivity on a record output is coarse-grained: a change to any field invalidates consumers of all fields. (§13.17.5)
029-46. Function-call form instantiates an operator directly: `let smoothed = smooth(source_cell, rate: 0.1, clock: tick)`. (§13.17.6)
029-47. Pipe form binds the `|>` LHS to the operator's first positional parameter, remaining arguments passing as in function-call form: `source_cell |> smooth(rate: 0.1, clock: tick)`. (§13.17.6)
029-48. The function-call and pipe instantiation forms are observationally identical. (§13.17.6)
029-49. Pipe steps chain, each `|>` step being an operator application whose result is consumed by the next: `0.0 |> clamp(min: 0.0, max: 1.0) |> smooth(rate: 0.1, clock: tick) |> ease_in_out`. (§13.17.6)
029-50. By convention, the first positional parameter of any operator is the implicit pipe target; library authors place the upstream cell first. (§13.17.6)
029-51. For binary operators (two upstream cells), the first cell is the pipe target and subsequent cells are passed by name: `source |> combine(other: another_signal, weight: 0.5)`. (§13.17.6)
029-52. Each instantiation allocates fresh internal cells; two call sites of the same operator do not share state. (§13.17.6)
029-53. The compiler emits one allocation set per operator call site at compile time; the reactive state buffer reserves space for each instance's internal cells. (§13.17.6)
029-54. An operator instance is identified by its enclosing scope, the operator name, and its argument bindings. (§13.17.6.1)
029-55. Identical `|>` chains in different scopes (modules, node bodies, placements) are distinct instances with independent state. (§13.17.6.1)
029-56. Operator instances have no user-assignable names; a `let` of an operator's output names the output cell, not the instance. (§13.17.6.1)
029-57. Reload identity uses the same scope/name/argument-bindings scheme, with tolerance for positional moves within the same scope; the binding name has no role. (§13.17.6.1)
029-58. Operator instances contribute to the runtime's graph specification the same way node placements and connection placements do. (§13.17.6.2)
029-59. Each instance's internal cells, including synthesized cells from the body and `let` bindings, count against the runtime's cell-storage allocation and per-type pool sizing for dynamic-size cells. (§13.17.6.2)
029-60. Recursion through operators is forbidden: an operator may not transitively instantiate itself. (§13.17.6.2)
029-61. The compiler enumerates operator call sites at compile time, so the static operator-instance count is bounded and known. (§13.17.6.2)
029-62. `|>` connects a source cell on the left to a destination on the right; the connection kind dispatches on the RHS kind: operator call or effect call. (§13.17.7)
029-63. With an operator-call RHS, `|>` instantiates the operator, binds the LHS to its first positional parameter, and yields the operator's declared output cell. (§13.17.7)
029-64. With an effect-call RHS, `|>` instantiates the effect, binds the LHS to its first positional parameter, and yields the effect instance value. (§13.17.7)
029-65. Effect instantiation via `|>` lives in a node's `effects:` clause: `f = current_url |> fetch`. (§13.17.7)
029-69. The `|>` LHS must be an expression of a reactive cell type (`Signal[T]`, `Derived[T]`, `Recurrent[T, N]`, `Stream[T]`, or other `Cell[T]`) or convertible to one. (§13.17.7)
029-70. A literal `|>` LHS is wrapped as an implicit constant signal cell automatically. (§13.17.7)
029-73. `|>` is low-precedence; most arithmetic and logical operators bind tighter: `a + b |> op` parses as `(a + b) |> op`. (§13.17.7)
029-74. `|>` is left-associative: `a |> op1 |> op2` parses as `(a |> op1) |> op2`. (§13.17.7)
029-75. Bitwise `|` binds tighter than `|>` and tighter than the logical operators per §4.4.7; `|>` remains the loosest-binding operator. (§13.17.7)
029-76. Using `|>` with a `fn` RHS is a compile error: `0.0 |> some_fn` ✗. (§13.17.7)
029-77. Operators may take type parameters with optional trait bounds: `operator running_total[T: Add & Copy](source: Cell[T]) -> Derived[T]`. (§13.17.8)
029-78. Operator type parameters are resolved at the call site from argument types. (§13.17.8)
029-79. Explicit operator type-parameter instantiation uses turbofish syntax where inference is ambiguous. (§13.17.8)
029-80. Operators carry the standard three-level visibility: `public`, `shared` (the default), `private`. (§13.17.9)
029-81. Module-private operators are not reachable from other modules. (§13.17.9)
029-82. Public operators may be re-exported. (§13.17.9)
029-83. An operator instance is a scoped reload boundary; each internal cell is identified by its declared name and type within the operator body. (§13.17.10)
029-84. Changes to recurrent expression bodies, `where` guards, or final-expression bodies are reload-safe. (§13.17.10)
029-85. Adding a new internal cell is reload-safe; new cells initialize fresh. (§13.17.10)
029-86. Operator signature changes (parameters added, removed, or retyped; return type changed) trigger per-instance restart on reload. (§13.17.10)
029-87. Internal cell type changes trigger per-instance restart on reload. (§13.17.10)
029-88. Any change to the text of a cell's `= initial` expression triggers per-instance restart of the operator; the criterion is textual and decidable, not a semantic-equivalence judgment. (§13.17.10)
029-89. Per-instance restart recreates only the affected operator instances, never the whole runtime. (§13.17.10)
029-90. Reload-unsafe changes involving buffer-layout relocation require full-runtime restart. (§13.17.10)
029-91. A call site switching operators (`source |> op_a` to `source |> op_b`) drops the old instance's cells via eviction and initializes the new instance fresh, even if the signatures match. (§13.17.10)
029-92. The reload diff identifies operator instances by (enclosing scope, operator name, argument bindings) rather than line/column, so a pure positional move within the same scope preserves state. (§13.17.10)
029-93. Moving a call site to a different enclosing scope is treated as a call-site change; state is lost. (§13.17.10)
029-94. Call sites in the same scope indistinguishable by (operator name, arguments) are matched old-to-new by syntactic order on reload. (§13.17.10)
029-95. Adding a new identical call between two existing indistinguishable calls treats the new call as fresh; the existing calls preserve state. (§13.17.10)
029-96. An operator instance has no `when` predicate of its own; gating is expressed on its output cell or its consumer. (§13.17.11)
029-97. Operator-internal cells participate in the standard cycle-detection rules. (§13.17.11)
029-98. A recurrent inside an operator acts as a delay element identical to a top-level recurrent. (§13.17.11)
029-99. Each operator instance's internal cells contribute their evaluation nodes to the per-commit DAG. (§13.17.11)
029-100. Operators do not cross commit boundaries; all internal evaluation happens within a single commit. (§13.17.11)
029-101. Operator bodies are not reactive-transparent: reading a cell-bound parameter reads through the reactive engine, with provenance tracked at the call site. (§13.17.11)
029-102. Operator calls inside an operator body create further instantiations. (§13.17.11)
029-103. `fn` calls inside an operator body remain reactive-transparent. (§13.17.11)
029-104. Operator-internal recurrents may hold dynamic-size types (e.g. `Vec[T]`), stored via the standard pool-with-index mechanism. (§13.17.11)
029-105. Operators share the composition surface — `|>` pipe form, instance identity, parameter rules, generics, visibility — with effects. (§13.17.11)
029-106. Operators produce or consume streams via the standard stream operators. (§13.17.11)
029-107. Operators perform pure reactive transforms with no outside-world side effects; effects align program state with external reality. (§13.17.11)
029-108. A normative diagnostic class rejects `|>` with a non-operator, non-effect right-hand side. (§13.17.12)
029-109. A normative diagnostic class rejects piping into an operator that declares no positional parameter to bind. (§13.17.12)
029-110. A normative diagnostic class rejects passing a non-cell, non-literal value expression to a `Cell[T]` parameter. (§13.17.12)
029-111. A normative diagnostic class rejects `attr` declarations inside an operator body. (§13.17.12)
029-112. A normative diagnostic class rejects a final expression whose value type mismatches the operator's declared return value type. (§13.17.12)
029-113. An operator has a structural type written `operator(P1, P2, …) -> U`: the parameter types in order, then the return type (any type). (§13.17.13)
029-114. Operators are carried structurally by signature, unlike the nominal node/connection/effect kinds carried by the `Type[…]` meta-type. (§13.17.13)
029-115. An operator type expression in value position (an operator parameter, attr, or return) carries or defers which operator the receiver instantiates: `kernel: operator(Cell[f32]) -> Derived[f32]`. (§13.17.13)
029-116. An operator value must name an operator declaration; there are no anonymous operator literals. (§13.17.13)
029-117. A supplied operator value resolves at compile time, and the receiver is monomorphized per supplied operator with no erasure, exactly as for a `Type[…]` value. (§13.17.13)
029-118. A value is admissible for an `operator(P…) -> U` parameter iff the named operator's signature matches structurally: the same parameter types in order and the same return type. (§13.17.13)
029-119. In an operator type, cell-bound parameters appear as their cell type and value parameters as their value type. (§13.17.13)
029-128. An operator may take function- and operator-typed parameters, but a function may not take an operator parameter: a function is a non-reactive runtime computation, and an operator is a compile-time value with no runtime representation that builds static reactive structure when instantiated. (§13.17.13)
029-120. A value parameter's default is not part of the operator type and does not affect signature matching. (§13.17.13)
029-121. A receiver instantiates a carried operator exactly as a named one, by applying it to arguments: `kernel(source)`. (§13.17.13)
029-122. The instance of a carried operator lives at the receiver's graph position, not the supplier's. (§13.17.13)
029-123. A receiver may instantiate a carried operator only against the parameter and output types its signature states; it cannot otherwise read or decompose the operator value. (§13.17.13)
029-124. A carried operator may be generic, its type written with the generic parameters bound at the use site: `operator(Cell[T]) -> Derived[T]`. (§13.17.13)
029-125. A carried operator composes in `|>` chains at the point it is instantiated, identically to a named operator. (§13.17.13)
029-129. Ruling A: a value-reading operator or function parameter is typed `Cell[T]` (the umbrella), and an operator's computed value output is a `Derived[T]`; a `Stream[T]` has no current value and is excluded at the read site, not by the signature. (§13.17.3)

## 030. Streams — 254 Rules

030-1. A stream is a reactive primitive for append-only event sequences governed by a buffering policy. (§13.18)
030-2. Streams are first-class reactive cells participating in the commit cycle, in cell identity for hot reload, and in the graph specification. (§13.18)
030-3. Streams are not values flowing through ordinary expressions; they are cells with read and write surfaces distinct from signals. (§13.18)
030-4. A stream carries an append-only sequence of typed events observed by zero or more consumers — operators or deriveds reading from the stream — each advancing its own cursor independently. (§13.18.1)
030-5. A stream event's producer is a stream-emitting operator chain or a host-side push. (§13.18.1)
030-6. A stream's storage is governed by a buffering policy. (§13.18.1)
030-7. The v1 policy set is exactly `ring` and `gate`, and both policies are bounded. (§13.18.1)
030-8. A bounded policy allocates a fixed-capacity region of typed slots once, at the stream's declaration site. (§13.18.1)
030-9. A bounded stream's capacity is part of its concrete type. (§13.18.1)
030-10. On a push to a full buffer, the `ring` policy — a true circular ring buffer — overwrites, displacing the oldest unconsumed event. (§13.18.1)
030-11. On a push to a full buffer, the `gate` policy rejects the new event with failure and never overwrites; gate is bounded-but-reject, not a ring buffer. (§13.18.1)
030-12. Boundedness and capacity are properties of the bounded policies, not of streams in general; a future unbounded policy (to which capacity would not apply) is not forbidden. (§13.18.1)
030-13. A stream's lifetime is scope-bound: it lives as long as its declaration's enclosing scope and is freed when that scope dies. (§13.18.1)
030-14. There is no garbage collection of stream events. (§13.18.1)
030-15. A bounded stream's buffer is one fixed memory region for the stream's entire lifetime, reused as events arrive. (§13.18.1)
030-16. `Stream[T]` holds zero or more pending events, each consumed independently, in contrast to `Signal[T]`'s single always-defined current value. (§13.18.1)
030-17. A stream has no current value; consumers project a stream to a signal only explicitly. (§13.18.1)
030-18. The stream declaration form is `stream policy[capacity]? name: Type? = source`. (§13.18.2)
030-19. The policy keyword, one of `ring` or `gate`, is mandatory; a stream declaration has no default policy. (§13.18.2)
030-20. An explicit `[capacity]` specifies the buffer's slot count and must be a compile-time-known positive `usize`: a literal, a `const`, or a const-generic parameter. (§13.18.2)
030-21. Omitted capacity with a single-stream source or a known-capacity stream-producing chain defaults to that output's capacity. (§13.18.2)
030-22. Omitted capacity with a reactive-expression source over streams and/or signals defaults to the sum of input capacities across all reactive inputs. (§13.18.2)
030-23. In the capacity-default sum, each stream contributes its declared capacity and each signal contributes the implicit `to_stream` default of 1024. (§13.18.2)
030-24. On a `recurrent[N] stream` with omitted capacity, the buffer defaults to `sum_of_input_capacities`; the self-history depth `N` is a separate allocation carried by the `RecurrentRing[B, N]`/`RecurrentGate[B, N]` policy, not added to the buffer. (§13.18.2)
030-25. In all other omitted-capacity cases (e.g., a bare `to_stream` without surrounding context), capacity defaults to 1024. (§13.18.2)
030-26. A stream's name is a snake_case identifier. (§13.18.2)
030-27. The element-type annotation is optional when inferable from the source expression: `stream gate[256] db_writes = pending_writes`. (§13.18.2)
030-28. Valid stream sources are a stream-producing operator chain, a reference to another stream (direct forwarding), a reactive expression over streams and/or signals, or a signal-only expression. (§13.18.2)
030-29. A signal-only source is equivalent to appending `|> to_stream`: `stream ring url_events: Url = current_url`. (§13.18.2)
030-30. Streams may be declared wherever reactive declarations may appear: module level, node bodies, connection bodies, operator bodies, and effect bodies. (§13.18.2)
030-31. Streams may not be declared inside function bodies, which are reactive-transparent. (§13.18.2)
030-32. Module-level streams carry the standard three-level visibility: `public`, `shared` (the default), `private`. (§13.18.2)
030-33. Streams inside node, connection, operator, or effect bodies inherit the enclosing declaration's visibility. (§13.18.2)
030-34. A stream's type encodes its element type and its policy; the policy carries its own buffer parameters (a bounded policy carries its capacity), so capacity is a property of the policy, not of `Stream`. (§13.18.3)
030-35. The erased supertype `Stream[T]` carries only the element type — no policy; every `Stream[T, P]` widens to it. (§13.18.3)
030-36. A stream type is `Stream[T, P]` where `P: StreamPolicy`; `RingStream[T, N]` is a transparent alias for `Stream[T, Ring[N]]` (likewise `GateStream`/recurrent). (§13.18.3)
030-37. Every stream value has a concrete policy `P` (one of the four `StreamPolicy` members) at runtime. (§13.18.3)
030-38. `Stream[T]` (policy erased) types signatures that accept any stream without threading its policy: `operator count[T](source: Stream[T]) -> Derived[i64]`. (§13.18.3)
030-39. A parameter naming a concrete policy accepts only that policy; passing a gate stream where `RingStream[T, N]` (= `Stream[T, Ring[N]]`) is expected is a type error. (§13.18.3)
030-40. A parameter typed `Stream[T]` (erased) is polymorphic over all policies. (§13.18.3)
030-41. Policy-preserving operators thread the policy parameter `P` from input to output (`operator map[T, U, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], f: fn(T) -> U) -> Stream[U, P]`); preservation is type-true, not a propagation rule. (§13.18.3)
030-42. Capacity lives in the policy type (`Ring[N]`), not on `Stream`; it is part of the type for analysis and pool sizing (§14.3.3). (§13.18.3)
030-43. An operator accepting erased `Stream[T]` accepts any policy and capacity. (§13.18.3)
030-44. An operator needing a specific policy or capacity names it: `RingStream[T, N]` (= `Stream[T, Ring[N]]`) for ring capacity `N`. (§13.18.3)
030-45. A `Stream[T, P]` is implicitly usable wherever erased `Stream[T]` is expected; `Stream[T]` is the supertype. (§13.18.3)
030-46. Widening `Stream[T, P]` to `Stream[T]` is zero-cost at runtime; only the type-system view changes. (§13.18.3)
030-61. `Cell[T]` is the umbrella trait over reactive cells carrying `T`, implemented by `Signal[T]`, `Derived[T]`, `Recurrent[T, N]`, and `Stream[T]` and their concrete sub-types. (§13.18.5)
030-62. `Cell[T]` types signatures accepting any kind of reactive cell: `operator monitor[T, C: Cell[T]](source: C) -> Derived[bool]` works with any value cell or stream. (§13.18.5)
030-63. `Cell[T]` has no required methods at the language level; it is a marker trait for participation in the reactive system as a typed cell. (§13.18.5)
030-64. Concrete cell behavior — current-value reads (value cells) and event observation (streams) — is expressed through each implementing type's own methods and operators. (§13.18.5)
030-68. Ductus has no ad-hoc union types at value position, so the common abstraction over heterogeneous reactive cells is the trait `Cell[T]`. (§13.18.5)
030-69. Every bounded stream automatically exposes derived signal cells describing its state, read via field syntax on the stream value: `events.pressure`. (§13.18.6)
030-70. `pending_count: Derived[i32]` counts events in the buffer not yet observed by every cursor. (§13.18.6)
030-71. `pressure: Derived[f32]` equals `pending_count / capacity`, in range `0.0..1.0`. (§13.18.6)
030-72. `is_full: Derived[bool]` is `true` when `pending_count is capacity`. (§13.18.6)
030-73. `dropped_total: Derived[i64]` cumulatively counts events displaced by overflow; it applies to ring only and is always `0` for gate. (§13.18.6)
030-74. `rejected_total: Derived[i64]` cumulatively counts pushes rejected by overflow; it applies to gate only and is always `0` for ring. (§13.18.6)
030-75. `last_overflow_at: Derived[Option[instant]]` holds the timestamp of the most recent overflow, or `None` if none has occurred. (§13.18.6)
030-76. Observation cells are ordinary `Derived[T]` cells for all purposes: commit cycle, derived dependencies, hot-reload identity. (§13.18.6)
030-77. Observation cells are never declared in user code; the compiler synthesizes them as part of the stream's storage. (§13.18.6)
030-78. Producer self-throttling is expressed by reading the stream's `pressure` (or `is_full`) signal and gating emission upstream of the producing chain. (§13.18.6)
030-79. The stdlib provides operators that combine with the observation surface, e.g. `throttle` driven by a pressure-derived gating signal. (§13.18.6)
030-80. Gate-stream producers observe back-pressure via `rejected_total` (or `is_full`) to take corrective action such as retry, log, or surfacing an error. (§13.18.6)
030-81. Streams participate in reactive expressions on equal footing with signals. (§13.18.7)
030-82. A reactive expression containing one or more streams evaluates per event, producing a derived stream. (§13.18.7)
030-83. A stream input contributes its events: the expression re-evaluates once per event, producing one output event per input event. (§13.18.7.1)
030-84. A signal input contributes its commits with `to_stream` semantics: the initial value as first contribution, then every subsequently changed committed value. (§13.18.7.1)
030-85. Same-value signal commits contribute nothing to a stream expression, per the value-change rule. (§13.18.7.1)
030-86. The expression recomputes whenever any reactive input emits, and the output stream emits the freshly computed value as its next event. (§13.18.7.1)
030-87. With multiple reactive inputs the default combining behavior is combine_latest: any input's emission yields an output event using the latest value of each other input. (§13.18.7.2)
030-88. The first combine_latest output is emitted once every input has emitted at least once, a signal's initial value counting as its first emission. (§13.18.7.2)
030-89. Combining behaviors other than combine_latest (`zip`, `sample`, `merge`, etc.) require explicit stdlib operators. (§13.18.7.2)
030-90. The combine_latest default governs only value-producing operations: arithmetic, function calls, `map`, conditional expressions, and similar. (§13.18.7.2)
030-91. LHS-driven operations are normatively per-LHS-event, not combine_latest: only the LHS stream drives emission. (§13.18.7.2)
030-92. The per-LHS-event rule covers subset operators that may drop events (`where`, `filter`, `skip`, `take`) and 1:1 operators (`pairwise`). (§13.18.7.2)
030-93. Reactive cells referenced in an LHS-driven operation's predicate or transformation contribute their current values at LHS-emission time; their commits between LHS events cause no re-emission. (§13.18.7.2)
030-94. `pairwise` never drops events and emits exactly one output per LHS input event. (§13.18.7.2)
030-95. A reactive expression emits its first output during the startup pass iff every reactive input has a value to contribute at that moment. (§13.18.7.2)
030-96. A stream expression whose reactive inputs are all signals emits one event during startup. (§13.18.7.2)
030-97. A stream expression with stream inputs emits its first event only once those streams have each produced at least one event, possibly later than startup. (§13.18.7.2)
030-98. A reactive expression containing zero streams has signal result kind. (§13.18.7.3)
030-99. A reactive expression containing one or more streams has stream result kind, and its surrounding declaration must be `stream` or `recurrent[N] stream`. (§13.18.7.3)
030-100. The result kind follows from input types alone; there is no type-directed dispatch on the binding's LHS context. (§13.18.7.3)
030-101. A stream-containing expression is never silently coerced to a signal; explicit projection via `to_signal(fallback)` is required. (§13.18.7.3)
030-102. `derived X = expr` with a zero-stream RHS is a standard derived signal. (§13.18.7.4)
030-103. `derived X = expr` with a stream-containing RHS is a compile error; use `to_signal(fallback)`. (§13.18.7.4)
030-104. A stream declaration whose RHS contains at least one stream produces an output stream with per-event evaluation. (§13.18.7.4)
030-105. A stream declaration whose RHS contains no streams but at least one signal produces an output stream; the signals participate via implicit `to_stream` with initial-as-first-event. (§13.18.7.4)
030-106. A stream declaration whose RHS has no reactive inputs is a compile error; a stream needs at least one reactive input. (§13.18.7.4)
030-107. Implicit conversion turns each signal's commits into output events; controlling the mechanism (e.g., skipping the initial value) requires explicit `to_stream` with an operator chain. (§13.18.7.4)
030-108. An explicit `[capacity]` is always allowed and overrides the capacity default. (§13.18.7.5)
030-109. A parenthesized stream-valued expression continues into a downstream operator chain: `stream ring filtered: i32 = (count * 2) |> filter(is_positive)`. (§13.18.7.5)
030-110. Assigning a stream-valued expression to a `derived` produces a dedicated diagnostic hinting `to_signal` projection: `derived count_signal: i32 = some_stream * 2` ✗. (§13.18.7.6)
030-111. A `recurrent[N] stream` is a stream declaration whose expression body may reference past events of itself and of its input streams via `.past(n, fallback)`. (§13.18.8)
030-112. The recurrent stream form is `recurrent[N]? stream policy[capacity]? NAME: Type? = EXPR`. (§13.18.8.1)
030-113. `[N]` is the output stream's self-history size: the maximum lookback `k` permitted in `NAME.past(k, ...)` self-references. (§13.18.8.1)
030-114. `N` must be a compile-time-known positive integer: a literal, a `const`, or a const-generic parameter. (§13.18.8.1)
030-115. `recurrent stream` without brackets is shorthand for `recurrent[1] stream`, one step of self-memory. (§13.18.8.1)
030-116. A recurrent stream follows the standard stream declaration constraints: policy mandatory, capacity optional. (§13.18.8.1)
030-117. The body may apply `.past(n, fallback)` and `.previous(init)` to any referenced stream including the declared name itself: `recurrent stream ring smoothed: f32 = (count + smoothed.previous(0.0)) / 2`. (§13.18.8.1)
030-118. In `stream_name.past(n, fallback)`, `n` is a compile-time-known positive `usize` — a literal, a `const`, or a const-generic parameter — giving the lookback distance: `n=1` is the immediately-previous event, `n=2` two events back. (§13.18.8.2)
030-119. `fallback` is an expression of the stream's element type `T`, returned when fewer than `n` events of the stream have been observed. (§13.18.8.2)
030-120. `.past` returns `T` directly, not `Option[T]`; the fallback keeps the read always well-typed. (§13.18.8.2)
030-121. `stream_name.previous(init)` is sugar for `stream_name.past(1, init)`. (§13.18.8.2)
030-122. History is per-stream: `count.past(1, 0)` is the immediately-previous event of `count` regardless of other streams' timing, each stream's history advancing per its own event count. (§13.18.8.2)
030-123. Each `.past(n, fallback)` invocation is an ordinary function call whose arguments are evaluated when read. (§13.18.8.3)
030-124. Multiple `.past` calls on the same stream in one expression may use different fallbacks: `if condition: count.previous(0) else: count.previous(99)`. (§13.18.8.3)
030-125. The output stream's self-history allocates `N` slots of `sizeof(T)`. (§13.18.8.4)
030-126. The compiler statically scans the body per input stream and allocates the maximum `k` among its `.past(k, ...)` calls: `.past(1, ...)` and `.past(2, ...)` allocate 2 slots. (§13.18.8.4)
030-127. An input stream not accessed via `.past` gets zero history slots. (§13.18.8.4)
030-128. Per-declaration history memory cost is `N * sizeof(T_output)` plus the sum over referenced inputs of `max_k(input_i) * sizeof(T_i)`. (§13.18.8.4)
030-129. `NAME.past(k, ...)` with `k > N` on the declared output is a compile error. (§13.18.8.5)
030-130. `.past`/`.previous` access outside a recurrent's producing context (a `recurrent[N] stream` or a value `Recurrent[T, N]` body) is a compile error. (§13.18.8.5)
030-131. The `n` argument of `.past(n, fallback)` must be a compile-time-known positive integer: a literal, a `const`, or a const-generic parameter. (§13.18.8.5)
030-132. A `recurrent[N] stream` produces a `RecurrentRingStream[T, B, H]` or `RecurrentGateStream[T, B, H]` (per its policy); its special semantics are contained in its own body, and it is usable as a `Stream[T]` downstream, to which operators apply normally: `avg |> map(fn(x): x * 2)`. (§13.18.8.6)
030-133. `recurrent[N] stream` is invalid in effect `observed:` blocks, which declare bare host-written stream cells with no expression body for `.past` to reference. (§13.18.8.7)
030-134. Effects needing history-aware behavior must compute it in the host's reconciler. (§13.18.8.7)
030-135. Value recurrents (`Recurrent[T, N]`, §13.2.4) use the same `.previous(fallback)` and `.past(k, fallback)` accessors with the same compile-time bounds as recurrent streams. (§13.18.8.7)
030-136. Value recurrents (`Recurrent[T, N]`) and stream recurrents are symmetric in syntax, memory model, and semantics, differing only in value-shaped `Recurrent[T, N]` versus event-shaped recurrent stream. (§13.18.8.7)
030-137. Stream-producing, stream-transforming, and stream-consuming operators are stdlib primitives. (§13.18.9)
030-138. Stream operators use the standard operator-application syntax, `|>` chains or function-call form. (§13.18.9)
030-139. `to_stream` is the signal-to-stream bridge: `to_stream[T, const N: usize = 1024](Signal[T]) -> RingStream[T, N]`. (§13.18.9)
030-140. At stream creation, `to_stream` emits the source signal's current value as event 0. (§13.18.9)
030-141. After creation, `to_stream` appends one event per commit of a new source value. (§13.18.9)
030-142. Same-value commits of the source signal emit no `to_stream` event. (§13.18.9)
030-143. `to_stream` capacity defaults to `1024`: `some_signal |> to_stream` is `RingStream[T, 1024]`. (§13.18.9)
030-144. `to_stream` capacity is overridable via turbofish: `some_signal |> to_stream::[Url, 2048]`. (§13.18.9)
030-145. `to_stream` always produces `ring` policy. (§13.18.9)
030-146. A signal is converted to a non-ring stream only by applying further operators/declarations after `to_stream` (e.g., threading into a `gate`-declared output). (§13.18.9)
030-147. The implicit signal-to-stream conversion in reactive expressions uses `to_stream`'s default capacity `N = 1024`. (§13.18.9)
030-148. `to_signal[T](source: Stream[T], fallback: T) -> Derived[T]` yields a derived value holding the latest observed event, updated on each new event. (§13.18.9)
030-149. `to_signal`'s `fallback` argument is required; the signal holds `fallback` until the first event is observed. (§13.18.9)
030-150. `skip[T, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], n: i32) -> Stream[T, P]` drops the first `n` observed events and passes the rest. (§13.18.9)
030-151. `skip_first` is equivalent to `skip(1)`: `current_url |> to_stream |> skip_first` drops the initial-value event. (§13.18.9)
030-152. `take[T, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], n: i32) -> Stream[T, P]` emits the first `n` events, after which the output stream is complete and emits no more. (§13.18.9)
030-153. `take_first` is equivalent to `take(1)`. (§13.18.9)
030-154. `skip` and `take` both preserve the source's policy and capacity by threading the policy parameter `P`. (§13.18.9)
030-155. `count[T](source: Stream[T]) -> Signal[i64]` is the running count of observed events, starting at `0`. (§13.18.9)
030-156. `fold[T, A](source: Stream[T], init: A, f: fn(A, T) -> A) -> Signal[A]` is a running accumulator whose initial value is `init`. (§13.18.9)
030-157. `any[T](source: Stream[T], pred: fn(T) -> bool) -> Signal[bool]` starts `false` and becomes `true` once any event satisfies `pred`. (§13.18.9)
030-158. `all[T](source: Stream[T], pred: fn(T) -> bool) -> Signal[bool]` starts `true` and stays `true` while every event so far satisfies `pred`. (§13.18.9)
030-159. `map[T, U, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], f: fn(T) -> U) -> Stream[U, P]` preserves policy and capacity by threading `P`. (§13.18.9)
030-160. `filter[T, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], pred: fn(T) -> bool) -> Stream[T, P]` preserves policy and capacity by threading `P`. (§13.18.9)
030-161. `merge(a: RingStream[T, A], b: RingStream[T, B]) -> RingStream[T, N]` interleaves events from both sources in commit order. (§13.18.9)
030-162. `merge`'s const-generic output capacity `N` defaults to `A + B`, the sum of the input streams' capacities. (§13.18.9)
030-163. `throttle[T, P: StreamPolicy](Stream[T, P], window: duration, clock: Cell[u64]) -> Stream[T, P]` rate-limits to at most one event per `window`; `clock` ticks are nanoseconds (§9.4.1), so an event passes when `clock - last_emit >= window`. (§13.18.9)
030-164. `merge`'s capacity is overridable at the call site via turbofish: `merge::[T = Event, N = 1024](a, b)`. (§13.18.9)
030-165. `merge_gate` is the gate-stream variant of `merge` with the same shape. (§13.18.9)
030-166. Cross-policy merges (one ring input, one gate input) are a compile error. (§13.18.9)
030-169. `scan[T, A, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P], init: A, f: fn(A, T) -> A) -> Stream[A, P]` emits `f(state, event)` per input event, threading state through. (§13.18.9)
