030-170. `scan`'s first output event is `f(init, first_input_event)`. (§13.18.9)
030-171. `pairwise[T, P: StreamPolicy](source: Stream[T, P]) -> Stream[(T, Option[T]), P]` emits each event paired with the previous event. (§13.18.9)
030-172. `pairwise`'s first output is `(first_input, None)`. (§13.18.9)
030-173. `where` filters a stream (or a signal implicitly lifted via `to_stream`) by a boolean predicate, emitting only the events for which the predicate is true. (§13.18.10)
030-174. The filter form is `A where C`, with stream-or-signal LHS `A` and boolean `C` evaluated per event of `A`: `numbers where numbers > 0`. (§13.18.10.1)
030-175. A signal LHS to `where` undergoes implicit `to_stream`, equivalent to `to_stream` followed by `filter`: `sensor_signal where sensor_signal > 100`. (§13.18.10.1)
030-176. Inside `C`, the LHS stream's name denotes the current event: `A.field` reads the event's field, and a bare reference to a primitive-typed stream is the event value. (§13.18.10.2)
030-177. Other reactive cells referenced in `C` contribute their current committed values at the moment the filter evaluates. (§13.18.10.2)
030-178. The filter evaluates exactly once per LHS event; the LHS stream is the sole driver of emission. (§13.18.10.2)
030-179. Commits to predicate-referenced cells between LHS emissions never trigger filter re-evaluation or re-emission: `events where active_signal is true` emits nothing when only `active_signal` changes. (§13.18.10.2)
030-180. `where` and the `filter` operator have identical per-LHS-event sampled-predicate semantics; the distinction is syntactic (keyword reactive expression vs closure). (§13.18.10.2)
030-181. A `where` output's element type matches its input's. (§13.18.10.3)
030-182. A `where` output inherits the LHS policy: a ring LHS yields a ring output and a gate LHS yields a gate output. (§13.18.10.3)
030-183. A signal LHS to `where` (implicit `to_stream`) produces a ring-policy output. (§13.18.10.3)
030-184. A `where` output's default capacity is the LHS stream's capacity alone; predicate-referenced cells contribute nothing to the capacity, unlike the all-inputs sum used for value-producing expressions. (§13.18.10.3)
030-185. An explicit `[capacity]` on the surrounding stream declaration overrides the `where` default capacity. (§13.18.10.3)
030-186. `where` is left-associative; chained filters group leftward: `clicks where clicks.area > 100 where clicks.priority is high`. (§13.18.10.4)
030-187. `where` composes with stream operators at any pipeline position: `(events where events.value > 0) |> pairwise`. (§13.18.10.4)
030-188. An observe arm-trigger `T where C` is a filtered stream that the arm treats as an ordinary trigger cell, indistinguishable from a bare trigger. (§13.18.10.5)
030-189. A filtered trigger emits when `T` emits and `C` is true under the sampled current values of cells `C` references: `on tick where active_mode: ...`. (§13.18.10.5)
030-190. Predicate-cell commits do not, by themselves, emit on a filtered trigger or activate its arm. (§13.18.10.5)
030-191. Arm selection over filtered triggers is uniform: the first arm whose (filtered) trigger emits in the current commit wins. (§13.18.10.5)
030-192. `where` applies only to reactive streams and signals; filtering non-reactive collections uses `Iterator` operations instead. (§13.18.10.6)
030-193. The `where` predicate must evaluate to `bool`; any other predicate type is a compile error. (§13.18.10.6)
030-194. Assigning a `where` expression (always stream-valued) to a `derived` binding is a compile error; a signal result requires explicit `to_signal(fallback)`. (§13.18.10.6)
030-195. Stream policy is encoded in the stream's type, not as a runtime attribute. (§13.18.11)
030-196. Policy-preserving operators thread the policy parameter `P` from input to output (`Stream[T, P] -> Stream[U, P]`); a `GateStream[T, 256]` input gives a `GateStream[U, 256]` `map` output by type-checking, not a propagation pass. (§13.18.11)
030-197. Policy-constraining operators declare the required policy concretely in their signature: `persist[T: Persistable, const N: usize](writes: GateStream[T, N]) -> EffectResult`. (§13.18.11)
030-198. Passing a stream of the wrong policy to a policy-constrained parameter is a compile error: `RingStream[Write, 1024]` to a `GateStream[Write, _]` parameter ✗. (§13.18.11)
030-199. Mixed-policy reactive expressions are permitted; the output's policy is the LHS-declared policy regardless of input policies: `stream ring X = ring_a + gate_b` is ring. (§13.18.11)
030-200. Output stream policy is never inferred from inputs. (§13.18.11)
030-201. A gate input feeding a ring-declared output still tracks its own `rejected_total`; its producer-facing guarantee is unaffected by the mix. (§13.18.11)
030-202. A gate input's lossless guarantee does not propagate to consumers of a ring-declared combined output; end-to-end losslessness requires declaring the output `gate`. (§13.18.11)
030-203. Every stream consumer maintains its own cursor marking the oldest event it has not yet observed; consumers of the same stream advance independently. (§13.18.12)
030-204. A consumer is any operator instance whose signature takes a `Stream[T]` parameter; each instantiation allocates a fresh cursor. (§13.18.12)
030-205. Multiple consumers of one stream see the same events, each observing the full sequence from its own point of attachment. (§13.18.12)
030-206. A cursor's identity follows the consuming operator instance's identity; two `|>` chains in different scopes consuming the same stream get distinct cursors. (§13.18.12)
030-207. Ring cursors are advisory: the buffer overwrites per ring policy regardless of cursor positions, so lagging consumers miss events. (§13.18.12)
030-208. Gate streams are lossless per consumer: retention is slowest-cursor-driven, and no event is evicted until every consumer has observed it. (§13.18.12)
030-209. When a gate buffer holds `capacity` events unobserved by the slowest cursor, further producer pushes are rejected — `rejected_total` increments and `is_full` is true. (§13.18.12)
030-210. When a ring advance overwrites a cursor's position, the cursor jumps forward to the oldest still-present event; the consumer sees a gap. `dropped_total` is per-stream — it counts events the ring displaces by overflow (§13.18.6), not per-consumer skips. (§13.18.12)
030-211. When a consumer's enclosing subtree is gated off, its cursor stops advancing; the default behavior is freeze-and-backlog. (§13.18.12)
030-212. A frozen ring consumer's buffer keeps overwriting; on resume the cursor jumps to the oldest still-present event, the gap observed via `dropped_total`. (§13.18.12)
030-213. A frozen gate consumer pins the buffer via slowest-cursor retention, so the buffer fills and producers are rejected (`rejected_total`, `is_full`) for the duration of the freeze. (§13.18.12)
030-214. On resume, a frozen gate consumer drains the full retained backlog; nothing is lost to it. (§13.18.12)
030-215. `@reset_on_reopen` opts a consumer out of backlog: on resume its cursor skips to the current head, discarding gap events. (§13.18.12)
030-216. On a gate consumer, `@reset_on_reopen` additionally releases the buffer hold during the freeze, so the frozen consumer neither pins the buffer nor back-pressures producers. (§13.18.12)
030-217. Cursors only advance; no operation rewinds a cursor to an earlier position. (§13.18.12)
030-218. A stream's buffer is a fixed array of `capacity` slots of `sizeof(T)` bytes, allocated once at stream creation and freed when the stream's scope dies. (§13.18.13)
030-219. A stream's head pointer and metadata counters (`pending_count`, `dropped_total`, `rejected_total`, `last_overflow_at`) are stored as ordinary reactive cells. (§13.18.13)
030-220. Per-consumer cursors are stored as part of each consumer's per-instance state. (§13.18.13)
030-221. Stream buffers are allocated from per-`(T, capacity)` pools; all instances with the same element type and capacity share a pool, each occupying one buffer-sized slot. (§13.18.13)
030-222. The compiler enumerates stream declarations at compile time and computes per-`(T, capacity)` pool sizes statically. (§13.18.13)
030-223. Hot reload may extend or shrink stream pools as declarations are added or removed, via the same per-`(T, capacity)` mechanism as other dynamic-size cell types. (§13.18.13)
030-224. Streams perform no dynamic allocation: producers and consumers use pre-allocated slots, and the fixed buffer is the stream's entire memory cost. (§13.18.13)
030-225. A `recurrent[N] stream X` allocates `N` output-history slots of `sizeof(T_X)` for `X.past(k, ...)` reads. (§13.18.13)
030-226. A recurrent stream without an explicit `[N]` defaults to `[1]`. (§13.18.13)
030-227. For each input referenced via `.past(k, ...)` in a recurrent stream body, the compiler statically determines that input's maximum `k` and allocates that many slots of `sizeof(T_input)`. (§13.18.13)
030-228. Total memory of a recurrent stream declaration is `ring_buffer(X) + N * sizeof(T_X) + Σ max_k(input_i) * sizeof(T_input_i)`. (§13.18.13)
030-229. Inputs not accessed via `.past` add no history overhead. (§13.18.13)
030-230. All recurrent-history allocations are made once at stream creation; nothing is allocated at runtime. (§13.18.13)
030-231. A stream's buffer survives hot reload iff its type signature — element type, policy, and capacity literal `N` — is byte-identical between old and new code. (§13.18.14)
030-232. Element-type identity for reload preservation is structural, not same-named. (§13.18.14)
030-233. On a signature-preserving reload, consumers preserved by their own identity rules continue from their previous cursor positions. (§13.18.14)
030-234. A stream's source expression may change freely across reload; only the type signature gates buffer preservation. (§13.18.14)
030-235. If any of element type, policy, or capacity differs at reload, the old buffer is discarded, a new empty buffer is allocated, and all cursors reset to its empty position. (§13.18.14)
030-236. `@reset_on_reload` on a stream declaration opts out of buffer preservation: after any reload affecting the declaration site, the buffer resets to empty regardless of type-signature match. (§13.18.14)
030-237. Adding `@reset_on_reload` applies prospectively: the adding reload still preserves the buffer per the type-signature rule, and the annotation takes effect on the next reload. (§13.18.14)
030-238. Removing `@reset_on_reload` still resets the buffer on the removing reload (the old annotation governs the in-progress reload); subsequent reloads preserve. (§13.18.14)
030-239. A consumer's cursor survives reload exactly when the consuming operator (or derived) instance is preserved per its own identity rule. (§13.18.14)
030-240. A consumer added by a reload starts its cursor at the current head, observing only events arriving after the reload. (§13.18.14)
030-241. A consumer removed by a reload has its cursor dropped. (§13.18.14)
030-242. Changes to a stream's element type, policy (`ring` ↔ `gate`), or capacity are reload-unsafe, requiring per-instance restart (the stream and its consumers are recreated) or full-runtime restart. (§13.18.14)
030-243. Implementations detect reload-unsafe stream changes during the reload diff phase. (§13.18.14)
030-244. Recurrent output history survives reload iff the quadruple `(element type, policy, capacity, N)` is byte-identical. (§13.18.14)
030-245. Rewriting a recurrent stream's `.past`-using source expression does not affect history preservation while the type signature matches. (§13.18.14)
030-246. Increasing `[N]` (e.g., `recurrent[3]` to `recurrent[5]`) is reload-safe: preserved history fills the lower-index slots and new slots start empty, serving fallbacks until filled. (§13.18.14)
030-247. Decreasing `[N]` (e.g., `recurrent[5]` to `recurrent[3]`) is reload-unsafe and forces per-instance restart of the affected recurrent-stream declarations. (§13.18.14)
030-248. Per-input history is preserved across reload iff the input stream's type signature is byte-identical and that input's max referenced `k` does not decrease. (§13.18.14)
030-249. Increasing an input's max `k` is reload-safe and extends its history allocation, older positions initializing from fallbacks. (§13.18.14)
030-279. Decreasing an input's max `k` is reload-safe: the allocation truncates to the most-recent `k` entries, which are retained (the window stays full; the oldest entries drop). (§13.18.14)
030-250. Removing all `.past` references to an input reload-safely releases its history allocation. (§13.18.14)
030-251. `@reset_on_reload` on a `recurrent[N] stream` resets the output history and all per-input history in addition to the base buffer. (§13.18.14)
030-252. `stream` declarations may not appear inside function bodies. (§13.18.15)
030-253. A stream's source expression must contain at least one reactive input (signal, stream, or other Cell); a pure-value expression cannot be a stream source. (§13.18.15)
030-254. Cursors are not first-class values: programs cannot construct, store, or pass them, and they are observable only through consumers' signal outputs. (§13.18.15)
030-255. Within one commit cycle, a stream consumer observes exactly the set of events committed by the end of producer evaluation. (§13.18.15)
030-256. Events emitted during a consumer's own evaluation are deferred to the next commit. (§13.18.15)
030-257. Streams may not be passed to `runtime.write_signal` or `runtime.write_attr`; host-side stream writes go through `runtime.push_stream`. (§13.18.15)
030-258. An output-stream lookback must satisfy `k ≤ N`: `recurrent[N] stream policy X = ... X.past(k, ...)` with `k > N` is a compile error. (§13.18.15)
030-259. Stream-valued expressions cannot be assigned to signal-typed bindings — `derived X = stream_expr` and `signal X = stream_expr` are compile errors; project via `to_signal(fallback)`. (§13.18.15)
030-260. A stream declaration without a policy keyword (`ring`/`gate`) is a normative error class: `stream my_events: Event = source` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-261. Operator and effect parameters typed `Stream[T]` are not satisfied by a signal (implicit `to_stream` covers bindings, not parameters); this is a normative error class: `persist(my_signal)` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-262. Assigning a stream-valued expression to a signal binding is a normative error class: `derived latest: Event = some_stream * 2` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-263. Reading a bare stream as a value is a normative error class: `derived latest: Event = events` ✗ when `events` is a stream. (§13.18.16)
030-264. A policy mismatch in a pipe chain is a normative error class: ring `writes |> persist` ✗ where `persist` takes a gate stream. (§13.18.16)
030-266. A `stream` declaration inside a function body is a normative error class: `stream ring[1024] events` inside `fn helper():` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-267. `.past`/`.previous` outside any recurrent's producing context — in a non-recurrent `stream` or `derived`, a non-recurrent `signal` arm — is a normative error class: `count.previous(0)` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-268. An output `.past(k, ...)` exceeding the declared `[N]` is a normative error class: `recurrent[3] stream ring x = x.past(5, 0)` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-269. A non-compile-time lookback `n` in `.past(n, fallback)` is a normative error class: `source.past(some_variable, 0)` ✗. (§13.18.16)
030-270. A stream has no separate write-side cell type (no `Sink[T]`); its write side is mechanism — events arrive through the declared `= source`, or for an effect's `observed:` stream the host API (`runtime.push_stream`). (§13.18.5)
030-271. The recurrent policies are `RecurrentRing[B, H]` / `RecurrentGate[B, H]` (buffer `B`, `H`-deep self-history as separate allocations); `RecurrentRingStream[T, B, H]` aliases `Stream[T, RecurrentRing[B, H]]`. (§13.18.3)
030-272. `Ring[N]` / `Gate[N]` are the no-self-history policies; recurrent streams use the `RecurrentRing`/`RecurrentGate` policies. (§13.18.3)
030-273. Capacity is carried by the policy (`Ring[N]`); a policy/capacity-polymorphic operator binds the policy with `P: StreamPolicy`. (§13.18.3)
030-274. `.past`/`.previous` are valid only in a recurrent's producing context — the body of a value `Recurrent[T, N]` (§13.2.4) or a recurrent stream (§13.18.8) declaration. (§13.18.15)
030-275. `StreamPolicy` is a sealed (language-closed) trait, like `Into`/`TryInto`; its members are exactly `Ring[N]`, `Gate[N]`, `RecurrentRing[B, H]`, `RecurrentGate[B, H]`, and users cannot add policies. (§13.18.3)
030-276. Each policy type carries its own buffer parameters: `Ring[const N: usize]`/`Gate[const N: usize]` carry capacity; `RecurrentRing[const B, const H]`/`RecurrentGate[const B, const H]` carry buffer and self-history. (§13.18.3)
030-277. An operator that requires a specific policy, or whose output policy is not its input's, names the policy concretely in its return type; there is no policy/capacity inheritance rule. (§13.18.3)
030-278. The stdlib provides transparent generic aliases for the common stream spellings: `RingStream[T, N]` = `Stream[T, Ring[N]]`, `GateStream[T, N]` = `Stream[T, Gate[N]]`, and the recurrent forms. (§13.18.3)

## 031. Effects — 152 Rules

031-1. An effect is a reusable, cell-allocating reactive construct describing a desired alignment between program state and external reality, declared with the `effect` keyword. (§13.19)
031-2. Effects are the mechanism by which programs interact with the outside world: network requests, persistent storage, long-lived resources, and event subscriptions. (§13.19)
031-3. Effects are distinct from `fn` (reactive-transparent pure computation), `operator` (stateful cell-to-cell transform, pure with respect to outside reality), and `node` (topological graph participant): only `effect` is the host-interpreted bridge to the runtime environment. (§13.19)
031-4. Every effect type intrinsically satisfies the `Effect` marker trait, so "any effect" is expressible as a bound: `[T: Effect]`. (§13.19)
031-5. Like nodes and connections, an effect instance is a graph member: instantiated only in a node's `effects:` clause, held by reference, never stored as a value. (§13.19)
031-6. An effect type (as opposed to an instance) is carried as a value via `Type[…]`. (§13.19)
031-7. An effect declaration consists of two record-shaped blocks, `desired:` and `observed:`. (§13.19.1)
031-8. Cells in `desired:` are written by the program (or flow from the effect's parameters) and read by the host. (§13.19.1)
031-9. Cells in `observed:` are written by the host and read by the program through the standard reactive machinery. (§13.19.1)
031-10. The reconciler reads an instance's parameters and desired cells, performs the real-world operations that align reality with the desired state, and writes the actual outcome into the observed cells. (§13.19.1)
031-11. An effect declaration has the form `effect name[GenericParams]?(params...):` followed by its cell blocks. (§13.19.2)
031-12. An effect's name is a snake_case identifier. (§13.19.2)
031-13. The `desired:` block and the `observed:` block are each individually optional in an effect declaration. (§13.19.2)
031-14. Every cell declaration in an effect block carries an explicit role keyword: `derived`, `recurrent`, or `stream` in `desired:`, `signal` or `stream` in `observed:`. (§13.19.2)
031-15. Reactive declarations other than the five role-keyword cell forms (e.g. `attr`, top-level `signal`) cannot appear inside an effect's body. (§13.19.2)
031-16. The `desired:` and `observed:` blocks may be declared in either order; the canonical order is `desired:` first, `observed:` second. (§13.19.2)
031-17. An effect does not return a value in the operator sense; it evaluates to its instance value, accessed through the binding name or expression position. (§13.19.2)
031-18. Effect parameters are cell-bound or value-typed, under the same rules as operator parameters. (§13.19.3)
031-19. A cell-bound parameter (`Cell[T]` for a value cell, or `Stream[T]` for an event stream) binds to a reactive cell at instantiation. (§13.19.3)
031-20. The reconciler reads a cell-bound parameter's current value — or observes its events, for stream parameters — during each invocation in which the parameter is dirty. (§13.19.3)
031-21. Inside `desired:` cell expressions, a cell-bound parameter is treated as a cell of value type `T` (auto-deref). (§13.19.3)
031-22. A value parameter (`name: T`) is snapshotted at instantiation and fixed for the effect instance's lifetime. (§13.19.3)
031-23. Value-parameter snapshotting is a category-B storage operation with implicit-move semantics; no `move` keyword is required. (§13.19.3)
031-24. Defaults are allowed on effect value parameters, under the standard parameter-ordering rules: `method: Method = Method::GET`. (§13.19.3)
031-25. Defaults are not allowed on `Cell[T]` effect parameters in v1. (§13.19.3)
031-26. An effect's first positional parameter is its implicit pipe target: `current_url |> fetch(method: Method::POST)`. (§13.19.3)
031-27. Effect parameters may be stream-typed (`Stream[T]`, `RingStream[T, N]`, `GateStream[T, N]`): `entries: GateStream[LogEntry, 4096]`. (§13.19.3)
031-28. A reactive composite parameter has each constituent field tracked independently for dirty propagation to the reconciler. (§13.19.3)
031-29. A `derived` cell in `desired:` is a parameter-tracking pure reactive expression over parameters and in-scope cells, recomputed when any input changes: `derived request: Request = Request(method: method, target: url)`. (§13.19.4)
031-30. Program code may read a `desired:` `derived` cell via the standard access path but cannot write it: `f.request`. (§13.19.4)
031-31. A `desired:` event-output cell is declared with `stream` (reusing the keyword): `stream ring[1024] outbound: Message = source`; the effect feeds the source from its own parameters or `observed:` cells, and the host's reconciler drains the buffer. (§13.19.4)
031-32. A desired-block event-output stream comprises a policy keyword (`ring` or `gate`), an optional bracketed capacity defaulting to 1024, a name, an element type, and a `= source` expression. (§13.19.4)
031-33. A desired-block event-output stream's `= source` is fed internally by the effect (from parameters or `observed:` cells); there is no external program-side wiring into it. (§13.19.4)
031-34. Cell names within a single `desired:` block must be distinct. (§13.19.4)
031-35. The reconciler reads `desired:` `derived` cells on each invocation where any of them is dirty. (§13.19.4)
031-36. The reconciler consumes a desired-block event-output stream's buffered events in order. (§13.19.4)
031-40. A `desired:` block may contain `when` and `given` selection blocks whose arms hold desired-cell declarations. (§13.19.4)
031-41. `when`/`given` arms in `desired:` carry standard freeze semantics (Model B): every arm is constructed, the active arm's cells are live, inactive arms' cells are frozen at their last value. (§13.19.4)
031-42. A frozen desired arm contributes static values to the reconciler: it neither goes dirty nor triggers an `update`. (§13.19.4)
031-43. When a gate over desired cells reopens, the arm's cells recompute, go dirty, and the reconciler updates with the now-live values. (§13.19.4)
031-44. Freezing a desired arm pins that part of the desired state and stops tracking it while gated; it does not release the resource. (§13.19.4)
031-45. Resource release on inactivity is the reconciler's response to the `suspend` hook, not an effect of `when`/`given` blocks in `desired:`. (§13.19.4)
031-46. A `signal` cell in `observed:` declares a name, a value type, and an initial value: `signal response: Option[Response] = None`. (§13.19.5)
031-47. An observed signal's initial value is what the program reads before the host's first write. (§13.19.5)
031-48. The host writes observed `signal` cells via `runtime.write_signal` against the effect instance ID. (§13.19.5)
031-49. A `stream` cell in `observed:` is a host-written event sequence: `stream ring[1024] inbound: Message`. (§13.19.5)
031-50. An observed `stream` declaration has no `= source` clause; its source is the host's reconciler pushing events via `runtime.push_stream`. (§13.19.5)
031-51. Policy and capacity on observed `stream` cells work as in regular stream declarations. (§13.19.5)
031-52. `recurrent[N] stream` is not valid in `observed:` blocks; observed cells have no reactive expression body. (§13.19.5)
031-53. Because `observed:` blocks admit no recurrent streams, an effect's history-aware behavior is computed in the host's reconciler. (§13.19.5)
031-54. `repeat` and the `when`/`given` selection blocks are not valid in `observed:` blocks. (§13.19.5)
031-55. Gating driven by an observed cell uses `when`/`given` in the effect's `desired:` block (or an `expose:` block); per-element materialization is a node's `effects:`-clause `repeat`, not an effect block. (§13.19.5)
031-56. An observed `stream` cell begins empty. (§13.19.5)
031-57. Cell names within a single `observed:` block must be distinct. (§13.19.5)
031-58. Writing to an observed cell from program code is a compile error: `f.response = some(custom_response)` ✗. (§13.19.5)
031-59. Host-side writes to observed cells — `runtime.write_signal` for signal cells, `runtime.push_stream` for stream cells — are keyed by effect instance ID and cell name. (§13.19.5)
031-60. Host writes to observed cells are dirty-tracked in the standard commit-cycle way; downstream deriveds re-evaluate on the next commit. (§13.19.5)
031-61. The identifiers `desired` and `observed` are reserved inside an effect declaration body; cells cannot bear these names: `derived desired: bool` ✗. (§13.19.6)
031-62. `desired` and `observed` are reserved keywords everywhere; within an `effect` body they introduce the desired and observed blocks. (§13.19.6)
031-63. An effect cannot declare cells with the same name in both `desired:` and `observed:`. (§13.19.6)
031-64. `instance.field` resolves to whichever block declares `field`; the compiler enforces that exactly one block does. (§13.19.6)
031-65. Effect instances expose their cells through a flat access path: `desired:` and `observed:` cells share a single namespace on the instance: `f.response`. (§13.19.7)
031-66. Assigning to any effect cell — signal, stream, or derived — from program code is a compile error. (§13.19.7)
031-71. Effect stream cells are consumed via stream operators, never via direct value reads; the stream value is the program-readable cursor view: `ws.inbound |> to_signal(empty_message)`. (§13.19.7)
031-72. A stream cell's observation surface is read by field access on the stream value: `ws.inbound.pressure`. (§13.19.7)
031-73. An effect instance is identified by its enclosing scope, its effect type name, and its argument bindings — the operator-instance scheme. (§13.19.8)
031-74. Instantiations of the same effect type in different scopes — different nodes' `effects:` clauses, or different `repeat`/gate arms within one clause — produce distinct instances with independent desired/observed cells and independent host-side reconciler state. (§13.19.8)
031-75. Effect instances have no user-assignable names in the language sense. (§13.19.8)
031-76. A named `effects:` entry (`name = source |> effect`) names the instance value for downstream reads; the entry name plays no role in instance identity. (§13.19.8)
031-77. Effect hot-reload identity follows the operator scheme, with tolerance for positional moves within the same clause. (§13.19.8)
031-78. The effect's declared name serves as the type in signatures, annotations, and bounds, and as the constructor in pipe chains and call form — unlike nodes, which separate type names from placement syntax: `effect fetch` yields both type `fetch` and constructor `fetch`. (§13.19.8)
031-79. Function-call form instantiates an effect equivalently to pipe form: `f2 = fetch(current_url)`. (§13.19.8)
031-80. Effects may take type parameters with optional trait bounds: `effect cached_fetch[T: Cacheable](...)`. (§13.19.9)
031-81. Effect type parameters are resolved at the call site from argument types, with explicit turbofish where ambiguous. (§13.19.9)
031-82. Reconciler registration is per-effect-type and per-type-parameter-instantiation: each concrete instantiation of a generic effect has a distinct registration key. (§13.19.9)
031-83. Effects carry the standard three-level visibility: `public`, `shared` (the default), `private`. (§13.19.10)
031-84. Module-private effects are not reachable from other modules; public effects may be re-exported. (§13.19.10)
031-85. Visibility applies uniformly to an effect's type and constructor. (§13.19.10)
031-86. An effect instance is a scoped reload boundary, like an operator instance. (§13.19.11)
031-87. For reload, each cell in an effect's blocks is identified by its declared name and type within the effect's body. (§13.19.11)
031-88. Changing the initial-value expression of an observed `signal` cell is reload-safe; existing live cells retain their committed values. (§13.19.11)
031-89. Adding a new cell in `observed:` or `desired:` is reload-safe; new cells initialize fresh — declared initial value for signals, empty for streams. (§13.19.11)
031-90. Changing a `desired:` `derived` cell's expression is reload-safe; the cell re-evaluates on the next commit with the new logic. (§13.19.11)
031-91. Effect parameter signature changes (parameters added, removed, retyped, or reordered) are reload-unsafe and force per-instance restart. (§13.19.11)
031-92. Cell type changes in `desired:` or `observed:` are reload-unsafe and force per-instance restart. (§13.19.11)
031-93. Policy or capacity changes on an effect's `stream` cells are reload-unsafe and force per-instance restart. (§13.19.11)
031-94. Changing a cell's role keyword (e.g. `derived` to `stream`, `signal` to `stream`) is reload-unsafe and forces per-instance restart. (§13.19.11)
031-95. Per-instance restart delivers a reconciler teardown for the affected instances and constructs new instances under the new declaration. (§13.19.11)
031-96. Per-instance effect restart leaves other effect instances and the rest of the runtime running. (§13.19.11)
031-97. A suspended effect instance reloads by its ordinary identity rules: a reload-safe change preserves the instance and its reconciler-side state, and it stays suspended with no spurious `resume`. (§13.19.11)
031-98. After a reload-safe change to a suspended instance, a later gate-open delivers `resume` against the reloaded declaration. (§13.19.11)
031-99. A reload-unsafe change to a suspended instance delivers `teardown` (subsuming the already-performed suspend release) and constructs a fresh instance. (§13.19.11)
031-100. A fresh instance constructed by reload begins in whatever activation state its gate evaluates to on the next commit. (§13.19.11)
031-101. Changing which effect a call site invokes tears down the old instance and constructs the new one fresh, even if cell shapes overlap: `source |> fetch` to `source |> cached_fetch`. (§13.19.11)
031-102. Pure positional call-site moves within the same scope preserve effect instance identity. (§13.19.11)
031-103. A stream cell's buffer inside an effect is preserved across reload iff `(element type, policy, capacity)` is byte-identical. (§13.19.11)
031-104. `@reset_on_reload` on an effect's stream cell forces a buffer clear on reload. (§13.19.11)
031-105. An effect instance lives as long as its enclosing scope; scope death drops the instance and all its cells. (§13.19.12)
031-106. On instance teardown the host's reconciler receives a teardown call with the instance ID, allowing release of external resources. (§13.19.12)
031-107. An effect in a node's `effects:` clause lives as long as the node instance is mounted. (§13.19.12)
031-108. An application-global effect is hosted in a top-level node instance and lasts the program's lifetime. (§13.19.12)
031-109. An effect inside a `repeat` in `effects:` lives until its element key is removed from the iterated source. (§13.19.12)
031-110. An effect inside a `when`/`given` block in `effects:` is suspended while the gate is closed and resumed when it reopens; gating never destroys it. (§13.19.12)
031-111. A desired value implying no resource is needed (e.g. `derived target: Option[Url] = None`) makes the host tear down the resource while the effect instance stays alive, ready to re-establish. (§13.19.12)
031-112. Only scope death causes effect instance teardown. (§13.19.12)
031-113. Gating off an effect's enclosing subtree delivers the reconciler's `suspend` hook: the resource is released, instance state preserved. (§13.19.12)
031-114. Reopening the gate delivers `resume`, re-establishing the resource. (§13.19.12)
031-115. Gating is transitive: closing any ancestor gate suspends every contained effect, regardless of the effects' own gates. (§13.19.12)
031-116. Effects are the sole construct the suspend/resume protocol concerns; pure nodes and connections have no `suspend`/`resume`. (§13.19.12)
031-117. The runtime guarantees the `suspend` signal on gate-close and `resume` on gate-open. (§13.19.12)
031-118. What releasing and re-acquiring a resource mean under suspend/resume is decided by the reconciler. (§13.19.12)
031-119. No source-level activation value must be threaded through `desired:`; the suspend/resume hook is the gating mechanism. (§13.19.12)
031-120. Effect instances are created by piping into them, identically to operators, with the instantiation in the node's `effects:` clause: `f = current_url |> fetch`. (§13.19.13)
031-121. The LHS of `|>` binds to the effect's first positional parameter. (§13.19.13)
031-122. The value of an effect-instantiating pipe is the effect instance: the composite of its `desired:` and `observed:` cells. (§13.19.13)
031-123. An effect instance never auto-projects to a single cell in pipe-out position. (§13.19.13)
031-124. Operators downstream of an effect take either the whole instance (parameter typed with the effect type) or a cell projected by field access: `f |> render_fetch_card` vs `f.response |> render_response`. (§13.19.13)
031-125. The host registers a reconciler for each effect type via the host API, keyed by the effect's type name. (§13.19.14)
031-126. A reconciler is a host-language object whose interface mirrors the effect's declaration. (§13.19.14)
031-127. A reconciler has read access to an instance's parameter values and `desired:` cell values. (§13.19.14)
031-128. A reconciler writes an instance's `observed:` cells via the host API: `runtime.write_signal` for signal cells, `runtime.push_stream` for stream cells. (§13.19.14)
031-129. A reconciler exposes lifecycle hooks: instance creation (effect appears in the live graph), update (parameters or desired cells change), and teardown (instance leaves scope). (§13.19.14)
031-130. After commit, the runtime enumerates effect instances whose parameters or desired cells became dirty during that commit. (§13.19.14)
031-131. For each such instance, the runtime invokes the registered reconciler with the instance ID; the reconciler reads the new desired state and reconciles. (§13.19.14)
031-132. Reconciler writes into observed cells are dirty-tracked in the standard way and take effect on the next commit. (§13.19.14)
031-133. Reconcilers are expected to be idempotent: re-applying the same desired state produces the same alignment, with no duplicated side effects or leaked resources — permitting the runtime to invoke the reconciler freely. (§13.19.14)
031-134. An effect type appearing in the graph with no registered reconciler triggers a startup diagnostic, and the runtime refuses to enter the live state. (§13.19.14)
031-135. Reconciler errors are reported to the program through the effect's `observed:` cells: `signal error: Option[E] = None`. (§13.19.14)
031-136. A reconciler does not panic the runtime; reconciler-internal errors are domain errors expressed through the value track. (§13.19.14)
031-137. A node's `effects:` clause is the single, exclusive host for effect instantiation. (§13.19.15)
031-138. Effect instantiation is forbidden at module level, in operator bodies, in connection bodies, and in function bodies. (§13.19.15)
031-139. Within a node, effect instantiation outside the `effects:` clause is a compile error: a free `f = url |> fetch` ✗. (§13.19.15)
031-140. An effect's `desired:` block may not contain another effect's instantiation: effects do not compose from effects. (§13.19.15)
031-141. Effects are composed at the consumer site by feeding one effect's observed cells into another effect's parameters. (§13.19.15)
031-142. Effects are not topological participants and are never placed via node-style `EffectName name /` placement syntax. (§13.19.15)
031-143. Effects are instantiated by appearance in expression position: pipe chains or function-call form. (§13.19.15)
031-144. An effect's body consists only of the `desired:` and `observed:` blocks containing role-keyword cell declarations. (§13.19.15)
031-145. Stateful behavior wrapping an effect — recurrent state, accumulators, retry logic — is expressed via wrapping operators, not via declarations inside the effect. (§13.19.15)
031-146. A normative diagnostic class covers use of a reserved cell name (`desired`/`observed`) inside an effect's blocks. (§13.19.16)
031-147. A normative diagnostic class covers a cell name duplicated across `desired:` and `observed:`. (§13.19.16)
031-148. A normative diagnostic class covers program-code assignment to an effect cell. (§13.19.16)
031-149. A normative diagnostic class covers an effect type with no registered reconciler, raised at runtime startup. (§13.19.16)
031-150. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation inside a function body. (§13.19.16)
031-151. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation inside another effect's body. (§13.19.16)
031-152. A normative diagnostic class covers a non-role-keyword declaration inside an effect's blocks, and `recurrent` in `observed:` (host-fed cells have no expression body); `recurrent` is allowed in `desired:`. (§13.19.16)
031-153. A normative diagnostic class covers an effect type used in node-style placement position. (§13.19.16)
031-154. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation in an `expose:` block. (§13.19.16)
031-155. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation at module level. (§13.19.16)
031-156. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation in an operator body. (§13.19.16)
031-157. A normative diagnostic class covers effect instantiation in a node body outside the `effects:` clause. (§13.19.16)
031-158. `recurrent` is allowed in an effect's `desired:` block (a desired value may depend on its own history) but forbidden in `observed:` (host-fed cells have no expression body for `.past` to read). (§13.19.4)
031-159. A `desired:` event-output stream's `= source` may read the effect's own `observed:` cells (feedback); such a cycle must pass a delay (a `recurrent`) or a commit boundary so it cannot inherently diverge. (§13.19.4)

## 032. Implementation Model — 176 Rules

032-1. The implementation model is normative for implementations of Ductus, not for source-level code; program behavior is determined by §§1–13. (§14)
032-2. A conforming implementation provides two compilation modes: interpreter mode and native mode. (§14.1)
032-3. Interpreter mode compiles Ductus source to a compact bytecode executed by an interpreter embedded in the runtime. (§14.1)
032-4. Native mode compiles Ductus source to a native executable. (§14.1)
032-5. Both compilation modes share one frontend — lexer, parser, type checker, semantic analysis — producing a typed intermediate representation; the modes diverge only after that point. (§14.1)
032-6. The two compilation modes must produce equivalent observable behavior (modulo performance and timing); an implementation that diverges observably between modes is non-conforming. (§14.1)
032-7. The frontend's first pass lexes and parses per this specification, producing an AST. (§14.1.1)
032-8. The frontend's second pass performs name resolution and type checking per §§2–10, yielding a typed AST with all generic instantiations resolved and all trait dispatch sites bound to concrete implementations. (§14.1.1)
032-9. The frontend's third pass performs borrow and ownership checking per §11, catching use-after-move, borrow conflicts, and other ownership violations. (§14.1.1)
032-10. The frontend's fourth pass performs reactive analysis per §13: it identifies reactive declarations, computes dependency graphs, and extracts the graph specification. (§14.1.1)
032-11. The frontend's fifth pass monomorphizes per §2.3, resolving all generic instantiations before lowering. (§14.1.1)
032-12. Monomorphization is never delegated to a backend; emitted code is fully concrete. (§14.1.1)
032-13. In interpreter mode the bytecode emitter lowers the typed IR to a stack-based bytecode executed directly by the runtime's interpreter. (§14.1.2)
032-14. No native compilation step occurs in interpreter mode. (§14.1.2)
032-15. Interpreter mode supports hot reload (§14.8): individual behaviors can be replaced in a running runtime without restarting. (§14.1.2)
032-16. The bytecode format is implementation-internal and not stable across Ductus versions. (§14.1.2)
032-17. The bytecode format is not a distribution format. (§14.1.2)
032-18. In native mode the backend lowers the typed IR to a native executable, and that binary is the distribution artifact. (§14.1.3)
032-19. How an implementation generates native code — its intermediate form and toolchain — is a backend concern outside the specification. (§14.1.3)
032-20. Native mode produces a single executable embedding both the compiled behaviors and the graph specification (§15.4). (§14.1.3)
032-21. Native mode does not support hot reload at runtime; changing the program requires a rebuild. (§14.1.3)
032-22. Emitted native code is fully monomorphic and Ductus-trait-free, with concrete type and function definitions per instantiation. (§14.1.3)
032-23. The trait system is not exported into emitted native code; trait dispatch sites are resolved to direct function calls during frontend processing. (§14.1.3)
032-24. A conforming implementation provides a command-line interface wrapping the compilation modes. (§14.2)
032-25. The CLI's operations are normative and required; specific flag spellings may vary across implementations. (§14.2)
032-26. The CLI `run` operation invokes interpreter mode, compiling to bytecode and executing immediately until program completion or interruption: `ductus run <file>`. (§14.2.1)
032-27. The CLI `watch` operation runs interpreter mode with file watching; saved source changes trigger recompilation and hot reload of affected behaviors per §14.8: `ductus watch <file>`. (§14.2.1)
032-28. The CLI `build` operation invokes native mode and outputs a single executable file, with `--release` enabling optimization: `ductus build <file> --release`. (§14.2.1)
032-29. The CLI `check` operation runs frontend passes only, producing diagnostics without invoking either backend, and is used by editor integrations (LSP): `ductus check <file>`. (§14.2.1)
032-30. The CLI `fmt` operation invokes the canonical formatter, rewriting the source in normalized form: `ductus fmt <file>`. (§14.2.1)
032-31. The CLI `test` operation runs tests via interpreter mode: `ductus test <file>`. (§14.2.1)
032-32. The CLI ships as a single binary that bundles or downloads on first use the Ductus frontend, the bytecode interpreter, the native-mode toolchain, and the Ductus stdlib and reactive runtime. (§14.2.2)
032-33. Users never install or manage the native toolchain separately; all of its invocations are internal. (§14.2.2)
032-34. Native-toolchain build output is suppressed in normal operation and surfaced only when a compilation failure prevents Ductus's output from being produced. (§14.2.2)
032-35. A Ductus project is a directory tree containing `.duc` source files. (§14.2.3)
032-36. No manifest file is required for single-file programs: `ductus run file.duc` works on a lone file. (§14.2.3)
032-37. Multi-file projects use a manifest file specifying the entry point and any external dependencies. (§14.2.3)
032-38. The manifest file format is implementation-specific. (§14.2.3)
032-39. The runtime holds the value of every reactive cell and keeps it current. (§14.3)
032-40. How cells are stored is a backend concern; every backend must preserve the per-cell value semantics. (§14.3)
032-41. Each reactive cell holds a value of its declared type, read and written losslessly (bit-exact). (§14.3.1)
032-42. A backend may represent cells however it likes as long as reads and writes are value-preserving. (§14.3.1)
032-43. Types wider than 8 bytes — `i128`, `u128`, multi-field records used as reactive values — occupy multiple consecutive cells. (§14.3.2)
032-44. An `i128` reactive value occupies two cells: the low 64 bits in cell N, the high 64 bits in cell N+1. (§14.3.2)
032-45. The canonical layout for a record used as a reactive value is one cell per field, and conforming implementations must support it: `Vec3` with `f32` fields `x`, `y`, `z` takes three consecutive cells. (§14.3.2)
032-46. In the canonical layout, sub-8-byte fields pad to a full 8-byte cell: an `f32` field pads from 4 to 8 bytes. (§14.3.2)
032-47. An implementation may optionally pack multiple sub-8-byte fields into one cell as an optimization: four `bool` fields in the low bits of one cell. (§14.3.2)
032-48. Field packing must not be observable from Ductus source: every cell read and write through the runtime API must match the canonical per-field layout exactly. (§14.3.2)
032-49. Records with fields wider than 8 bytes follow the same per-field layout, each multi-cell field occupying its own consecutive cells within the enclosing record's allocation. (§14.3.2)
032-50. Dynamic-size cell types per §13.12.4 (`Vec[T]`, `SmallVec[T, N]`, `RingBuf[T, N]`, `string`, etc.) cannot live in fixed-size cell slots; their storage uses extensible pools. (§14.3.3)
032-51. The runtime allocates one pool per dynamic-size type used in the program. (§14.3.3)
032-52. A dynamic-size reactive cell holds a fixed-size index into its type's pool; the variable-size value lives in the pool. (§14.3.3)
032-53. On commit the runtime writes the new value into the pool and updates the cell's index so subsequent reads resolve to it. (§14.3.3)
032-54. An observer that reads a cell never sees a partial write. (§14.3.3)
032-55. An index read resolves to a value coherent with the snapshot it was read from. (§14.3.3)
032-56. Each pool is an arena of slots; a slot holds one value of the pool's type plus the metadata needed to keep values of distinct committed states distinct until released. (§14.3.3)
032-57. A pool read resolves the cell's index through the pool to the value's address — one pointer dereference — then reads the value. (§14.3.3)
032-58. A value observed under a committed state remains valid until that state is released, even after a later commit updates the cell's index. (§14.3.3)
032-59. For persistent data structures (e.g., `Vec[T]` as a persistent vector trie), pool slots may share internal nodes across versions. (§14.3.3)
032-60. The pool tracks persistent-structure node-level refcounts and reclaims old nodes when no live state references them. (§14.3.3)
032-61. For value types (`SmallVec[T, N]`, `RingBuf[T, N]`), each retained value is a complete copy, bounding per-commit work by the value's size. (§14.3.3)
032-62. Pool sizes are chosen at runtime construction from the graph specification's static per-type cell count plus a configurable versioning headroom. (§14.3.3)
032-63. Pools may grow at runtime when the configured headroom is exceeded; growth is amortized but not guaranteed wait-free. (§14.3.3)
032-64. Hosts needing strict wait-free guarantees should configure sufficient pool headroom up front. (§14.3.3)
032-65. Pool slot acquire is O(1) amortized per commit, via a free-list in the pool. (§14.3.3)
032-66. Per-commit value copy into a pool costs O(value-size). (§14.3.3)
032-67. Persistent structures copy O(log n) nodes per push. (§14.3.3)
032-68. Per-cell memory overhead for pool-backed types is one 8-byte index slot. (§14.3.3)
032-69. Stream buffers (§13.18) are pool-managed: a stream declaration with element type `T` and capacity `N` allocates a buffer of `N * sizeof(T)` bytes. (§14.3.3)
032-70. All stream instances sharing the same `(T, N)` combination across the program draw from one per-`(T, N)` pool. (§14.3.3)
032-71. The runtime enumerates stream declarations at compile time, groups them by `(T, N)`, and sizes each pool by the number of instances of that combination. (§14.3.3)
032-72. Each stream-pool slot holds one complete buffer. (§14.3.3)
032-73. A stream's metadata cells — head pointer, dropped/rejected counters, observation cells per §13.18.6 — live in the standard reactive state buffer; only the buffer's slot array lives in the per-`(T, N)` pool. (§14.3.3)
032-74. Hot reload can grow or shrink stream pools as stream declarations are added or removed, via the same extensible-pool mechanism. (§14.3.3)
032-75. A preserved stream (per §13.18.14's preservation rule) retains its pool slot across hot reload; a new stream allocates a new slot. (§14.3.3)
032-76. Stream buffer slot arrays are not versioned per snapshot. (§14.3.3)
032-77. Stream synchronization relies on the head pointer, which is part of reactive state: the runtime writes events into slot positions, then advances the committed head pointer at commit. (§14.3.3)
032-78. An observer sees stream events only up to the head pointer of the snapshot it reads; writes beyond the committed head are invisible until the next commit. (§14.3.3)
032-79. Under `ring` policy, previously committed slots are overwritten only at positions past any caught-up cursor. (§14.3.3)
032-80. Lagging cursors that were pointing at overwritten positions jump forward per §13.18.12. (§14.3.3)
032-81. Only reactive cells are reactive state: values the runtime owns and keeps current across commits. (§14.4)
032-82. Values held by `signal` declarations are reactive state. (§14.4)
032-83. Values held by `attr` declarations on node and connection instances are reactive state. (§14.4)
032-84. Values held by `recurrent` declarations on node and connection instances are reactive state. (§14.4)
032-85. A `derived` declaration's cached computed value is reactive state. (§14.4)
032-86. A `stream` declaration's reactive state is its head pointer, metadata, and synthesized observation cells per §13.18.6; the buffer slot array itself lives in the per-`(T, N)` pool per §14.3.3. (§14.4)
032-87. Cells declared inside an `effect`'s `desired:` and `observed:` blocks (§13.19.4, §13.19.5) are reactive state — ordinary Signal or Stream cells per their declared type. (§14.4)
032-88. An `effect` is a grouping construct, not a new storage category. (§14.4)
032-90. Local bindings, parameters, return values, iterator state, closure captures, and non-reactive record/array/tuple values are not reactive state; they are ordinary stack or heap values under §11's ownership and borrow rules. (§14.4)
032-91. A record type may be reactive state in one context and an ordinary value in another within one program; the compiler decides by declaration site, not by type. (§14.4)
032-92. The runtime maintains a separate string pool that stores all string content. (§14.5)
032-93. The string pool is logically a refcounted-shared, append-mostly arena: each unique string is stored once and shared via reference counts. (§14.5)
032-94. Reactive cells of type `string` store a u64 pool index rather than the content; the pool resolves the index to the string data. (§14.5)
032-95. The string pool is shared across all snapshots: cells and the snapshots retaining them hold indices; the pool holds the data. (§14.5.1)
032-96. Commit cost is O(N) in cell count, not in string content size: changing a 1-megabyte string updates a single 8-byte index. (§14.5.1)
032-97. A string may be referenced by multiple cells, in the same or different snapshots, via shared indices. (§14.5.1)
032-98. An observer acquiring or releasing a snapshot never touches per-string refcounts, keeping index-to-string resolution wait-free. (§14.5.1)
032-99. Per-string refcount changes happen producer-side — at write/publish and at producer-side retirement of a superseded snapshot — never on the observer's read path. (§14.5.1)
032-100. String allocation stores a new string in the pool and returns an index, with the refcount initialized to 1 for the cell that will hold it. (§14.5.2)
032-101. Copying a string index into another cell increments the pool's refcount on that string. (§14.5.2)
032-102. Overwriting a cell or retiring a snapshot holding a string index decrements that string's refcount. (§14.5.2)
032-103. When a string's refcount reaches zero, the pool reclaims its storage. (§14.5.2)
032-104. Lookup resolves an index to its string value and is wait-free with a proper pool structure. (§14.5.2)
032-105. String-pool allocation and refcount operations are atomic but may block briefly under contention. (§14.5.2)
032-106. Which execution context performs string-pool writes versus reads is a backend concern; the contract is only that index reads stay wait-free. (§14.5.2)
032-107. The behavior ABI — how a runtime invokes a behavior by ID with input-cell values and receives its outputs — is defined abstractly in the Behavior IR (§15.4.4). (§14.6)
032-108. The runtime reaches a behavior only through the behavior ABI; it never reaches inside. (§14.6)
032-109. Behaviors are stateless at the runtime level: pure transformations that read inputs from cells, compute, and write outputs, with all state in reactive cells. (§14.6.1)
032-110. A stateful computation (filter with history, oscillator with phase, accumulator) is structured as a record whose attrs hold the state plus a behavior that reads the state-attrs, computes new state, and writes back. (§14.6.1)
032-111. Local mutation within a behavior — `mut` bindings, indexed assignment, iterator state — is permitted per §§11–12 but visible only within that invocation; it does not escape. (§14.6.1)
032-112. Behaviors follow the two-track failure model uniformly: trap-track failures (overflow under default operators, division by zero, out-of-range indices, explicit `panic`) abort the process. (§14.6.2)
032-113. Recoverable conditions in behaviors are expressed as value-track `Option`/`Result` values flowing through the type system. (§14.6.2)
032-114. The runtime does not isolate behavior traps: no `catch_unwind` boundary, no errored-cell sentinel, no continuation past a trap. (§14.6.2)
032-115. Behavior IDs are assigned at compile time and are content-addressed: a stable hash of the canonicalized typed IR of the behavior body. (§14.6.3)
032-116. Canonicalization before hashing alpha-renames locals, sorts declaration order where order is irrelevant, and strips position information. (§14.6.3)
032-117. Cosmetic changes — added whitespace, reordered independent declarations, renamed local bindings — do not perturb a behavior's ID. (§14.6.3)
032-118. Semantic changes — different operations, different inputs, different output type — produce different behavior IDs. (§14.6.3)
032-119. The behavior-ID hash algorithm is fixed per Ductus toolchain version (§14.9). (§14.6.3)
032-120. Canonicalization may change across major toolchain versions. (§14.6.3)
032-121. Cross-version hot reload is not supported. (§14.6.3)
032-122. Each behavior carries a debug name, the qualified source path: `module::path::clip_name::derived_name`. (§14.6.3)
032-123. The runtime resolves behaviors by ID; debug names appear only in diagnostic output (diagnostics, profiles, error messages). (§14.6.3)
032-124. Which execution context invokes each behavior, and how it maps onto OS threads, is a backend concern; Ductus source does not specify thread roles. (§14.6.4)
032-125. Behaviors are thread-safe by construction: no shared mutable state exists outside reactive cells, which the runtime coordinates. (§14.6.4)
032-126. The `Drop` trait declares a single method receiving the value by `own`: `trait Drop: fn drop(own value: Subject)`. (§14.7.1)
032-127. A type implementing `Drop` provides cleanup logic that runs when a value of the type goes out of scope. (§14.7.1)
032-128. The `drop` method is the value's final owner. (§14.7.1)
032-129. A `drop` body may move fields out to release them and, for in-place teardown, rebind the value to a `mut` local (§11.7.3) — the ordinary `own`-parameter-to-`mut`-local pattern. (§14.7.1)
032-130. `Drop::drop` involves no `mut` parameter; §11.7.2's prohibition on `mut` parameters holds without exception. (§14.7.1)
032-131. The compiler emits the consuming `drop` call — a `move` of the value into `drop` — at scope exit. (§14.7.1)
032-132. Fields left un-moved by a `drop` body drop afterward via drop glue, in reverse declaration order. (§14.7.1)
032-133. Drop glue does not re-invoke `Drop::drop` on the value itself. (§14.7.1)
032-134. A drop call is inserted at the end of a value's lexical scope, when its `let` or `mut` binding goes out of scope. (§14.7.2)
032-135. A drop call is inserted at the end of a function for un-returned locals. (§14.7.2)
032-136. A drop call is inserted at the point of `break` for non-yielded iterator elements (§12.9.3). (§14.7.2)
032-137. Compound values (records, enums) drop their fields in reverse declaration order: the last-declared field drops first. (§14.7.2)
032-138. When only some fields of a record have been moved out at scope exit, only the un-moved fields drop. (§14.7.3)
032-139. The compiler tracks per-binding move flags during semantic analysis. (§14.7.3)
032-140. A panic inside a `drop` method aborts the process, per the standard trap behavior of §4.6.1. (§14.7.4)
032-141. The runtime manages drop for reactive cells. (§14.7.5)
032-142. Signals declared at program startup live for the program's lifetime; their cells drop at program shutdown. (§14.7.5)
032-143. The unit of hot reload is the behavior. (§14.8.1)
032-144. Hot reload begins when the CLI's watch mode detects a source change and the frontend re-runs on the changed file. (§14.8.1)
032-145. Reload diffs behaviors by content-addressed ID per §14.6.3: IDs only in the old program are removed, IDs only in the new program are added, IDs in both carry over unchanged. (§14.8.1)
032-146. Reload diffs cells by fully-qualified declaration path per §13.15.2: cells with matching path and type carry forward preserving values, new cells are added, removed cells are dropped per §14.7. (§14.8.1)
032-147. Hot reload matches operator instances by (enclosing scope, operator name, argument bindings), tolerating positional moves within the same scope. (§14.8.1)
032-148. Reload-matched operator instances preserve their internal cell state; unmatched instances are dropped or added with the corresponding cell churn. (§14.8.1)
032-149. An added behavior registers in the behavior table at its content-addressed ID; graph-spec edges and cell allocations referencing that ID become live, and subsequent invocations dispatch through it. (§14.8.1)
032-150. An added cell gets storage allocated and initialized per the new source. (§14.8.1)
032-151. An added operator instance gets internal cell state allocated and initialized per the new source. (§14.8.1)
032-152. A removed behavior is deregistered from the behavior table. (§14.8.1)
032-153. A removed cell is dropped per §14.7 in reverse-declaration order. (§14.8.1)
032-154. A removed operator instance has its internal cells dropped per §14.7. (§14.8.1)
032-155. A re-initialization pass recomputes, from current inputs, the initial value of each derived whose behavior body changed (different content-addressed ID). (§14.8.1)
032-156. Deriveds whose behavior body is unchanged retain their values. (§14.8.1)
032-157. Reload commits the reloaded state as an explicit step after re-initialization. (§14.8.1)
032-158. After commit, the reload lock is released: signal/attr writes resume and any queued writes apply to the new state. (§14.8.1)
032-159. Reactive cell values persist across hot reload: signal, attr, and derived cached values change only when the source explicitly changes them. (§14.8.2)
032-160. Graph topology persists across hot reload. (§14.8.2)
032-161. Matched operator instances preserve their internal cell state across reload by the operator-instance-identity scheme of §13.17.10, via the same cell-identity mechanism (§13.15.2) used for top-level cells. (§14.8.2)
032-162. Changing the body of an existing behavior (same signature, different implementation) is reload-safe. (§14.8.3)
032-163. Adding new behaviors, signals, attrs, or derived declarations is reload-safe. (§14.8.3)
032-164. Reload-unsafe changes fall into two classes per §13.15.4: those requiring full-runtime restart and those needing only per-instance restart. (§14.8.3)
032-165. Changes to the reactive state buffer layout that would relocate live cells require a full-runtime restart. (§14.8.3)
032-166. Operator-specific changes (operator signature changes, internal cell type changes per §13.17.10) need only per-instance restart: only the affected operator instances are recreated. (§14.8.3)
032-167. All other changes — cell removal (compile-gate-verified unreferenced), cell type changes (remove + add per §13.15.2), connection topology changes (remove + add per §13.15.2) — are reload-safe and need no restart. (§14.8.3)
032-168. The implementation diagnoses unsafe changes at reload time and either rejects them — the runtime keeps running the old version — or cleanly applies the appropriate restart, full-runtime or per-instance per §13.15.4. (§14.8.3)
032-169. Whether an unsafe change is rejected or handled by restart is implementation-defined. (§14.8.3)
032-170. If the new source fails to compile (parse, type, or ownership error), the reload is abandoned and the runtime keeps running the previous version. (§14.8.4)
032-171. The CLI surfaces reload compilation errors to the user. (§14.8.4)
032-172. Hot reload never yields an inconsistent runtime: either the old version continues running or the new version is fully applied, never a mix. (§14.8.4)
032-173. Each Ductus release is a matched set of four versions: source format, graph specification schema, behavior ABI, and runtime binary. (§14.9)
032-174. Cross-version mixing is not supported: a program produced by version X.Y compiles with and runs against the same X.Y toolchain. (§14.9)
032-175. Forward and backward compatibility across major version boundaries are explicit, not implicit. (§14.9)
032-176. Version metadata is recorded in the graph specification header (§15.4), where cross-version compatibility checks happen. (§14.9)
032-177. There is no source-level version directive; matched-set versioning is carried entirely by the toolchain and the graph-spec header. (§14.9)

## 033. Compilation Model & IR — 247 Rules

033-1. Compilation transforms Ductus source files into executable form plus the build-time artifacts the runtime consumes at startup. (§15)
033-2. The compiler emits exactly two artifact classes: executable code and the reactive graph specification. (§15.1)
033-3. Executable code is bytecode in interpreter mode or a native executable in native mode. (§15.1)
033-4. The reactive graph specification is a build-time description of the program's reactive shape that the runtime consumes at startup and hot reload diffs against. (§15.1)
033-5. Both artifact classes are produced by the same shared frontend. (§15.1)
033-6. Backends fork only at the final lowering step, where each consumes the IR to produce its executable artifact. (§15.1)
033-7. The spec does not prescribe the compiler's internal IR shape; any phase organization satisfying the §15.2 obligations is permitted. (§15.1)
033-8. The required analyses are obligations, not phases; the compiler may organize them into any combination of passes as long as each program is accepted or rejected per the semantics of §§1–13. (§15.2)
033-9. Lexical and syntactic parsing per this specification is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-10. Name resolution (per §3.4, §10) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-11. Type checking (per §2, §4, §6, §7, §9) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-12. Trait resolution (per §3.4.2, §3.7) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-13. Borrow checking (per §11.9) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-14. Monomorphization (per §2.3) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-15. Reactive-graph extraction (per §13, §15.4) is a required compiler obligation. (§15.2.1)
033-16. Failing any required obligation is a compile error; the compiler rejects the program before emitting any artifact. (§15.2.1)
033-17. Name resolution depends on parsing. (§15.2.2)
033-18. Type checking depends on name resolution. (§15.2.2)
033-19. Trait resolution depends on type checking. (§15.2.2)
033-20. Borrow checking depends on type checking. (§15.2.2)
033-21. Monomorphization depends on trait resolution and type checking. (§15.2.2)
033-22. Reactive-graph extraction depends on monomorphization. (§15.2.2)
033-23. Cells in generic node types are extracted per-instantiation. (§15.2.2)
033-24. The compiler may interleave the obligations across passes as long as their dependency partial order is preserved. (§15.2.2)
033-25. The spec prescribes no particular compiler IR layering or pass count. (§15.2.2)
033-26. The reference implementation's canonical AST → HIR → MIR → backend layering is non-normative. (§15.2.2)
033-27. Diagnostic quality, error-message formatting, and recovery behavior are implementation-defined. (§15.2.3)
033-28. The spec requires only that ill-formed programs be rejected and well-formed programs be accepted per the semantics of §§1–13. (§15.2.3)
033-29. The graph specification carries all build-time metadata the runtime needs at startup: behavior table, string-pool seed, and schema and format versions. (§15.3)
033-30. Both backends produce the same graph specification; only the executable-code form differs. (§15.3)
033-31. In interpreter mode both compilation artifacts are held in memory by the running runtime. (§15.3.1)
033-32. In interpreter mode hot reload replaces the in-memory artifacts in place. (§15.3.1)
033-33. In native mode both artifacts are embedded in the compiled binary. (§15.3.1)
033-34. At native-mode program startup the runtime deserializes the graph specification and registers the behavior table. (§15.3.1)
033-35. The Ductus IR is the backend-agnostic contract between frontend and runtime and the unit a hot reload diffs. (§15.4)
033-36. Every backend — compiled, interpreted, or a future alternative — is implemented against the Ductus IR. (§15.4)
033-37. An IR module has three parts over a shared type table: `module <name> { types {…} graph {…} behaviors {…} }`. (§15.4)
033-38. The `types` table maps each `%TypeId` to its layout: size, align, and field/variant order. (§15.4)
033-39. The graph IR is declarative data — cells and their dependency edges, gates, streams, effects, and scopes — referencing behaviors by id. (§15.4)
033-40. The behavior IR is the pure compute that each derived, gate, or effect-`desired` runs. (§15.4)
033-41. Graph and behaviors meet only through the behavior ABI: the graph names a behavior by id and the runtime invokes it. (§15.4)
033-42. Both IR parts speak one monomorphic type vocabulary; the frontend has specialized all generics before emission. (§15.4)
033-43. The IR type vocabulary is the §4.1 primitives plus `str`, tuples `(T,…)`, arrays `[T;N]`, record/enum `%TypeId`s, `pool_index<%PoolId>`, and `closure<(T,…)->R>`. (§15.4)
033-44. IR `str` is a pooled-string index. (§15.4)
033-45. Records and enums appear in the IR as `%TypeId` references whose layout lives in the `types` table. (§15.4)
033-46. `pool_index<%PoolId>` is the IR type for dynamic-size values. (§15.4)
033-47. Behaviors are fully typed. (§15.4)
033-48. The graph is type-erased to tag plus size/align. (§15.4)
033-49. The IR's text form is normative: it is the only serialization this specification defines and what tests assert against. (§15.4)
033-50. No binary or wire encoding of the IR is mandated; any such encoding is implementation-defined. (§15.4)
033-51. An implementation need not externalize the IR at all; holding it in memory across the frontend→backend boundary is conforming. (§15.4)
033-52. The graph is built from exactly six primitives: `cell`, `connection`, `gate`, `stream`, `effect`, and `scope`. (§15.4.1)
033-53. Each graph primitive references behaviors by id and is identified by a stable fully-qualified path. (§15.4.1)
033-54. All surface reactive constructs desugar into the six graph primitives. (§15.4.1)
033-55. Surface `signal`, `attr`, `derived`, `recurrent`, and `const` all lower to `cell`. (§15.4.1)
033-56. Surface `node` and `part` lower to `scope`. (§15.4.1)
033-57. `repeat` lowers to a dynamic `scope`. (§15.4.1)
033-58. `when` and `given` lower to `gate`. (§15.4.1)
033-59. Surface connections and `|>` lower to `connection`. (§15.4.1)
033-60. An operator lowers to a `scope` with ports. (§15.4.1)
033-61. A surface stream lowers to the `stream` primitive. (§15.4.1)
033-62. A surface effect lowers to the `effect` primitive. (§15.4.1)
033-63. A `scope` is the structural container for a node, part, or operator instance, or a `repeat`'s per-element instance. (§15.4.1)
033-64. A scope's `exposes` list holds the ordered placements the runtime traverses — child scopes and references to the scope's connection entries, in exposition order (engagement positions). (§15.4.1)
033-65. A scope's hosted effects live in a separate `effects` set and never appear in `exposes`. (§15.4.1)
033-66. A `dynamic` scope additionally carries a `keyed_by` identity for `repeat`. (§15.4.1)
033-67. `repeat` is the only mount/unmount construct; gates merely freeze. (§15.4.1)
033-68. Scope hierarchy is encoded in the entries' fully-qualified paths. (§15.4.1)
033-69. A cell entry's `id` is the cell's fully-qualified declaration path. (§15.4.1)
033-70. A cell entry's `type` is a primitive type tag per §4.1, extended with the string-pool-index and dynamic-pool-index types. (§15.4.1)
033-71. A cell entry's `observability` is one of `cross_thread_snapshot`, `cross_thread_atomic`, or `confined`. (§15.4.1)
033-72. A cell entry's optional `cadence_hint` is one of `realtime`, `bounded`, or `lazy`. (§15.4.1)
033-73. A cell entry's optional `initial_value` is the compile-time initial value for reactive-safe initializers. (§15.4.1)
033-74. A cell entry records `size` and `alignment` explicitly — derivable from `type` but recorded for cross-implementation interop. (§15.4.1)
033-75. A connection entry's `from` is the source instance's fully-qualified path. (§15.4.1)
033-76. A connection entry's `to` is the destination instance's fully-qualified path, or `null` for sink-side connections. (§15.4.1)
033-77. A connection entry records the connection's declared `connection_type`. (§15.4.1)
033-78. A connection entry's `attrs` is an ordered list of `(name, value)` pairs. (§15.4.1)
033-79. A connection entry's optional `when` is a gate predicate encoded as a behavior ID plus its input-cell list. (§15.4.1)
033-80. Derived dependency edges are recorded as `(derived_cell_id, [input_cell_ids])` pairs. (§15.4.1)
033-81. The runtime uses derived dependency edges for dirty-set propagation and topological evaluation ordering. (§15.4.1)
033-82. Recurrent dependency edges are `(recurrent_cell_id, [input_cell_ids], output_history_N, input_lookback_map)` tuples. (§15.4.1)
033-83. A recurrent edge's `input_cell_ids` are the non-self references that drive re-evaluation (implicit triggers). (§15.4.1)
033-84. A recurrent edge's `output_history_N` is the declared `[N]` self-history depth, defaulting to 1. (§15.4.1)
033-85. A recurrent edge's `input_lookback_map` maps input cell IDs referenced via `.past(k, ...)` to their maximum `k`. (§15.4.1)
033-86. A recurrent whose expression is an `observe` block additionally carries the observe's per-arm trigger sets. (§15.4.1)
033-87. Each gated instance records its predicate in compiled form: a behavior ID plus the input cell IDs the predicate reads. (§15.4.1)
033-88. Each gated instance records a `gate_parent`: the path of the nearest enclosing gated instance, or `null` if none. (§15.4.1)
033-89. The runtime composes an instance's own predicate with its `gate_parent` chain to obtain effective activation, which drives transitive freeze and suspend/resume delivery. (§15.4.1)
033-90. Block selectors `when` and `given` lower to per-arm gates: each arm becomes a gated subtree. (§15.4.1)
033-91. A `when`-block arm's gate predicate is the arm's guard. (§15.4.1)
033-92. A `given`-block arm's gate predicate is the arm's variant test against the scrutinee. (§15.4.1)
033-93. The compiler encodes declaration-order priority and exhaustiveness into arm predicates so arms are mutually exclusive by construction (arm i's effective predicate conjoins the negations of all earlier arms' guards). (§15.4.1)
033-94. Arm predicates still evaluate against runtime cell values each commit. (§15.4.1)
033-95. The behavior table is a list of `(behavior_id, debug_name, input_cell_ids, output_cell_id?)` entries. (§15.4.1)
033-96. Behavior IDs are content-addressed per §14.6.3. (§15.4.1)
033-97. The runtime binds behavior IDs to function pointers at program startup. (§15.4.1)
033-98. A stream entry's `id` is the stream's fully-qualified declaration path. (§15.4.1)
033-99. A stream entry's `element_type` is the element's type tag, per §15.4.1's cell `type` encoding. (§15.4.1)
033-100. A stream entry's `policy` is `ring` or `gate`. (§15.4.1)
033-101. A stream entry's `capacity` is an integer literal. (§15.4.1)
033-102. A stream entry's `source_dependencies` are the input cells the source expression reads, used for dirty-set propagation when the source is a derived chain. (§15.4.1)
033-103. A stream entry's `observation_cell_ids` name the synthesized observation cells: `pending_count`, `pressure`, `is_full`, `dropped_total`, `rejected_total`, `last_overflow_at`. (§15.4.1)
033-104. A stream entry's `reset_on_reload` boolean is true exactly when the stream carries `@reset_on_reload`. (§15.4.1)
033-105. A stream entry's `output_history_size` is the integer N from `recurrent[N] stream`, or 0 for a non-recurrent stream declaration. (§15.4.1)
033-106. `output_history_size` determines the number of past-event slots allocated for `.past(k, ...)` access on the stream's output. (§15.4.1)
033-107. A stream entry's `input_lookback_map` maps input cell IDs read via `.past(k, ...)` in a recurrent stream's body to integer max-`k`, determining per-input history allocation. (§15.4.1)
033-108. A stream's `input_lookback_map` is empty for non-recurrent streams and for recurrent streams whose body calls no `.past` on inputs. (§15.4.1)
033-111. An effect entry's `id` is the instance's fully-qualified path — the binding name or pipe-position site. (§15.4.1)
033-112. An effect entry's `effect_type_name` is the effect's declared type name, used to dispatch to the host's reconciler. (§15.4.1)
033-113. An effect entry's `parameter_bindings` are `(parameter_name, source_cell_id | value_literal)` pairs. (§15.4.1)
033-114. An effect entry's `desired_cell_ids` name the cells declared in the instance's `desired:` block. (§15.4.1)
033-115. An effect entry's `observed_cell_ids` name the cells declared in the instance's `observed:` block. (§15.4.1)
033-116. An effect entry's `gate_parent` is the path of the nearest enclosing gated instance, or `null` if the effect is never gated. (§15.4.1)
033-117. The runtime uses an effect's `gate_parent` to compute effective activation and to time `suspend`/`resume` reconciler-hook delivery on enclosing-subtree gate transitions. (§15.4.1)
033-118. Reconciler dependencies are `(effect_type_name, [concrete_type_parameters])` pairs the host must register via `runtime.register_reconciler` before the runtime can enter the live state. (§15.4.1)
033-119. A non-generic effect's reconciler-registration key has an empty type-parameter list. (§15.4.1)
033-120. Each generic-effect instantiation is a distinct reconciler-registration key. (§15.4.1)
033-121. The string pool seed lists the program's string literals, pre-loaded into the pool at startup. (§15.4.1)
033-122. The graph specification records a schema version: the Ductus toolchain version that produced it, per §14.9. (§15.4.1)
033-123. The graph specification records a format version: the abstract data model's own version, distinct from the toolchain version. (§15.4.1)
033-124. A cell ID is a dot-separated identifier path from module root through enclosing instances to the cell name: `audio.synth_a.osc_1.frequency`. (§15.4.1.1)
033-125. Cell IDs are derived deterministically from source: lexical nesting plus declared instance/cell names. (§15.4.1.1)
033-126. Cell-ID syntax is identical to the hot-reload identity of §13.15.2; the two are one mechanism, stated in source-level versus wire-format terms. (§15.4.1.1)
033-127. A cell ID for anonymous or duplicated sibling placements carries the ordinal suffix `:N` in the wire format, with N the zero-based declaration-order index among same-type siblings at the same nesting depth. (§15.4.1.1)
033-128. Cell IDs are stable across the same source compiled by any conformant compiler. (§15.4.1.1)
033-129. Cross-implementation hot reload at the same source version yields matching cell IDs by construction. (§15.4.1.1)
033-130. `observability` declares the concurrency contract the cell's storage must honor. (§15.4.1.2)
033-131. `cross_thread_snapshot` guarantees multi-thread readers a snapshot-consistent view, cross-cell consistency within one commit transaction, and no torn reads. (§15.4.1.2)
033-132. `cross_thread_atomic` guarantees single-cell atomic reads with no cross-cell consistency guarantee. (§15.4.1.2)
033-133. A `cross_thread_atomic` cell's value must fit in one 64-bit atomic slot. (§15.4.1.2)
033-134. A `confined` cell is accessed only from one thread; no atomics are required. (§15.4.1.2)
033-135. The mapping from observability class to storage mechanism is the runtime's concern; any mechanism satisfying the class's contract conforms. (§15.4.1.2)
033-136. `cadence_hint` is informational; the runtime uses it only to bias storage-mechanism selection. (§15.4.1.3)
033-137. `realtime` means updates are deadline-bound and readers cannot block. (§15.4.1.3)
033-138. `bounded` means updates are committed but not deadline-bound; it pairs with any observability value. (§15.4.1.3)
033-139. `lazy` means updates are best-effort and the cell tolerates large staleness. (§15.4.1.3)
033-140. A cell without a `cadence_hint` is treated as `bounded`. (§15.4.1.3)
033-141. The compiler assigns each cell's observability class from its declaration kind and the access pattern observed during reactive analysis. (§15.4.1.4)
033-142. Top-level `signal` cells default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-143. `attr` cells on node/connection instances default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-144. `recurrent` cells on node/connection instances default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-145. `derived` reactive cells default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-146. Stream cells (head pointer plus metadata) default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-147. Stream observation cells default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
033-148. Host-written effect `observed:` cells default to `cross_thread_snapshot`. (§15.4.1.4)
